## Page 51

Chapter = c03 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:27 am
CHAPTER 3
Assessing Opportunities and Risk
Focusing on the Largest Fish1
Many companies, after completing a recovery risk assessment, want to
immediately catch every ﬁsh—big or small. However, just as it’s impossi-
ble for a batter to hit ﬁve baseballs pitched at the same time, a company
with a limited recovery and fraud detection budget won’t be able to correct
every problem at the same time. You need to determine not just the likeli-
hood of the frauds but also their signiﬁcance as you make your priority list.
You can do this qualitatively but because we need to follow the money, the
quantitative data analysis is usually most beneﬁcial.
Prioritizing with the Qualitative Approach
Before we discuss the meat of this section—detecting the most signiﬁcant
areas for recovery—let’s step back and ask some questions about the orga-
nization, or even more speciﬁcally, key departments.
■ Who has the most spending authority within the company?More author-
ity leads to higher spending and, therefore, more opportunity for fraud.
■ Which departments are prone to error based on past reviews? History
tends to repeat itself at companies, so there is no need to be creative;
just look to the past.
■ Has the organization recently been part of a merger or acquisition?Obvi-
ously, such events increase the size of the organization. But they also
result in the stretching of employees, who can become quite unhappy.
■ Has the organization experienced rapid growth, increasing business
demands, and exposure? Transaction volume can rise at these times and
processes become even more complex, which can be fertile ground for
cost recovery.
25

---

## Page 52

Chapter = c03 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:27 am
26 Cost Recovery
■ Has your organization recently had a system conversion? With new
technologies or system implementations, the priority becomes simply
ensuring the transactions are processed, with little time left for verifying
their accuracy.
Answering these questions can identify departments that, generally
speaking, have the largest amounts of company processing prone to error
and fraud.
Prioritizing with the Quantitative Approach
After the qualitative assessment, identify a few departments for review but
validate and expand this assessment based on a dive into the company
databases. The main areas of focus will be revenue, cost of goods sold, and
sales and general administrative, and so you should obtain these data ﬁles
(for the last two or three years).
■ General ledger trial balance.General ledger ﬁle providing the summary
balance for every general ledger account.
■ Accounts payable invoice header. Accounts payable invoices with one
invoice listed per row in the data.
■ Accounts payable invoice detail distribution. Working from the invoice
header ﬁle, this ﬁle provides the general ledger account distribution for
every invoice so each row in this data ﬁle is a portion of the invoice
amount allocated to a general ledger account.
■ Payroll register header.Compile a list of all employee payments with one
employee payment in each row of the table. Preferably, a department
code is provided for each employee.
■ Sales history invoice header. Sales invoices with one invoice listed per
row in the data. This will mostly be used to identify order to cash issues
whereby the company is not charging its customers for the full value of
its products and services.
After obtaining these data ﬁles, ﬁlter and summarize to home in on those
departments and the employees (as the data allow) with the most unusual
trends. You can complete this analysis in Microsoft Excel (Excel 2007 might
be best because it allows for a little more than one million rows of data)
through the use of the AutoFilter option and Pivot Tables. Also, perform the
following analysis to prioritize speciﬁc opportunities:
■ Align the company’s trial balance expense categories to external bench-
marks for the industry and review any unusually high trends more
closely. Visit bizstats.com for a free tool for industry benchmarking

---

## Page 53

Chapter = c03 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:27 am
Assessing Opportunities and Risk 27
trial balance data. Also, visit bizminer.com and proﬁtcents.com, among
others, for paid subscription services.
■ Compare material spending categories, based on a summary by general
ledger account, to the list of cost recovery categories discussed in this
book.
■ Find any increasing trends over time that could be in a general ledger
account or a vendor, which could, for example, warrant a vendor audit
to ensure contractual and other pricing compliance.
■ Create a scattergraph of volume to values for general ledger accounts or
vendor balances. For more information on creating a scattergraph, see
the next section.
■ Summarize vendor history by general ledger account and vendor, which
will quickly identify any sole-source vendors that might be the best can-
didates to competitively bid. It might also detect general ledger accounts
that are serviced by too many vendors and therefore could use a cen-
tralization of spending to improve purchasing power in a given expense
area.
■ Perform simple duplicate payment tests based on the invoice header
ﬁle by invoice number and vendor. A simple test using a Microsoft
Access query might yield recoveries or spot an opportunity for a recov-
ery ﬁrm to perform a more detailed analysis. It might also highlight a
vendor scheme to purposely send a repetitive duplicate invoice to a
company.
So while a qualitative approach can lead to savings opportunities, it’s
best to enhance that approach with quantitative analysis. Through some
simple data analysis of company spending, revenue, and other general
ledger accounts balances—most of which can be performed in spread-
sheet tools—opportunities can be identiﬁed for fraud detection and other
bottom-line savings.
Scattergraphing: A Powerful Tool to Identify Changes
in Company Activity2
Sometimes a cost recovery exercise appears daunting because of an exten-
sive amount of data that needs to be sifted through to identify exceptions.
Scattergraphs can be developed for any data that have an X- and a Y-axis
designation. What is uncanny is that thousands of unique data points can
be represented in the sample scattergraph and even more so that the vast
majority will cluster around the center axis of the graph. This leaves a
few exceptional accounts to review more closely, which is done using a
methodology discussed later in this section.

EXHIBIT 3.1 DataforScattergraph
As a practical example, an Excel spreadsheet can be developed with
each supplier listed on a row, and in one column, the invoice amount
difference between this and last year, and in another column, the invoice
transaction volume difference between this year and last year. These data
can be seen in Exhibit 3.1.
By selecting columns B and C, the Chart option can be selected from
the Insert menu item and then the scattergraph can be selected to arrive
at the graph in Exhibit 3.2. Please note that 5,726 suppliers are represented
in the graph, yet only 30 (within the boxes on the graph) are exceptional
for additional review.
Now that the graph has been created, it can be explained using a
sectional analysis as further displayed and discussed in Exhibit 3.3.
■ Sections 1. Given that these supplier accounts are high-amount swings
with low invoice count changes, such activity could suggest high-
amount phony or erroneous invoices posted in the current (positive
X-axis) or prior year (negative X-axis). They could also be regular activ-
ity that would be very strange given the relative size of other supplier
purchases by the company.
■ Sections 2. Areas falling into the second section are relational in that
as the number of invoices increases, so do the amounts associated with
those invoices. This linear increase or decrease, while not contradictory,
may also represent the increased opportunity for error and fraud.

30 Cost Recovery
Scattergraphs allow an auditor to focus on the forest rather than the
individual trees in the supplier spend activity. “A picture is worth a thousand
words,” and in this case, a scattergraph can talk to thousands of suppliers
in one simple diagram.
Going Deeper and Data Auditing Vendor Accounts3
In this section, we go one level deeper by using automated tools in uncov-
ering associated error and fraud within supplier accounts. Even if a recovery
auditor is brought in to do the heavy lifting in the audit, these data analysis
tests allow one to gauge the potential recovery levels. When considering
automated tests for this area, one needs to ask the following questions:
■ Does the vendor provide actual goods and services to the company or
is it a fraudulent billing scheme?
■ Has the vendor returned mistaken overpayments to the company?
■ Is the vendor charging the negotiated pricing and is it consistent with
the marketplace?
■ Are vendor inventories becoming obsolete?
Does the vendor provide actual goods and services to the company or
is it a fraudulent billing scheme? The following reports can ﬂush out any
vendors that aren’t providing anything to the company and are, in essence,
shells. (The list isn’t complete, of course, but it does provide some of the
most likely places where a fraudulent vendor will turn up.)
■ Check blank address information. Filtering or sorting a vendor master
ﬁle by address, phone number, bank account details, and tax ID can
reveal shell vendors.
■ Identify vendors added during the period under review. It’s completely
normal for companies to frequently add new vendors. But it’s proba-
bly more unusual than normal to see a new vendor’s material valued
invoices shortly after the vendor is signed. Therefore, scrutinize those
new vendors with material new purchases.
■ Review payments with little or no sequence between invoice numbers.
Complex algorithms can detect such occurrences in phony invoices,
but you could have the same result by sorting the data ﬁle by vendor
and invoice number and then manually scanning the records.
■ Match the vendor master ﬁle to the employee master ﬁle on various key
ﬁelds. It’s amazing how many employees still forget to use different
addresses or a post ofﬁce box from its own when fraudulently paying
themselves. Match the two master ﬁles by telephone number, address,
tax ID numbers, or bank account details.

---

## Page 57

Chapter = c03 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:27 am
Assessing Opportunities and Risk 31
Has the vendor returned mistaken overpayments to the company? Run-
ning the following reports might identify actual fraudulent transactions on
the company’s books or vendors that are ripe for an audit because they have
the highest probability for holding on to credits due the company.
■ Duplicate payments based on the invoice number, invoice date, ven-
dor number, and invoice amount. You can ﬁnd duplicate invoices with
any combination of these data ﬁelds by using the Microsoft Access
Duplicates Wizard or ActiveData for Excel. The duplicates could be
fraudulent attempts by employees or vendors to increase payments to
the vendor or innocent errors. But they could all be eligible for cash
recovery.
■ Extract round dollar payments and summarize by vendor.Most software
uses the MOD( ) function to identify round dollar items paid to vendors.
For example, =MOD(A1,1000) would determine if cell A1 in Microsoft
Excel is perfectly divisible by 1,000. If the result of this function is 0.00,
then the amount in A1 is perfectly divisible by 1,000. Also, the 1,000
can be changed to 10000000 to determine all invoices that are perfectly
divisible by 1,000,000.
■ Final payments to any vendor that exceed the 12-month average pay-
ments to that vendor by a speciﬁed percentage (such as 200 percent).
Scrutinize all vendor payments that are larger than usual but particularly
concentrate on categories that are more likely to be associated with
recovery and fraud such as consulting, cleaning services, maintenance,
and so on.
■ Summarize debit memos by vendor, issuer, and type. In the accounts
payable trial balance, which represents all open accounts payable
invoices, debit memos are negative items that are unusual because they
are associated with company-vendor adjustments. Increasing trends or
unapplied debits could be employee attempts to cover unauthorized
payments or could represent forgotten credits from vendors that repre-
sent cash recovery to the company.
■ Review vendor statements to company activity.If you can obtain vendor
statements that have all open and paid invoices and adjustments on the
vendor’s books, you can compare the statements to company activity.
You will quickly notice any overpayments of invoices or adjustments not
realized by the company and they could highlight issues of a vendor’s
management of the company’s account.
Is the vendor charging the negotiated pricing and is it consistent with
the marketplace? You’ll probably have to look outside your company for
marketplace trends to assess the reasonableness of pricing. You might
have to purchase external databases that track product pricing or team up

---

## Page 58

Chapter = c03 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:27 am
32 Cost Recovery
with strategic sourcing consultants with experience in the area. The cost is
usually offset by identifying any overpriced items and improving future ven-
dor negotiations. Remember that any kickbacks a vendor gives to employees
are generally based on increased proﬁt margins offered to that vendor. If
you can minimize such margins, the kickbacks and other perks offered to
employees also will be minimized.
■ Identify increasing vendor relationships. Align vendor activity sum-
maries for the previous three years and you can identify increasing or
other unusual trends.
■ Locate sole-sourced vendors. Summarize accounts payable activity by
general ledger account and vendor and you can quickly identify any
general ledger accounts with one vendor. Those might be legitimate
but also could represent inappropriate bidding procedures and lack of
competition.
■ Match negotiated price tables to company purchase orders. Pricing on
purchase orders normally can be adjusted to be higher or lower than the
contract price with the vendor (stored in a separate data table). Compare
the two to highlight unique error situations and long-term inappropriate
trends.
■ Calculate the ratio of the largest purchase to the next-largest purchase by
vendor. Any large ratio difference might identify a fraudulently issued
largest purchase.
■ Calculate the annualized unit price changes in purchase orders for the
same product in the same year. Increasing trends within the past year
compared to the two previous years can quickly locate speciﬁc products
wrapped up in a kickback scheme.
Are vendor inventories becoming obsolete? While much of the focus to
this point has been on identifying overpayments from the perspective of
pricing and invoice payments, a vendor can also be overpaid through quan-
tities ordered. This will become apparent when looking at inventory through
the lens of normal company usage and obsolescence.
■ Age inventory by the date of last part issuance. Quickly ﬁnd inventory
that has been overpurchased and is sitting on shelves by looking at the
last time parts or products were issued or sold.
■ Calculate for each part or product the number of months that it’s been in
the inventory and extract those with a high number of months. A sum-
mary of the inventory usage–sales ﬁles by part number over the previous
year can be aligned back to the current inventory. Then a simple divi-
sion of the on-hand inventory units to the total sales over the previous
year can determine the number of years remaining in inventory usage.

---

## Page 59

Chapter = c03 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:27 am
Assessing Opportunities and Risk 33
Any exceptionally high “years left” represent a potential for intentionally
overpurchasing inventory.
■ “Trend in” obsolete inventory over two or more periods. Inventory older
than a few years will most likely be considered obsolete. Summarize by
vendor any inventory falling into and, if possible, trend to vendors in
the prior period with obsolete inventory.
If a company did even half of the above reports, it will most likely
identify enough strange occurrences to warrant a full recovery audit in at
least one vendor area. These reports help to get beyond the normal fear,
uncertainty, and doubt associated with supplier payments to provide speciﬁc
examples for use by the organization in deciding the best next direction for
recovery audit.
Using Vendor Toolsets
While these work efforts focus on the general accounts payable spend, there
is no reason why one could not rely on speciﬁc recovery provider systems
to audit its spend. This could be done on a fee basis, but it is usually given
for free at the start of an audit to help show the opportunity. While this can
even be done in accounts payable to save the company the effort of doing
the aforementioned work, these speciﬁc toolsets could be in the areas of:
■ Advertising agency and media spend
■ Freight
■ Health care
■ Payroll tax credits
■ Order to cash
■ Sales and use tax
■ Travel and entertainment
By allowing the service provider to assess risk using its data analysis,
it puts the company in the position of determining whether it wants to
move ahead with the full audit. Most providers will still suggest to press on
even if there is not yet seen any opportunity in the reports because some
recoveries exist beyond what is in the data. For example, vendor credits
held by a vendor may never be seen on the client’s books.
The Top 10 Questions to Ask When Deciding to Do a Recovery Audit
While this chapter has focused mostly on performing a variety of data anal-
ysis to identify opportunities for recovery, the following is a simple list of

---

## Page 60

Chapter = c03 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:27 am
34 Cost Recovery
questions for consideration. While this is never as effective as upfront data
analysis, it is a quick acid test to determine if you need a recovery audit.
1. Is your organization under pressure to reduce costs, increase cash ﬂow,
and thereby raise proﬁtability and shareholder value? Finance profes-
sionals need to spend more time working on strategic ﬁnance initiatives
that deliver more value, rather than plug proﬁt leaks, which is a detailed-
driven, time-consuming activity.
2. Has your organization had a merger or acquisition recently, resulting in
stretched limits of IT capacity and increased opportunities for proﬁt
leaks? Fragmented across disparate systems, applications, and orga-
nizational boundaries, the procure-to-pay cycle and processes suffer,
opening the door for payment errors.
3. Has your organization experienced rapid growth and increasing busi-
ness demands and exposure? Transaction volume rises and processes
become even more complex, laying fertile ground for proﬁt loss. The
results are duplicate and erroneous payments, unrecognized returns,
pricing errors, penalties, and unclaimed discounts.
4. Has your organization experienced turnover in its accounts payable
department? Even if controls are in place, employee turnover can be
draining on a company’s process knowledge and efﬁciency.
5. Has your organization recently had a system conversion? With new
technologies or system implementations, the priority becomes simply
ensuring that transactions are processed, with little time left for verifying
their accuracy.
6. Does your organization process a large number of disbursements,
demanding higher levels of thoroughness and security? When hundreds
of thousands of transactions are handled each year, all it takes is for
one-tenth of 1 percent to be inaccurate, thereby leading to vendor
overpayments.
7. Does your organization experience a large volume of returns to vendors
or adjustments to their accounts? Vendors do not send credit memos
and many do not mail credits. It is the policy of many vendor com-
panies to take old overpayments into income that are one year old.
This presents an opportunity for recovering credits that are due to the
company.
8. Are your organization’s contracts complicated, producing greater price
volatility and multiple sourcing options? Even a good contract can end
up exposing a company to legal liabilities and unforeseen expenses if
a company does not ensure that the terms of the contract are properly
enforced.
9. Does your organization experience frequent price changes on goods
or increasing transactional volumes? Changes in vendor relationships

---

## Page 61

Chapter = c03 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:27 am
Assessing Opportunities and Risk 35
present a need for organizational structures and policies. Recovery ven-
dors can provide these best practices and assess whether the changes
are being properly reﬂected in the vendor payments.
10. Does your organization have complex freight arrangements? Freight
terms and freight payment processes need to be closely monitored in
an effort to keep transportation expenditures down and ensure accurate
processing of related invoices.
As all of these focus on general trends across the entire procurement
cycle, speciﬁc risk assessment questions are provided in Chapter 5 that can
be asked to determine whether speciﬁc recovery areas are more prone to
risk, and therefore a cash recovery opportunity.
Notes
1. Richard B. Lanza, “Focusing on the Largest Fish,”Fraud Magazine, Association of
Certiﬁed Fraud Examiners, Austin, Tex., July/August 2007.
2. Richard B. Lanza, “Scattergraphing, Quickly Finding Needles in a Haystack,”Fraud
Magazine, Association of Certiﬁed Fraud Examiners, Austin, Tex., May/June 2007.
3. Richard B. Lanza, “Auditing Vendor Accounts for Fraud, or at Least Some Cash
Recovery,” Fraud Magazine, Association of Certiﬁed Fraud Examiners, Austin,
Tex., September/October 2007.

## Page 62

Chapter = c04 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 11:45 am
CHAPTER 4
The Overall Cost Recovery
Marketplace
How the Industry Worked in the Past
Twenty-ﬁve years ago, a recovery auditor who has helped in the production
of this book worked for a Toronto-based recovery audit consulting ﬁrm. Its
specialty was in comparing the volume discounts that different clients were
getting, and helping negotiate reductions for anyone not getting the lowest
rate. The ﬁrm received a contingency fee for the savings achieved, typically
50 percent. In those days, every step in the process was manual. By one
method or another, a thick ﬁle for each client soon accumulated in the
consulting ofﬁce, sometimes running hundreds of pages. All of these data
were reviewed by hand, looking for an opportunity for a better discount.
It was a business built almost entirely on personal experience and
insight, and on the credibility of personal relationships. There were few
uniform procedures, no standards, no courses, and no books. Mechanically
processing the client’s energy bills, checking the arithmetic, would not reveal
anything by itself. Success depended on broader knowledge of market con-
ditions, on knowing what everyone was paying and what changes to the
price of oil were likely in the near future. The analyst had to judge whether
the client’s current price could realistically be reduced or not, and make
recommendations accordingly.
For the recovery ﬁrm, the answer to the three questions was the same for
each client: perform a prospective analysis of the client’s purchases against
industry benchmarks. At the time, there was simply no alternative. Every
scrap of client information had to be processed manually since all the infor-
mation was on photocopied, manually generated invoices. The digital age
had barely begun in 1984. The ﬁrst tentative steps toward automated analysis
were becoming possible with the arrival of personal computers, but no one
knew then just how business methods would evolve. The utility recovery
36

---

## Page 63

Chapter = c04 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 11:45 am
The Overall Cost Recovery Marketplace 37
ﬁrm had one of the early ofﬁce computers, a suitcase-sized Kaypro with Per-
fectCalc spreadsheets, but it was used only to generate billing of clients, not
to do analysis for them. Once the IBM AT became available, with dBASE
database software, the ﬁrm began to explore methods of tracking prices
automatically and handling larger client volumes with the same number of
staff.
As often happens at small audit and consulting ﬁrms, these ambi-
tious plans were overtaken by events. The recovery ﬁrm ﬁrst won, and
then quickly lost, an enormous contract with a Canadian utility that would
have doubled its annual revenues. In the wake of the ﬁasco, the salesman
responsible for landing the deal died of heart failure, and several staff mem-
bers defected to form their own independent spinoff. The automated price
tracking system was never built, and the ﬁrm eventually ceased operations.
Recovery Consulting Today
That was the recovery audit business a generation ago. In some ways, of
course, consulting never changes. It is still a business dominated by personal
experience and personal relationships. Today’s recovery experts with market
insight make forward-looking recommendations very similar to those issued
by the utility recovery ﬁrm of the 1980s, only in many more ﬁelds. What
has changed fundamentally is the practice of accounting itself, and the tools
and methods available.
There has been an evolution in recovery over the past three decades.
Recovery auditors have gradually been shifting from specialized to generic
methods as computers have allowed recovery ﬁrms to automate what was
once in the brains of recovery ﬁrm managers. Recovery service is an infor-
mation business whereby consultants who have prior experiences are able
to apply this knowledge to clients. Automation allows for this knowledge
to be collected and programmed for routine generic audits with auditors
following speciﬁc process steps.
Not to say that specialized audits are not needed, but rather it is easier
to have one person transfer his knowledge to a computer system and then
have a team of less experienced auditors follow speciﬁc procedures and
programmed computer reports to get to a better result. As evidence for this
trend, the accounts payable and procurement area is now well-serviced in
terms of the number of recovery audit service providers available. There are
close to a hundred nationally known ﬁrms operating in the United States
alone that offer recovery in some form. Most were founded in the 1990s.
This leads to another beneﬁt that automation has provided to recovery
ﬁrms. The more generic fact-checking variety of recovery analysis that has
become common today, focused on ﬁnding and ﬁxing errors, was not on

---

## Page 64

Chapter = c04 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 11:45 am
38 Cost Recovery
offer 25 years ago. Looking for duplicate bills based on manual account-
ing was long, tedious, labor-intensive work. At most consulting ﬁrms, such
work was left to internal auditors. Even for internal auditors an item-by-item
review was a practical impossibility, and they tended to limit themselves to
sampling.
This is where huge changes have occurred: An in-depth retrospective
analysis of every transaction for a ﬁscal period is now standard (otherwise
known as 100 percent auditing using computer-assisted techniques). Now
the answer to the three questions posed at the beginning of this chapter
can be different. Recovery clients are starting to say: Let’s do in-depth fact
checking and analyze hypothetical information in relation to all of these
areas—the customers, the vendors, internal staff, and the government. The
computing tools exist to do much more.
The Evolving and Continuous Recovery Process at Companies
The cost recovery industry mostly started on the backs of a few specialists
who decided to turn their knowledge of a given cost area into a proﬁtable
business. While some have automated their techniques to not only encapsu-
late that knowledge into a computer system for safekeeping but also to help
audit 100 percent of the client data, most still work with less-than-superior
technology. Even where the best technology is used, the internal audit staff
is unlikely to have the time, experience, energy, or enthusiasm to undertake
a review in the same way an external consultant might.
Companies in turn have been realizing that they should work with
recovery ﬁrms, but that they need to not only automate what they do
internally, they also need to bring some of the cost recovery knowledge in-
house. Some have started their own internal recovery projects, some use the
recovery ﬁrms’ technology, and most do a hybrid of the two. Given this, the
following is a usual way that cost recovery happens at a company. Not that
it is standard or even ideal but rather what has evolved as a way companies
are integrating their internal departments with cost recovery ﬁrms. The step
process is as follows:
1. Begin with a quick data gathering of accounts payable processing met-
rics, which include accounts payable spend, number of invoices, and
number of vendor accounts. Then, ask questions around the risks and
controls within the accounts payable process.
2. Identify any low-hanging fruit, such as large duplicate payments or rec-
onciliation of material wire payments to vendor invoices. The goal here
is to simply make sure that high-value recoveries are not detected by
outsiders.

---

## Page 65

Chapter = c04 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 11:45 am
The Overall Cost Recovery Marketplace 39
3. Hire an accounts payable recovery ﬁrm to identify duplicate payments,
overpayments, taxes owed, and any other values owed to the com-
pany. Also request from the accounts payable recovery ﬁrm any best
practices for improving their processes. At the end of this step, enough
cost recoveries should be detected to whet the appetite of the company
to complete additional recovery efforts.
4. Using the accounts payable audit as a baseline, and now with data ﬁles
around speciﬁc vendor invoices extracted from the computer system,
risk-assess additional areas for cost recoveries into the deeper areas of
vendor compliance and industry benchmarking. As with the accounts
payable effort, risk-assess each area from a value spend (quantiﬁcation)
and from a risk-and-control (qualiﬁcation) perspective.
5. Based on the ﬁndings from the recovery efforts, use consulting ser-
vices to remediate any issues in company processes, technology, and in
the people themselves, through improved knowledge training. Recov-
ery ﬁrms can provide a wealth of knowledge that can be reapplied by
internal staff for the beneﬁt of the company. This knowledge can also
translate into computer processing routines, such as an improved dupli-
cate payment test or a system reconciliation control between vendor
invoices and expected vendor invoice payments.
6. Consider establishing a recovery department in-house that will either
be a part of the department itself (that is, a person within the accounts
payable department spends a portion of her time looking for com-
pany overpayments) or could work independently of the department
to identify savings.
7. Have recovery auditors come back for select areas to complete sec-
ondary audits that will act as a second defense against any recoveries
not identiﬁed through internal efforts. The idea here is that regardless of
the internal controls existing within a company and any internal recov-
ery efforts, mistakes still can be made. Furthermore, recovery ﬁrms are
constantly identifying new avenues to cost recovery and can bring new
ideas to the company for cost savings.
Integration is the key, as the more a company combines its external
recovery efforts into its routine operations, automating techniques as much
as possible along the way, the more the company will beneﬁt.
Why Recovery Consulting Is so Popular
When considering some of the key reasons why recovery auditing has
gained popularity in the last 20 years, one has to consider some of the
business trends that lend to cost recovery’s success:

---

## Page 66

Chapter = c04 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 11:45 am
40 Cost Recovery
■ People in companies are always ﬁghting a six-headed hydra,constantly
focusing on the dragon’s head that is currently snapping at them. There-
fore, there is little time for improvement, best practices, and the tedium
involved in reviewing transactions for recovery. The less they improve
their business processes, the more that falls into the net of cost recovery
ﬁrms.
■ People don’t want to get their hands dirty. Employees don’t want to
do a lot of the dirty work before rising up the ranks of management.
However, diamonds are in coal... not in the sky with Lucy. Seemingly,
no one wants to carry out the tedious task of reviewing the details that
then lead to recoveries. Recovery ﬁrms can meet this need while saving
employee time in performing the review.
■ Many times, companies will add employees to their workforce rather
than improve processes or technology. This leads to more people work-
ing a bad process and, hence, more issues processed, leading to
recoveries.
■ Companies are constantly downsizing and outsourcing. This creates
additional issues due to a lack of touch by the people working the
process with those who developed the process. This is coupled with
the situation that accounts payable departments are constantly under-
staffed, overworked, and receive a relatively small portion of the budget
for technology improvement purchases. Since they are the main peo-
ple paying the bills, they tend to be the main culprits for processing
errors.
■ Most accounting systems were built to process transactions and not to
provide users access to the data for reporting purposes. Reporting mod-
ules are usually the last components to be implemented into a computer
system and, hence, they are not very effective. Occasionally, every sys-
tem has to allow duplicates to be forced on to the system for legitimate
reasons. Inevitably, this allows staff to force on other less legitimate
transactions. Recovery auditors are skilled in data analysis and the types
of reports that are the most fruitful, given that they have seen various
accounting systems. Therefore, it is best to learn from them by watching
them on a few engagements.
■ Some company managers believe it is simply cheaper to hire a recovery
auditor than it is to acquire better processes, people, and technology. In
other words, it is sometimes viewed as better to pay a recovery fee than
it is to invest in company improvements. Assuming a 25 to 30 percent
recovery fee on the 0.1 to 0.3 percent of processing failure (which works
out to an average cost of 0.0005 multiplied by the transaction ﬂow),
that could be simply viewed as a cost of doing business. While this
looks risky on the surface, it is a smart ﬁnancial move, especially if the
processing volumes or values are not massive.

---

## Page 67

Chapter = c04 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 11:45 am
The Overall Cost Recovery Marketplace 41
While recovery is a large and growing consulting sector, with a large
pool of consulting talent, most ﬁrms still do not take advantage of it. Even
though generic recovery is growing in popularity, and ought to be practiced
by everyone, it is not. There are several paradoxical yet important truths at
work here.
The ﬁrst is that the basic fact-checking recovery has become compara-
tively easy to do—certainly easier than some other kinds of recovery—and
it is absolutely universal in its applicability. Organizations vary enormously
in what they buy, or how much they spend, but, for example, all accounts
payables departments overpay or miss discounts. So it follows that nearly
every consultant, whatever other expertise he offers, ought to offer generic
recovery analysis. Areas like telecommunications, utilities, taxes, freight, and
so on are the territory of specialists. They require experience and knowledge
beyond basic audit principles. But in theory at least, any auditor should be
able to do generic payables recovery.
The second, related truth about recovery is that while lots of people do
it, few excel at it. This is true of specialized recovery work, but it is just as
true of generic recovery. For anything above the level of basic audit, there
is a surprising skill shortage, not only among internal auditors but among
consultants as well. Although the trend is toward automation, a surprising
proportion of recovery consultants still manage to get along without soft-
ware tools, preferring to rely on spreadsheets, simple databases, and manual
review. They combine basic audit principles with basic audit tools and do
what amounts to a basic audit.
Dimensions of Recovery
Before plunging into the various categories of recovery (the varied sections
of Chapter 5), some deﬁnition of terms is in order. These terms are more
like dimensions of a 3-D cube:
■ Directions of recovery
■ Methods of recovery
■ Timing of recovery
Thus, at the start of any recovery audit we have three main questions
to answer:
1. What audit area does it relate to and what is its direction? (Internal
error and fraud, external error and fraud, industry benchmarks, or tax
advantages)
2. What method of analysis will be used? (Fact checking or analysis of
hypothetical situations)

---

## Page 68

Chapter = c04 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 11:45 am
42 Cost Recovery
3. What timing direction will the audit proceed in? (Prospective or retro-
spective)
In practice, real-world recovery audits are often a mixture of these cat-
egories. These are handy concepts but an actual audit sprawls across these
neat boundaries very quickly. For example, a payables audit that ﬁnds fre-
quent and suspicious overpayments for the current calendar year is not
necessarily just an internal problem, or just an external one. In the begin-
ning, one cannot know for sure. The problem may involve in-house staff
as well as vendor representatives, and may be the result of error, fraud, or
both.
In other words, the answer to the three questions today might be: all
of the above. While most audits do not ﬁt into one neat category to the
exclusion of all others, these categories are useful because they relate to the
development of the recovery audit industry.
Dimension 1: Directions of Recovery
There are four broad directions for organizations that want to pursue recov-
ery auditing that are relatively self-explanatory and will be further outlined
in the case studies later in this chapter:
■ Internal error and fraud (focus on employees)
■ External error and fraud (focus on vendors and customers)
■ Industry benchmarking (focus on competitors)
■ Tax advantages (focus on government)
Dimension 2: Methods of Recovery
All of these areas can be productive. For each area, the auditor can choose
between two primary methods:
1. Basic fact checking (what happened as compared to what should have
happened)
2. Analysis and forecasting (what will happen next)
The ﬁrst and more common method is a generic audit that starts with the
existing facts on ﬁle, that is, the actual invoices, canceled checks, customer
orders, usage records, contracts, correspondence, and so on. The auditor
compares documents in search of duplicate payments, missed discounts,
failure to follow agreed pricing, contract violations, and other kinds of dis-
crepancies or processing errors. This kind of audit tends to be very literal. It
operates by the same broad rules regardless of the speciﬁc content—whether

---

## Page 69

Chapter = c04 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 11:45 am
The Overall Cost Recovery Marketplace 43
the subject is revenue coming in or payables going out, and whether trans-
actions relate to capital equipment, travel, rent, services, or any other item.
For example, if two checks are made out to the same payee, show
the same amount, and reference the same vendor invoice, then they may
be duplicates of one another. A basic contractual analysis simply com-
pares what is in the contract with what was actually done. If the contract
references special purchasing terms or discounts, a returns policy, or allo-
cation of freight charges, then the auditor’s task is simple. She just wants to
know if the discount was actually taken, if returns were credited, if freight
charges were split fairly in accordance with the signed agreement, and
so on.
The second method is analysis based on speciﬁc expertise. This usu-
ally requires a certain amount of hypothesizing and inductive logic; that is,
bringing together both the facts on ﬁle and background knowledge not in
the ﬁle. It can be a little more creative as a consequence.
Generic fact checking simply aims to conﬁrm that the documents cor-
respond to the facts, and that whatever was done was supposed to happen.
Beyond generic fact checking, we enter the realm of possibility—how the
outcome could have been better, and what might happen if circumstances
change. Reviewing a particular purchasing area for tax advantages or price
sheet compliance requires knowledge of the relevant tax law and the mar-
ket for that area. Obviously, when it comes to these speciﬁc areas, the more
knowledge, the better. This is the realm of specialization, where experience
and creative imagination can both sometimes play a big role. Consultants
who specialize in freight rates, advertising, telecoms, or some other area are
often paid not for mechanically conﬁrming what was done, but mostly for
their ingenuity in ﬁnding a better way something could have been done.
Dimension 3: Timing of Recovery
Apart from dividing audits into two methods, we can also divide them into
two orientations: toward the past (retrospective auditing) or future (prospec-
tive auditing).
A retrospective audit usually has several motives in mind. First, it is
always worth considering whether funds paid out can actually be recovered.
This kind of literal recovery after the fact is very attractive, even though it
presents obvious challenges. Vendors and other payees are understandably
reluctant to reopen a completed transaction to their disadvantage. Yet it can
be very worthwhile, and in the case of duplicate payments and other simple
errors, the negotiation need not be long or complicated. We should never
turn down easy money!
Apart from recovering funds, a retrospective audit can aim at ﬁxing pro-
cess problems, identifying risks, developing a better policy, sizing a fraud,

---

## Page 70

Chapter = c04 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 11:45 am
44 Cost Recovery
and much more. The retrospective audit can take place in any of the ﬁve
areas listed earlier, and can use either or both of the two analytical meth-
ods noted. Thus, a retrospective audit of price sheet compliance in corporate
travel might combine rigorous computer-aided analysis of the past ﬁve years
of purchasing in that area, plus interviewing and research into market con-
ditions, eventually arriving at the conclusion that the organization has been
consistently overpaying for travel.
The simpler investigative direction is the prospective or future-oriented
approach. All audits are in some sense prospective, because they all aim
at adding value to future operations through incoming cash and process
improvements. The difference is that a strictly prospective audit treats the
past not merely as a sunk cost, but as no longer relevant even as simple
information. The auditor engaged in a prospective recovery project is natu-
rally interested in preventing future problems from occurring, but chooses
to focus on facts relating to the present and the future.
Sometimes the facts considered by a prospective recovery audit are
really just hypothetical, which is why the past does not matter. For exam-
ple, an oil and gas company might perform a study relating to possible tax
advantages in the next federal budget. In such a case, whatever rules applied
in the past are treated as irrelevant; all that matters is the present set of rules,
and how changes to those rules will affect future operations.
To make sense of the overpayments will typically require meticulous
study of all recent invoices and payments, as well as contracts, price sheets,
and other agreements with vendors. If the subject matter is at all complex, the
investigation will also require interviews and outside research, as mechani-
cally sorting and comparing a ﬁle of transactions will not reveal the reasons
for events. Determining the scope of the problem will involve a careful ret-
rospective review, going back as many years as necessary. Then to actually
stop the overpayment pattern and repair the controls will require a prospec-
tive approach, perhaps including ﬁnding and qualifying a new supplier, or
terminating one or more employees.
Recovery Categories
With the dimensions of recovery understood, the main subject areas of
recovery work (to be further explained in Chapter 5) need to be quickly
explained. Each will be dealt with in more detail later. For each of these
areas there are specialist ﬁrms willing to do consulting, and there are also
foolproof techniques of basic audit that a ﬁrm’s own audit department ought
to apply on its own. Apart from describing the various areas of work, this
chapter lays down some guidelines on when and how to engage an outside
consultant in each area, and what tasks are suited to “do it yourselfers.”

---

## Page 71

Chapter = c04 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 11:45 am
The Overall Cost Recovery Marketplace 45
■ Accounts payable. To identify duplicate payments, overpayments,
statement errors, the elimination of redundant vendors, and missed dis-
counts. The recovery ﬁrm then reclaims the monies owed by suppliers
or vendors and aids in repairing any issues in the process. This is the
broadest and most straightforward area of recovery audit, one that never
fails to produce results. Every organization should consider doing this
for itself.
■ Audit fee benchmarking. To assess audit fees paid by a peer group of
companies within a similar industry using updated SEC audit fee data.
This can be done with minimal help from an information service.
■ Advertising agency. To identify duplicate payments, unapplied credits,
vendor rebates, contract pricing variances, overall contract compliance,
and the accuracy of vendor and supplier transactions. Specialist ﬁrms
are a great help in this area.
■ Document ﬂeet. To improve the productivity and proﬁtability of doc-
ument imaging assets by using best practices, industry expertise,
advanced technologies, and right-sizing techniques. This is perhaps best
done internally, by using technology and methodologies to capture the
cost and usage of the document ﬂeet, which can lead to a right-sizing
of current assets and improve the negotiation for future equipment
decisions.
■ Freight. To shed light on the many areas susceptible to proﬁt leaks,
including rate negotiation, logistics, inventory overpurchasing, customer
pricing, loss claim ﬁling, and collection and minimum order strategies.
Here again, a specialist in freight audits can add value.
■ Health beneﬁts (for insurance carriers). To assist insurers in the
identiﬁcation and recovery of overpaid claims, duplicate payments,
coordination of beneﬁts (COB) errors, and excess payments based on a
client’s contract.
■ Health beneﬁts (for providers). To identify and rectify processing errors
related to the increasingly intricate claim submission process. Sorting
through complicated contract terms reduces back-end rework due to
errors and credits. Health is one of the most challenging audit areas,
and outside consultants can help both insurance carriers and providers.
■ Lease. To audit lease payments. Because leases are complex, it is not
uncommon for landlords to make errors when calculating expense
bills. Unfortunately, most tenants do not have the time or resources to
scrutinize these bills for errors. A lease audit includes analysis and inves-
tigation of expense charges, scrutiny of lease wording, cost comparison
to market rates, physical review of landlord records, the management
of claims, and the recovery of overpayments.
■ Media. To help evaluate the historical performance of media delivery.
Outside experts can also quantify the media efﬁciencies that could be

---

## Page 72

Chapter = c04 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 11:45 am
46 Cost Recovery
attained from a consolidation of media planning and buying activities,
and can assess the media buyer’s stewardship processes and perfor-
mance.
■ Order to cash.To analyze customer pricing and credit afforded to clients,
and to ensure that all orders are invoiced. Expert help in this area is
plentiful, but it is also one of the more straightforward, basic audit tasks
that automated systems can help perform.
■ Payroll tax credits. A variety of U.S. state and federal tax credits exist
for companies. The credits are generally based on employee demo-
graphics and geographic location of the company and employee. There
are hundreds of federal and state credits worth billions of dollars that
go unclaimed every year by U.S. businesses. The value of the credits
vary—depending on the type of credit—but a company can earn up to
$9,000 per eligible employee.
■ Proactive fraud detection. To perform agreed-upon procedures, which
includes data analysis, interviews, and documenting other company
observations. The goal is to identify actual fraud so that it can be recov-
ered and to deter employees from committing fraud in the future. Expert
knowledge of currently popular fraud schemes is a big advantage in
this area, but knowledge of basic antifraud controls is needed in every
organization.
■ Project fraud. To identify fraud or related mismanagement on major
projects. This can involve gray areas such as misrepresentation of a
project’s mission or progress to secure project ﬁnancing, misuse of
project resources, and improper dealings with project vendors. Experts
in large-scale project fraud will be better equipped to draw the line
between poor judgment and deliberate exploitation.
■ Real estate depreciation. To reassign all construction-related costs that
were originally depreciated under 30-year lives in more realistic and
accurate 5-, 7-, and 15-year lives. A study can be performed on any
remodeled, constructed, or acquired property as far back as 1987. Own-
ers can then take those missed depreciation deductions in the year a
study is conducted without amending prior-year tax returns. Tax recov-
ery work is another area in which paying for expert help saves money
in the long run.
■ Research and development tax credits. This credit was created by
Congress as part of the Economic Recovery Tax Act of 1981 to encourage
American industry to invest in research and development activities by
providing tax incentives. These incentives are not realized by many com-
panies because of the stringent requirements and misunderstandings
regarding their application, yet recovery ﬁrms will assist in translating
the tax code in return for a contingency beneﬁt.
■ Sales and use tax. To ensure that the organization manages according
to sales and use tax guidelines. A recovery audit can provide not only

---

## Page 73

Chapter = c04 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 11:45 am
The Overall Cost Recovery Marketplace 47
potential refunds, but also the assurance that taxes have not been
overpaid. State audits only uncover underpayments, not overpayments.
Again, expert help is strongly recommended.
■ Strategic sourcing. To analyze spending, negotiate, collaborate, and
manage suppliers. Most procurement departments rely on ineffective,
fragmented, and largely manual procedures. Such inadequacies limit
the ability of midsize enterprises to leverage already diminutive spend-
ing volumes, assess supply market opportunities and costs, and drive
continuous improvements in supply costs and performance. This is a
growing ﬁeld and calls for creative partnerships with outside experts.
■ Telecom. To review the organization’s telecommunications costs. This
includes all services, both ﬁxed (line rentals) and variable costs (call
charges), including local, long-distance, and wireless billing errors,
unnecessary services, and unused equipment. Most consultants will also
perform an analysis of local calling patterns, leading to suggestions for
the service option that will maximize savings. Many consulting ﬁrms will
also negotiate calling plans and equipment upgrades as well as collect
credits for past billing errors.
■ Travel and entertainment. To maintain appropriate controls over
employee spending on travel and entertainment. The areas typically
analyzed are payment tools (corporate cards, cash advances, and so
forth), expense reporting systems, T&E policies, and contracts made
with airlines, hotels, and car rental companies. Savings are identiﬁed
and improved controls are implemented to reduce future proﬁt leaks.
Often the most effective technique is also the most basic: simply to make
the effort to conﬁrm employee expenses line by line.
■ Utilities. To seek out discounts and equipment upgrades that will reduce
overall expenditures. Utility bills have traditionally been dismissed as
uncontrollable. Electricity, water, heating and cooling, or sewage service
costs and taxes are often paid blindly. Recovery audit specialists will
provide clients with cost reduction strategies after assessing equipment,
consumption patterns, state regulations, and billing practices, as well as
taxed and nontaxed rate structures. Many ﬁrms will collaborate directly
with the vendors to obtain credits and negotiate discounts.
How the Recovery Areas Are Deﬁned in This Book
When reviewing the many sections in Chapter 5, the reader should under-
stand the layout of these chapters, which is as follows:
■ Case studies. Actual experiences are used to illustrate the nature of
recoveries.

---

## Page 74

Chapter = c04 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 11:45 am
48 Cost Recovery
■ Case study wrap-up. Knowledge gleaned from the case studies is sum-
marized in the wrap-up section.
■ Recoveries to ﬁnd. While it should be apparent from the case studies
and associated wrap-up, this section speciﬁcally details the cost savings
one will ﬁnd in the recovery area.
■ Questions to ask when assessing the opportunity. The best way to assess
an opportunity is to ask questions, and these focused questions will
help gauge the level of cost recovery available.
■ Conclusion. This section will quickly summarize the recovery area and
provide some ﬁnal statements regarding the recoverable costs.
