Chapter = c05 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:20 pm
CHAPTER 5
Accounts Payable and Procurement
T
he case studies in this section illustrate some key truths about payables
recovery. They are based on real clients of real recovery ﬁrms, but details
from several engagements have been combined and identifying information
altered to maintain conﬁdentiality. Following these case studies will be a
further explanation of accounts payable recovery.
Case Study 5.1: American Auto Runs a Tight Ship
Parts supplier American Auto Corporation (AAC) hired Reputable Recoveries
to perform a payables analysis. The initial scope of the analysis was all
invoices issued in the past two years, nearly $1.5 billion in total. This was
a large job for Reputable, and it had worked hard to sell the client on its
abilities.
AAC top management had been impressed by Reputable’s demon-
stration of its ability to perform a 100 percent automated review of all
transactions, and even more impressed by the sales representative’s claim
that Reputable averaged $2 in recoveries on every $10,000 in transactions.
On the positive side, this might mean actual cash recoveries of $300,000
to AAC. That it would also mean paying roughly $105,000 in contingency
fees to Reputable was quite acceptable, if the savings were actually achieved.
The sales rep was supremely conﬁdent of earning a nice commission, saying,
“When we are dealing with a volume this large, the recoveries are always
worthwhile. Errors happen even in the best-run operation.”
The chief ﬁnancial ofﬁcer for AAC signed off on the agreement, and the
ﬁle summarizing 72,000 invoices to 1,048 suppliers was transferred by secure
This section was completed in collaboration with Meridian Cost Beneﬁt (recov-
eryaudit.com), Pinpoint Proﬁt Services (pinpointproﬁt.com), and Professional Audit
Services (pas-cash.com).
49

---

## Page 76

Chapter = c05 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:20 pm
50 Cost Recovery
means to the Reputable ofﬁce. The AAC audit manager privately doubted
the recoveries would be so large. She had conducted an in-depth audit
of the payables controls just three years earlier and thought AAC was excep-
tionally good at spotting their own errors. But it never pays to contradict
top management in situations of this kind, so she downloaded the data as
requested, and then waited for Reputable to send back the results.
The ﬁrst step for the Reputable analyst was to size the data. The major-
ity of transactions were small, with 41,000 invoices under $1,000 each.
There were another 21,000 invoices between $1,000 and $10,000, and 9,000
invoices larger than $10,000. According to standard AAC procedure, every
transaction over $10,000 was reviewed manually for errors.
The Reputable analyst then used a custom-written proprietary utility
based on ACL Software to carry out a series of standard tests in these
categories:
■ Cash discounts
■ Invoice errors
■ Duplicate payments
■ Credit memos
■ Overpayments
The analyst was especially interested in the lengthy ﬁle of credit memos,
which was much larger than usual for a company this size. In discussions
with the audit department manager, it had quickly become clear why. Like
many auto parts manufacturers, AAC had intricate supply chain relationships.
Depending on the particular item, the company bought from and sold to
many of the same vendors. Sales were sometimes offset by purchases and
vice versa. The process looked very complicated and vulnerable to error.
The reports were surprisingly short. There had been just $225,000 in sus-
picious duplicate payments, the strongest recovery category, and not even
one of the suspicious duplicates was over $10,000. There were some small
discounts not taken, and a number of transactions ﬂagged as overpayments,
but the totals in every category were relatively small. For this kind of vol-
ume, the analyst expected more like $1 million in suspicious duplicates, and
perhaps $2 million in other transactions, but the total across all categories
was less than $1 million in total. The credit memo ﬁle was a different story,
as was expected, given the complicated nature of credit memo usage at the
organization. Five hundred and seventy thousand dollars in ﬂagged items
were in this report.
The list of ﬂagged items in all ﬁve categories was sent to the AAC audit
department for follow-up. Based on past experience, the analyst expected
that about half would turn out to be false positives. These were items that
were ﬂagged by the automated report but could be explained by a review

---

## Page 77

Chapter = c05 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:20 pm
Accounts Payable and Procurement 51
of additional documentation at AAC without contacting the vendor. The
others would be sent out with covering letters to several dozen suppli-
ers. Obtaining a deﬁnite answer on all items typically required from 30 to
90 days, and Reputable’s analyst would often be called in to help explain the
claims. Vendors, not surprisingly, took their time in responding to queries
about billing errors from the previous year.
Nearly all of the outstanding items had been dealt with, and AAC was
prepared to issue a check to Reputable for the recoveries to date. Most of the
recoveries were in the credit memo area because this was the most material
valued report and was due to a process that was more prone to error:
Recoveries so far: $316,420
Contingency fee at 28 percent: $88,598
AAC’s chief ﬁnancial ofﬁcer was more than satisﬁed by the result, as
was the audit department manager. A material amount of money had been
made, and for every dollar the company kept, it only had to pay out 28
cents with minimal staff effort. The exercise had proved that the company’s
internal controls were working very well, as the recoveries were lower than
expected but still a valuable exercise for all interested parties. Reputable
would be asked back in two or three years’ time to repeat the experiment.
There was less rejoicing at the ofﬁces of Reputable Recoveries. This
was not the lowest recovery in percentage terms that Reputable had ever
achieved, nor the smallest fee it had ever earned, but it was somewhat lower
from the $105,000 that had been expected. As the analyst pointed out, it
could have been worse. The client was happy and they would be asked
back. There had been two cases the previous year involving exceptionally
well-run audit shops in which the lists of ﬂagged items had been similarly
short, but both times the negotiations with suppliers had gone badly, and
there had been no recoveries at all. That had been much more humiliating.
This audit still found that an area with expected issues (credit memos) did
lead to a majority of the fee payments and there is no reason why such
an area could still not be fruitful in the future given the nature of AAC’s
business.
Case Study 5.2: Public Utility with Stafﬁng Problems
A Canadian public utility was suffering from severe stafﬁng problems. The
local economy had recently recovered from a decade-long slump, and in
neighboring Alberta, the current price of $140 per barrel for oil had led to a
drilling and production boom unlike anything since the 1970s. Traditionally

---

## Page 78

Chapter = c05 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:20 pm
52 Cost Recovery
underpaid accounting staff were deserting the utility and taking jobs paying
twice as much in the Alberta oil patch.
Audits were already 18 months behind plan before the boom hit, and
the planned scale of audit had been very modest to begin with. The utility
was owned by the Canadian government and, hence, shareholder value
ranked well below other concerns, and for the past decade management
had chronically underfunded the audit department.
The utility audit manager had suggested a recovery audit the previous
year, but the idea had not appealed to the vice president of Internal Services.
“That’s what your staff are supposed to do,” the audit manager was told. “We
don’t like to pay for the same work twice.”
Now with permanent audit positions going unﬁlled for months at a time,
and temporary staff impossible to hire, the audit manager tried again. This
time the VP was more sympathetic, but still did not like the idea of paying a
contingency fee. “Can’t you just get them to come in for a ﬁxed hourly rate?
Why should we hand over a percentage?”
“It seems fair enough. They only get paid if they ﬁnd money for us. And
they have to come from out of province.”
The VP frowned. “They’ll ﬁnd money. What I’m worried about is that
they’ll ﬁnd a lot of money, and we’ll have to write them a huge check. I don’t
want to see this leaked to the newspapers. We’ll have people complaining
about inefﬁcient government monopolies, and even if it was a good idea,
you and I will both wind up regretting hiring them.”
After much discussion, Total Value Audit Solutions (TVAS) was invited
to perform a recovery audit of the most recent six months of invoicing.
If there were signiﬁcant ﬁndings, the audit department would then repeat
the test method on the previous 18 months. This would keep TVAS from
earning any enormous windfall fees and embarrassing the Canadian gov-
ernment. The utility was insistent on another point: TVAS would have to
take a lower contingency percentage, 25 percent of all recoveries instead of
33.3 percent. Also, all work would have to be performed inside the utility’s
headquarters. No data could leave the building. The TVAS sales representa-
tive had dealt with Crown corporations in the past and knew when not to
argue.
The utility had payables transactions totaling $1.2 billion Canadian for
the most recent two ﬁscal quarters. These were collected for analysis by
TVAS. TVAS used an in-house program custom written in Visual Basic and
running on a laptop to review data onsite. It revealed several hundred sus-
picious duplicate transactions, plus some overpayments and other kinds of
errors, worth a total of $3.2 million Canadian.
Receiving such numbers from the analyst left the audit manager visibly
upset, and it was more than a month before a meeting could be scheduled
with anyone from the utility to follow up. The VP of Internal Services sat in

---

## Page 79

Chapter = c05 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:20 pm
Accounts Payable and Procurement 53
on the meeting, and tersely informed the TVAS team, “Not all of those ﬂagged
items were errors. It took a lot of work to review all the false positives. But
we’ve started sending out inquiries on the genuine errors, and so far about
ten percent of the transactions you ﬂagged have led to refunds from our
vendors.”
The TVAS staff looked at one another in amazement. “Ten percent? That’s
great. Our average runs lower than that, about three percent.” Now it was
the turn of the utility employees to look surprised. “We thought you recov-
ered forty or ﬁfty percent. When you said three point two million dollars in
ﬂagged payments, we were thinking we had a public relations disaster on
our hands.” “No, no; that’s the success percentage for items that actually go
out to the vendors and get claimed, after all the false positives are removed
in-house.”
In the end, after the misunderstandings had been sorted out and all
vendors contacted, the utility recovered $374,000 in overpayments, duplicate
payments, and discounts not taken, out of six months’ operations. This was
$3.10 per $10,000 of transaction volume. The total was higher than what
TVAS had expected, and far lower than what the utility had feared. The fee
to TVAS was 25 percent, or $93,500.
When the audit department turned to the task of duplicating TVAS’s
work on the previous 18 months, it was hampered by a lack of staff skilled
in programming and wound up settling for only a partial analysis. Over the
next year, their efforts yielded an additional $312,000. The audit manager
estimated that close to a million dollars in recoveries were missed by not
using TVAS for the full two years. Stafﬁng in the utility audit department
remains a problem.
Case Study 5.3: Contract Compliance with Pinpoint
Proﬁt Recovery Services, Inc.
Two contract compliance audits were performed for Pinpoint’s client, which
covered a two-year rotation audit. The ﬁrst compliance audit performed
resulted in a $2.2 million recovery for the client and then, two years later,
an additional $1.7 million was recovered on a second follow-up audit. The
work centered on integrating client procurement data that were previously
managed in separate systems.
Pinpoint used its data analysis tools to build a model system that
integrated the pricing, purchase order, and accounts payable module sys-
tems into one data analysis engine. As would be expected, the client’s
nonintegrated approach to managing its business led to additional costs.
These included:

---

## Page 80

Chapter = c05 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:20 pm
54 Cost Recovery
■ Pricing changes completed by the procurement department in the
pricing module that were not reﬂected in the purchase orders, also
developed by procurement.
■ Purchase order changes that were made by procurement did not carry
forward to the ultimate accounts payable payments, so overpriced
invoices received by vendors were not kicked out in error.
■ Freight charges for goods not meeting company quality standards
were returned to the company’s vendors but the freight charges were
still paid for by the company. In these instances, since the ven-
dor caused the issue, it is required to pay such charges yet the
company’s systems could not isolate these returned products and
hence, the freight supplier was able to charge the company accor-
dingly.
While Pinpoint did recover funds for identifying these issues, it also
helped the client realize that it had a problem, and the client subse-
quently implemented an integrated system to reduce its recoveries in
the second-year audit. Future audits also led to fewer recoveries for the
free recommendation to implement an integrated system as suggested by
Pinpoint.
Case Study 5.4: International Personal Goods
Manufacturer with Retail Outlets
Pinpoint Proﬁt Recovery Services was engaged to perform a recovery audit
of historic payments over a three-year period. Pinpoint’s audit methodology
calls for conducting a customized and comprehensive supplier statement
review for its clients.
The ﬁrst step in the process was to compile a list of suppliers who should
receive a request for a statement. Pinpoint worked closely with the client to
customize and compile the list. The list was initially determined by supplier
spend size and transaction volumes. On the basis of Pinpoint’s experience
conducting scores of supplier statement reviews, however, the list was ana-
lyzed thoroughly before presenting it to the client for approval. It was just
as important to determine what suppliers to eliminate from the list as what
suppliers to include for mailing. For example, certain individuals on the ven-
dor master ﬁle, such as members of the board of directors or employees,
must be excluded. Also to be excluded were governmental agencies, taxing
jurisdictions, and some utilities. By becoming familiar with and understand-
ing of the client’s business and operations, a number of additional suppliers
appearing on the list were excluded.

---

## Page 81

Chapter = c05 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:20 pm
Accounts Payable and Procurement 55
Pinpoint presented the edited supplier mailing list to the client for review
and approval. The client whittled the list down further by eliminating suppli-
ers with whom there were ongoing disputes or with whom the company was
currently working on a statement reconciliation. The client then informed
procurement to make it aware of the soon-to-be-ready supplier statement
request.
The client-approved list contained the names of 840 suppliers who
were to be contacted. Based on Pinpoint’s experience and the process it
employed, conducting an effective and comprehensive statement review
would take at least 250 supplier contacts, which take the form of an initial
letter, follow-up letters, phone calls made to nonresponders, and e-mailed or
faxed correspondence to complete the review and reconciliation for every
100 suppliers on the mailing list. This amounts to over 2,000 supplier contacts
in various forms to thoroughly complete 100 percent of the tasks involved
in conducting the statement review. One may ask, “Why so many supplier
contacts?” The main answer is that supplier “remit to” addresses used for
the mailing that go to a lockbox often do not reach the accounts receivable
department. Supplier tardiness is also a key factor.
The audit team began to receive replies to the request for a supplier
statement. Each statement was reviewed for completeness and accuracy.
Questions asked included: “Has the supplier included all of the clients’
accounts in the submission?”; “Why did the supplier submit an Excel ﬁle
statement rather than an accounts receivable system–generated statement?”;
and “Has the supplier included open credit transactions in the statement?”
Pinpoint’s experience shows that supplier oversight results in negative
answers to those questions. Regardless, all of these questions require con-
tacting the supplier to request additional information and to obtain further
clariﬁcation of the statement information provided.
Pinpoint did precisely that to record 49 recoveries totaling approxi-
mately $118,000. Some of the typical recoveries Pinpoint recorded involved
obtaining open and unprocessed credit memos for returned goods, unap-
plied cash in an account that was not refunded, miscellaneous overpayments
and supplier overcharges, and repayment of unused deposits. Pinpoint
obtained refund checks, copies of unprocessed credit memos, and written
supplier authorization to deduct the open credits from a future payment.
Pinpoint noted that the majority of the recovery dollars were beyond
the control of the accounts payable department. Open credit memos for
returned goods were either not issued by the supplier or were not for-
warded to accounts payable for processing from internal company areas.
Pinpoint consequently went on to perform a more detailed review of the
returns process, yielding additional recoveries and process improvement
recommendations.

---

## Page 82

Chapter = c05 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:20 pm
56 Cost Recovery
Case Study Wrap-Up
Accounts Payable in General
While many of the specialized recovery areas are mostly focused efforts
with fewer actual recovery claims, accounts payable auditing is probably
the most volume-based. This requires technology and organized process
steps for vendor follow-up.
An efﬁcient analyst equipped with suitable software tools can handle a
million transactions about as easily as 10,000. The result in either case will
be a set of ﬂagged items—ranging from dozens to thousands—that need
further investigation. The next step, reviewing each item and, when neces-
sary, contacting vendors, is much more labor intensive. This means that the
process of sorting and ﬂagging transactions is where the engagement either
succeeds or fails. There is no universally recognized method for ﬂagging
items. Different analysts approach automated testing in different ways. Ide-
ally, they want a low false positive rate, but not at the expense of missing
items that could have been recovered. Analysts who are skilled in balancing
these conﬂicting priorities are relatively rare, and there is a perpetual labor
shortage in the ﬁeld.
This leads to a question that should always be asked: What is the con-
sultant’s recovery efﬁciency; that is, what proportion of items identiﬁed for
recovery are actually paid? It is not unusual for the success rate in actual
recovery to be well below 50 percent, even among items that are forwarded
to vendors as high priority. A payables recovery consultant whose clients
enjoy high recovery efﬁciency may be preferable to one that generates
numerous recovery targets (and lots of labor by the client to follow them
up) but ends up delivering a low proportion of actual dollars in savings.
One last learning point from the case studies is that while recovery tests
could be proﬁtably carried out by everyone, they rarely are. In practice,
organizations vary widely in their enthusiasm and level of preparation for a
recovery engagement. Some are very proﬁcient at in-house recovery, while
others are lax. Some welcome outside help, others resist it. Ideally, any
recovery engagement should be accompanied by a plan to acquire the skills
in-house. This is most apparent in the accounts payable recovery area, as
many of the basic ﬁndings (that is, duplicate payments, tax adjustments, and
so forth) can be easily understood and identiﬁed by anyone given simple
training.
Supplier Statement Reviews
A few considerations need to be kept in mind when a company considers
a vendor statement audit.

---

## Page 83

Chapter = c05 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:20 pm
Accounts Payable and Procurement 57
■ Company needs to approve the list. Only when approved does the
supplier statement mailing happen, because sending such a statement
request prematurely could result in negative vendor feedback (for ven-
dors with company disputes) or wasted efforts (for companies that are
too small or have no intention to respond).
■ Keep it professional. As one can see, a signiﬁcant amount of supplier
communication needs to occur in order to complete the statement
review in an effective and timely manner. All supplier communication
must be cordial, sensitive, and professional. Maintaining and enhanc-
ing strong supplier relations in conducting the statement review is of
paramount importance to Pinpoint’s audit team.
■ A lot of deep digging.Although the supplier statement review recovered
some low-hanging fruit, a signiﬁcant amount of money that was recov-
ered and gained was as a result of the audit team working with suppliers
to obtain complete and accurate statements beyond their initial submis-
sion. For example, Pinpoint noted that several large suppliers provided
incomplete statements because they were unable to locate all of the
client’s accounts. Pinpoint was able to identify additional unreported
client accounts to the supplier’s attention based on Pinpoint’s review
of the supplier’s paid invoice history in the accounts payable system to
obtain the missing account numbers.
■ Don’t let it go stale. For a variety of reasons, the majority of suppliers
no longer issue monthly or timely statements of account as a part of
their accounts receivable process. This lack of statement visibility also
presents a number of business issues to accounts payable and procure-
ment departments, one of which is the ability to prevent and curtail
supplier mischief in dealing with open credits. Accounts payable man-
agement, therefore, needs to become more aggressive in order to regain
the visibility of company funds retained by their suppliers in the form
of open credits or unapplied cash on account.
Furthermore, aged open company credits on supplier’s accounts
receivable systems have a shelf life. As a result, sometimes both the
supplier and the buyer question the credit validity because of the age of
open credits. Thus, the complexity and the age of the credit transactions
could result sometimes in fuzzy rationale and logic that aged credits
should be written off.
It is, therefore, prudent and necessary to conduct a statement review
on an ongoing basis by a qualiﬁed and experienced recovery audit ﬁrm. A
supplier statement review by a recovery audit ﬁrm can often yield as much
as 10 to 25 percent of the recovery dollars recorded during a typical recovery
audit engagement.

---

## Page 84

Chapter = c05 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:20 pm
58 Cost Recovery
Recoveries to Find
Payables recoveries commonly occur in eight categories:
1. Duplicate payments are the most frequent form of recovery and the
largest as measured by total dollar volume. Duplicates often arise near
the end of a ﬁscal period. An invoice is paid and then paid again in
the next cycle. The discrepancy is harder to detect because the records
do not all appear in the report for the current month or quarter. These
are generally easy to obtain refunds on because they are so clearly the
result of error.
2. Pricing errors typically consist of mistakes made by vendors and are not
caught by the purchasing department. They might be simple mechanical
errors (improper completion of the purchase order price) or more sub-
tle policy-related errors such as improper price protection, improper
margin protection, missed markdowns, overlooked promotions, and
so on. Recovery from a pricing policy error can be more challeng-
ing. The purchasing department should have documentation regarding
vendor policies. If a purchase is eligible for a promotion, the pur-
chasing department should know it and should have some record
of it.
3. Discount errorsare mostly mechanical and relatively easy to detect. They
happen when cash is paid but the standard 2 percent cash discount is
not taken. Sometimes, there is an arithmetic error so the wrong amount
is taken instead.
4. Overpayments can arise from paying the wrong invoice, or paying an
invoice that was already handled using a credit memo, or from other
kinds of error. Overpayment becomes challenging to negotiate with
suppliers if there is a high volume of monthly transactions. Many organi-
zations are moving to monthly statements from vendors so that charges
and payments are consolidated in one record.
5. Allowances that are not claimed properly can amount to large sums of
money. They may relate to many speciﬁc areas: advertising, freight, pro-
motions, defective items, market development. Allowances are usually
a policy matter, and there may be unwritten understandings concerning
them.
6. Taxes can easily be overpaid if there are errors relating to speciﬁc
questions (such as the proper application of sales and use tax, or of
value-added tax).
7. Open vendor debits. Based on all of these error patterns, vendors will
maintain open credits (otherwise known as debit balances to the com-
pany), which are based on reconciliations of vendor payments and the
entire account. These vendor debits will be maintained by many vendors

---

## Page 85

Chapter = c05 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:20 pm
Accounts Payable and Procurement 59
until the company asks for them or takes them off a payment. Some ven-
dors go so far as to hide them from statements they send the company.
8. Accounts payable and procurement process improvements. While this
may not lead to immediate cash into the company, the recovery ﬁrm
will bring a host of process improvements to the company, which will
bear fruit in subsequent years. Improvements may be in the areas of
days payable outstanding (the speed to pay a vendor and the interest to
be earned for paying vendors slightly later), implementing procurement
cards for small dollar purchases, and practices to prevent duplicate
payments in the future.
These eight general categories are constantly being expanded as ﬁrms
identify additional error patterns at one client that they can apply to all of
their clients. One recovery ﬁrm, Professional Auditing Services, has identiﬁed
a speciﬁc list of 72 recovery categories that it shares with clients, which goes
into more explicit detail around the eight categories and then talks to the
following additional areas:
■ Excess freight, freight allowances, and duplicated freight charges
■ Returned goods allowances
■ Improper application of credit memos
■ Misstated handling charges
■ Sales taxes paid in error
The list of recovery types will change over time. For example, as com-
panies move their small-amount purchases to a procurement card, thereby
improving payment efﬁciency, they also open the door for paying a credit
card charge through the card statement and then on the speciﬁc invoice that
may be mistakenly sent by the vendor to the company. Each door that opens
to change the accounts payable process also opens the door to another error
pattern.
Questions to Ask When Assessing the Opportunity
Taking into consideration the case study experiences and the types of recov-
ery, the following is a list of questions that can help frame the opportunity
size of an accounts payable recovery. They have been organized into the
areas of people, process, and technology:
I. People
1. Has the organization experienced turnover in its AP department?
Even if controls are in place, employee turnover can be draining
on a company’s process knowledge and efﬁciency.

---

## Page 86

Chapter = c05 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:20 pm
60 Cost Recovery
2. Has there been an increase in the number of personnel in the AP
department to make up for invoice processing issues? Adding many
additional people at once can affect the error rates at a company.
II. Technology
1. How easy is it to extract accounts payable information from the com-
puter system? If this question is answered positively, an audit is then
easier to do. If this question is answered negatively, however, while
it will take more time to start an audit, it should be more fruitful
because it will probably mean that no one has previously analyzed
the data.
2. Has the organization recently had a system conversion? With new
technologies or system implementations, the priority becomes simply
ensuring that the transactions are processed, with little time left for
verifying their accuracy.
3. Are there controls in the accounts payable system to ensure complete
and accurate invoice entry, with no duplication?Under the garbage-
in–garbage-out principle, the better the computer system is at pre-
venting garbage from being entered into the system, the better chance
there is that an error would be detected in an invoice payment. The
following are some additional detailed questions to ask in this area:
a. Are preprogrammed keying formats used to ensure that data are
recorded in the proper ﬁeld, format, and so on?
b. Are the following checked for validity on all input transactions:
i. Characters?
ii. Reasonableness?
iii. Consistency? (Are the data consistent with other ﬁelds?)
iv. Completeness? (Are any ﬁelds missing?)
v. Extraneous data? (Have too many ﬁelds been completed?)
c. Are error messages produced for each transaction containing data
that do not meet edit requirements?
d. Are users prevented from overriding or bypassing data vali-
dation and editing problems? If users are not prevented, are
error reports produced that summarize errors for management’s
review?
e. What system controls prevent (or detect) duplicate recording of
transactions?
f. What system controls prevent (or detect) duplicate recording of
vendors?
III. Process
1. What is the volume and value of the accounts payable transactions?
While almost every company can beneﬁt from accounts payable
auditing, at lower levels (that is, under $10 million or under 1,000
transactions per year) it is less likely that recovery audit beneﬁts

---

## Page 87

Chapter = c05 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:20 pm
Accounts Payable and Procurement 61
will outweigh the costs associated with the audit. When hundreds
of thousands of transactions are handled each year, all it takes is for
one-tenth of 1 percent to be inaccurate, therefore leading to vendor
overpayments.
2. Does the organization have an allowance and rebate program with
your vendors? Such programs increase complexity and, therefore,
error rates.
3. Does the organization experience a large volume of returns to vendors
or adjustments to their accounts? Vendors do not send credit memos
and many do not mail credits. It is the policy of many vendor com-
panies to take old overpayments into income that are one year old.
This presents an opportunity for recovering credits that are due to the
company.
4. Does the organization have complex freight arrangements? Freight
terms and freight payment processes need to be closely monitored
in an effort to keep transportation expenditures down and ensure
accurate processing of related invoices.
5. Are the organization’s contracts complicated, producing greater price
volatility and multiple sourcing options?Even a good contract can end
up exposing a company to legal liabilities and unforeseen expenses if
a company does not ensure that the terms of the contract are properly
enforced.
6. Does the organization experience frequent price changes on goods,
or increasing transactional volumes?Changes in vendor relationships
present a need for organizational structures and policies. Recovery
vendors can provide these best practices and assess whether the
changes are being properly reﬂected in the vendor payments.
7. Are there controls in the accounts payable process to ensure complete
and accurate invoice entry, with no duplication?The more controls in
place to review an invoice entry or payment, the more likely an error
would be detected in an invoice payment process. The following are
some additional detailed questions to ask in this area:
a. Are budgets maintained and reviewed to compare against actual
expenses on a periodic basis?
b. Do adequate written procedures exist regarding activities on
accounts payable and cash disbursements?
c. Are purchase orders used and how are associated prices approved?
If purchase orders are not used, how are purchases approved
before executing services and products with the vendor?
d. Are prices charged by vendors reconciled to a vendor agreement
or reviewed for reasonableness?
e. Does a vendor master ﬁle clean-up process take place on a routine
basis?

---

## Page 88

Chapter = c05 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:20 pm
62 Cost Recovery
f. Do procedures ensure all cash discounts are taken when provided
on invoices?
g. What controls exist to ensure payment terms provided by the ven-
dor are actually taken?
h. Do you request vendor statements and reconcile such statements
to vendor invoices for material vendor accounts?
i. What controls exist to provide timely detail and summary informa-
tion to decision makers? In particular:
i. Is an accounts payable aging reviewed on a periodic basis?
ii. Is the accounts payable subledger reviewed on a periodic basis
for unusual items?
iii. Are reconciling items between the subledger and general ledger
reviewed periodically for reasonableness?
Conclusion
Accounts payable recoveries are simple for anyone to understand (that is,
forgot to take a discount on an invoice, paid an invoice twice, and so forth)
but difﬁcult for someone to detect and then collect. Such tasks require
technology tools, skills in reviewing vendor invoices, telephone tact when
contacting vendors, and, most important, the persistence to continue recov-
ering claims that may not be the largest amounts. Extensive recoveries are
frequently made in the amount strata band of 1,000 to 10,000, which to many
companies is not sizable enough to be too concerned about, but when you
add together 100 of them ... they start to really add up to something that is
quite concerning.

---

## Page 89

Chapter = c06 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
CHAPTER 6
Advertising Agency Audits and
Media Reviews
Overview
Historically, the advertising and marketing industry has not experienced
the same level of ﬁnancial scrutiny that is common in other ﬁelds. Large
corporations routinely spend hundreds of millions of dollars per year with
and through advertising and media buying vendors with minimal attention
paid to the vendor’s accounting practices or with completely insufﬁcient
client-side compliance testing. As well, the advertising industry’s prepay
environment creates a high level of risk for advertisers because huge sums
of money are transferred in advance to vendors, based on cost estimates,
and the advertiser generally relies on the vendor to repay unused funds and
adhere to proper ﬁnancial controls.
1 In this complex, high-dollar environ-
ment, such reliance is not appropriate.
The case studies in this chapter demonstrate advertising audits with
varying degrees of complexity. These case studies are from real audits; client
names have not been used so as to protect conﬁdentiality.
Case Study 6.1: Reluctant Marketing Department
Hiding Its Intentional Agency Beneﬁt
Advertising Audit and Risk Management (AARM) was brought in by the
ﬁnance team to audit the advertiser relationship, given concerns that ﬁnan-
cial controls in the marketing department were lax. Once AARM arrived
for its introductions meeting, AARM found the marketing department to be
This chapter was written in collaboration with Advertising Audit and Risk
Management (aarmusa.com) and Media Management, Inc. (MMI) mediaaudit.com.
63

---

## Page 90

Chapter = c06 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
64 Cost Recovery
quite reluctant to undertake an audit of its advertising agency vendor. AARM
saw that this was due to the thought that if ﬁndings were made then it
would make the marketing department look as if it had not been man-
aging the relationship well, and that it and the agency may be working
together to spend the budget rather than manage it for their actual costs and
contract terms. This reluctance led it to want to delay the audit for an addi-
tional six months, given that the agency had many pressing project dead-
lines.
AARM explained that its review would not affect the advertising produc-
tion because it was aﬁnancial review and, therefore, unless the accountants
were working on the creative development project, there would be no inter-
ference. Once the audit progressed, AARM was in constant discussions with
the marketing department, which noted that the relationship was under con-
trol and the agency had been bending over backward so that the audit would
not send the wrong message. As the audit moved forward, AARM discovered
that the marketing director had been paying the media buying ﬁrm vendor
$50,000 per month more than what had been agreed to in the contract. This
was an intentional act and not in his company’s best interest.
Once the breach was discovered by the audit team, the monies were
returned to the company, the CFO and global procurement director were
briefed, the marketing executive was reassigned, and the agency agreement
was renegotiated with newfound leverage to further beneﬁt the advertiser-
client. Furthermore, marketing and the agency changed their go-forward
practices by including the requirement for more timely reconciliation and
reporting. The company, including the marketing group (which now fully
bought in to the audit process), continues its audits to this day on an annual
rotational basis.
Case Study 6.2: Exit Audit after an Agency Transition
In this particular case, Advertising Audit and Risk Management (AARM)
worked with a client that terminated the relationship with its creative adver-
tising agency vendor after several years. Whenever such changes occurred,
the client found it to be a best practice to perform an exit audit to ensure an
accurate ﬁnancial relationship closure and an open project reconciliation.
AARM has found that if an audit is not performed, the company’s risk is
signiﬁcantly enhanced because it is leaving money on the table in the form
of prepaid work in progress, open credits on account, and media vendor
rebates and refunds and the like.
When AARM reviewed the relationship, it found that the agency was
billing the company roughly $50 million per year for fees, studio work,

---

## Page 91

Chapter = c06 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
Advertising Agency Audits and Media Reviews 65
advertising production, and certain media buying services. AARM’s audit
scope coved three years of prior agency billings but focused on jobs that
were in process at the time of termination. AARM’s audit team identi-
ﬁed credits due for refund, studio billings that had been made in excess
of agreed-upon rates, duplicate invoicing, and other errors that yielded
the advertiser $4.5 million in refunds, money that it would have com-
pletely walked away from had it not been for the decision to audit. This
represented 10 percent of the next year’s advertising budget and was a
welcome ﬁnd for the organization in establishing its next year’s budgeted
spend.
Case Study 6.3: Media Management
Media Management, Inc. (MMI) was asked by a consumer brand company
to review its three media partners. The client had decided to investigate the
media efﬁciencies that could be gained by consolidating its media partners
from three to one. MMI was engaged to help the client evaluate the his-
torical performance of each partner and to quantify the media efﬁciencies
that could be attained from a consolidation of media planning and buying
activities.
MMI’s media planning and buying performance assessment included
thorough reviews of the brand media plans, strategies, buying guide-
lines, buys, post-buys, and billing reconciliation activities for each of the
three media partners. Onsite interviews were conducted with both the
client’s brand marketing and media company personnel to supplement
the formal media audit process. MMI also performed a comprehensive
review of its contract, insertion order management, billing remittance,
and discrepancy resolution systems. Source documents reviewed ranged
from publisher contracts to monthly billing statements and print orders
for publications representing a signiﬁcant portion of its annual media
expenditures.
Based on the ﬁndings of the media assessment, the client was able
to ﬁnancially support its recommendation to consolidate media planning
and buying activity with one media ﬁrm. This move generated $13 mil-
lion in savings related to media cost efﬁciency gains and agency fees.
Furthermore, MMI identiﬁed $3 million in media discrepancies, as well
as introduced ﬁnancial and legal contract language amendments. These
enhancements included performance validation processes for both the
agency and the newspapers and billing reconciliation improvements to
speed up the collection of rebates, discounts, and credits. Findings included
unapplied rebates, incorrect circulation levels billed by the publications,

---

## Page 92

Chapter = c06 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
66 Cost Recovery
contractual terms and conditions not being adhered to, and insertion order
inaccuracies.
Case Study 6.4: Global Software Company
Advertising Audit and Risk Management (AARM) recently concluded an
advertising review for a high-proﬁle global software company. The com-
pany’s main creative advertising agency was the auditee and all client
operational groups (Internal Audit, Procurement, Finance, and Marketing)
were on board for the review. The company had never performed a detailed
marketing vendor review with a third-party service provider and it had
a good ongoing relationship with the vendor. The audit was conducted
as a routine risk management procedure and with an upcoming agency
agreement renewal in mind. The objectives were to test for agency contract
compliance for billing rates, travel activity, reconciliation practices, project
estimating practices, and the like for process improvement and efﬁciency
opportunities. It was found that the agency:
■ Was not properly reconciling projects cost and billings to actual. It was
simply keeping as extra revenue any difference between its original
project cost estimate and the actual costs.
■ Had been billing studio rates at higher levels per hour than the agreed-to
rate sheet had referenced.
■ Had been duplicate billing certain project costs due to weak internal
controls of its own.
■ Was buying extravagant gifts for client marketing personnel and then
charging the client back for those gifts.
■ Had been booking client personnel on ﬁrst-class travel to circumvent
certain policy requirements, and then billing the costs to the client
through nontransparent lump sum invoices.
Findings were obviously signiﬁcant and recoveries represented 3 per-
cent of total client payments to the agency during the period under review
(three-year audit scope). Tough business discussions were held, the agree-
ment was renegotiated and modiﬁed to reﬂect new client-initiated controls
and reporting requirements, and the relationship was set back on track with
all parties on the same page. What may be surprising to the reader of this
chapter is that these types of ﬁndings are very usual and unfortunately are
the result of an industry in which, for the most part, service providers
have been given a historical pass on detailed scrutiny and compliance
monitoring.

---

## Page 93

Chapter = c06 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
Advertising Agency Audits and Media Reviews 67
Case Study Wrap-Up
What Are the Special Dynamics Associated with Marketing Departments?
Marketing departments can be more difﬁcult to audit than an accounts
payable department, given:
■ Their direct reporting to the CEO.
■ Given the preceding bullet point, they tend to act as an untouchable
department, which leads to above-the-law thinking.
■ Revenue and associated growth is a top concern of most CEOs.
■ Marketing departments don’t want to worry about costs... because “We
bring in the sales!”
■ They are not used to the ﬁnancial scrutiny of an audit that other depart-
ments reporting to the CFO would be used to feeling. For example,
accounts payable departments would normally be used to an annual
audit and, under recent Sarbanes-Oxley regulations, a quarterly inter-
nal controls certiﬁcation review. Marketing departments have probably
never been introduced to Sarbanes-Oxley regulations.
■ Chief Marketing Ofﬁcers (CMOs) generally stay for two years—turnover
at the client level is a factor that can contribute to the erosion of brand
identity and awareness. According to Spencer Stuart’s 2005 tenure sur-
vey, the average CMO’s time in position is 23.2 months. This frequent
change is especially problematic when it comes to cross-platform mar-
keting, leveraging target audience insights, and marketing and media
accountability initiatives.
2
Has There Been Enough Financial Scrutiny Devoted to Marketing Budgets?
An advertiser’s marketing budget is often one of the largest expense items
within the company’s proﬁt and loss statement. With this in mind, it is good
corporate governance and business practice to ensure maximum value for
and visibility over money spent, including requiring the vendor to transact
ﬁnancial matters in a completely transparent fashion. Noncompliance with
an advertiser or vendor agreement can be intentional or may simply be
based on any number of errors. From a visual perspective (see Exhibit 6.1),
advertising dollars ﬂow to and through the vendor as follows, all with the
intent of creating a message compelling to the advertiser’s target consumer
or customer.
Time frames vary from company to company but, on average, it is advis-
able to undertake an audit every two years during the life of a contract,
and to ensure that the initial review is conducted by an industry specialist
audit ﬁrm. Once a focused initial set of testing has been performed and

maintained and will prevent poor practices from creeping in as a result of

3
Simply stated, marketing represents huge dollars, prepaid, in an envi-
ronment that has historically received very little scrutiny. According to
a recent study by Marketing Management Analytics (MMA) and Finan-
cial Executives International, only 7 percent of ﬁnancial executives report

---

## Page 95

Chapter = c06 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
Advertising Agency Audits and Media Reviews 69
being satisﬁed with their company’s ability to measure marketing return
on investment.4 Furthermore, based on a 2006 study by the Association
of National Advertisers and MMA, it was determined that about half of
all companies have no accountability budget, which means that these
companies are guessing what their marketing programs are doing for
them.
5
From a media auditing perspective, the Association of National Advertis-
ers 2004 survey “Trends in Agency Compensation” revealed that 53 percent
of the advertisers surveyed indicated that they audited their agency’s per-
formance, with only 30 percent indicating that they used an independent
media auditor. From a transparency and oversight perspective, the simple
translation is that 70 percent of the ANA members polled are relying on their
buying agents (advertisers) to provide critical self-assessments of their own
performance as the sole basis for media investment performance feedback.
This reliance on agency post-buy analyses and purported savings projections
based upon self-reported data may not accurately reﬂect actual performance,
thus increasing the advertiser’s risk in fully accounting for its media invest-
ment. Questions that may not be properly answered in this environment
include:
■ Did the agency’s buys deliver the media plan?
■ Were the buys in compliance with the client’s approved buying guide-
lines?
■ Were the media rates paid in line with normative market benchmarks?
■ Did the media schedules run as ordered?
■ Was the quality of the schedules consistent with the rates paid?
■ Did the agency recover all funds or media restitution owed to the adver-
tiser because of audience underdelivery?
■ Was the billing reconciled on an accurate and timely basis?
■ Did the agency’s remittance to the media properties occur on a timely
basis?
What Is the Difference between an Advertising Agency and a Media Audit?
Advertising An advertising agency audit is an essential component to ensure
that an advertiser’s money is handled as intended. Areas covered in an audit
include:
6
■ Is the agency complying with the agreed terms of business?
■ Are correct billing practices being followed?
■ Where can efﬁciencies be introduced to improve the process and the
relationship between agency and client?

---

## Page 96

Chapter = c06 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
70 Cost Recovery
More speciﬁcally, the following questions should be answered by an
advertising agency audit:
■ Did the client overpay the agency because of an error in paying the
agency’s invoice?
■ Did the client overpay the agency because of an error by the agency in
paying the agency’s vendor invoices?
■ Are there charges posted by the agency that were paid on invoices by
the client that do not conform with the agency contract?
■ Are there cleansing adjustments or unsupported charges made by the
agency that were invoiced and paid by the client?
■ What is the ﬂoat cost absorbed by the client for paying the agency before
releasing funds to their vendors or internal studio?
■ Are there any monies owed to the client that are currently being held
on the books and records of the agency?
■ Does a conﬂict of interest exist with the agency supplier that leads to
inﬂated charges being made to the client than what is common in the
marketplace?
Media The media’s role is to deliver ad impressions to people within an
advertiser’s target audience who are actually exposed to the advertiser’s mes-
sage. Unfortunately, estimating delivery isn’t an exact science. Impressions
are estimated by the media planning team well in advance of the actual
performance and may or may not be monitored or updated once the media
buy has been approved. While the tools are there for an agency to assess
audience delivery trends and ad placements, they are not always applied,
which is particularly the case if an advertiser is not independently auditing
media performance.
7 A media audit tests to ensure billings from the media
company are accurate on the basis of media company performance against
agreed-to media plan requirements and actual media run. The following
areas would fall under a media review:
■ National television auditing (network, cable, and syndication) and
billing reconciliation
■ Local television (spot TV and spot cable) auditing and billing reconcili-
ation
■ National print auditing and billing reconciliation
■ Local print (ROP, FSI, shared mail) auditing and billing reconciliation
■ National radio clearance analysis and billing reconciliation
■ Local radio rotation analysis and billing reconciliation
■ Schedule quality analysis across all media
■ Communications plan performance assessment
■ Interactive auditing and click fraud detection assessments

---

## Page 97

Chapter = c06 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
Advertising Agency Audits and Media Reviews 71
Broadly speaking, an independent media audit will assist an advertiser
in objectively assessing how effective its media planning and buying is,
whether or not the media investment processes are as transparent as desired,
and how they can improve their media efﬁciencies through improved media
coordination and control. At a granular level, an independent media audit
can help substantiate that the advertising actually ran, that the correct media
rates have been applied, that all credits and rebates have been received
and reported, that media buys have been properly managed and tracked,
and that the processes employed to steward the media planning and buying
activities of an advertiser are sound.
Numerous external database licenses (i.e., Nielsen, Arbitron, SQAD,
MMG-National Report, SRDS, Market Norms, and so on) are required to
be maintained, understood, and consistently reconciled against. In sum, an
independent media audit seeks to connect the linkage between media adver-
tising dollars spent and the actual level of media received. Given advertisers’
increasing use of sophisticated econometric forecasting tools and market-
ing mix models, the timely and accurate veriﬁcation of media delivery can
be critical to the advertiser’s efforts to optimize its sales and market share
initiatives. Six questions to be answered from a media audit include:
1. Are the agency’s buys delivering the media plan?
2. Are the buys in compliance to the client’s buying guidelines?
3. Did the schedules run as ordered?
4. Did the agency recover all monies owed because of underdelivery?
5. Was the billing reconciled accurately?
6. Did remittance to the media properties occur on a timely basis?
When Is the Best Time to Perform an Audit?
The best time to complete an audit is:
■ If full compliance audit has never been done
■ Changing agencies and exit audit opportunity
■ Acquiring companies or audit terminated agency
■ Preparing for agency agreement renegotiation
■ If relationship has extended past two years
Recoveries to Find
Areas where errors can occur include the following, many of which are
supported by the case studies discussed in this chapter.

---

## Page 98

Chapter = c06 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
72 Cost Recovery
■ Agency credits and rebates. Based on AARM’s experience, 1 to 2 per-
cent of past advertising spend is routinely recovered. Advertising agency
audit recoveries relate to:
■ Lack of a contract may lead to improper contract to billing rates
■ Production jobs may not be fully reconciled with any refunds returned
to the advertiser
■ Miscalculation of media commissions
■ Advanced billed media and production costs not reconciled to actual
costs
■ Duplicate payments and billings
■ Overpayments and billings
■ Excessive studio costs charged
■ Proﬁts and fees made in excess of agreement speciﬁcations
■ Tax miscalculations
■ Misuse of client credits
■ Time-value interest on use of client’s funds
■ Excessive travel and entertainment expenses incurred and billed
■ Third-party production or media vendor refunds never returned
Media audits lead to the following recoveries, which take the form
of credits or “make goods” (credit given to an advertiser or advertising
agency by a publication or broadcast medium for an advertisement or
commercial spot to make up for an error or unavoidable cancellation on
the part of the publication or broadcast medium. The credit is usually
in the form of a rerun of the advertisement or commercial), for media
planned, paid for, and never ran or ran incorrectly:
■ Bumped ads
■ Moved ads
■ Ads run partway
■ Ads not meeting expected delivery requirements
■ Ads paid for without considering all associated discounts
■ Billing inaccuracies for ads
■ Reduce fraud. While errors are the main focus of the audit, as seen
in the ﬁrst case study, intentional acts can be taken to overpay agen-
cies or by the agencies to the company. When billings relate to creative
and, therefore, intangible assets (i.e., brand name, market understand-
ing, and so forth), it is difﬁcult to assess the true value of them at times,
which opens the door to fraudulent activity. Couple this with marketing
departments not having had the ﬁnancial scrutiny of other departments
and one has a recipe for a fraudulent event.
■ Best practices for managing spend.Advertising administration, ﬂoat anal-
ysis, and other internal controls can all come from a proper audit.
■ Contractual updates. An audit provides an opportunity to identify holes
in the contract that can be ﬁlled.

EXHIBIT  A- SCOPE OF SERVICES 
Add to Section 1.6.2
Monthly Hours Report. Actual hours should be reported as per the time recorded in the Supplier’s time-keeping software system.
Supplier acknowledges that Client shall rely on the Monthly Hours Reports provided by Supplier as complete and accurate real-time
reports.
Supplier agrees:
(i)   Estimates of Account Team members’ time shall not be allowed and
(ii)  Once a Monthly Hours Report has been submitted to Client, Account Team members cannot record time for a
      prior period into subsequent periods.
Add to Section 1.6.2.2
Time spent by Account Team members on finance, accounting, secretarial, administrative, Supplier related computer services, or
executive management and similar matters that are not directly related to rendering Service to Client shall not be included in the
Recorded Time, directly or indirectly.
Business Rationale: It is important for Client to be assured that the Agency (and its Affiliates) have an accurate time
reporting process in place. Time keeping is fundamental to a professional services-oriented business and is crucial
in providing accurate reports to clients on the levels of effort necessary to service their accounts and scope of work.
It is essential for a Client to understand if their fees paid are within reason compared with the time consumed by
Account Team members and their respective staff-mix. Equally valuable is for the Client to understand the level of
effort that the Account Team utilizes to service each of its Market Segments, etc. Armed with accurate costing and
utilization information, both Client and Agency will be able to build on their joint efforts in fostering a strong
partnership based on a fair underlying compensation structure.
EXHIBIT 6.2 AARM’sAdvertisingContractualChanges
Prioritizing the Opportunity
Taking into consideration the case study experiences and the types of recov-
ery, the following is a list of questions that can help prioritize and frame the
opportunity for an advertising audit.
Size
■ How many advertising agencies are used by the company?
■ What is the level of spend with each agency?
■ Have any agencies been terminated in the last year? Please note that
audit rights normally stay open for a year to three years after the rela-
tionship.
■ What is the ﬁnancial health of the agencies and is it on an expected
upward or downward trend in the next year?
Marketing Administration
■ What is the experience level of the marketing administration team?
■ How well staffed is the marketing administration function?
■ Does the marketing administration function have enough time to ade-
quately review agency invoices before payment?
■ Has there been turnover in the marketing department at either senior
levels or in the ﬁnancial area of the department?
■ Has the company performed comprehensive agency compliance audits
or media audits?
■ Has the company documented internal processes for control and risk
mitigation in this area?

---

## Page 100

Chapter = c06 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
74 Cost Recovery
■ What budget to actual advertising analysis is performed at the company?
■ Does the company receive system-generated agency reporting?
■ Does each agency provide or maintain required support for each billing
to the company?
■ Does each agency appropriately reconcile estimated job and fee billings
to actual costs?
Contract Language
■ Does an audit clause exist related to advertising agency spend?
■ Are there audit restrictions to understand in the contract that could
dictate the timing of the audits?
■ Are the agency contracts simple or do they include complex language?
Media Audit Speciﬁc
For many of the following questions, it should be noted that most of these
data already exist and can be easily transmitted electronically to a media
audit ﬁrm for review and consideration.
■ How much is the company owed in underdelivery weight?
■ Is the company owed money for buying guideline infractions?
■ Did the print publications use or achieve their rate-based guarantees?
■ Has the company received all credits, discounts, and rebates?
■ How will the company benchmark any new agency’s performance?
■ How does the company monitor media savings and performance going
forward?
■ Does the company’s media process optimize effectiveness?
■ Can media execution and control be improved?
Conclusion
In the modern environment of internal audit, enterprise-wide risk manage-
ment, and regulatory oversight, organizations marketing vendors have been
mostly ignored. Any large advertiser that has not thoroughly tested whether
or not its advertising agreement has all the good controlling requirements,
and then whether or not the agency vendor is wholly complying with these
terms, is truly missing the boat.

CHAPTER 7
Audit Firm Benchmarking
Overview
This is a speciﬁc example of how improved information can be used in
a vendor negotiation, which is further explained in Chapter 18, which is
focused on strategic sourcing.
As any successful businessperson knows, benchmarking is a respect-
ed—and expected—practice in business operations. However, most busi-
nesspeople also know how difﬁcult and time-consuming the process is, yet
this can be aided through technology and database development.
Historically, companies answered questions about their auditor in an ad
hoc, informal way. Most prospective clients would have chosen to interview
different ﬁrms, ask friends and colleagues what they know, read news arti-
cles, and so on. The process is hardly perfect, but it leads to an answer. A
better method by far would be to systematically survey the industry and the
available auditors on a variety of questions and score the results to arrive
at a clear, thorough, objective answer. The survey would include questions
like the following:
■ Look at auditor fee trends (consulting versus audit fees), restatements by
auditor, unqualiﬁed (clean) opinions to disclaimer or qualiﬁed opinions,
industry concentration of the auditor to the competition, disengage-
ments of the auditor in the industry.
■ Look at fee rates for all audits in the industry, by auditor.
■ Review the composition of fees by auditor for the industry.
■ Review industry concentration for the auditor in relation to others in the
industry.
This section was written in collaboration with the Ives Group Inc., using its
product Audit Analytics (auditanalytics.com).
76

---

## Page 103

Chapter = c07 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:51 am
Audit Firm Benchmarking 77
The bottom line is that it makes good business sense to benchmark
business practices, especially audit service and fees. Such an exercise pro-
vides quantitative evidence that the current auditor is the best choice and
that its fees are appropriate for the involved work effort. Furthermore, hav-
ing an audit fee or service analysis completely customized for a company
is good business practice at the end of every ﬁscal year, especially given
the relative increase in work effort and fees over the last few years. Please
note that “audit fees” relates to the fees charged by an external audit ﬁrm
in a review of the company’s ﬁnancial statements as part of an annual
audit.
The foundation for doing audit fee or service benchmarking is up-to-
date data, not stale information, compiled over a few years, and also by
obtaining independent industry analysis across as wide a peer group as
possible. It can be simple to look up a few competitors’ audit fees in the
SEC’s EDGAR database (http://sec.gov/edgar.shtml), yet this can become
quite time-consuming to do for 75 to 100 companies in the same industry,
which is a more valid peer group for analysis. Furthermore, a comprehensive
benchmarking of an auditor takes into consideration many additional factors,
including the service a company provides, market share, and clientele for
the particular audited location, which is trickier to capture from publicly
reported company ﬁnancial documents as more fully displayed in the case
studies that follow.
Case Study 7.1: An Auditor in Trouble?
An audit fee benchmarking exercise was completed for a food services com-
pany (FSC) for which the audit committee sensed that the fees were in line
or actually low for the market, but the quality of the audit work being com-
pleted did not seem as up to par in relation to other companies. While
this could not be validated in any quantitative way, and was based more
on the FSC’s intuition, given a few quarterly meetings with the external
auditors, a suggestion was made to complete an audit fee benchmarking
exercise.
A consultant was contacted who had experience in using the Audit
Analytics database. While the company could have purchased a subscrip-
tion to the Audit Analytics database itself, the company felt it would be
quicker to simply have a consultant process some customized reports
for it because he had already mastered the learning curve with the
database.
The consultant quickly identiﬁed another 200 companies that had public
ﬁnancial reports on the basis of the food service company North American
Industry Classiﬁcation System (NAICS) code. To reﬁne this peer group down

---

## Page 104

Chapter = c07 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:51 am
78 Cost Recovery
to about 75 companies, he then ﬁltered out those companies that had similar
revenue and net assets as FSC. This was conﬁrmed with FSC and, more
speciﬁcally, FSC realized that its top three competitors were listed in the peer
group, which gave comfort that this data analysis exercise should prove to
be at least comparative.
The consultant produced a variety of reports, some more useful than
others, and from this exercise realized that the audit committee had good
cause for concern. The following three are a sample of the more telling
reports.
1. Percent of fees to revenue and assets. Exhibit 7.1 notes that the fees being
charged, in relation to the revenue and net assets for FSC, were much
lower than the industry. While this can be a sign of a good deal, it can
also highlight that the audit ﬁrm is not doing enough work and may
consequently miss material ﬁnancial disclosures.
2. Audit fee changes. With a now more validated concern, the audit com-
mittee turned its attention to the changes of auditors (when a company
changes ﬁrms from one auditor to another) to ﬁnd that not only were
there a fair number of changes in the last three years, but that the cur-
rent auditor topped the list as the ﬁrm most frequently replaced. More
concerning was that one of FSC’s top competitors, who also used FSC’s
audit ﬁrm, dropped them in the past year. Please see the Audit Analytics
Web page for selecting auditor changes and a graph highlighting such
changes in Exhibits 7.2 and 7.3.

EXHIBIT 7.3 NumberofAuditorChangesbyYear
3. Restatements and deﬁciencies. A ﬁnancial statement restatement is when
a company goes back to prior years and makes changes to its ﬁnancial
statements, generally due to issues being missed in that year’s audit. This
alerts investors and third parties that the company may not be telling
the truth as to its exact ﬁnancial position and usually, when there is
one restatement, there could be more in future years. While not always
due to a poor audit, a strong audit will normally identify issues in the
current-year review. Most times, restatements come when there is a

---

## Page 106

Chapter = c07 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:51 am
80 Cost Recovery
change in auditors and the new, more diligent, auditor sees the ﬁnancial
statements from a new perspective not originally seen by the previous
ﬁrm.
Similar to restatements are reported control deﬁciencies, which highlight
company business processes that do not produce the most reliable ﬁnancial
statements. While not the auditor’s fault (auditors are not there to correct
such deﬁciencies but rather to independently report on them as all changes
are for management to implement), they do highlight auditors that are work-
ing with clients with not-so-perfect ﬁnancial reporting business processes.
While, again, not completely the auditor’s fault, they start to become guilty
by association.
FSC’s current auditor not only led the list with companies with control
deﬁciencies but also had many companies that, in future years when an
auditor change occurred (see Exhibit 7.4), also had a restatement at that
company.
Based on the analysis, the auditor was confronted by the audit com-
mittee in a special meeting regarding the data and its performance in the
food service industry. The auditor admitted to losing key personnel to other
ﬁrms in the past three years that had more experience in the industry.
With less-experienced staff, it still wanted to perform the audit but the
audit committee decided to rebid the engagement for other ﬁrms that were
identiﬁed in the Audit Analytics exercise. While the audit fees may actually

be increased, they saw this as a small cost in relation to the devastating
impact a ﬁnancial restatement would have on their organization.
Case Study 7.2: Utility Service Firm Paying too Much
A utility services ﬁrm was starting to grow rapidly (over the last two years)
and decided that it wanted to make sure it had the best audit ﬁrm for the
job and was not paying more than it should for service. It contacted Audit
Analytics to complete its own analysis using a subscription to the database
because it felt it could use this system on a recurring annual basis to negotiate
terms with the audit ﬁrm.
It ﬁrst produced an analysis of all ﬁrms within the same industry based
on a NAICS code that was reported to Audit Analytics, downloaded to a
spreadsheet, and then summarized in a pie chart (see Exhibits 7.5 and 7.6).
Nothing surprising was found from this analysis except that the utility
services ﬁrm did see the entire range of audit ﬁrm competition for its indus-
try, which if it did decide to move ahead with a bidding process, it knew
precisely who to contact. It even went one step further to see those ﬁrms
that had a local presence to its corporate ofﬁce (which can also be com-
pleted by Audit Analytics, which can report the ofﬁce location where the
audit opinion is issued).
The utility service ﬁrm then decided to turn its attention to the fees being
charged by its current auditor and competitors. It ﬁrst scattergraphed the
entire industry of fees to company revenue to see that it was in the middle of
the pack, being neither overcharged nor undercharged (see Exhibit 7.7). This
was a little deceptive, though, as it did notice that many other companies
within its revenue band were paying less for the services, and that most
companies had less revenue but also much smaller audit fees.
To reﬁne its audit fee analysis, it looked at the trend in fees based on
the relation to revenue and net assets to quickly realize that it was paying
at a much higher percentage than its peer group. While the percentage to
revenues was decreasing from 2006 to 2008, it was further decreasing for
the industry (see Exhibit 7.8).
Based on this chart, the auditor was confronted by the CFO of the util-
ity services ﬁrm and a rebidding process was completed in the upcoming
audit year. Another ﬁrm was selected from this process that charged roughly
$150,000 less, yet provided the same level of service. This was accomplished
by the audit ﬁrm, given that it provided more local talent for the audit (as it
had a higher percentage of the local utility service ﬁrms being serviced by
a nearby ofﬁce).
