EXHIBIT 7.8 PercentofFeestoRevenueandAssets
Case Study Wrap-Up
Generally speaking, what should the Audit Analytics database be searched
on in an audit ﬁrm comparative analysis?
The Audit Analytics database should be reviewed with the following
perspectives:
■ Competitive position and industry experience. An audit ﬁrm experienced
with speciﬁc topics can often accomplish more in less time and be relied
upon to raise critical issues before they become a problem. Furthermore,
clients that pose potential problems are being quietly let go for someone
else to deal with, and auditors can lead to these problem clients by not
completing a quality audit in the ﬁrst place.
■ Cost. Fees are going up, and audit ﬁrms are being more selective in who
they will take as clients. Without a comparative analysis, it is difﬁcult to
understand who is charging more or less.
■ Red ﬂags sent by the auditor. Not all audit ﬁrms are the same. The Pub-
lic Company Accountability Ofﬁce (PCAOB) focuses on the auditor, as
much as on the company being audited, so one needs to carefully assess
who is the best business partner for reporting activities. For example,
the PCAOB may sense that an auditor with a high consulting-to-audit-
fee ratio for the industry, or disproportionate unqualiﬁed opinions for
the industry, may need to have its work further scrutinized. These are
all red ﬂags that may highlight them, and hence the audited company,
for further scrutiny:

---

## Page 111

Chapter = c07 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:51 am
Audit Firm Benchmarking 85
■ Auditor change analysis
■ Restatements
■ Control weaknesses and signiﬁcant deﬁciencies
What Information Is Available from Audit Analytics?
Audit Analytics has many information modules although the Audit + Com-
pliance data modules are most aligned with this audit ﬁrm analysis, which
can be further deﬁned by the following:
■ Auditor changes. Tracking over 16,500 auditor changes made by public
companies since the year 2000.
■ Auditor engagements. Updated daily tracking of who audits every public
company.
■ Audit fees. Over 100,000 observations of audit fee data culled from public
disclosures since 2001.
■ Audit opinions. Tracks all auditor reports on ﬁnancial statements dis-
closed since 2000. Key data points include ofﬁce of auditor and going
concern qualiﬁcations.
■ Beneﬁt plan opinions. Tracks all auditor reports found in 11-K ﬁlings.
■ D&O changes. Updated daily, they monitor all director and ofﬁcer
changes.
■ Disclosure controls. Every SOX 302 assessment analyzed back to 2002.
All assessments are categorized using proprietary issues taxonomy.
■ Internal controls. All SOX 404 assessments analyzed back to 2004 and
categorized with proprietary issues taxonomy.
■ Late ﬁlers. Tracks all late-ﬁling notiﬁcations since 2000. Each notiﬁcation
is categorized with proprietary issues taxonomy.
■ Restatements. Tracks all ﬁnancial restatements disclosed since 2000. All
accounting and accounting-related disclosed issues are categorized for
each restatement.
What More Speciﬁcally Should Be Taken into Account for a Decision?
Using the aforementioned information categories, a thorough benchmarking
report should contain analysis on, but not limited to:
■ Developing a peer group that contains up to 100 companies within the
industry and company size. Comparable competitors may be based on
revenues, assets, location, services provided, or a combination of these
factors.
■ List actual audit fees for the peer group and assess fees in relation to
revenue and net assets.

---

## Page 112

Chapter = c07 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:51 am
86 Cost Recovery
■ Identify restatements, internal control disclosures, and deﬁciencies
reported by auditors over the last three ﬁscal years for use in the coming
year. This is critical information not only for an auditor selection process
but also for the company to know where to place its audit focus in the
current year.
■ View a concentration of a small group of audit ﬁrms from within the
peer group of companies. If a relatively small group of ﬁrms is handling
auditing for a high percentage of the peer group, costs may be higher
because of a lack of competition.
■ Assess the size of the audit to all of the audits completed by that ﬁrm’s
location. One can essentially calculate how much the company’s audit
means to that local ofﬁce.
■ Summarize audit ﬁrm changes within a two-year period. This gives an
organization solid information about what companies are changing its
audit ﬁrms and may indicate that the fees charged by these providers
are out of line or that the level of services provided is inadequate.
Why Should a Company Pay for an Information Service?
Many economy-minded companies will ask, “Why should I pay for access
to information I could get for free?” As with many decisions of this kind,
the real issue is how much value this kind of information might add to the
process of choosing an external auditor. Particularly in the present audit
climate, making the right choice can be priceless.
Using online data sources provides instant information that allows a
company to beneﬁt from better understanding their ﬁnancial statements, as
well as determining the overall quality and fee structure of various auditors.
Such online data sources, however, are not meant for novices. Finding the
right online data provider and accumulating that data accurately and for
the right price can be a daunting challenge. For example, to determine a
full peer group of companies in the industry could take hours, if not days,
of looking at public SEC databases (i.e., http://sec.gov/edgar.shtml). Once
identiﬁed, fee information would then need to be culled from such online
documents in a manual fashion by looking at 10-K and 10-Q documents,
not to mention all of the other audit-related information that is useful in
conducting a proper fee analysis.
This is where online database providers come in to ﬁll a need in the mar-
ketplace. Such providers spend all of their time organizing the vast amounts
of data into usable information, saving companies time in the information
retrieval process. For example, Audit Analytics combs through all SEC ﬁl-
ings so that it can tag information associated with external audits, audit fees,
company controls, internal control opinions, company legal matters, and the
makeup of audit committees. Once in a database, searches could be mod-
iﬁed to look at a limited company size range, a particular state, even just

---

## Page 113

Chapter = c07 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:51 am
Audit Firm Benchmarking 87
one particular ofﬁce location (to review all the clients of auditors in a city,
for example). Fine-tuning of the analysis can be done with a few keystrokes
versus hours of additional analysis.
Recoveries to Find
The recoveries to ﬁnd are more forward-looking and, hence, cost savings
a company would have in paying smaller audit fees for the same or better
level of service. Another recovery that can’t be overlooked, but the value of
which is difﬁcult to assess, is the avoidance of having a poor audit performed
in any year. A poor audit in one year may lead to a strong audit completed
in a future year by the same or another ﬁrm, which will lead to ﬁnancial
statement restatements or control deﬁciencies, two phrases that should never
be uttered to investors.
Questions to Ask
So, when it comes to ﬁnding the right audit market analysis for the company,
several questions and concerns come to mind:
■ What is the company currently paying for its audit services, tax services,
and consulting services?
■ Is it material and, hence, requiring research into whether the company
is getting the best rates?
■ How long has the company worked with the audit ﬁrm?
■ Are the audit service fees increasing or decreasing in relation to the
industry?
■ What is the market share of the auditor in the company’s industry?
■ What is the market share of the auditor in the company’s region and
speciﬁc area?
■ How many changes from the current audit ﬁrm to other ﬁrms is hap-
pening in the company’s industry?
■ What level and type of control deﬁciencies are issued by the auditor in
relation to others in the industry?
■ How many ﬁnancial restatements are issued by the auditor in relation
to others in the industry?
Conclusion
Data sources like Audit Analytics afford an opportunity to learn more about
the company’s auditor and its interaction within the speciﬁc industry. More
likely than not, once the analysis is started, it will be difﬁcult to stop the
learning and improved future-year audits.

---

## Page 114

Chapter = c08 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 1:19 pm
CHAPTER 8
Escheatment (Unclaimed Property)
Overview1
Before discussing case studies associated with escheatment, it is useful to
answer some basic questions.
What Is Unclaimed Property?
Unclaimed property laws have been around for millennia. The ancient
Romans had ofﬁcers whose duty was to collect the property on behalf of
the emperor of persons who died without heirs or legatees. The English, at
about the time of William the Conqueror, instituted laws that claimed prop-
erty, both real and personal, on behalf of the Crown when someone died
without heirs or had committed certain types of crimes. Escheat was specif-
ically mentioned in the Magna Carta, written in 1215, in feudal England.
When the United States’ forefathers came to the colonies, they brought their
traditional laws, including English common law, with them.
Originating from an old French term meaning change or accident,
escheat is the means by which land returned to the tenant’s lord or the Crown
when some event interrupted the normal course of descent, such as when
someone died without leaving an heir. In the early common law era, the
escheat laws dealt only with real property. Bona vacantia, meaning vacant
or ownerless goods, was the way that the Crown claimed abandoned per-
sonal property against everyone but the rightful owner. In the United States,
the states, not the federal government, are the benefactors of the escheat
and bona vacantia laws.
This chapter was written in collaboration with the Tracey L. Reid and her
publication Unclaimed Property: A Reporting Process and Audit Survival Guide as
published by John Wiley & Sons as well as Pinpoint Proﬁt Recovery Services
(pinpointproﬁt.com).
88

---

## Page 115

Chapter = c08 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 1:19 pm
Escheatment (Unclaimed Property) 89
Over the years, the states have gotten together and attempted to make
one universal set of unclaimed property laws that could be applied across
the country to simplify unclaimed property reporting and collection. Why is
this history lesson important, one might ask? There are two main reasons.
First, one needs to understand that there is no uniform set of unclaimed
property laws. The laws in each state and territory are different, and you
need to understand the differences so that you can keep your company in
compliance.
What Is the Purpose of Unclaimed Property Laws?
The original purpose of abandoned property laws was to protect the inter-
ests of the true owners of unclaimed property until they could take control
of it themselves. The states purport to be protecting the rights of individuals
everywhere who have misplaced their valuable property and keeping that
property safe instead of allowing individual businesses to beneﬁt from some-
one’s loss. The theory is that, if property is lost, the general public should
beneﬁt from the use of that property until the true owner can be found
rather than letting one individual business reap the beneﬁt of a windfall that
does not actually belong to them. “Finder’s keepers, loser’s weepers” deﬁ-
nitely does not apply! Meanwhile, the state is allowed to audit businesses to
ensure that they are not hoarding property that does not belong to them.
Once the state has recovered the unclaimed funds, the state is charged
with safeguarding the assets for the true owner. The reason that the states
are so willing to go after these lost assets on behalf of their citizens may be
that states can invest that money and keep all of the interest earned without
ever having to pay the extra money earned over to the true owner, even if
or when the owner comes to claim it. The state is even permitted to charge
an administrative fee to the true owner, which they must pay to recover
their own property, which the state has been collecting interest on, and can
require the true owner to jump through multiple paperwork hoops to prove
that they actually are the owners of the property. No wonder only about
20 percent of the abandoned property held by the state is ever successfully
claimed by the true owner!
What Is the Key Terminology Related to Unclaimed Property?
First, let’s address some terminology that may not be familiar to the reader.
The most important term used in this chapter is unclaimed property. One
will hear it called Abandoned Property, Escheat Property, and Unclaimed
Property, but it all means the same thing: something tangible or intangible
that the true owner has not claimed or exercised any powers of ownership
over for a statutorily prescribed amount of time.

---

## Page 116

Chapter = c08 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 1:19 pm
90 Cost Recovery
Another term that is bandied about isholder.What is a holder? Generally,
it is the entity that is in possession of or controls abandoned property until
it is transferred to the state on behalf of the lost owner. This is usually the
business that issued the property, but that is not always the case. When
a company buys another company, for example, the acquired company’s
debts may become the obligation of the purchasing entity. In some cases,
outstanding unclaimed property is transferred to the new company, along
with the reporting obligation.
Another term to become familiar with is dormancy period. This is the
period of time, according to state law, that must pass since the last date when
an owner generated any action on its property. This can be the period of time
that has passed since the date that an uncashed check was written. It can
be the last time that a savings account holder made a deposit, withdrawal,
or inquiry regarding their account. The date of last contact by the owner
as listed in the records of the holder is the date that state’s use to deter-
mine the start of the dormancy period for purposes of reporting unclaimed
funds.
What Types of Property Can Become Abandoned Property?
Just about any type of tangible personal property can become abandoned.
Some common examples of property that can become abandoned are ﬁnan-
cial institution safe deposit box contents, money orders, traveler’s checks,
payroll checks, insurance settlement checks, utility deposits, securities, court
deposits, mineral proceeds such as royalties and the underlying shares of
mineral property, and many, many more items. Our society is more mobile
than ever before and people sometimes lose track of their assets.
When Does an Item Become Unclaimed Property?
Although there is no uniform set of unclaimed property laws that apply to
all of the states and territories, the way property becomes abandoned is
fairly uniform. Property is considered abandoned or unclaimed if it is held
in the ordinary course of a holder’s business, constitutes a debt or obliga-
tion of the holder to a creditor or owner, and remains unclaimed for more
than the statutory dormancy period. It is important to know that reissuing
a check to replace a check that is about to become dormant does not start
the dormancy clock over again! A check is just a document representing
the underlying debt created to the payee when the check is written. The
underlying debt is the focus of the dormancy clock, not the date of the
piece of paper. Exchanging one piece of paper for another (i.e., reissuing
a check) does not satisfy the debt. If the new check is not cashed before
the date that the original check would have become escheatable, then the

---

## Page 117

Chapter = c08 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 1:19 pm
Escheatment (Unclaimed Property) 91
amount must be reported on the date that the original check would have
been due.
When Is Property Not Considered Abandoned?
It is not unclaimed if the owner has increased or decreased the amount in
an account, if there has been written communication with the holder, if the
owner has indicated an interest in the property that is reﬂected by a memo
or record in the holder’s ﬁle, or if the owner has another relationship with
the holder where there has been owner-generated activity.
What Are the Holder’s Responsibilities?
The unclaimed property laws put a lot of responsibility for trying to ﬁnd
the true owners of abandoned property and reunite them with their assets
squarely in the laps of the holders. Holders have the obligation, under the
law, to report any escheatable unclaimed property that they are holding
to the states. Before that property is turned over, however, most states also
require holders to perform due diligence, or make some attempt to locate the
true owner themselves. If they cannot ﬁnd the true owner, the states require
holders to remit that property to the state for safekeeping. The states all
have enforcement powers to provide incentives for holders to do their duty
under the law. If the property is not turned over to the state, the holder can
be subject to late reporting and nonreporting ﬁnes and penalties that can
double and even quadruple the amount of money that the holder should
have reported initially.
Another aspect of the holder’s obligations is the responsibility to keep
the unclaimed property safe until it is either reunited with its rightful owner
or turned over to the state for safekeeping. Holding unclaimed property can
be more of a headache than you may think. This is also an area in which
some businesses are the victims of fraud. If businesses do not safeguard
property for the true owner during the time that it is in their custody, the
holder may not be fully indemniﬁed by the state they report the property
to. The true owner may have a cause of action against a holder that was
negligent with their property during the time they held it and caused the
property to lose value. A good example is a bank that transferred funds
from dormant interest-bearing accounts into noninterest-bearing accounts
until they were escheated. Because the true owner lost the extra money
from interest while the bank was still in possession of the funds, the former
account holders might still have a cause of action against the bank, even
after the funds were escheated to state care. It is important to recognize
your responsibilities as a holder to both the true owners and the states.
Otherwise, you risk costing your company money in a lawsuit.

---

## Page 118

Chapter = c08 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 1:19 pm
92 Cost Recovery
Unclaimed property is frequently the target for internal and external
fraud. Because it is historically ignored by many companies, their outstand-
ing unclaimed property can be a very tempting and unsupervised area for
the few employees in the know. Employees could siphon unclaimed funds
away from a company for years before anyone notices that funds have been
misplaced. Outside parties set up elaborate schemes to pretend to be rightful
owners and claim unclaimed funds belonging to other people. If a com-
pany is the victim of unclaimed property fraud, it will have to pay the funds
twice—once to the fraudster and again to either the true owner or the state.
In fact, companies can become the subject of a civil lawsuit brought by the
true owners of property that their property was not properly safeguarded
against fraud.
There are many different avenues that can lead to recovered money in
a company’s unclaimed funds. The easiest and shortest route to savings is to
investigate the accounting system for accounting errors. The following two
case studies highlight unclaimed property recoveries.
Case Study 8.1: Accounts Receivable Overcrediting
A large medical manufacturing company’s sales force has wide discretion to
issue credits for merchandise to the doctors and hospitals that it sells prod-
ucts to. Because the products are of a cosmetic nature, most doctors will
purchase several sets of a product and will then use only one set for the
ﬁnal procedure. Products that are sealed can be returned for a full refund.
Even products that have been opened for demonstration can be returned,
for a partial refund, as long as they have not been used in a medical pro-
cedure, as the company can resterilize and certify them as appropriate for
medical use. The unused items, as a policy, may be returned for a credit
that can be applied to future purchases. Credits are not reversed and sit on
the customer’s account until they either use them for purchases or request
a refund check.
The nature of this company’s business means that almost every sale
of merchandise will result in a partial refund and an accounts receivable
credit. The manufacturer is constantly shipping merchandise and processing
returns. Very rarely do doctors or hospitals keep an inventory of products.
Items are ordered on an as-needed basis. These credit balances continu-
ally change, and the actual credit balance on invoices sent for payment is
often different from the credits available on the company’s books because
of returns and purchases crossing in the lines of distribution. Because cus-
tomers may have more credit on their account than appears on their invoice,
they generally overpay. These overpayments create an even larger credit
balance.

---

## Page 119

Chapter = c08 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 1:19 pm
Escheatment (Unclaimed Property) 93
Because outstanding credits do not become unclaimed property unless
and until a customer stops doing business with a company for a statutorily
prescribed period of time, these credits may never become reportable as
escheat property. As soon as your customer stops ordering, however, the
timer starts running for unclaimed property purposes. This manufacturer had
about $3.5 million in credits more than six months old sitting on its ledger.
The manufacturer set out to investigate all credit balances larger than $50 in
order to reduce its potential unclaimed property exposure.
Its ﬁrst round of review was to eliminate any amounts that were statuto-
rily exempt from the manufacturer’s potentially reportable list of unclaimed
property. It reviewed customer billing addresses for locations in states
with the statutory business-to-business reporting exemption. If its customer
was another business entity (as opposed to an individual) with a mailing
address in one of the 13 states with some form of the business-to-business
exemption, with credits falling into the appropriate exempt category, those
items were removed from the list of reportable property. As these cred-
its were statutorily exempt from unclaimed property reporting, they could
immediately be taken into income and removed as payable credits on the
manufacturer’s books. This resulted in a $900,000 reduction in its list of
escheatable property.
The manufacturer’s next round of review was to contact customers with
balances larger than $500 and request that the customer conﬁrm whether
or not its records showed any balance due and owed to them from the
manufacturer. When contacting clients, the accounts receivable employees
ﬁrst attempted to call on the telephone. If it was able to reach the customer,
it made careful notes of the customer responses. If it could not conﬁrm
the amounts by telephone, the manufacturer sent a letter to the last known
address in its records to request a written response. If the customers con-
ﬁrmed that they did not show a credit balance owed from the manufacturer,
any amount listed as a credit balance on manufacturer’s books was reversed
to income. This resulted in a $650,000 reduction in the manufacturer’s list
of outstanding credits due to be reported as unclaimed property.
Case Study 8.2: Pinpoint Proﬁt Recovery Assists Large
Services Corporation
A large services corporation engaged Pinpoint Proﬁt Recovery Services to
perform a disbursement recovery audit compensated on a 100 percent con-
tingency basis. The client provided Pinpoint with four years of historical
accounts payable data to review and analyze. During a client meeting dis-
cussing the audit scope, Pinpoint offered to review outstanding accounts

---

## Page 120

Chapter = c08 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 1:19 pm
94 Cost Recovery
payable checks so it could assess whether speciﬁc outstanding payments
were required to be escheated. Pinpoint’s client said it was in full escheat-
ment compliance by using specialized application software that it had
licensed. The client also told Pinpoint that all due diligence in the form
of formal written correspondence (form letters) had been completed as
required by the states. Also, as time allowed, the client’s internal staff had
been assigned to investigate the outstanding payments and had concluded
it was appropriate to escheat these monies to the state.
Nevertheless, Pinpoint offered to review escheatable accounts payable
outstanding payments before their actual ﬁling with the states after the client
had exhausted its own efforts to research the outstanding payments before
escheatment. The client agreed and said, “What do we have to lose?”
The client asked Pinpoint how the review would be conducted. Pinpoint
explained that its proprietary audit tools should surface a number of poten-
tial overpayments, if any, embedded in the escheatable list of outstanding
payments. The remaining outstanding payments would be thoroughly inves-
tigated by using Pinpoint’s proven pre-escheatment research methodology.
Pinpoint explained that a recovery would be valid and substantiated only
by obtaining written (never verbal) vendor documentation and conﬁrmation
that the client had no existing open liability (no monies were owed or due
to the payee or vendor) supporting the outstanding payments. The client
then gave Pinpoint the green light to proceed.
The Pinpoint audit team received a list of 468 payments to investigate
55 days before the escheatment ﬁling period. First, to meet the deadline,
the audit team began to investigate each payment above $500, using its
proprietary audit tools. Pinpoint got expected results by identifying eight
duplicate payments and one wrong vendor payment, totaling approximately
$85,000. Of the eight duplicates, Pinpoints audit tools had little difﬁculty
tracking down two duplicate check requests and a lone duplicate payment
as a result of the client being invoiced by two separate divisions of the same
company. Locating the other ﬁve duplicate payments required much more
analysis time and required pulling scores of paid documents to review. Two
of the ﬁve duplicate payments were legal bills approved when the vendor
rebilled the charges using different invoice numbers and different dollar
amounts.
After exhausting the hunt for duplicate payments using its audit tools,
the audit ﬁrm now sought to discover if its client vendors had open and
valid accounts receivables supporting the outstanding payments due to be
escheated. Next, vendors had to be contacted by the audit ﬁrm. The audit
ﬁrm would communicate speciﬁcs behind the outstanding payments and
then wait for the vendors to complete their research. It should be noted
that scores of vendors did not, nor were they willing to respond. The age
of the transactions being years old and the time involved to research these

---

## Page 121

Chapter = c08 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 1:19 pm
Escheatment (Unclaimed Property) 95
payments precludes many vendors from responding. One can understand
this, since the audit trail grows cold and the knowledge base is no longer
available to shed any light as to why the payments went uncashed.
Because of the escheatment ﬁling time constraints on the audit team,
the recovery process needs to be effective and efﬁcient. An audit ﬁrm with
the experience understands how to ask the right questions directed to the
right people, sometimes both to the vendor side and to the client company
side. Having access to outstanding payment source documents, the auditors
are then able to follow the audit trail to help them work with the vendor and
the client contacts to determine the existence of an open liability. The audit
ﬁrm, however, needs to have the payee conﬁrm in writing that no open
liability exists before the payment can be removed from the up-and-coming
escheatment ﬁlings.
Pinpoint was able to obtain vendor conﬁrmation in writing that 16 of the
outstanding payments, totaling approximately $137,000, could be voided,
thus avoiding escheating this amount of money to the states. A review
of the vendor reasons that supported the decision to avoid escheating 16
outstanding payments were as follows:
■ Received vendors’ written conﬁrmation that prior credit card payments
satisﬁed the outstanding check request payments for three local restau-
rant events.
■ Received eight vendors’ written conﬁrmations after vendors completed
their research that said they could not locate any open accounts receiv-
able matching the outstanding payment.
■ Received two vendor-written conﬁrmations after the vendors completed
their research disclosed that stated no open accounts receivable existed
since the outstanding payments were as a result the payment of canceled
invoices.
■ Received three vendor-written conﬁrmations after the vendors com-
pleted their research disclosed that no open accounts receivable existed
since the outstanding payments were as a result of the payment of
balance forward amounts.
The audit ﬁrm contacted internal company personnel to assist them with
its analysis, which led to three additional recoveries that totaled approxi-
mately $1,800. One company individual provided the audit ﬁrm with a copy
of a 33-month-old e-mail instructing that the payment in question should
have been voided and stopped, which was never acted on. The two other
recoveries were discovered when responsible company personnel noted
that both estimated payments issued but not cashed by the vendor could
be voided since actual payments were issued and cashed 30 days after the
estimated payments were issued.

---

## Page 122

Chapter = c08 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 1:19 pm
96 Cost Recovery
The audit ﬁrm provided the client detail documentation supporting the
proposed recoveries. The audit ﬁrm recommended that the payments be
stopped and voided. The client spent a fair amount of time performing its
own due diligence and agreed with the audit ﬁrm’s recommendations. The
audit ﬁrm provided documented reasons why these outstanding payments
should not be subject to escheatment ﬁlings. The company also realized
that its own best effort at due diligence fell short because payees failed to
respond to their prior correspondence and contacts. Furthermore, company
resources were just not available to complete a thorough and detailed review
of the hundreds of outstanding payments.
As a result, the company was delighted with the audit ﬁrm’s results of
booking close to $224,000 in incremental proﬁt to its bottom line before the
payment of recovery fees due the audit ﬁrm.
Case Study Wrap-Up and Recoveries to Find2
■ Search diligently for unclaimed funds due to your company. Each state
and territory in the United States that collects unclaimed property also
maintains a list of unclaimed property in its possession and the name of
the true owner that it rightfully belongs to. If the company has been
in business for a number of years or if it has acquired a company
that has been in business for more than ﬁve years, chances are it has
outstanding unclaimed property sitting in some state’s coffers. Every
state maintains a Web site where one can search for your company’s
name (or the previous name of a company you acquired) to see if you
have unclaimed funds coming to you. There is a centralized Web site
(missingmoney.com) where 31 states collectively report their unclaimed
property, making it even easier to search for your forgotten wealth. Each
state has a formal reimbursement process that requires proof of own-
ership and will require some high-ranking member of your company’s
leadership to sign forms, but you can often recover signiﬁcant amounts
of cash that your company had forgotten it was owed or had, perhaps,
written off as uncollectible in the past.
Caution: This recovery process should not be entered into if the
company is not ﬁling unclaimed property reports correctly. If the com-
pany has never ﬁled unclaimed property reports or if it was formerly
ﬁling, but stopped being compliant, it should not attempt to recover
funds from a state agency until it is up to date with its compliance. If it
attempts to recover funds from a state, the state will automatically review
the ﬁling status and could possibly contact the company for an audit if
the company appears to not be ﬁling properly. The most likely state

---

## Page 123

Chapter = c08 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 1:19 pm
Escheatment (Unclaimed Property) 97
to audit the company is the one in which it attempts to recover funds
without ﬁling themselves is your company’s state of incorporation. Most
companies owe some amount of unclaimed funds to a state each year.
Many states require each company that is incorporated or organized in
their state to ﬁle an unclaimed funds report each year, even if it owes
nothing, so the state can track compliance with the state laws. If the
company is not ﬁling and should be, it will automatically be put on an
audit list for requesting a refund.
■ Invest the dormant unclaimed property that the company is holding in
an interest-bearing vehicle. As state laws require holders to safeguard
unclaimed property for a signiﬁcant period of time before turning it over
to the states, the company has a signiﬁcant opportunity to make some
return on these funds while they are in legal limbo. Payroll checks that
are dormant are to be held for a minimum of one year in most states, but
other types of property, like accounts receivable credits and accounts
payable checks are to be held for at least three years, if not ﬁve years,
before they are turned over to a state as unclaimed property. Some
states have seven-year holding periods for unclaimed amounts before
they are reportable. Industry averages show that checks are generally
cashed within 90 days of their issuance, if they are ever going to be
cashed at all. After credits or checks have aged 90 days and are still
outstanding, chances are they will not be cashed or cleared until you
do some extra follow-up. Once the company has attempted to clear the
issue up through the normal channels and the amounts appear to be
unresolvable, let them create some income to offset the cost to your
company of maintaining the assets for the statutorily prescribed dor-
mancy period. Make the most of these funds by investing them while
the company is legally responsible to maintain the amounts. Even a
short-term investment like a six-month certiﬁcate of deposit will gener-
ally bear more interest than leaving the funds sitting in your company’s
checking account. Administering unclaimed property is a cost for com-
panies. If one can recover something to offset the cost of this burden,
it would certainly help the bottom line.
■ Implement service charges on dormant accounts. As a holder, one has
the responsibility to maintain and safeguard funds that belong to some-
one else. This is the law. Obeying the law is costly. The states recognize
that there is a ﬁnancial cost that companies bear for maintaining out-
standing unclaimed property. To help offset the cost, most states allow
companies to charge reasonable service charges on dormant accounts
as long as the holder follows strict guidelines.
The legal treatment of unclaimed property service charges and dor-
mancy fees are not always limited to the unclaimed property laws in
each state. Consumer protection laws, banking regulations, and other

---

## Page 124

Chapter = c08 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 1:19 pm
98 Cost Recovery
federal and state statutory provisions can affect these charges, so it is
imperative for a legal adviser to thoroughly review the entire body of
law in this area for each state involved before imposing any of these
types of fees.
In states where dormancy fees and service charges are allowed to
be applied to unclaimed property, there are generally three criteria that
must be met for the charges to be considered legitimate:
1. An enforceable contract must exist between the holder and the owner
to establish the holder’s right to impose the charge.
2. The holder must, in fact, regularly and consistently apply the charge
without regularly refunding the charge if an owner claims the aban-
doned property.
3. The amount of the charge must be reasonable, based on the facts and
circumstance of the transactions.
While some states, like Ohio, permit holders to deduct a small fee
from the value of each unclaimed item remitted to cover their admin-
istrative costs, most states frown upon any attempt that holders make
to reduce the value of abandoned property for their own gain. Most
holders would be wise to avoid charging these types of service charges
at all, but if the company feels that it can legitimately meet the require-
ments, remember that the policies must be strictly enforced. The policy
to apply service charges or dormancy fees must be clearly spelled out to
the customer, in writing, somewhere conspicuous. Just putting notice of
these charges in ﬁne print somewhere in the transaction documents will
most likely be disallowed as notice by unclaimed property enforcement
agencies.
One will also need to be reasonable about the rate and amount
of any service charges that are imposed. If the dollar-or-two-a-month
charge after a gift certiﬁcate or savings account has been abandoned
for the statutory dormancy period is to cover the cost of tracking the
true owner, performing due diligence, and paying for postage is rea-
sonable, most administrators would probably allow the deductions. If,
however, the amount of the charges is unreasonable, like $1 a day after
the property reaches dormancy, and it appears that the sole purpose of
the charge is to reduce the value of the outstanding unclaimed property
to zero and avoid escheat, an administrator will probably disallowall of
the dormancy charges. Service charges are tricky. These dormancy fees
should carry a warning: “Proceed with Caution.”
■ Reduce your administrative cost with early reporting—Unclaimed prop-
erty laws generally require that one hold unclaimed property for the
entire statutorily determined dormancy period before turning it over to

---

## Page 125

Chapter = c08 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 1:19 pm
Escheatment (Unclaimed Property) 99
the state in the hope that the holder will be better equipped to ﬁnd
the true owner than the state. This is not always the case, however.
In many situations, the company has absolutely no way of contacting
a true owner to return his funds. Maybe the company has attempted
to call or send a letter and the number was disconnected or the letter
was returned by the post ofﬁce. No matter how long one holds the
unclaimed funds, chances are that he is not going to be able to ﬁnd
the true owner. States understand this situation and will often permit
holders to report funds early, with adequate explanation.
It is not suggested that the company report all of its unclaimed
property before the statutorily required dormancy period has passed.
States will often just reject the ﬁling and return the funds. The company
must contact the states that are due to receive the funds and request
permission to ﬁle early, with appropriate reasons for doing so. If the
company does not wish to invest the unclaimed funds in an interest-
bearing vehicle, early reporting can free company personnel from the
burden of administering dead-end unclaimed property a little early, thus
saving the costs of administering and protecting the assets.
Questions to Ask When Assessing the Opportunity
Taking into consideration the case study experiences and the types of recov-
ery, the following is a list of nine questions that can help prioritize and frame
the opportunity for an escheatment review:
1. Has the company been in business for more than ﬁve years or has the
company acquired another company that has been in business for more
than ﬁve years?
2. Does the company report to more than three states for unclaimed prop-
erty on an annual basis?
3. Does the company operate in more than ﬁve states across the country?
4. Does the company actively follow up on outstanding checks and credits
that have not been cleared within 90 days?
5. Does the company have more than 100 items aged more than
six months on the books (that is, accounts receivable or accounts
payable)?
6. Does the company issue more than 50 accounts receivable credits each
month?
7. Does the company post more than 50 accounts payable debits each
month?
8. Does the company have a large number of employees that are not on
direct deposit each pay period?

---

## Page 126

Chapter = c08 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 1:19 pm
100 Cost Recovery
9. Does the company issue a large number of checks for small dollar
amounts each month?
Conclusion
Let’s face it ... administering unclaimed property costs a company time
and staff resources. Furthermore, overﬁling because of accounting errors
and allowing the dormant funds to sit without earning interest can cost a
company real dollars that can otherwise be recovered. This is a real ﬁnancial
burden, but can be worth the effort. Even ﬁling property early or implement-
ing a modest dormant account service charge can reduce the company’s
cost of doing business. Lastly, once your unclaimed property compliance
is running smoothly, don’t forget to search for and recover any unclaimed
funds in state coffers that rightfully belong to the company. The real price
of unclaimed property administration is leaving the company’s own money
on the table. If the company doesn’t have the resources with some expertise
and do not possess the audit tools, then it can hire an unclaimed property
consulting or recovery audit ﬁrm to maximize recoveries.

CHAPTER 9
Freight Bill Auditing
Overview
Manufacturers and shippers of all types, and any company responsible for
the paying for transportation services either into or out of their company’s
facilities, must process and reconcile transportation-related invoicing. Freight
payment companies specialize in the auditing, processing, and payment of
transportation-related invoices, also known as freight bill auditing. These
companies are more familiar with the nuances of transportation-related
invoices and legalities associated with transportation bills than the typical
accounts payable professional.
Freight bill auditing falls into two speciﬁc classiﬁcations, both of which
have their uses in certain applications:
■ A post-audit freight bill auditing company typically performs an audit
on the company’s transportation invoices after invoices have been paid.
These companies are typically contracted on a contingency basis to
receive a percentage (usually half) of all billing errors plus a ﬁxed or
rate per audited invoice amount.
■ A pre-audit freight bill auditing company performs auditing func-
tions on transportation-related invoices before payment to the carriers.
Its ultimate goal is to present to the customer a consolidated batch
(typically weekly, biweekly, or bimonthly) of audited and approved
transportation-related invoices approved for payment. Customers at that
time may pay transportation providers directly off the batch reports pro-
vided by the freight bill auditing company or wire funds to the freight
bill auditing company, which in turn pays the transportation carriers
directly.
This chapter was written in collaboration with Adcco Incorporated (adcco.com).
101

---

## Page 128

Chapter = c09 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 1:22 pm
102 Cost Recovery
Case Study 9.1: When Freight Classes and
Descriptions Make a Difference
Adcco Incorporated was provided six months of transportation invoices for
a pharmaceutical company in New Jersey. Upon a post-audit review of
hundreds of less-than-truckload transportation invoices, it was noted that
out of the three transportation providers, one carrier was classifying the
outbound manufactured product freight as foodstuffs and rated the prod-
uct class 85, while the other carriers were correctly classifying the freight
as National Motor Freight Item 60000: Drugs, Chemicals, Medicines, and
Pharmaceuticals, which is class 70 product.
Clearly, the same manufactured product should be classiﬁed as either
one or the other, but not both. Although the carrier in question invoicing
shipments at class 85 could legally do so because the descriptions on the
bills of lading were vague, Adcco Incorporated was able to prove that all
outbound manufactured shipments were more accurately described under
NMFC Item number 60000, and, hence, a claim was ﬁled correcting all freight
bills to class 70 from class 85. This class correction resulted in the car-
rier’s base rate being reduced by 17.8 percent, saving this speciﬁc customer
over $70,000 in the six-month post-audit study, and approximately $140,000
annually going forward, as this problem was obviously corrected.
Case Study 9.2: Aluminum Manufacturer and
Distributor Post-Audit
Adcco obtained an account that manufactures and distributes aluminum arti-
cles. Upon review of the pricing documentation provided by the shipping
department at the beginning of the new year, a decrease in the amount
of $48 in the ﬂat truckload rate between two speciﬁc points was negotiated
with a major carrier. The carrier, however, continued to invoice the previous
rate from the year before. When the invoices were sent to the company’s
accounts payable department, leaving the rate unchanged from last year,
nothing was found to be amiss. Upon a post-audit review of the data, how-
ever, it was revealed that this major carrier overbilled in 63 occurrences,
resulting in a collected overcharge claim of $3,024.
Case Study Wrap-Up
What Are the Key Error Types in the Freight Payment Process?
Generally speaking, transportation freight bill errors are typically the result
of lacking checks and balances in three major areas:

---

## Page 129

Chapter = c09 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 1:22 pm
Freight Bill Auditing 103
1. Transportation professionals and typical transportation departments
within companies negotiate rates with sales representatives of various
transportation providers. These negotiated rates must then be properly
conveyed by the sales representative back to her company’s pricing
and rating departments to create a proper transportation invoice. Large
transportation providers often have thousands of customers, each with
unique pricing agreements, and the negotiated rates as understood by
the sales representative are often not accurately conveyed within their
own company.
2. Unfortunately, transportation invoices are then normally sent from the
providers to the company’s accounts payable department (which typi-
cally does not have any transportation experience or may not even be
aware of what rates were negotiated), where they are processed and
paid, and the rates are not properly scrutinized by the people who
negotiated them.
3. Another eventuality is inadequate, illegible, or vague descriptions of
addresses or product descriptions on the bills of lading by the company’s
shipping department.
What Are Some Considerations for Post-Audits?
Post-audits of transportation-related invoices typically occur when there is
an ownership or management change, or some speciﬁc reason or event has
occurred to question the handling of transportation invoices going back in
time. Some issues to consider include:
■ Depending upon the language in the contract or pricing agreements
between the carrier and shipper, there will often be varying time limit
constraints pertaining to when an overcharge claim may be ﬁled with
the carrier. For example, one carrier may speciﬁcally state these terms
in its bill of lading language or refer the company to its carrier rules
on the bill of lading, which may state that no claims may be ﬁled after
180 days, including overcharge or duplicate payment claims paid in
full. If no speciﬁc time limits are stated or referred to, however, then
according to Title 49 of the U.S. Code, Subtitle IV, Part B, Chapter 147,
Section 14705, civil actions to recover overcharge amounts must begin
within 18 months. This makes the attempt of post-auditing transportation
invoices greater than 18 months a wasted effort.
■ If considering a post-audit service, one should make sure the auditing
company is compensated its portion only on fully recovered amounts.
Post-auditing companies often provide their customer with their list or
reports of overcharges and leave it to them to ﬁle the claims with the car-
riers, and often, a substantial portion of the overcharge amounts on their
lists are not fully recoverable or can be outright denied or adequately

---

## Page 130

Chapter = c09 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 1:22 pm
104 Cost Recovery
defended by the carrier. The most difﬁcult part of the post-audit is the
actual collection of funds from carriers.
■ One needs to consider the internal time investment cost of employ-
ees required to ﬁnd, collect, send, and process past invoices and the
reported results when making a decision. Past transportation invoices
may often be ﬁled or stored in several facilities or departments in a
company with multiple shipping or receiving points.
What Are Pre-Audits of Freight Invoices and Why Do Them Instead
of Post-Audits?
Pre-audits of transportation invoices will verify the amounts to be paid to
carriers in advance of making payment. This is a considerably more effec-
tive method than seeking a return of funds from a carrier or transportation
provider that has been previously paid in full. Pre-audits are real time and
have a much higher degree of success in recovery efforts than post-audits,
which, as the name suggests, is performed after the original amounts have
already been paid. Post-audits are always behind in regard to reviewing
information, usually in excess of two months. It is common sense to ﬁnd
problems with freight invoices and correct them before payment is made.
But the primary difference between the two auditing services is that
pre-audits can be combined with freight payment services saving internal
processing costs and accounts payable labor. The soft-savings potential men-
tioned in this section would only occur under the pre-audit scenario. In the
post-audit scenario, the auditing is being performed in addition to the regular
course of business of the company.
The pre-audit freight bill auditing process may be simple or elaborate,
and it is important that one understand the company’s needs and weigh the
cost versus returned beneﬁt regarding auditing services. A typical pre-audit
freight bill auditing process has the following processes:
■ Initial matching process. Carrier invoices received by the freight bill
auditing company are matched to company documentation (either
hardcopy or electronic) to verify the freight bill being processed is
indeed a valid invoice for the company. Sometimes carriers invoice
the wrong party because of an oversight or illegibility of payment
terms as described on a bill of lading, so it is imperative that the com-
pany’s information is used to verify a shipment occurred and the freight
terms (which identiﬁes the party obligated to pay transportation-related
charges). An additional process must be put in place to address trans-
portation invoices that cannot be veriﬁed with company information.
■ Enhanced matching and use of customer information.Once the match-
ing process is complete, transportation invoices are audited against

---

## Page 131

Chapter = c09 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 1:22 pm
Freight Bill Auditing 105
customer-provided information for proper origin, destination, freight
description and applicable freight classiﬁcation, and weight. Errors
related to poor or misinterpreted descriptions resulting in an incorrect
freight class being assigned to the shipment (affecting charges) and dif-
ferences in weight (getting billed 5,000 lbs. for a 500-lb. shipment) are
found here. Upon discovery, carriers should be contacted, provided suf-
ﬁcient company documentation, and given the opportunity to issue a
corrected invoice.
■ Rate reviews. Transportation invoices that have shown to be accurate to
customer information are then audited for rate accuracy. A good freight
bill auditing company should be able to verify transportation charges to
the penny, using just the company’s provided information, that is, the bill
of lading information, as well as the company’s negotiated pricing tariffs
and contracts with the transportation providers. Customized freight bill
auditing company software or carrier provided software can be used to
independently verify charges. If charges do not match, again a process
must be put in place. Either an adjustment form is created, citing a
speciﬁc transportation provider’s error and pricing agreement reference,
and the transportation invoice is short paid, or the carrier is contacted
to issue a corrected invoice.
■ Database management. If the audited transportation charge amount
does indeed match the transportation provider’s charges, relevant
information is entered into a database for reporting and consolida-
tion purposes. Speciﬁc ﬁelds to be captured should be agreed upon
before implementation. Databases should also have previously agreed-
upon criteria for accepting data—for example, unique transportation
provider’s invoice or pro number, sales order number, purchase order
number, time limits, and so forth.
Prooﬁng reports are typically generated from the database. These
reports can be looked over by either a company representative for approval
or by the freight bill auditing ﬁrm acting on the company’s behalf.
Upon ﬁnal approval, the database generates any and all required sum-
mary, detail, and exception reporting for the company. Upon receipt of
reports and original freight invoices, the company either pays transporta-
tion providers directly from reports or wires funds to the freight bill auditing
company to pay the transportation invoice on the providers’ behalf.
What about Small Parcel Auditing?
Small parcel shipping (e.g., FedEx or UPS) should also be audited. Most
ﬁndings here by the freight bill auditing company are related to guar-
anteed service failures, shipments manifested but not actually shipped,

---

## Page 132

Chapter = c09 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 1:22 pm
106 Cost Recovery
lost shipment refunds, and invoice errors. A good small parcel freight
bill auditing service will ﬁle carrier claims on behalf of the company and
provide exception reporting. It should also provide metrics in terms of
carrier performance and visibility to small parcel expenses and shipping
trends.
What Are Issues to Avoid Related to Freight Bill Auditing Companies?
The primary concern of a company employing the services of a freight bill
auditing service (beyond the accuracy of the audit) is to make certain car-
rier invoices are paid in a timely fashion and within the negotiated terms of
the transportation provider. Clear agreements and a deﬁnition of an accept-
able time delay between when transferred funds are provided to the freight
bill auditing company and when the freight bill auditing company pays the
transportation providers must be deﬁned. Some less-than-reputable freight
bill auditing companies will accept wired or transferred funds for a pro-
cessing batch, but then play with the ﬂoat and hold on to the funds for an
unacceptable amount of time before making payment to the transportation
providers.
Keep in mind that the company is ultimately responsible for making
payment to the transportation providers, and this responsibility to make
payment does not end with the wiring of funds to the freight bill auditing
company. Freight bill auditing companies should:
■ Have wired or transferred funds from the company going into a separate
bank account set up speciﬁcally for the company that one can access
or verify the balance.
■ Be able to verify that payment has been made to the carriers within
two business days of receipt of funds from the company. Do not allow
freight bill auditing companies to hold on to issued checks.
It is strongly recommended that upon implementation of a freight bill
auditing process involving a third party that one periodically monitor the
average time from the carrier’s invoice date to the carrier’s receipt of funds
from the third party by requesting statements directly from the transportation
providers. The whole freight bill audit process with a reputable freight bill
auditing company and the timely transfer of funds to the freight bill auditing
company should be capable of paying transportation invoices within 14
calendar days of the carrier’s presentation of the freight bill to the auditing
company (if the transportation provider’s invoice was accurate and correct).
Any delays beyond the 14 days should be attributable to the company’s
delay of funding the third-party freight bill auditing company and not any
delays by the freight bill auditing ﬁrm.

---

## Page 133

Chapter = c09 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 1:22 pm
Freight Bill Auditing 107
Recoveries to Find
In addition to the actual transportation invoicing errors captured during a
pre- or post-audit, which lead to freight invoice adjustments and cash ﬂow
to the company, there is great potential to obtain additional internal soft
savings. These are highlighted here.
■ The process of pre-auditing transportation invoices involves outsourcing
to a freight bill auditing company that will typically receive trans-
portation invoices directly from the carriers. The consolidation of
transportation invoices should almost always save internal accounts
payable labor. Instead of company employee(s) entering 2,000 trans-
portation invoices for 10 different carriers on the internal accounts
payable system, now a report from the freight bill auditing company
should provide a simple summary report showing total amounts due
to all 10 carriers. One lump wire transfer of funds to the freight bill
auditing company is all that is required to pay the 2,000 invoices.
On average, a company can expect to save one internal person-hour
in accounting labor cost per 50 transportation invoices if internally
all individual transportation invoices were entered into the accounting
system.
■ Freight bill auditing companies also save transportation professionals
a considerable amount of time from approving every transportation
invoice. Rather than having to inspect and approve all invoices cre-
ated by routine shipping, the freight bill auditing company audits and
approves the carrier invoices and can be directed to provide excep-
tional reporting back to the transportation department for approval
before processing. Please note that criteria surrounding what are con-
sidered routine invoices versus exceptional ones are worked out before
implementation of the review process.
■ Depending upon the freight bill auditing company, the capturing of
freight bill, bill of lading data, and associated costs can become a
valuable asset for forecasting transportation costs, measuring key trans-
portation metrics of manufactured products (cost per case, cost per
pound, cost per piece) or average cost per shipment to a speciﬁc cus-
tomer, and can be used to monitor transportation costs as a percentage
of sales. The freight bill auditing company can become a valuable asset
to sales, accounting, and customer service departments.
■ Freight bill auditing companies should be capable of providing the com-
pany with summary and detailed reports on a monthly, quarterly, or
annual basis. These reports can be used to facilitate detailed bids when
it comes time to negotiate transportation services. It should be noted
that freight audit ﬁrms can also assist in this negotiation process.

---

## Page 134

Chapter = c09 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 1:22 pm
108 Cost Recovery
Questions to Ask
Taking into consideration the case study experiences and the types of recov-
ery, the following is a list of questions that can help frame the opportunity
size of a freight audit:
■ Is the company under pressure to reduce transportation costs and
improve operational efﬁciencies?
■ Is the company concerned about the current freight bill auditing proce-
dures (if they exist) and payment accuracy?
■ Are transportation invoices being processed, approved, and paid by the
accounts payable department?
■ Is transportation invoice processing consuming many staff hours of labor
of the accounts payable department?
■ Has the transportation budget risen unexpectedly or dramatically with-
out explaining the speciﬁc reasons?
■ Does the company desire to capture customer or carrier-speciﬁc trans-
portation cost data and track metrics (proﬁtability, transportation costs
as a percentage of sales, cost per unit, shipment, or order)?
■ Does the company wish to know the impact transportation cost changes
have had on the company’s bottom line?
Conclusion
Clearly, there is a need for freight bill auditing companies. The transportation
industry has some unique language and rating requiring a degree of aptitude
and knowledge to properly audit transportation-related invoices. The costs
of employing a freight bill auditing company should reduce company costs
when considering not only the audit ﬁndings, but the time savings of inter-
nal transportation professionals and accounting personnel. These savings
will typically increase as freight bill volume increases. There are also many
intangible beneﬁts related to the monitoring of key company metrics and
access to the freight expense database to generate specialized informational
reports for several other areas of the company.
