Chapter = c10 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:10 pm
CHAPTER 10
Health Beneﬁts Auditing
Overview
“I never met an atheist in a foxhole or a cost containment specialist in an
emergency room” is a good phrase to begin this chapter with, as health
expenses have risen dramatically over the past few years, and while there is
no escaping the rising costs, it is in large part to no one asking for a lower
level of service when they are in the doctor’s ofﬁce. PricewaterhouseCoop-
ers reported in its Behind the Numbers: Health Care Costs Trends for 2009
that the growth in medical cost trends for the private sector was expected to
level off in 2009 following ﬁve years of deceleration. Costs were expected
to grow 9.6 percent in 2009 compared with 9.9 percent in 2008. While a
slowing down, an almost 10 percent increase in costs is nothing to be upset
over, because any business with such growth should be celebrating. Further-
more, in times of recession, there is a higher dependence on the health care
industry as demonstrated during past recessions when health care increased
its portion of gross domestic product (GDP) and medical prices rose faster
than other prices.
In working with various health care auditors, the general sense is that
insuring an employee costs between $7,500 and $8,500 per year in 2008.
This is an estimated average for a self-insured plan, which is one in which
the company pays the full cost of the medical expenses to a third-party
administrator who handles the claims ensuring these expenses meet plan
requirements before being paid. Also, while not true for all companies, for
any company with a substantial employee population, health care costs tend
to account for the majority of expense, excluding payroll. Using the averages
given here, and assuming $8,000 per employee, if there are 500 employees,
that represents $4 million in annual spend and with an audit covering three
This chapter was written in collaboration with Si Nahara at Health Decisions, Inc.
(healthdecisions.com).
109

---

## Page 136

Chapter = c10 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:10 pm
110 Cost Recovery
years, that quickly expands to $12 million in scope for an audit. If even 1
percent of that number is incorrect, it could represent a $120,000 savings to
the organization.
With savings in mind, the case studies in this section explore key facets
of health plan auditing and how it can produce recoveries and reductions
in the already rising health plan costs. The focus of these efforts is to ﬁnd
and eliminate, or at least reduce, waste (payments made by the health plans
that are not their responsibility) and abuse (actions taken by parties outside
the health plan that are not in the plan’s interests). In addressing waste and
abuse, fraud will be encountered, which is simply waste and abuse done
with criminal intent.
Certain terms used in the case studies are deﬁned here:
■ Health plans, or more simply plans, refer to group health arrange-
ments sponsored by an employer (and, in some instances, collectives
of employers through a trust). Plans can be self-funded, whereby the
employer is responsible for all plan costs whatever the amount; or
insured, whereby the employer pays a ﬁxed premium to an insurance
company and the insurance company pays plan costs whether these
are more or less than actual plan costs. The distinction between self-
funded and insured plans is more than deﬁnitional. Self-funded plans
are exempt from state insurance regulations and are instead subject to
federal law. Also, employers sponsoring a self-funded plan have legal
(ﬁduciary) responsibilities that make auditing not simply a nicety but a
necessity.
■ Payers are the organizations that handle the paperwork associated with
plan operations. They process enrollment applications and changes from
employees; receive, review, and pay bills submitted by providers; and
invoice employers for payments due. In self-funded plans, payers are
simply administrators who work for the plan. With insured arrange-
ments, the payer is also the insurer. Payer performance is a key feature
of plan audits.
■ Providers are doctors and hospitals that care for people covered by
health plans (employees, retirees, spouses, and dependents). They sub-
mit bills to payers for payment and are reimbursed by payers based on
plan contract provisions. Provider billing and payment is another key
feature of plan audits.
■ Participants are employees, retired employees, and their dependents
who are covered under the plan.
The case studies in this section illustrate the two main types of plan
recovery audits (enrollment and claims) and how those two audits inter-
relate.

---

## Page 137

Chapter = c10 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:10 pm
Health Beneﬁts Auditing 111
Case Study 10.1: Midwest Municipality Cuts Costs
Like many municipalities, Midwest Municipality faced a budget crunch and
was looking to ﬁnd ways to reduce costs. With health plan expenses its
largest expense after payroll, this was a logical place to look. The problem
was where to start and how to go about it. With 5,000 employees, 1,000
retirees, and an equal number of spouses and dependents, auditing a plan
of this size was daunting. To further complicate matters, this enrolled popu-
lation was scattered across a self-funded plan and two insured arrangements
with health maintenance organizations (HMO).
Midwest Municipality had done a standard claim sample audit through
its self-funded payer that showed the payer was doing a good job. This had
been a random sample of claims that were reviewed. It was determined
that errors were below the 5 percent performance target set by the payer’s
contract. No review of enrollment information was done. This payer, like
all health payers, took the position that enrollment maintenance was the
employer’s responsibility. No audit had ever been performed of the two
insured HMOs.
Health Decisions was retained to perform an independent audit to deter-
mine if there were recovery opportunities or other savings that Midwest
Municipality might pursue. The audit started with conﬁrming enrollment
facts. The most overlooked aspect of health plan costs is that they are
all driven by enrollment. Since, as already noted, payers rely on employ-
ers for enrollment facts and assume no responsibility for their accuracy,
enrollment veriﬁcation is not often included in a standard payer plan
audit.
The simplest of enrollment veriﬁcations was performed, by which the
internal payroll and retiree rosters maintained by Midwest Municipality for its
own purposes was compared to each of the enrollment rosters for the self-
funded plan and the two HMOs. Then, a cross-reference of the enrollment
rosters of each payer to each other was made. This direct comparison and
reconciliation audit produced startling results.
■ A total of 150 employees were enrolled in more than one plan. This
meant that either the premiums paid for the insured plan coverage were
for no coverage at all, or the claims paid by the self-funded plan should
have been paid under the insured plan. In some cases, claims were paid
by both plans. This one area of ﬁndings accounted for over $500,000 in
immediate savings
■ Of the 1,000 retirees, 200 were enrolled in plans for active employees,
not in one of the plans designated for retirees. This is more than a clerical
error. It meant that any claims for these retirees that should have been
paid by Medicare would be denied. This is explained further on.

---

## Page 138

Chapter = c10 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:10 pm
112 Cost Recovery
■ Another ﬁve enrollees were never employed by Midwest Municipality.
Two were clerical errors, in which a group number had been input
incorrectly by another plan and that person was enrolled with Midwest
Municipality. The other three turned out to be associates of a former
clerk at Midwest Municipality who had enrolled them prior to leaving.
The next step in the enrollment audit was to conﬁrm facts about all
enrollees. Employers often rely on annual open enrollments to update facts.
This is a ﬂawed process, because in most open enrollments, only people
changing plans respond. If a person does not want any changes, they do not
respond and all the facts (correct and out-of-date alike) about that person
and that person’s family members remain unchanged and unchecked.
Knowing this human nature pattern, Health Decisions took a different
approach for Midwest Municipality. As part of open enrollment, employ-
ees and retirees were told that soon after open enrollment was done, they
were going to be asked to conﬁrm coverage for all family members. If they
wanted to remove any family members during open enrollment, no further
documentation or review of past enrollment would be done. This amnesty
offer yielded a drop of 2 percent, or 120 ineligible dependents.
About a month after open enrollment was completed, each employee
and retiree received a summary of all the enrollment facts about themselves
and all of their covered family members. People were asked to conﬁrm
these facts and update any errors. Those employees covering dependents
were also asked to provide documentation conﬁrming that dependents were
actually dependents. With Health Decisions handling all the logistical details,
Midwest Municipality was able to review results and conﬁrm the removal of
another 5 percent, or 300 dependents.
With enrollment facts correct and current, the table was set for a modern
claim audit. Please note that this must be done because a company needs
to ﬁrst know whether a person in the plan should be there in the ﬁrst place;
otherwise, all of its claims should not be paid. Claim auditing is best charac-
terized as seeking the proverbial needle in the haystack. The vast majority of
claims (95 percent-plus) are paid correctly. Traditional sample audits spend
most of their time and effort conﬁrming that fact. The modern claim audit
as practiced by Health Decisions uses the power of data processing like
a giant magnet to pull the needles out of the haystack while leaving the
correct claims, the hay, behind.
Dozens of audit tests are expressed as computer program logic. All
paid claims (along with associated enrollment data and contractual facts)
are reviewed. For Midwest Municipality, this initial computer run eliminated
80 percent of all claims, leaving behind cases with claims that warranted a
closer look. Technology was applied to classify these ﬁndings into common
categories that could be reviewed by trained staff. After expert review by

---

## Page 139

Chapter = c10 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:10 pm
Health Beneﬁts Auditing 113
Health Decisions, claim recovery opportunities totaling 5 percent of paid
claims were found in the following areas:
■ 2 percent of claims ($780,000) were associated with retirees who had
been classiﬁed as active employees. These recoveries would come from
Medicare payments to providers that are then refunded to the plan.
■ 1 percent of claims ($390,000) were associated with claims that should
have been paid by another plan under what is known as “coordination-
of-beneﬁts,” or COB. Facts about other coverage were documented
through the employee survey responses and conﬁrmed with the other
payer who would be the source of recovery.
■ 1 percent of claims ($390,000) were associated with persons who were
not eligible. These recoveries would come from the individual or their
family.
■ 0.5 percent ($195,000) of claims was associated with payer error in
the form of duplicate payments and payments for noncovered beneﬁts.
These recoveries would come from the payer.
■ 1.5 percent ($585,000) of claims was due to provider billing error in the
form of excess service billings and payments. These recoveries would
come from the providers.
As is often the case, Midwest Municipality decided that not all recovery
opportunities would be pursued for collection. They opted to not pursue
payments from individuals at all. Medicare and COB recoveries were pur-
sued with a collection rate of just over 50 percent because of payment
differences between plans and ﬁling limits that prevented full recovery. The
payer did refund the amount of claims paid in error in the form of reduced
administrative fees. The payer was selective as to which providers to be
contacted for collection and agreed instead to address these payment issues
with the providers accounting for most of the errors as part of its fraud
review. This was acceptable to Midwest Municipality.
In the end, the claim audit recovered $585,000 for Midwest Municipal-
ity. This was in addition to the $500,000 savings from duplicate enrollments
found in the enrollment reconciliation audit. The 420 ineligible dependents
removed from the plan reduced future plan liabilities by another $1,260,000.
This yielded combined recoveries and savings of $2,345,000. Health Deci-
sions audit fees were offset by recoveries before any contingency fees were
taken. Contingency fees were applied to only collected claim recoveries and
not to any savings from enrollment. In addition to a return on investment
of $20 to $1, Midwest Municipality was also able to gain a stronger work-
ing relationship with its self-funded payer and provide a positive report of
progress to elected ofﬁcials and the citizens of Midwest Municipality.

---

## Page 140

Chapter = c10 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:10 pm
114 Cost Recovery
Case Study 10.2: Employer Takes Control
Acme Widget has 500 employees in its self-funded health plan, who all
work at the same manufacturing site, with most working for the company
for years. Enrollment issues were not a big concern for Acme Widget since
most of the employees and their families were well known to the human
resources staff and the owners. Acme was essentially able to do a self-audit
of the enrollment, given its strong personal knowledge of the town’s family
histories.
The big concern at Acme Widget was that its health plan expense was
growing rapidly and the company had no idea why. Health beneﬁts repre-
sented Acme Widget’s third-biggest expense, exceeded only by payroll and
raw material used in widget making. The inﬂation rate on health plan costs
was running well above any other expense. This is a common story for most
employers. Most simply shrug and consider it a cost of doing business, but
Acme Widget was not most employers.
It decided it needed to do a health plan claims audit to see what was
going on. Its payer was reluctant to cooperate, citing standard audit proce-
dures that needed to be followed and offering explanations about general
trends in the health market that were beyond Acme Widget’s control. Health
Decisions was brought in to help. The ﬁrst order of business was getting
the claims, enrollment, and other payment data from the payer. The payer
raised a number of objections.
■ Privacy and conﬁdentiality concerns came ﬁrst. These were readily
addressed by pointing out that the plan’s ﬁduciary responsibilities
required such access and that federal privacy laws (HIPAA) expressly
permitted getting such data. This was followed by a request that Health
Decisions indemnify them for data use, a condition that was agreed to
without hesitation. It should be noted that Health Decisions has never
failed to get data for its self-funded clients from their payers. All the
client need do is simply repeat, “They’re my data and I want them.”
■ Next were concerns about how much time it would take to produce a
special ﬁle just for the payor. These evaporated when it was made clear
that all it needed to do is download ﬁles with related documentation,
because they are stored on its system.
■ Finally, it voiced concerns about the scope of the audit and certain
conditions it wanted met, which got Acme Widget riled. It pointed out
the obvious: that as a self-funded plan, Acme Widget should determine
when, how, and what it would audit. The only appropriate response
expected from its payer, whom it hired, was, “What can we do and how
can we help?” This was Acme Widget’s ﬁrst step toward getting control
of its health plan.

---

## Page 141

Chapter = c10 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:10 pm
Health Beneﬁts Auditing 115
With data in hand, Health Decisions performed its claim audit. Despite
initial misgivings, results showed the payer was doing a good job. No dupli-
cate claim payments or payments for ineligibles were found. There were,
however, some payments for eye procedures Acme Widget had excluded
in a recent plan revision. It turned out that these changes had not been put
into the payment-processing program. This was corrected, although Acme
Widget did not seek to recoup the $1,500 from the three covered members
who had the procedure.
Coordination of beneﬁts recovery opportunities was identiﬁed by Health
Decisions by following up with spouses (after getting Acme Widget’s per-
mission) to update information about their coverage with their employer.
A total of ﬁve spouses had coverage that had gone unidentiﬁed and had
claims totaling $50,000 that were another plan’s liability.
The largest area of recovery involved ﬁve large cases. These were found
to be stop-loss cases. Stop-loss is a form of insurance coverage some self-
funded plans purchase. It takes two forms, speciﬁc (coverage for individual
large cases above a given dollar amount) and aggregate (coverage for a
whole plan year when overall claims go past a certain level). The ﬁve cases
found for Acme Widget were all speciﬁc cases. Acme Widget had purchased
speciﬁc insurance for any case that exceeded $75,000 in claims for the plan.
What Health Decisions found is that claims that occurred in one plan year
had not been paid until the next plan year. Had the claims been paid in
the appropriate year, stop-loss levels would have been hit and the excess
claims paid. For these ﬁve claims, excess payments amounted to $500,000
or 15 percent of plan costs.
Negotiations with the stop-loss payer recovered $100,000. More impor-
tant, it taught Acme Widget three things. First of all, a majority of its claims
were concentrated in a small number of cases. Second, a key part of con-
trolling that expense was a carefully constructed stop-loss agreement with
terms understood by all and enforced in an agreed-to fashion. Third, and
most important, no one was going to look out for Acme Widget’s interests
better than Acme Widget.
Acme Widget got a good return on its audit investment that year. After
the audit and contingency fees paid to Health Decisions, Acme Widget
received a $5.83-to-$1 return on investment. It kept the payer but got a new
stop-loss carrier. Acme Widget also embarked on a new health plan cost
management strategy. The data used by Health Decisions were provided
to Acme Widget as Microsoft ﬁles for its direct use. De-identiﬁed versions
(removing personal information) of this ﬁle were provided for analysis by
others specializing in large case management and plan analysis. Each year,
Acme Widget reviews plan performance to conﬁrm changes it made have
been implemented and to monitor progress in areas highlighted from prior
audits and reviews.

---

## Page 142

Chapter = c10 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:10 pm
116 Cost Recovery
Case Study 10.3: Holding Company Uses Comparison
to Advantage
Holding Company had recently acquired three new companies, increasing
its total number of employees by 5,000. One of the recent acquisitions used
the same health plan payer already retained by Holding Company. The other
two, though, each had different payers. All three payers wanted to keep the
business they had and get the rest of Holding Company’s business. Holding
Company did not want to have three different payers but was not sure
which one to pick. Hence, Holding Company asked Health Decisions to
audit each payer and provide them a comparative performance of the three
payers, which can be summarized as follows:
■ Payer 1 is a small specialty payer that only services self-funded plans.
These entities are referred to as Third-Party Administrators, or TPAs. It
covered just 705 of Holding Company’s 13,763 total workers. It pro-
cessed $3,720,651 in claims during the year being audited.
■ Payer 2 was a national insurer with a separate TPA operation as part
of its offering. In both the insurance and TPA markets it was a mid-
sized company. It processed $23,979,141 for 9,910 Holding Company
employees.
■ Payer 3 was a large, well-known national insurer that used the same
processing systems for its insured and self-funded clients. It administered
beneﬁts for 3,148 Holding Company employees and paid an annual total
of $26,834,457 in claims.
Health Decisions’ audit provided Holding Company with a standard
yardstick that could be used to measure and compare performance across
the three payers. Audit results are highlighted in the following table.
Payer1 Payer2 Payer3
% claims not linked to
employee
0.16% 2.63% 18.74%
$ not linked to employee $4,057 $1,973,716 $11,608,510
% cases ﬂagged for
investigation
21.95% 44.23% 61.31%
% payments submitted for
collection/settlement
1.0% 8.7% 45.1%
The metric “% claims not linked to employee” should be zero or near
zero in a well-run plan. It is the most basic of audit tests because it assesses
the volume of paid claims that are not associated with an employee. Small
variations for surviving spouses and COBRA elections can occur; but these

---

## Page 143

Chapter = c10 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:10 pm
Health Beneﬁts Auditing 117
are not likely to be widespread. Payer 1 came close to the zero percent target
while Payer 2 missed the mark but not by a wide margin, and Payer 3 was off
the charts. The dollars associated with the unmatched claims are reported
in line two. Both Payer 1 and Payer 2 looked into this matter promptly
and professionally and reported back to Holding Company on what could
be explained and what was to be recovered. Payer 3 adopted a blame-the-
victim attitude and told Holding Company that these were enrollment-related
errors that were not its responsibility.
The measure “% cases ﬂagged for investigation” is another standard
benchmark that can be used for comparative purposes. At this stage in the
audit, the computerized tests have been completed but expert review has
not begun. A typical level of return at this point is 20 to 25 percent. This
benchmark produced a similar pattern across the three payers, with Payer 1
at expected levels, Payer 2 at higher-than-expected levels, and Payer 3 off
the charts.
Not all cases ﬂagged for investigation will be deemed recoverable.
Expert review reduces levels to the 1 to 5 percent range. Payer 1 is at the low
end of this range. Payer 2 levels are high but not as high as previous mea-
sures. Expert review was able to explain most of the issues encountered by
the audit tests. Expert review had little impact on Payer 3, however. In fact,
Payer 3 got more resistant and less cooperative as ﬁndings were investigated.
Holding Company found the results of the comparison surprising. Going
in, it expected Payer 3, the largest and best-known payer, to be the best-
performing. To learn that the small, niche vendor was its best performer gave
them the conﬁdence to continue this relationship and reward Payer 1 with
the business previously handled by Payer 3. Holding Company kept Payer 2
in place but on a probationary basis. While Payer 2 had not performed as
well as hoped, it had been responsive to audit ﬁndings and had aggres-
sively pursued collection of recoveries. Rather than putting all employees
with Payer 1, Holding Company decided to revisit Payer 1 and Payer 2 per-
formance in a follow-up comparative audit after two years. The fact that
Holding Company knew that payer performance could be monitored and
compared proved to be the major beneﬁt for them with the fruits of this
effort seen in future year claim processing.
Case Study Wrap-Up
Why Are There Issues with Health Care Claim Processing?
There are a variety of factors that affect claim processing with the most
prevalent factors cited here:

---

## Page 144

Chapter = c10 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:10 pm
118 Cost Recovery
■ Employees have a ﬁnancial interest in misreporting enrollment.If a com-
pany is not checking, some employees are willing to ride the wave,
keeping their family or other dependents on the plan well after they
should be in an effort to help themselves and their dependents.
■ In self-insured plans, third-party administrator’s work is mostly
unchecked. Company beneﬁt departments can review only so much,
which generally means that they review summary plan information
regarding enrollment and payments. Even if they had the time, it is
questionable whether the administrator would send any details, given
that it opens them to a risk for an audit and there are valid privacy con-
cerns. For the most part, company beneﬁt departments need to work
with summary information, general company and employee trends, and
respect the analysis of their administrator as reported by that adminis-
trator.
■ Claims must be paid quickly. Payers have only a certain amount of
time to pay health care invoices, which is usually within two weeks
and, hence, need to use auto-adjudication (technology to automati-
cally process most claims). Such quick turnaround time can lead to
errors in processing. This is further not helped by providers who
stack the deck in their favor by hiring consultants to maximize rev-
enue through upcoding, unbundling, and incremental service charge
increases.
■ Information is fragmented. To do a proper review, data need to be
obtained from the company regarding its participants and the plan itself,
which then need to be reconciled to the payer(s) system(s). What ensues
is a data management exercise to organize, consolidate, and ﬁnally
reconcile various computer systems and data tables. This creates the
opportunity for error because it is rare that the data are combined for
analysis or audit, given its fragmented nature.
■ Fraud is rampant. The Ofﬁce of the Inspector General of the federal
Department of Health & Human Services reported the potential dol-
lar amount of false health care claims is between 3 percent and what
could be as high as 10 percent.
1 There is a ﬁne line for employees and
providers that misreport to the plan information that leads to claim pay-
ments. While some will rationalize what they are doing is acceptable
for plan payment, many times they are committing fraud. Others simply
see the holes in the health care payment system as opportunities for
committing fraud.
What Holds Back a Review?
As there are probably many reasons for preventing a review, the following
are some of the top concerns:

---

## Page 145

Chapter = c10 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:10 pm
Health Beneﬁts Auditing 119
■ Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act (HIPAA) standards
makes it difﬁcult and dangerous to obtain and maintain associated
data. While there are clear concerns regarding gaining access to private
information, it is no reason to stop an audit. Rather, proper controls
should be maintained to prevent any unauthorized access to such infor-
mation.
■ Data are fragmented across various systems and owners.This has already
been explained earlier.
■ TPAs can be difﬁcult to audit. TPAs have been audited for many years
and have learned how to prevent auditors from camping out for too
long, which is mainly done by contractually managing the audit scope.
Generally speaking, most TPAs only allow a set number of sample claims
for auditing, which limits audit time in the ﬁeld. TPAs also are expert in
negotiating claim repayments for any errors on their part.
■ Company beneﬁt departments believe that a review will stress employee
relations. This is especially true for enrollment audits that focus directly
on employees validating their information and learning that they may
need to drop some dependents from the plan. Even though it may be
completely appropriate, some beneﬁt departments are already dealing
with employees who are working more for less, and such a change in
their family’s health coverage could lead them to a breaking point from
a relationship perspective.
Audit Types
There are two main types of audits, which are enrollment audits and claim
audits.
Enrollment audits. Exhibit 10.1 is a graphic that represents all partici-
pants in a company health care plan, which gives some perspective to the
audit reviews to be completed related to these participants.
It is not uncommon to have current or former employees incorrectly
enrolled. In rare instances, employers ﬁnd that persons enrolled have never
worked for them. An employee reconciliation service detects and documents
enrollment error and potential fraud related to employees. Furthermore,
enrollee information changes in both small and signiﬁcant ways. Over time,
these changes can accumulate to the point where they affect claim payment
and regulatory compliance. Another service related to enrollment works to
conﬁrm all enrollment facts, correct out-of-date or erroneous information,
and gather new information when needed.
Lastly, facts about dependents change constantly, which go unreported
or undetected. Typically, 4 to 12 percent of dependents enrolled in a group
health plan no longer meet eligibility criteria. A dependent review conﬁrms
dependent status through the collection and review of required documen-
tation, as provided by employees.

EXHIBIT 10.1 HealthCareEnrollmentAuditTests
Claim recovery and overpayment audits. Every health plan has waste
(claims paid that should not have been paid) and abuse (actions taken that
are inconsistent with client interests). Fraud is waste and abuse done with
criminal intent. This audit provides a basis for documenting waste and abuse
levels with precision, exposing areas for potential fraud investigation, taking
steps to recoup recoveries, and directing corrective action. Another way to
look at the process is through questions at a few key stages.
Person Presents for Care
■ Does this person report the correct source of his health coverage?
■ Is this person still eligible for that coverage?
■ Has this person met all the entitlement requirements to get coverage?
Provider Renders Service
■ Is this service a covered beneﬁt?
■ Have all required authorizations been received?
■ Are all beneﬁts limits and exclusions known?
Provider Sends Bills to Payer(s)
■ Are the diagnostic and procedure codes accurate and complete?
■ Have all provider contractual relations been reported?
■ When multiple payers are billed, is only one payer primary?
Payer Adjudicates Claims
■ Are eligibility, enrollment, and entitlement all conﬁrmed?
■ Are all plan exclusions and limits enforced?
■ Are all provider discounts and fee limits assessed?

---

## Page 147

Chapter = c10 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:10 pm
Health Beneﬁts Auditing 121
Payers Coordinate Beneﬁts
■ Are sources of other coverage identiﬁed and updated?
■ Are other payers contacted to verify primary and secondary status?
■ Are duplicate payments or overpayments identiﬁed?
Medicare Status Review
■ Are all types of Medicare eligibility identiﬁed?
■ Are Medicare facts used in claim adjudication?
■ Are procedures for Medicare collection in place?
■ Are processes for Medicare drug subsidies in place?
Judicial Decree Enforcement
■ Are all subrogation cases identiﬁed and pursued?
■ Does plan language clarify coordination with liability coverage?
■ Are divorce decrees documented and followed?
Use of Technology
It is amazing to think about how health care claims were paid before the
current capabilities of computer technology because the current use of this
technology from the initiation of the claim to the ﬁnal audit is so advanced.
From a recovery audit perspective, auditors have developed expert systems
that audit 100 percent of company data for standard errors that answer the
questions posed in this section. The main focus is to verify the eligibility of
plan participants, whether claims are being paid in accordance with the plan
for that particular company, and whether there is an error or fraud pattern
that is occurring based on past records. While the participant reconciliation
is more of a comparison of two databases, the validity of plan claims is
based on ﬁrst loading the company’s plan into the auditor’s system and
then matching claim payments to the company plan (per the auditor’s test
deck system). As to historical errors and fraud, auditors develop databases
of claim patterns that appear to be incorrect or an act of malfeasance on
the part of a participant or provider looking to get paid from the health care
plan.
These claim tests can be seen graphically in Exhibit 10.2.
Recoveries to Find
Areas where errors and fraud can occur include the following, many of
which are supported by the case studies reported in this chapter.

EXHIBIT 10.2 HealthCareOverpaymentModules
■ Cash. Cash returned from a review take the form of cost recovery for
past errors that are repaid to the company and cost avoidance, which is
the reduction or elimination of future expenses.
■ Control. Audits provide the client with direct access to all facts about
plan performance. This can help them prioritize future actions regarding
plan changes and monitoring.
■ Compliance. Since health care claims represent one of the largest com-
pany expenses, a company has a ﬁduciary responsibility to ensure its
complete and accurate processing.
Questions to Ask
Taking into consideration the case study experiences and the types of recov-
ery, the following is a list of questions that can help frame the opportunity
size of a health beneﬁt audit recovery. The list has been organized into the
areas of plan organization and size and plan monitoring.
Plan Organization and Size
■ What plans are provided to employees:
■ Medical
■ Dental
■ Prescription

---

## Page 149

Chapter = c10 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:10 pm
Health Beneﬁts Auditing 123
■ Is the company self-insured in that it will pay all employee health care
claims for each of these plans?
■ Does the company use the services of a third-party administrator to pay
claims?
■ Does the company have multiple third-party administrators?
■ What is the relationship between the company and the third-party
administrator?
■ How many enrolled employees and retirees are in each of the health
care plans?
■ How many total enrolled employees and retirees are in each health care
plan?
■ How many plan designs are there for each health care plan?
■ What were the health beneﬁt costs for the last two years?
■ Claims expense
■ Administrator service charges
Plan Monitoring
■ What technology tools does the company use in the audit?
■ What controls are in place to verify third-party administrator bills?
■ When was the last audit completed by an experienced professional of
the payer(s) of health care claims?
■ When was the last eligibility review completed for each health care plan?
Conclusion
With rising costs in an already material area, limited audit scopes afforded
by third-party administrators, and an almost full reliance on technology to
ensure payment, health care costs represent one of the top areas for an audit
at a company. Even if employee relations are a concern, leading a company
to complete only a claims audit, such reviews can yield high percentage
recoveries. One must remember that i f a 2 percent error rate is assumed,
that means $160 is saved for every employee audited as part of a health
care review.

1. Department of Justice, Federal Bureau of Investigation, Financial Crimes Report
to the Public (Washington, D.C.: Federal Bureau of Investigation, 2005).

CHAPTER 11
Lease Audits
Overview
All businesses require physical space in which they can operate. In the
service sector, workers typically occupy ofﬁce space. In the manufacturing
and distribution environment, space is needed for factories and warehouses,
while retailers typically lease stores to display their wares. In most cases,
businesses choose to lease rather than own space; the costs to rent these
premises can be a substantial component of the ﬁrm’s overall cost structure.
Many ﬁrms report that real estate–related costs are the largest administrative
cost in their income statement after personnel salaries.
Before describing the lease audit function, and the opportunities pre-
sented to ﬁrms leasing space, let’s ﬁrst review some basic information
concerning the structure of commonly used commercial real estate leases.
There are several different varieties of ofﬁce leases.
■ A full-service gross lease obligates the tenant to pay a ﬁxed sum for base
rent, typically paid monthly. The landlord is responsible for paying all
costs of building operation, repair, maintenance, insurance, and taxes.
■ A modiﬁed gross lease obligates that tenant to pay base rent, plus a
proportionate share (typically depending on what percentage of the
building is occupied by the tenant) of any increases in expenses over
a base amount. The base amount may be a ﬁxed amount stipulated
by the parties, or it may be determined by a formula (e.g., the actual
amount of expenses incurred by the landlord during the ﬁrst year of the
lease).
■ A net lease obligates the tenant to pay base rent, plus a proportion-
ate share of building expenses. These expenses are often paid by the
This chapter was written in collaboration with Corporate Real Estate Services
(CRS), Inc., the Lease Audit Specialists (crslease.com), and the Robert Thomas Group
(rentaudit.com).
124

---

## Page 151

Chapter = c11 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 2:31 pm
Lease Audits 125
landlord as they are incurred; the tenant, however, is obligated to reim-
burse the landlord for all such costs.
Retail stores are typically leased on either a full-service gross amount
or a net basis. Under the net structure, the tenant typically pays base rent
plus a proportionate share of expenses of operating and maintaining the
common areas of the shopping center, also known as cam (common area
maintenance) costs. Industrial leases, used for warehouse and factory space,
are often negotiated as net or triple net leases, in which the tenants are
responsible for paying all costs for operating, repairing, and maintaining the
facility, in addition to insurance and real estate taxes.
The case studies in this chapter demonstrate lease audits with varying
degrees of complexity. These case studies are from real audits; client names
have not been used so as to protect conﬁdentiality.
Case Study 11.1: The Portfolio Assessment of a
National Consulting Firm
The Robert Thomas Group (RTG), a specialty lease audit service provider,
was contacted by the vice president of real estate of an educational services
ﬁrm, which was interested in exploring opportunities to reduce and control
costs on the ﬁrm’s portfolio of 127 ofﬁce locations throughout the United
States. The ﬁrm’s lease administration function had already been outsourced
to a major real estate ﬁrm; the vice president, however, recognized that while
a diligent lease administration function is necessary, it is not sufﬁcient to
prohibit all landlord overcharges.
The ﬁrm engaged RTG under a contingency fee arrangement, wherein
RTG would be paid a percentage of any savings arising from the lease
audit program. As a ﬁrst step, RTG’s team spent several days reviewing
ﬁles at the client’s headquarters, with a goal of screening all leased proper-
ties in the portfolio for audit feasibility. Files included lease documents and
copies of recent landlord annual expense reconciliations. RTG constructed
a database summarizing lease structure, premises and building size, audit
rights, operating expense deﬁnitions, and lease start and end dates for every
lease.
In doing so, RTG learned that four locations were actually owned by the
client, and these were excluded from further analyses. Of the remaining 119
locations, RTG noted numerous locations for which the lease restricted the
tenant’s right to audit to a certain time (for example, within 30 days of receiv-
ing the landlord’s billings). Based on such restrictions, audits on some leases
would need to be deferred until a subsequent year. Alternatively, analyses
of some leases were expedited to accommodate such time restrictions and
requirements.

---

## Page 152

Chapter = c11 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 2:31 pm
126 Cost Recovery
RTG’s analyses of operating expense deﬁnitions in each lease provided
further guidance. Some leases included language so favorable to the landlord
that the auditor would be unlikely to have signiﬁcant basis to challenge the
landlord’s billings. In all, RTG rejected 62 leased locations that were unlikely
to be good audit candidates for the duration of the year, either because
they were too small to offer the possibility of generating material savings,
had excessively landlord-favorable provisions, had audit restrictions, or were
simply too new to be ripe for audit.
The remaining 57 locations were subjected to additional analyses to help
further screen and establish priorities. For example, audits of base years offer
the potential for greater savings, since improperly low bases often create
multiyear overcharges. The way this works is a landlord will start with a
low base year in expenses (which mask the true expenses for the facility)
and in future years, the true expenses are posted and the subsequent year
charges increase inappropriately. That way, a landlord can sign a client to a
low starting total cost ﬁrst year and then quickly ramp rates up in years two
and later.
The client and RTG eventually agreed that 10 leases would be audited
in the ﬁrst year of the process. The audits were performed and the program
resulted in substantial recoveries on ﬁve leases during the ﬁrst year. Savings
on those ﬁve leases, which comprised approximately 350,000 square feet of
rented space, totaled $930,000.
Case Study 11.2: Variety of Issues at a National
Banking Corporation
A top-10 national banking corporation had an audit performed by CRS Lease
Specialists (CRS), a nationally recognized lease audit ﬁrm, on its home ofﬁce
location in which the bank leased approximately 75 percent of a 310,857-
square-foot building.
After a review of the lease, the auditors uncovered the following issues:
■ Building Square Footage Discrepancies
This error created an overcharge due to the landlord using the cor-
rect building square footage in the base year and an incorrect reduced
square footage in the later years, thereby resulting in a greater cost per
square footage assessment against the bank.
■ Improper Assessment of Asset Management Fee and Salaries
According to the lease, the highest level of employee that the land-
lord was entitled to pass through was building superintendent. The audit
of personnel charges uncovered the names of two principals of the own-
ership group, who were paid far more than a superintendent’s salary,

---

## Page 153

Chapter = c11 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 2:31 pm
Lease Audits 127
included in the salaries and 100 percent of the asset manager, who split
his time between two buildings. Without a detailed review of the per-
sonnel charges held at the landlord’s ofﬁce, this error would have never
been detected by the company.
■ Capital Improvements Included in Operating Expenses
The lease was landlord-friendly in that most capital improvements
were allowable, if amortized, in the operating expenses. As inducement
for the bank to sign a long-term lease, however, a capital renovation
project was undertaken by the owner. The cost of this project was to
be excluded from the expenses pursuant to a letter agreement and in
a lease exhibit. The landlord systematically included various compo-
nents of the renovation among other allowable capital charges. Similar
to the personnel charges, only an onsite review of the detail cost charges
would had ever detected these charges.
■ Elevator Warranty in Base Year
The base year was bereft of any contract expense for elevator
repairs. This caused an increase in that category to nearly quadruple
from 1997 to 1998. This was due to the elevator renovation being com-
pleted in the base year.
Once these overcharges were detected, CRS went to work to get
these recoveries paid by the landlord, which is when things got tricky.
The total amount of the overcharges due to the aforementioned errors
was approximately $1.6 million for the years 1998 through 2006. Because
of ﬁnancial difﬁculties, the landlord needed to restructure his ﬁnanc-
ing for the building. Because of this, the landlord was an unwilling
and uncooperative participant in the audit. They needed to show more
revenue, not the inﬂated revenue that the audit was uncovering. The
landlord needed an additional commitment from the bank to do this.
The bank was able to use the audit ﬁndings to obtain a restructure of the
lease that included a rent credit for the full extent of the audit ﬁndings
and more favorable lease terms going forward, ultimately saving them
well in excess of the audit ﬁndings.
Case Study 11.3: Park Avenue, New York City, with
Inappropriate Cleaning and Utility Costs
CRS Lease Specialists (CRS) has an international ﬁnancial services corpo-
ration as a client. One of the audits performed involved its Park Avenue
location in New York City, wherein the client was the third-largest tenant of
a 1.1-million-square-foot building. The issues uncovered were as follows:

---

## Page 154

Chapter = c11 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 2:31 pm
128 Cost Recovery
■ Monthly Above-Standard Cleaning Costs
The tenant was required to contract with the landlord’s cleaning
company for any cleaning that was above standard and not included in
the cleaning costs billed through operating expenses. The services sec-
tion of the lease was speciﬁc in what was to be included in the cleaning
cost component of operating expenses, and all additional cleaning was
the tenant’s responsibility. The schedule of monthly cleaning services
provided to CRS by the client indicated that it was being billed for cer-
tain cleaning services that should have been already included in the
services section of the lease and billed through operating expenses. It
was also determined that the landlord was charging more for certain
services than an unrelated third party would have charged, which the
tenant claimed was unfair, given that such costs should be cheaper or at
least comparable to outside service providers. The total amount of these
overcharged cleaning costs was approximately $920,000 for the period
2001 to 2008.
■ Condenser Water Charges
The landlord sent a letter to the tenant indicating that the tenant
was to be billed for 50 tons of condenser water, at what the landlord
deemed to be market rates, for the supplemental cooling units on one
of the ﬂoors the tenant occupied. First, CRS determined that the tenant
should have been billed for only 30 tons based on the terms of the lease.
Second, CRS was unable to ﬁnd any language in the lease that allowed
the landlord to bill the tenant market rates for these condenser water
charges. The lease indicated that the maximum amount the landlord
could charge for the lease-stipulated tonnage was $600 per ton (which
was far below the $1,470.66 that the tenant was paying). The tenant
was overcharged in excess of $180,000 through 2008 alone because of
this issue. If unchecked, it is almost certain that the charge would have
continued in future months.
The total amount of the overcharges due to these errors was approx-
imately $1.1 million for years 2001 through 2008. The tenant will receive
an additional $1.1 million in savings through to the expiration of the lease.
Because of the magnitude of the claim, both the tenant and the landlord
had reservations. During discussions with the tenant, it explained its con-
cern regarding the time limitations to dispute charges that are set forth in
the lease. These limitations did not apply to these costs because they were
not considered operating expenses under the terms of the lease.
The real motive was that the landlord, naturally, did not want it to
become common knowledge that he was charging such high premiums
for these additional services. CRS, however, through professionalism and
expertise, resolved both issues in favor of the tenant in the amounts noted.

---

## Page 155

Chapter = c11 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 2:31 pm
Lease Audits 129
The landlord agreed to reduce future-year lease payments for the next two
years to pay back the value of the claim, with some interest imputed for
the time frame of payment. While agreeing to pay in full, they did ask the
tenant to sign a conﬁdentiality clause so that word did not get out to other
tenants of the agreed-to ﬁndings.
Case Study 11.4: Multinational Corporation Audits
One Lease for Big Savings
A large multinational corporation retained CRS as its exclusive lease audit
provider. One audit location involved a 179,308-square-foot space in its thir-
teenth year of a 25-year lease located in the northwest United States. Four
issues involved in the initial audit claim were:
1. Operating expenses were not allowed to increase more than 5 percent
from the previous year. The landlord was not applying the 5 percent cap
to the expenses, causing an overcharge.
2. The lease had a limit of a 3 percent management fee, and the landlord
was charging 4.5 percent. CRS had the percentage reduced to the lease
limitation of 3 percent.
3. The inclusion of disallowed expenses for the retail and parking com-
ponent of the building (value: $85,075) were hidden in allowable cost
categories, almost in an attempt to fool the tenant.
4. The reimbursement of audit fees due to exceeding a minimum allowable
error.
All of these current values are savings or overcharges through December
2007. The issue of the annual cap was resolved after some negotiations
regarding lease interpretation. All future savings remained in place. With
respect to the reimbursement of the audit fee, the client asked CRS to settle
at less than the total contingent fee for current savings, which was nearly
$252,000. The client did so gladly as the landlord, client, and CRS worked
methodically and amiably through all of the relevant issues.
The ﬁnal settlement of the claims breaks down as follows:

Case Study Wrap-Up
How Leases Are Negotiated and Administered
Major real estate decisions may get attention from top management. Loca-
tion strategies are discussed and major lease commitments are analyzed. In
larger ﬁrms, a team of dedicated experts is assembled to provide necessary
support. These teams may include in-house or outsourced experts such as
real estate brokers, attorneys, lease negotiators, space planners, and facility
construction managers. Additional support is often provided by other depart-
ments such as the ﬁnance and accounting departments, human resources,
and information technology.
After the initial work is done to set the strategy, negotiate leases, and
occupy the premises, however, a different team, including accounts payable
clerks, lease administrators, and facility managers, may take over to admin-
ister the leased portfolio. The ﬁrm may also employ or hire independent
lease auditors. The tenant’s focus in this phase is on operating the premises
smoothly as intended while avoiding unfavorable surprises vis-`a-vis the bud-
getary plan. One aspect of this is making sure that landlord’s billings comply
with the lease terms.
In typical situations, landlords send monthly invoices to the tenant for
base rent, as well as other additional rent that may be required under the
lease. Additional rent might include items such as contributions toward
building operating expenses, real estate taxes, parking, special tenant
requests for additional cleaning, overtime heating and air conditioning, addi-
tional keys, replacement light bulbs in the premises, and so forth.
In smaller or decentralized tenant ﬁrms, the landlord’s bills may be
received and paid by the tenant’s local ofﬁce manager. Firms with larger
leased portfolios often have one or more designated lease administrators.
These lease administrators receive and approve payment to the landlord
for each of the landlord’s billings. Administrators may access the lease doc-
uments and understand the essential aspects of each lease to ensure that
the landlord’s billings are generally consistent with the tenant’s obligations.
Internal company lease administrators can identify and work with the land-
lord to correct any simple landlord billing errors on issues such as the
following (provided by the Robert Thomas Group):
■ Does the tenant still rent space at the address as billed by the landlord
(or did the lease expire last month!)?
■ Did the landlord use the correct base for calculating escalations in a
modiﬁed gross lease?
■ Did the landlord use the correct proportionate share of operating
expenses?

---

## Page 157

Chapter = c11 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 2:31 pm
Lease Audits 131
■ Has the landlord included major categories of charges that were specif-
ically excluded under the negotiated terms of the lease? (For example,
has the landlord billed for real estate taxes when the tenant is not
responsible for taxes?)
The typical lease administrator has limited time (given that it may man-
age hundreds of leases), however, and limited access to detailed landlord
data. As can be seen from the case studies, most of the errors were arguably
focused attempts by the landlord to increase inappropriate expenses for
payment by the company. This is where the value of a lease auditor can be
seen in playing the role of an independent lease administrator on a periodic
basis, as well as a detailed auditor of the landlord’s ﬁles.
While there is presently no recognized professional designation granted
to qualiﬁed lease auditors, they are often drawn from the ranks of certiﬁed
public accountants, attorneys, or others with comparable skill in addition to
experience in dealing with leases and accounting for commercial buildings.
Lease auditors employ a systematic process for ensuring that tenants pay no
more than required under the terms of their leases.
The Approach to Auditing Leases
The following is the methodology used by Corporate Real Estate Services
(CRS), the Lease Audit Specialists, in conducting a lease audit, and can be
used as a standard model for such audits.
■ Preliminary consultation.The purpose of the preliminary consultation is
to organize all project information with an understanding of the client’s
procedures before beginning any detailed review. CRS will work with
the client to review the lease portfolio, deﬁne locations to be audited,
and establish basic procedures, lines of communication, and a time
schedule for the work. This step will allow CRS to seamlessly become
an extension of the client’s real estate department. The preliminary
consultation process may include the following:
■ Work onsite at the client’s ofﬁces in conjunction with the lease admin-
istration team
■ Assemble and review basic documents and information, including, but
not limited to, leases, amendments, riders, correspondence, assess-
ments for rent and additional rent, statements, notices, and so forth
■ Copy all pertinent documents and set up an audit ﬁle on each location
■ Identify any recognizable missing information to be obtained
■ Preliminary audit review. During the preliminary audit review for the
client, CRS will thoroughly review the audit ﬁle, and for each loca-
tion conduct analyses, identify potential irregularities, and recommend

---

## Page 158

Chapter = c11 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 2:31 pm
132 Cost Recovery
actions to be taken. During this phase, CRS will not contact any land-
lords directly. Any information required from landlords will be requested
through the client, and CRS will prepare any correspondence required
for the client to send to landlords. The preliminary audit review typically
includes the following for each location:
■ Read and thoroughly study contents of audit ﬁle
■ Conduct a compliance review of assessed charges for occupancy
expenses
■ Compare expenses to market indexes
■ Identify issues and opportunities
■ Prepare a list of missing information needed from landlord
■ Prepare correspondence for the client to send to landlord, if required
A preliminary audit review report will be prepared and reviewed
with the client to determine future actions.
■ Audit. For each client location selected for a detailed audit, the respec-
tive landlord will be contacted by CRS (with the client’s full knowledge
and prior written approval) to investigate, validate, and resolve any
potential claims for overcharges. CRS will provide all necessary actions
and correspondence to accomplish the following:
■ Inform landlord of certain potential claims and request speciﬁc collat-
eral information and records
■ Perform ﬁeld audit of landlord records where recommended by CRS
and approved by the client
■ Survey site conditions, where appropriate
■ Conduct an in-depth audit of materials and records received and con-
duct an issue-by-issue analysis of each potential claim
■ Where appropriate, conduct preliminary discussions with the landlord
concerning settlement of potential claims
A lease audit report will be prepared to include all detailed analyses
and ﬁndings. This report is then carefully reviewed with the client to
explore all issues, obtain any veriﬁcation, and clear up any internal
questions. Once the lease audit report is fully reﬁned, it is issued to the
landlord.
■ Resolution of claims. The resolution of a claim, once identiﬁed, usually
requires the most time and expertise of a lease auditing ﬁrm. It is at this
juncture that the client will rely on CRS to devote its resources and expe-
rience, including the direct involvement of principals as counselors and
negotiators. For each location reviewed, CRS will work with the client
to plan and execute negotiating strategies to obtain the most favorable
resolution of all potential claims. To ensure the greatest cost savings
results, CRS will coordinate with the client to:
■ Compare NPV savings of potential options
■ Negotiate lump-sum settlements and future payments as appropriate
■ Negotiate trade-off of potential savings for desired lease modiﬁcations

---

## Page 159

Chapter = c11 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 2:31 pm
Lease Audits 133
■ Leverage savings into lease extensions, contractions, cancellations,
and so forth
■ Project closeout and account maintenance. Lease audit savings may
take many forms and often involve future credits and adjustments. The
results achieved today may be eroded quickly by other adjustments a
landlord makes to its property management practices as a result of the
audit. It is therefore critical that the appropriate measures are taken to
protect the client going forward. Once the entire project is complete,
CRS will prepare a ﬁnal status report identifying total savings for the
project and a schedule of follow-up activities that may be required to
ensure that the savings are fully realized over time, such as:
■ Regular spot audits of selected items
■ Conﬁrmation of credits and reimbursements
■ Review of future escalations and reconciliations
It Is Not so Much Finding It, but Recovering
It that Matters
More often than not, initial claim amounts will not vary substantially from
the ﬁnal settlement. Of course, there are instances when documentation is
provided after a claim is formulated that can eliminate the original basis
for the claim. Best-case and worst-case scenarios are discussed along with
strategies to achieve maximum results that will best suit the corporate culture
of that particular client, or landlord-tenant relationship. Lease audit claims
should not be pursued that are not well documented or issues that do not
have the backing of the client. All questionable claims should always be
discussed with the client before presentation to the landlord.
This is where the value of an internal lease administrator or external
lease auditor can be best seen in that these individuals have the countless
years of negotiation experience to rely on, which acts almost like a case
law for their respective employer. They can use previous negotiation set-
tlements as examples for current settlements and identify new strategies for
repayment not previously considered in the current negotiation.
Recoveries to Find
Areas where errors can occur include the following, which are brieﬂy
explained. Many of them are supported by the case studies recorded in
this chapter.
■ Rent. Raising rent on incorrect dates.
■ Operating expense charges. Gross-up issues; the landlord may not be
following gross-up language in the lease, for example, not grossing
up to 95 percent occupancy.

---

## Page 160

Chapter = c11 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 2:31 pm
134 Cost Recovery
■ Real estate and other taxes.The landlord may not be properly account-
ing for exemptions or deductions. Also, not using a fully assessed
building could cause allocation errors to be made. This is most speciﬁc
to a base year lease.
■ Common area maintenance.The landlord could be charging a higher
or lower pro rata share or charging for common areas that a tenant is
not using.
■ CPI rate escalations. The landlord may be using the wrong Consumer
Price Index.
■ Utility usage and charges. The landlord could be sub-metering the
usage incorrectly or not using the correct rates. Some leases allow for
a certain number of free hours of utility usage that the landlord may
inadvertently be charging for. The methodology that the landlord uses
in grossing up the utilities also leaves room for errors.
■ Management fees. The landlord may be exceeding the cap or may be
charging management and administration fees, which some leases do
not allow for.
■ Space measurement. There may be a speciﬁc square footage that was
agreed to and deﬁned in the lease, and the landlord may be using a
different measurement, which would lead to incorrect billings. Some
leases deﬁne square footage as an approximation to be veriﬁed at
a later time by the landlord’s architect or by using Building Owners
and Managers Association standards. When this veriﬁcation does not
occur, errors can be made when invoicing.
■ Construction costs. Major improvements or repairs on the building
occur, such as a new roof or an addition of a parking lot. Most leases
do not allow for capital expenditures, and the landlord may try to
pass those expenses through to the tenant.
■ Porters wage formulas. Porters wage is normally only found in New
York City. There are many components that factor into a porters
wage rate, which can lead to room for error or creative billing in
the landlord’s favor.
■ Lease calculations. In addition to all of the other categories in this list,
discrepancies can also occur because of pure mathematical calculation
errors.
Questions to Ask When Assessing the Opportunity
Taking into consideration the case study experiences and the types of recov-
ery, the following is a list of questions that can help frame the opportunity
size of a lease audit recovery. The list has been organized into the areas of
facility, lease language, and lease administration.

---

## Page 161

Chapter = c11 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 2:31 pm
Lease Audits 135
Facility Portfolio
■ What are the sizes of the leased locations (in square feet)?
■ How many real estate leases do we have in our portfolio, and what
is our rent outlay on them?
■ Have there been major repairs to the building?
■ Has the building ownership changed in the past two years?
■ Has the building management company changed in the past two
years?
■ Have there been major tenant ﬁt-up projects going on in the building?
■ What is the ﬁnancial health of the landlord and is it on an expected
upward or downward trend for the next year?
Lease Language and Experience
■ Are there audit restrictions to understand in the leases, which could
dictate the timing of the audits?
■ Are the leases simple (full gross) rent or are they more complex and
thus requiring expenses such as common area maintenance to be paid
by the company?
■ Have there been noted increases in operating expenses? Do the
increases exceed normal inﬂation for the area of the country?
■ Have there been any accounting changes in invoices from the land-
lord?
Lease Administration
■ Is there a lease administration team? If so, is it performing in-depth
audits or just desktop reviews?
■ What is the experience level of the lease administration team?
■ How well staffed is the lease administration function? Does it have
enough time to review landlord invoices before payment?
■ Is the number of leases material enough to require technology to
manage the leases? If so, what technology are they using to manage
the lease information?
Conclusion
Assuming the organization leases ofﬁce space, there is a good chance that it
is one of the top-three expenses of the organization. Given this materiality
level, it only makes sense to complete a review of the lease expense, even
if it is simply a risk assessment using the questions posed in this chapter.
Assuming the company has leases that go beyond simple full gross leases
(and probably regardless of this fact), the next step should be to review the
lease terms with the landlord’s detailed accounting books.
