## Page 162

Chapter = c12 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:41 pm
CHAPTER 12
Order to Cash Reviews
Overview1
Revenue recognition poses signiﬁcant risk to a company’s proﬁtability and
has also contributed to an erosion in the integrity of ﬁnancial reporting. In
recent years, several high-proﬁle incidents of improper revenue recognition
attracted the attention of the business media and led to unﬂattering cover-
age. Therefore, accounts receivable, and the revenue and cash streams that
drive it, is arguably the most risky part of a ﬁnancial statement audit. Any
restatement, due to error or fraud, has the potential to bankrupt an organi-
zation (see Chapter 14, “Proactive Fraud Detection”, for more information).
Aside from the ﬁnancial statement impacts, the effects of improper billings
or efﬁciency of the revenue-to-cash transformation process could have siz-
able impacts on the company’s proﬁtability and ability to sustain operations.
This is not to mention cash misappropriation fraud, which, based on recent
studies, occurs in 89 percent
2 of all reported fraud cases.
Case Study 12.1: Construction Materials Not
Reporting All Cement Loads or Pricing Them
Properly
An internal auditor working for a construction material company was called
in to review one particular shipping facility that had above-average sales
activity but somehow was not as proﬁtable as other locations. When he
arrived, he learned that the staff had been working overtime to meet the
summertime demand for construction projects, were constantly low on
inventory, yet could not ﬁgure out why they were consistently under-
reporting sales ﬁgures.
This chapter was written in collaboration with Ekaros Analytical, Inc. and its
publication Revenue Test Set for ACL as written by the author of this book.
136

---

## Page 163

Chapter = c12 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:41 pm
Order to Cash Reviews 137
The auditor quickly went to work to map out the business process asso-
ciated with the revenue cycle, including mapping out all internal controls
along the way. He noticed a few key control lapses:
■ The system that recorded shipments was not integrated with the billing
system. While other locations had implemented the latest shipping and
billing modules that automatically reconciled themselves at the end of
each working day, this location needed to manually marry the shipping
system to the billing system.
■ The shipping bay itself required a clerk to manually register the sale,
whereas at other locations, the system would automatically calculate
a sale and print a bill of lading when the truck pulled up to the
scale.
■ One of the largest customers, while busy with activity, was also ﬁnan-
cially unstable. To counteract this, the company had raised prices on its
construction materials in a way to manage its credit risk. It also planned
to discount the customer once all invoices were paid. It was a form of
insurance for product that may not get paid for if the company went
under.
After mapping out the process and procedures, the auditor decided to
have a stakeout of the operations. He had received the day before a most
updated price listing for product and then parked his car about a mile away
(but still within distance of binoculars). He watched each truck pull in, saw
what product was being provided, and even tried to estimate the truck’s
tare and gross weight, all in an attempt to do his own physical count of the
inventory being shipped that day.
When the auditor returned the next day to the shipping location, he
worked with the billing clerk to reconcile his facts with her ﬁgures. The
clerk found that the billings had not included four loads during the hours
of 9
A.M. to noon and then an additional three loads from the noon to 5P.M.
shift. That was seven full loads, which were estimated to cost roughly
$18,000, in one day! When they went back to the shipping records, they
found that ﬁve of the shipments had bills of lading but were confused with
other shipments to the same customer and never billed.
What was alarming was that an additional two shipments, presumably
due to the manual nature of the shipping function, were never recorded at
all. When they confronted the shipping clerk during that time period, he
looked as if he wanted to run out of the building, he started to stutter and
looked as pale as a ghost. While the auditor had thought it was a simple error,
the clerk immediately confessed that he had worked a side deal with one of
the company’s customers, his uncle, to get free product for his landscaping
business. The shipping clerk was terminated soon thereafter and was later

---

## Page 164

Chapter = c12 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:41 pm
138 Cost Recovery
heard to be working for the landscaping business (as it seemed he was even
when working for the construction material company).
The reconciliation process went further to check the pricing list the
auditor had received from the sales manager the day before to also note that
not all prices were properly registered, especially to the largest customer.
It seemed that prices changed in the last week had not made their way
to the billing department and, hence, the old lower prices were used for
product pricing. This price differential led to a $4,000 recovery to add to
the $18,000 in missed shipments. The auditor calculated that the $22,000 in
daily error was very close to what was missing in the monthly sales reports,
when extrapolated over the month, and represented valid sales that should
have been charged to customers in the ﬁrst place.
Case Study 12.2: Too Many (Fraudulent)
Fourth-Quarter Sales
A ﬁnancial statement auditor, simply looking at the ﬁnancial statements of
a clothing retailer, realized that the company’s third-quarter results were
underestimates, and yet in the fourth quarter, they seemed to overshoot all
targets to report one of the highest quarterly sales ever. When approaching
the marketing and sales director, she mentioned that the sales team went
over and above that quarter to post sales, working overtime, using new
advertising approaches, and pricing product lower than normal in an effort
to maximize sales volume.
The auditor, while trying to believe the story, was not convinced, so
he used a data analysis program to collect all sales to customers for the
ﬁrst three quarters of the year. He then received the same data ﬁle for the
fourth quarter. These two ﬁles were summarized on customers to arrive at a
ﬁrst-three-quarters and a fourth-quarter sales summary data ﬁle. These two
ﬁles were exported to Microsoft Excel because they were small enough to
manage in this program.
Using the VLOOKUP() function in Excel, the auditor was able to relate
to the ﬁrst three quarters the fourth-quarter sales activity using the customer
number as the look-up ﬁeld between the two worksheets. He found some
interesting trends and outliers:
■ Most customers were below sales targets when you combined their
quarterly sales. Therefore, on average, sales were down by customer.
■ Eighteen customers posted above-average sales in the fourth quarter.
While six of them did seem to have improved businesses, the remaining
12 had difﬁculty paying invoices, given their slowing sales.

---

## Page 165

Chapter = c12 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:41 pm
Order to Cash Reviews 139
■ One customer that had no sales in the previous three quarters posted
a precise $4 million sale in the fourth quarter, which, when looking
back at the sales details, was due to four $1 million entries being
posted.
Instead of the auditor going back to the marketing and sales director,
he went to the salespeople associated with the accounts. Most of them had
no idea of the increased sales and, surprisingly, the $4 million customer was
assigned directly to the director. After a further investigation and review of
actual invoices, the auditor concluded that roughly $6.5 million in sales had
been ﬁctitiously added to the sales ledgers, almost single-handedly by the
marketing and sales director. She was terminated but not before admitting
that she had great pressure from the company to hit sales targets and felt
that she was going to get ﬁred anyway if sales were lower than expected
for the year.
Case Study Wrap-Up
What Goes Wrong in the Order-to-Cash Process?3
■ Ineffective controls. Any system that is managed by humans is prone to
error. It is hoped that through appropriate internal control, such errors
will be prevented. Given that any internal control can be circumvented,
however, veriﬁcation of the appropriate processing is critical to the orga-
nization’s success. Company internal controls are simply good business
practices that are established to ensure that, among other things:
■ Sales are authorized at the right level of management before being
made
■ Data entry errors are minimized in the shipment and delivery to sales
process
■ Sales are completely registered
■ Prices are at the most-recently approved levels
■ Customers are entered into a master ﬁle before being invoiced, and
duplicate customer identities are detected and promptly removed
When one looks at controls, one needs to remember that they are
based on process, technology, and people. As in the case studies, some-
times a bad process was at fault, such as when prices were not timely
updated in the system. At other times, faulty technology led to fewer
shipments being recorded. Some people, too, who had inappropriate
motives, are to blame.
■ Fraudulent ﬁnancial misstatements. Management is under pressure
every day to meet sales and associated income targets. Stock price, man-

---

## Page 166

Chapter = c12 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:41 pm
140 Cost Recovery
agement compensation, and analyst and media commitments exacerbate
this pressure, so much so that it can persuade management to mis-
state earnings. Although such misstatements may start as a laissez-faire
reading of Generally Accepted Accounting Principles, they tend to snow-
ball into larger and larger entries until they become outright fraud.
It is therefore no surprise that a 10-year study commissioned by the
Committee of Sponsoring Organizations of the Treadway Commission
4
concluded that more than half of ﬁnancial statement frauds involved
overstating revenue. This is corroborated by the 2008 Report to the
Nation on Occupational Fraud and Abuseby the Association of Certiﬁed
Fraud Examiners, which also found that the median ﬁnancial statement
misstatement was for $2 million. If the business model is sound, and
industry conditions do not pose a threat, there is nothing for manage-
ment to feel pressured about. Unfortunately, business models are not
always realized because they are envisioned and the industry may be
highly affected because of change and competition. Management will
be the primary identiﬁers of these trends but may not be compelled
to explain such vulnerabilities with investors or creditors. Thus, it is
an imperative for the auditor to not only understand the business and
industry conditions, but also to use computer-assisted tools to iden-
tify trends underlying the ﬁnancial reports. Such a need has recently
been regulated for U.S. ﬁnancial statement audits through the Statement
on Auditing Standards 99: Consideration of Fraud in a Financial State-
ment Audit. The standard requires that revenue recognition fraud be
speciﬁcally considered a risk, unless proven otherwise, on all ﬁnancial
statement audits.
What Computer Reports Can Be Run to Review the Order-to-Cash Process
for Integrity?5
One effective tool proven in the case studies was the use of computer
reports to identify issues as errors and fraud, which tend to live in the 1
percent area (if not less) of company transactions. This can make manual
procedures to identify fraud almost impossible, which was the case in the
customer-by-customer analysis in the second case study. The following are
some suggested computer audit tests. (Please also see the Proactive Fraud
and Technology chapters for more information on computer reporting.)
■ Changes in customer or salesperson sales invoice totals (most recent 12
months compared with the previous 12 months or most recent quarter
to the previous three quarters).
■ Average sales by customer for a given time to those in past X days (X
is set by the user).

---

## Page 167

Chapter = c12 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:41 pm
Order to Cash Reviews 141
■ List customers with more than X invoices in the period (X is set by the
user).
■ Summarize by customer all shipments or invoices with no sales orders,
and list detail transactions.
■ Review customers with little or no sequence between invoice numbers.
■ Sequence gaps in invoice numbers, sales orders, shipments, and cash
receipt numbers.
■ Extract sales where the shipping or invoice quantity exceeds the order
quantity.
■ List possible duplicate invoices, sales orders, shipments, or cash receipts
based on matching two to ﬁve ﬁelds (use selection). Please note this
application will remove all voided transaction amounts before process-
ing.
■ Summarize sales orders to sales invoices and sort from high to low (sales
orders and invoices) by customer.
■ Sales prices on a sale more than two and three deviations of the mean
sales price for that product and location.
■ Sales with prices that exceed the approved maximum price for that
product.
■ Sales with prices that are higher or lower than the approved price list.
■ Summarize sales and cash receipts by customer and sort from high to
low (sales and cash receipts).
■ Summarize credit memos or refunds by customer, issuer, and type.
■ Compare current year to prior year customer master ﬁle to detect addi-
tional or deleted customers.
■ Identify customers with net credit balances.
■ Customer with open invoices exceeding credit limits.
■ Customers with no credit limits.
■ Extract material dollar receivables that are past due (past due is set by
the user).
■ Summarize customer activity with weighted days sales outstanding and
interest lost for not receiving cash in 30, 45, and 60 days.
■ Calculate the difference between the order and shipping or invoice date,
as well as the interest lost for the delay. Then, identify orders with no
shipping or invoice dates.
■ Age invoice balances or sales this period and a prior period, reporting
changes in each stratum.
Recoveries to Find
Areas where errors and fraud can occur include the following, many of
which are supported by the preceding case studies:

---

## Page 168

Chapter = c12 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:41 pm
142 Cost Recovery
■ Complete, accurate, and timely sales processing.6 Efﬁciency has become
commonplace in the wake of right-sizing and outright downsizing of
employees. Companies need to do more with less to stay competitive
or someone else will. In the accounts receivable life cycle, cash is king.
Therefore, the efﬁcient transformation of sales orders into cash needs to
be paramount to sustain a healthy operation. To that end, the following
recoveries should be considered in any revenue cycle review.
■ Identifying unfulﬁlled sales orders or shipments never invoiced
■ Ensuring all sales orders are priced appropriately
■ Calculating the time to turn a sales order into a shipment, a shipment
into an invoice, and an invoice into cash
■ Ensuring cash payments are made for all accounts, and that write-offs
are the exception versus the norm
■ Reducing manual processing for ordering, shipments, invoicing, and
cash handling
■ Reducing uncollectible accounts. Everyone in business wants to get
paid for the work they do for the customer. While a little lateness can
be accepted, a customer simply not paying should be assessed and
appropriate credit measures taken. A company can be assessed to how
tight or loose they are in credit procedures, as well as the results of
their transforming receivables into cash, mostly with computer-assisted
reports.
■ Fraud reduction.7 In the capital and third-party lending marketplace,
even the hint of ﬁnancial restatement can have damaging effects on the
company. Aside from ﬁnancial statement fraud explained more fully in
the Proactive Fraud chapter, there are three major types of fraud in the
accounts receivable and revenue area, one of which presented itself in
the aforementioned Case Study 2.
■ Skimming, in which cash is stolen from an organization before it is
recorded on the organization’s books and records. Skimmed cash is
then lapped between accounts to conceal the skimming.
■ Cash larceny, in which cash is stolen from an organization after it has
been recorded on the organization’s books and records.
■ Fraudulent shipments made to employee or business friend locations.
Using the statistics in the 2008 Report to the Nation on Occupational
Fraud and Abuse,8 a company loses 7 percent to fraud, of which roughly
26.9 percent of all cases involves misappropriation of accounts and revenue.
This means that a company with $250 million in sales loses $17.5 million
to fraud, of which $4.7 million involves the misappropriation of accounts
receivable and revenue. This is a sizable savings, which goes straight to the
bottom line. This savings excludes the immeasurable beneﬁts of preventing
a ﬁnancial misstatement. Financial misstatements can lead to an extreme

---

## Page 169

Chapter = c12 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:41 pm
Order to Cash Reviews 143
market decline in the value of a company, potential ﬁnes, and the ultimate
demise of an organization.
Questions to Ask When Assessing the Opportunity9
The following is a list of ﬁve speciﬁc audit objectives that an auditor would
use when reviewing the sales, shipping, cash receipts, and accounts receiv-
able functions for a ﬁnancial statement audit. Regardless of a ﬁnancial
statement or recovery audit, the objectives are just as relevant.
1. Sales, shipping, cash receipt, and accounts receivable activities are oper-
ating effectively and efﬁciently.
2. Sales, cash receipts, and accounts receivable exist and are properly
authorized.
3. Sales, shipping, cash receipt, and accounts receivable are accurate and
complete.
4. Accounts receivable balances are properly valued and presented.
5. Audit trails are maintained and timely information is provided to deci-
sion makers.
With the aforementioned audit objectives as a backdrop, the following
questions should be asked, with additional test work completed to support
all answers received (more likely by a recovery audit ﬁrm). This test work
could include vouching of documents, observation of the process, inquiry of
management, and recalculation of balances. While such procedures sound
manual in nature, there is no reason why a majority of them could not be
automated using computer reports.
1. Sales, shipping, cash receipt, and accounts receivable activities are oper-
ating effectively and efﬁciently.
■ Do written sales order, revenue recognition, shipping, cash receipt,
and accounts receivable policies and procedures exist and are they
kept up to date?
■ Does a written code of ethics exist for the organization that speciﬁcally
identiﬁes conﬂicts of interest, cash larceny, and fraudulent ﬁnancial
statements for signature by all employees?
■ Are budgets maintained and reviewed to actual expenses on a periodic
basis?
■ Do all employees processing orders, shipments, cash receipts, or
accounts receivable take vacations and have their duties carried out
by other employees?
■ Is data entry (i.e., sales order processing) kept to a minimum?

---

## Page 170

Chapter = c12 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:41 pm
144 Cost Recovery
■ Are input error reports run and reviewed on a periodic basis?
■ Are shipments made on a timely basis in relation to the order date?
■ Are cash receipts deposited on a timely basis in relation to the receipt
date?
■ Are expedited procedures taken on slow-paying customer or delin-
quent accounts?
■ Are performance measures reviewed of the ordering, shipping, cash
receipts, and accounts receivable functions?
■ Are customer complaints reviewed and is corrective action applied
based on the issues raised by customers?
2. Sales, cash receipts, and accounts receivable exist and are properly
authorized.
2A. Exist
■ Are customers sent statements on a periodic basis (i.e., monthly)?
Furthermore, are these statements sent by someone independent
of any accounts receivable life cycle function? Are customer bal-
ances positively conﬁrmed on a periodic basis?
2B. Authorized sales orders
■ Are sales orders approved by the appropriate level of management
before entry?
■ Is the creditworthiness of potential customers determined before
acceptance of the sales order?
■ Are sales orders not allowed by the system for customers exceed-
ing their credit limit?
■ Are credit limits monitored and adjusted on a timely basis? Fur-
thermore, do all customers have credit limits in the system?
■ Are prices charged to customers reconciled to a customer agree-
ment or standard price ﬁle?
■ Are prices charged to customers reviewed for reasonableness?
■ Are sales returns and refunds issued for valid customer returns
and refunds? What controls are in place to independently review
such processing?
■ Are only authorized employees allowed to enter transactions
into the sales system? Is a list of these authorized employees
reviewed on a periodic basis to ensure that it is accurate and
complete?
■ Is there a segregation of duties between those entering sales and
any other function in the accounts receivable life cycle? (Inquiry
and Observation)
2B. Authorized shipments
■ Are only authorized employees allowed to enter transactions into
the shipping system? Is a list of these authorized employees

---

## Page 171

Chapter = c12 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:41 pm
Order to Cash Reviews 145
reviewed on a periodic basis to ensure it is accurate and
complete?
■ Is there a segregation of duties between those shipping product
and any other function in the accounts receivable life cycle?
2B. Authorized cash receipts
■ Are only authorized employees allowed to enter transactions into
the cash receipt system? Is a list of these authorized employees
reviewed on a periodic basis to ensure it is accurate and complete?
■ Is a lockbox primarily used to receive cash receipts?
■ For any checks sent to the company (instead of the lockbox),
are all checks received restrictively endorsed upon receipt in the
mailroom?
■ Are checks received stored in a safe place prior to depositing?
■ For cash received directly from the customer, are loss prevention
procedures in effect (e.g., cameras to monitor activity)?
■ Is there a segregation of duties between those receiving cash and
entering the cash to client accounts?
■ Is there a segregation of duties between those entering cash
receipts and any other function in the accounts receivable life
cycle?
2B. Authorized accounts receivable
■ Are only authorized employees allowed to enter transactions
into the accounts receivable system? Is a list of these authorized
employees reviewed on a periodic basis to ensure it is accurate
and complete?
■ Can employees with access to accounts receivable processing
functions also enter new customers?
■ Are reports produced of all changes to master ﬁle information
(e.g., customer master ﬁle) on a timely basis?
■ Are adjustments to the accounts receivable subledger properly
authorized by the appropriate level of management prior to entry?
■ Is there a segregation of duties between those maintaining the
accounts receivable subledger and any other function in the
accounts receivable life cycle?
3. Sales, shipping, cash receipt, and accounts receivable are accurate and
complete.
3A. Accurate
■ Are invoices to be sent matched with the related sales order (or
other internal documentation of order) and any shipping informa-
tion?
■ Is the quantity shipped and description of items shipped matched
to the sales order prior to sending?

---

## Page 172

Chapter = c12 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:41 pm
146 Cost Recovery
■ Are shipments with no sales orders or other internal documenta-
tion researched periodically?
■ Are general ledger accounts reviewed for accuracy prior to post-
ing?
■ Are the invoices checked for mathematical accuracy?
■ Are preprogrammed keying formats used to ensure that data are
recorded in the proper ﬁeld, format, and so on?
■ Are the following checked for validity on all input transactions:
■ Characters?
■ Reasonableness?
■ Consistency? (i.e., are the data consistent with other ﬁelds?)
■ Completeness? (i.e., are any ﬁelds missing?)
■ Extraneous data (that is, have too many ﬁelds been completed?)
■ Are error messages produced for each transaction containing data
that do not meet edit requirements?
■ Do error messages provide clear, understandable corrective
actions to be taken?
■ Are users prevented from moving ahead in the system until the
edit check issues are rectiﬁed?
■ Are users prevented from overriding or bypassing data validation
and editing problems? If users are not prevented, are error reports
produced summarizing errors for management’s review?
■ What system controls prevent (or detect) duplicate recording of
transactions?
■ What system controls prevent (or detect) duplicate recording of
customers?
■ Are bank reconciliations completed on a periodic basis? If so,
is this completed by an individual independent of the accounts
payable function? (Inquiry and Observation)
3B. Complete
■ Are all sales order numbers sequential? Furthermore, is there a
periodic veriﬁcation to ensure that all sales orders are accounted
for and that any old, outstanding sales orders are researched?
■ Are all shipment numbers sequential? Furthermore, is there a peri-
odic veriﬁcation to ensure that all shipments are accounted for and
that any old, outstanding sales orders are researched?
■ Are all cash receipt (or related cash receipt batches) numbers
sequential? Furthermore, is there a periodic veriﬁcation to ensure
that all cash receipts are accounted for and that any old, outstand-
ing sales orders are researched?
■ Are cash receipts batched prior to entry with reconciliation to
batch and hash totals prior to entry submission?

---

## Page 173

Chapter = c12 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:41 pm
Order to Cash Reviews 147
■ What control ensures transactions are recorded in the proper
period, especially sales orders at period end?
■ What control ensures adjustments to transactions are recorded in
the proper period, especially customer account write-offs before
period end?
■ Related to the reconciliation of the subsidiary accounts receivable
ledger to the general ledger:
■ Is it completed on a periodic basis?
■ Is this function completed by someone independent of the sales,
shipping, and cash receipt functions?
■ Are unusual items between the subsidiary ledger and general
ledger investigated?
■ Furthermore, is the subsidiary journal reviewed on a periodic basis
for unusual items (e.g., credit balances)?
4. Accounts receivable balances are properly valued and presented.
■ Does an allowance for doubtful accounts exist and is it kept
current?
■ Is this allowance based on a formula with speciﬁc reserves for
identiﬁed customers and general reserves based on a predeﬁned
formula?
■ Is this allowance reviewed periodically for reasonableness?
■ Are old accounts receivable balances (i.e., over 120 days) reviewed
for valuation or full write-offs?
■ Are accounts receivable balances reviewed for valuation or full write-
offs based on speciﬁc customer or industry issues?
■ Are sales returns measured (i.e., by product) and estimated periodi-
cally, especially at year end?
■ Are credit balance customers in the accounts receivable subledger
reversed to a payable in the ﬁnancial statements?
■ Does management analytically review sales, shipping, cash receipt,
and accounts receivable reports on a periodic basis?
■ Is a risk assessment done in regard to the potential for fraudulent
ﬁnancial reporting, including assessing:
■ Management’s motivation (i.e., compensation or stock price volatil-
ity)
■ Management’s inclination toward aggressive versus conservative
reporting
■ Management’s concern toward appropriate internal control
■ Industry conditions (i.e., competition, rapid change, economic
downturn, and so on)
5. Audit trails are maintained and timely information is provided to deci-
sion makers

---

## Page 174

Chapter = c12 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:41 pm
148 Cost Recovery
■ Is the following information entered into the database for each trans-
action?
■ Operator
■ Transaction date
■ Transaction result
■ Transaction ID
■ Are source documents received from customers stored in a logical
manner so that lost data can be recreated?
■ How frequently are backups of key transaction data completed and
are they stored offsite?
■ What controls exist to provide timely detail and summary information
to decision makers? In particular:
■ Is an accounts receivable aging reviewed on a periodic basis?
■ Is the accounts receivable subledger reviewed on a periodic basis
for unusual items?
■ Are reconciling items between the subledger and general ledger
reviewed periodically for reasonableness?
Conclusion
It must be noted that many recovery ﬁrms have not taken on these order-
to-cash assignments on a contingency fee basis (but do so as consultants)
because the client will generally not want to ask for a customer to pay them
back more money after the sale event. Customer relations aside, companies
have every right to ask for their money back if they have billed a customer
erroneously. On a day-to-day level, erroneous billings can tarnish relation-
ships with customers, but more so they can hamper the proﬁtability of the
company and open a company up to accusations of fraud. Regardless of the
fee structure, assistance should be taken whenever possible to maximize
this process ﬂow.

Chapter = c13 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:50 pm
CHAPTER 13
Payroll Tax Credits
Overview and Recoveries: Savings to Find
Payroll or employment tax credits are a valuable way to reduce a com-
pany’s federal and state tax liabilities. Credits are granted to encourage a
wide variety of hiring behavior but are generally aimed at encouraging the
employment of targeted groups or the development of targeted geographic
areas. As many as 900 or more programs may exist at any moment. They are
often combined with education and workforce training programs to enhance
regional economic development. While available to any employer, these
credits have their largest impact on those that rely heavily on entry-level
workers, such as the food service, hospitality, and health service industries.
Five factors result in the underuse of these credits by qualifying
employers:
1. Filing requirements are both complex and dynamic.
2. Ongoing monitoring and reporting is required.
3. Their inherent latency occasioned by lengthy processing cycles.
4. Their fragmented nature that exacerbates factor 1.
5. Unpredictable results in the absence of hiring practices aimed speciﬁ-
cally at maximizing beneﬁts that, in turn, results in diminished rigor with
respect to factor 2.
Unlike the other cost recovery methods covered in this book, employ-
ment credits are not based on any sort of look-back. They are, instead,
an operating issue because they affect the employer’s everyday procedures.
Beneﬁts are obtained through rigorous operational compliance and report-
ing, rather than after-the-fact audits, studies, and so forth. While credits are
generally applied to reduce quarterly payments as they become available,
they may be carried back up to three years in some cases.
This chapter was written in collaboration with Jeff Pawlik (jmpawlik@gmail.com).
150

---

## Page 177

Chapter = c13 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:50 pm
Payroll Tax Credits 151
The Work Opportunity Tax Credit (WOTC)1
The single most signiﬁcant opportunity is the federal Work Opportunity
Tax Credit. The WOTC was originally authorized by the Small Business Job
Protection Act of 1996 (P.L. 104-188) to encourage employers to hire from
among nine targeted groups. To qualify, new hires must belong to one of
the following nine WOTC target groups:
1. Long-term TANF recipient. A member of a family who:
i. Received or recently received Temporary Assistance to Needy Fami-
lies (TANF) payments for at least 18 consecutive months ending on
the hiring date, or
ii. Received TANF payments for any 18 months (whether or not con-
secutive) beginning after August 5, 1997, and the earliest 18-month
period beginning after August 5, 1997, ended during the past two
years, or
iii. Stopped being eligible for TANF payments during the past two years
because federal or state law limited the maximum time those pay-
ments could be made.
2. Other TANF recipient.A member of a family who is receiving or recently
received TANF beneﬁts for any nine-month period during the 18-month
period ending on the hiring date
3. Qualiﬁed food stamp recipient.An 18- to 39-year-old member of a family
that received food stamps for the previous six months, or received food
stamps for at least three of the past ﬁve months
4. Designated community resident.An 18- to 39-year-old resident of one of
the federally designated Empowerment Zones (EZs), Enterprise Commu-
nities (ECs), Renewal Communities (RCs), and for individuals who begin
to work for an employer after May 25, 2007, this High-Risk Youth group
has been renamed “Designated Community Resident” and expanded to
include residents of Rural Renewal Counties
Note: All Round I Enterprise Communities (ECs) including enhanced
Enterprise Communities expired on December 31, 2004. Round II ECs
are still in existence, as are all the EZs.
5. Summer youth employee.A 16- to 17-year-old EZ/EC or RC resident hired
between May 1 and September 15
Note: All Round I Enterprise Communities (ECs) including enhanced
Enterprise Communities expired on December 31, 2004. Round II ECs
are still in existence, as are all the EZs.
6. Qualiﬁed veteran. A veteran who is a member of a family that is receiv-
ing or recently received food stamps for at least three months during the
past 15 months; and for individuals who begin to work for an employer
after May 25, 2007, the veterans group is expanded to include disabled

---

## Page 178

Chapter = c13 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:50 pm
152 Cost Recovery
veterans, who are entitled to compensation for a service-connected dis-
ability and who, during the one-year period ending on the hiring date,
were discharged or released from active duty in the U.S. armed forces,
or unemployed for a period or periods totaling at least six months.
The ﬁrst-year wages taken into account for these disabled veterans are
capped at $12,000.
7. Vocational rehabilitation referral. An individual who completed or
is completing rehabilitative services from a state-certiﬁed agency, an
employment network, or the U.S. Department of Veterans Affairs.
8. Qualiﬁed ex-felon. An individual who has been convicted of a felony
and has a hiring date that is not more than one year after the last date
on which he was so convicted or released from prison.
9. SSI recipient. A recipient of Supplemental Security Income (SSI) beneﬁts
for any month ending during the past 60-day period ending on the hire
date.
The program has since been extended several times and combined with
the federal welfare-to-work tax credits. At present, the combined programs
are authorized under the Small Business and Work Opportunity Tax Act of
2007 (P.L. 110-28), which extends the WOTC Program through August 31,
2011. The consolidated WOTC for hiring most target group members can
now be as much as:
■ $2,400 for each new adult hire
■ $1,200 for each new summer youth hire
■ $4,800 for each new disabled veteran hire
■ $9,000 for each new long-term family assistance recipient hired over a
two-year period
The credit is 25 percent of qualiﬁed ﬁrst-year wages for those employed
at least 120 hours but fewer than 400 hours and 40 percent for those
employed 400 hours or more. There are limitations and restrictions, how-
ever. The WOTC amount an employer may claim depends on the hours the
employee works. All new adult employees must work a minimum of 120 or
400 hours. Individuals hired as Summer Youth employees must work at least
90 days, between May 1 and September 15, before an employer is eligible
to claim the tax credit.
For many, the most difﬁcult aspect of the WOTC is that state certiﬁcation
of individual employees must be obtained to qualify for federal beneﬁts. To
receive certiﬁcation that a new employee qualiﬁes the employer for this tax
credit, the employer must follow these three steps:
1. Complete page 1 of IRS Form 8850 by the day the job offer is made.
2. Complete page 2 of IRS Form 8850 after the individual is hired.

---

## Page 179

Chapter = c13 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:50 pm
Payroll Tax Credits 153
3. Complete either the one-page ETA Form 9061 or Form 9062 as appro-
priate. For example:
■ If the new employee has already been conditionally certiﬁed as
belonging to a WOTC target group by a state workforce agency (SWA)
or participating agency, complete the bottom part of ETA Form 9062,
sign, and date it, or
■ If the new employee has not been conditionally certiﬁed, the employer
and the new employee must complete, sign, and date ETA Form 9061.
To qualify, the completed and signed original IRS and ETA forms must
be mailed to the employer’s state workforce agency within 28 days after the
employee’s employment start date. If the applicant meets the criteria, the
agency will send the employer a tax credit certiﬁcation form. Employers are
advised to retain these certiﬁcations and supporting documents for at least
ﬁve years from the certiﬁcation date. Employers can apply for the credit
with IRS Form 8861. Employers who cannot take the full tax credit amount
in the current tax year may claim a refund of all or part of taxes paid in
the previous three years. Unused credit is carried back to the earliest of the
previous three tax years, then to the second earliest, and so on. Credit that
cannot be taken in these years may be carried forward for up to 15 years
until the entire credit is used.
Other Federal Programs
While lesser known, other federal programs work similarly to the WOTC: A
few are listed here:
■ Federal empowerment zones and renewal communities. These most
commonly apply to disaster zones, for example, New Orleans after Hur-
ricane Katrina. Beneﬁts include not only employer tax credits but project
facility bonds and capital gain tax exemptions on cash investment earn-
ings.
■ The Native American Indian employment credit. Encourage employ-
ers to hire and retain individuals who are enrolled members (or their
spouse) of a Native American tribe and who live on or near a reserva-
tion. Employers may receive a federal income tax credit of up to $4,000
per eligible employee.
■ Disabled access credits. Encourage the removal of architectural, com-
munication, physical, or transportation barriers, or for providing certain
services or accommodations, which would prevent a business from
being accessible to, or usable by, individuals with disabilities. These
are available to an eligible small business and equal to 50 percent of

---

## Page 180

Chapter = c13 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:50 pm
154 Cost Recovery
the eligible expenditures exceeding $250 but not more than $10,250,
for a maximum credit of $5,000 a year. Any business with fewer than 30
full-time employees or $1,000,000 gross revenues may generally qualify
and beneﬁts may be claimed anytime in the period expenditures were
incurred.
State and Local Incentives and Credits
Although changing over time, virtually every state offers an array of state and
local tax credits and incentives to encourage growth and development. As
with the federal WOTC, ﬁling and reporting requirements vary by program.
A sampling of these programs include:
■ Enterprise zones. Speciﬁc geographic locations that offer an assortment
of incentives, including investment tax credits, wage tax credits, property
tax abatements, low-income ﬁnancing, gross receipt tax exemptions,
and sales and use tax refunds and credits.
■ Training grants. Income tax credits and wage subsidies of up to 100
percent of training expenses or 50 percent of wages for an employer
hiring qualiﬁed individuals, while providing job training.
■ Sales and use tax recoveries. Refunds and credits based on certain qual-
iﬁed purchases.
■ State point of hire tax credits. Wage-based credits for hiring individuals
from targeted groups such as welfare recipients, disabled workers, and
employees who reside in economically distressed areas.
■ Job tax credits. Directly offset the costs of creating or expanding a
company’s workforce.
■ Property tax credits and expenditures.Exemptions and abatements from
taxation on property, both real and personal.
■ Investment tax credits and expenditures. Tax credits based on invest-
ments on qualiﬁed machinery and equipment.
■ Sellable tax credits. The ability to sell your net operating losses (NOLs)
and unused tax credits for cash.
Questions to Ask When Assessing the Opportunity
Taking into consideration the case study experiences and the types of recov-
ery, the following is a list of questions that can help frame the opportunity
size of a payroll tax credit management process:
■ What is the potential magnitude of beneﬁts the company might already
be qualiﬁed to receive?

---

## Page 181

Chapter = c13 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 2:50 pm
Payroll Tax Credits 155
■ Do a signiﬁcant proportion of our employees come from targeted
groups?
■ Would the company alter the proﬁle of candidates sought if the proﬁt
economics were different?
■ Could recruiting be changed to attract more employees from targeted
groups?
■ Does the business have signiﬁcant operations in designated geographi-
cal zones?
■ Would site selection be altered if the proﬁt economics were different?
■ Could increased reporting and monitoring requirements be best met
using internal or external resources?
Conclusion
As stated previously, payroll tax credits require a process to manage the
application for federal and state programs that, rather than being a past-
focused cash recovery, most of these programs focus on tax savings from
new hires. While a company could try this process itself, given that it is
a process and there are a large number of programs to understand, it is
probably best to have a service provider manage the effort. Please note these
providers will work under a contingency whereby the company savings will
be shared as the tax credits are earned.
A few questions to consider when looking for a service provider include:
■ Does the vendor provide services covering all states in which the ﬁrm
does business?
■ Does the vendor speciﬁcally cover state and local programs?
■ Does the vendor have established relationships with payroll processors
to simplify the ongoing data collection and reporting processes?
■ Can a maximum of credits be stipulated that is reﬂective of projected
tax liabilities?
■ Is payment for service due upon application or upon award of credits?

business/Incentives/opptax/.

CHAPTER 14
Proactive Fraud Audits
Overview
The term occupational fraud may be deﬁned as “The use of one’s occupa-
tion for personal enrichment through the deliberate misuse or misapplication
of the employing organization’s resources or assets.” 1 Based on the 2008
Report to the Nation on Occupational Fraud and Abuse(RTTN) as issued by
the Association of Certiﬁed Fraud Examiners, the median response indicated
that the typical U.S. organization loses 7 percent of its annual revenues to
fraudulent activity.
2 Occupational fraud can be further deﬁned using the
following three categories, which are even further detailed in the Fraud
Classiﬁcation System (Exhibit 14.1):
3
1. Fraudulent statements. Involving the falsiﬁcation of an organization’s
ﬁnancial statements. This includes frauds that are outright lies and earn-
ings management that is a shading of the true picture of the company’s
health.
2. Corruption. When fraudsters wrongfully use their inﬂuence in a business
transaction to procure some beneﬁt for themselves or another person
contrary to their duty to their employer or the rights of another.
3. Asset misappropriations. Involving the theft or misuse of an organiza-
tion’s assets.
As much as companies try to avoid it, the fact remains that companies are
made up of people who unfortunately, at times, will commit fraud. The goal
of this chapter is, therefore, to explain how a proactive antifraud program
within the organization can reduce cash outﬂows and ﬁnancial statement
misstatements associated with malfeasance. There is no stopping the human
This chapter was written in collaboration with the Institute of Internal Auditors
and its publication GTAG Fraud Detection in an Automated World as co-authored
by the author of this book.

Case Study 14.1: Association of Certiﬁed Fraud
Examiners Report to the Nation4
Since no one will go on record as reporting their own frauds, this section
will rather focus on the key ﬁndings from the RTTN. This study is based on
data compiled from 959 cases of occupational fraud that were investigated
between January 2006 and February 2008. All information was provided by
the Certiﬁed Fraud Examiners (CFEs) who investigated those cases.
It must be noted that of all the fraud schemes, accounts payable and
procurement process ﬂows were the most affected by fraud. As can be seen
in Exhibits 14.2 and 14.3, corruption and billing schemes, all associated with
vendor conﬂicts of interest and schemes to pay phony invoices to vendors,
respectively, top the list not only in frequency but also for the most part in
a median loss perspective.

Proactive Fraud Audits 159
One other key note associated with fraud occurrences was that the trend
associated with small business, as seen in previous RTTN studies, continued
in the 2008 study. Small businesses, deﬁned as those with fewer than 100
employees, suffered both a greater percentage of frauds (38 percent) and a
higher median loss ($200,000) than their larger counterparts.
5
Case Study Wrap-Up6
As can be seen from the deﬁnition, fraud classiﬁcation system, and study
results, occupational fraud can present itself in various ways and, while there
are trends, no two companies are alike in the precise types and occurrences
of fraud. Given this varied nature of fraud, the approach to reducing it must
also be varied, similar to a shotgun that hopes among its pellet rounds that at
least one will hit the target. These will be referred to as antifraud procedures
or controls throughout the remainder of this section.
When one looks to antifraud procedures to incorporate into an organi-
zation, some key documents emerge that capture the collective knowledge
on combating fraud.
■ The American Institute of Certiﬁed Public Accountants (AICPA) in coor-
dination with the Institute of Internal Auditors (IIA), Association of
Certiﬁed Fraud Examiners (ACFE), Information System Accountability
and Control Auditors (ISACA), Financial Executives Institute (FEI), Insti-
tute of Management Accountants (IMA), and Society of Human Resource
Professionals (SHRM) drafted an exhibit on Management Anti-Fraud
Program and Controls, which was integrated into AICPA’s Audit Stan-
dard 99 (Consideration of Fraud in a Financial Statement Audit). This
audit standard and antifraud exhibit can be freely downloaded from
aicpa.org/download/members/div/auditstd/AU-00316.PDF.
■ The Institute of Internal Auditors (IIA), along with the Association of
Certiﬁed Fraud Examiners (ACFE) and the American Institute of Certiﬁed
Public Accountants (AICPA), recently released Managing the Business
Risk of Fraud: A Practical Guide. This new guidance outlines ﬁve key
principles of a fraud risk management process and recommends ways
in which boards, senior management, and internal auditors can ﬁght
corporate fraud. The report is the result of two years of work from a
dedicated task force of more than 20 experts in the ﬁeld of fraud risk
identiﬁcation, mitigation, and investigation. It can be freely downloaded
at iia.org/recent-iia-news/index.cfm?i=6465.
■ The Association of Certiﬁed Fraud Examiners’ (ACFE) Report to the
Nation on Occupational Fraud and Abuse, recently updated for 2008, is
one of the most widely quoted sources of information on fraud and

---

## Page 186

Chapter = c14 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
160 Cost Recovery
white-collar crime. Based on information gathered from more than
1,000 CFEs, the Report to the Nation sheds light on the prevalence
and costs of occupational fraud and offers insights into the most effec-
tive means of prevention and detection. It can be freely downloaded at
acfe.com/resources/publications.asp?copy=rttn.
The three texts speak many of the same truths, with the SAS 99 exhibit
being the most directional because it was written at a time when the
largest frauds were affecting the world, namely, Enron and WorldCom,
and incorporated the thinking of more professional organizations than ever
before on ﬁghting fraud. While many of the publications speak to statis-
tics around fraud and key characteristics of organizations that ﬁght fraud,
they offer only a few actionable procedures that can be incorporated into a
company.
For example, a good part of the listed publications focus on an organiza-
tion’s governance structure, which speaks to such topics as tone at the top,
reporting lines of internal audit, job responsibilities, effective audit commit-
tees, and the like, yet these are difﬁcult to translate into actual prevention
and detection procedures. Another example is a risk assessment, which is an
enlightening experience but will not lead to any fraud being prevented or
detected. Rather, it will simply point to the key weak points within an organi-
zation. In many ways, an organization is either grown with such foundational
blocks or it is not, as it is difﬁcult to infuse such behaviors. Furthermore,
without any actions to apply and thereby mitigate risk, fraudulent activities
will continue to ﬂourish.
In contrast, and based on a review of the documents and the author’s
personal experience, the following actionable steps can be taken by an
organization to prevent and detect fraud. The purpose of this section is to
go one step further and explain how such procedures can be automated for
improved effectiveness and efﬁciency. The procedures have been organized
into the following three major categories:
1. Explaining and conﬁrming employee responsibilities. Employees need
to read a fraud policy, understand it, and understand what fraud looks
like in an organization. Automation can be used to distribute this
information and conﬁrm signatures, as well as attendance to training
programs.
2. Organizing and reviewing employee feedback. Employee feedback can
be obtained in various ways, yet when such feedback relates to fraud-
ulent activity, it needs to be extracted and organized more delicately.
Employees need to be provided anonymity and the data need to be
secured so that the data review is done only by those responding to the
suspected fraud.

---

## Page 187

Chapter = c14 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
Proactive Fraud Audits 161
3. Analyzing data trends using internal and external data sources. Data
analysis can be completed proactively and continuously to detect fraud
as soon as it emerges. It also can be done to respond to fraudulent
behaviors when such tools are used in fraudulent investigations.
These three categories will be further deﬁned in the following.
Explaining and Conﬁrming Employee Responsibilities
CODE OF CONDUCT CONFIRMATION Employees need to understand their
responsibilities in preventing and detecting fraud and then should also con-
ﬁrm this understanding through a standard process that includes a manual
or electronic signature. This is best memorialized in a code of conduct,
which has a speciﬁc mention of a fraud policy. A sample fraud policy can
be found as an appendix in the document Managing the Business Risk of
Fraud: A Practical Guide, as already referred to earlier for free download
from the Web. Another sample fraud policy can be found on the ACFE’s
Web site at acfe.com/documents/sample
fraud policy.pdf.
To automate the conﬁrmation of a code of conduct and a fraud policy, a
survey tool can be used that not only allows for electronic conﬁrmation, but
also improves the survey efﬁciency by sending e-mail reminders if employ-
ees forget to complete the exercise, and allowing for explanatory help that
is hyperlinked throughout the form. While a survey can be developed in-
house, online versions can be purchased from companies such as ConﬁrmIT
(conﬁrmit.com).
Probably the most useful and unseen beneﬁt of an electronic collection
tool is that it collects all information into a database for later reporting and
comparisons. For example, a data analysis report of increasing vendor pay-
ments in accounts payable transactions may coincide with a code of conduct
form in which an employee notes a potential conﬂict of interest with that
same vendor. While this may relate to normal purchasing activity, it would
be highly coincidental and worthy of investigation to determine the true
nature. Also, to detect such a conﬂict in a sea of manually signed code of
conduct forms would be very difﬁcult, if not impossible.
EMPLOYEE FRAUD TRAINING Most employees would not know fraud if it came
up and bit them on the cheek, as they have grown accustomed to many gray
areas that are approved by their management. For example, most accounts
payable personnel, when asked, will see excessive travel and entertainment
by their senior management as part of the job when it directly conﬂicts
with stated travel policy. They simply don’t realize that wrong is still wrong,
regardless of the company position.

---

## Page 188

Chapter = c14 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
162 Cost Recovery
Training is a necessary step, yet this training should be tailored to the
organization and, more to the point, to the employee’s position. While
generic fraud training can be helpful, it is more effective to identify the
top fraud risk areas in the company and develop training so that employees
in those positions can better understand their role in company fraud detec-
tion. Please note that the fraudsters may even attend the training, which
actually can further beneﬁt the company because the fraudster will see for
themselves a company fraud watch system being developed right before
their eyes, which should help to deter them from future malevolent activity.
Free Web-based resources can be obtained to help jumpstart a training
program. For example, free videos such as “How Fraud Hurts Your Organi-
zation” along with other learning tools can be downloaded from the ACFE
at acfe.com/resources/fraud-tools.asp?copy =video. Also, the AICPA has
developed a host of case studies on ethical behavior that can be obtained
at http://fvs.aicpa.org/Resources/Antifraud+Forensic+Accounting/Fraud+
Detection+Investigation+Prevention/Case+Studies+and+Fraud+Schemes/.
These case studies are useful because they translate fraud down to actual
dilemmas that employees faced, the decisions they made, and then experts
voice their opinions on their decisions.
Tailored training videos can be produced economically by taping meet-
ings for later use using such tools as WebEx or GoToMeeting. Another
approach is to use a product called Camtasia (techsmith.com), which uses
screen capture technology with audio from a microphone to build and pro-
duce videos for later use. Please note that the advantage of such screen
capture technology is that it allows for editing, voiceovers, and many other
publishing effects that should improve the efﬁciency and effectiveness of
the produced video.
While developing in-house training is usually the most effective way
to tailor a training event to an organization, another approach is to have
a training company produce such training materials. For example, Fraud-
Aware (fraudaware.com) offers online employee fraud awareness training
as developed by fraud prevention experts. They also customize the training
to an organization’s speciﬁc antifraud objectives and to employee positions
themselves.
Regardless of the method used to produce and disseminate the training,
one key goal is to ensure that company employees actually listen to and learn
from the training. This can be done through online surveys that not only
conﬁrm attendance but also ensure through quick exams that the employees
gained the necessary knowledge from the training event.
Organizing and Reviewing Employee Feedback
METHODS TO ELICIT EMPLOYEE FEEDBACK Internal controls have been seen
as the panacea to ﬁghting fraud, although they only go so far since internal

---

## Page 189

Chapter = c14 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
Proactive Fraud Audits 163
controls provide only reasonable assurance. In other words, controls can
be compromised by an overriding manager. However, such acts do not go
unnoticed to all employees within the organization, yet the auditor could not
cost-beneﬁcially interview all of the employees in a company to detect such
fraud. They normally wait for such employees to speak up on a whistle-
blowing hotline.
The Sarbanes-Oxley legislation made at a requirement for companies to
establish a whistle-blowing hotline. In the RTTN, the ACFE found that tips
are the number one method for reporting fraud. In fact, the ACFE found
that organizations with a hotline typically cut their fraud by 60 percent.
Organizations without a hotline lost an average of $250,000, while those with
a hotline lost only $100,000. The ACFE also found that tips were the leading
common detection method for uncovering million-dollar frauds, accounting
for 42 percent of initial reports.
7
Focusing on employee feedback capitalizes on the fact that people, not
computers, commit fraud, and many witnesses within the company want to
share what they know about organizational control issues. The most effec-
tive way to learn about fraud is to provide employees, suppliers, and other
stakeholders with a variety of methods for reporting their concerns about
illegal or unethical behavior. These intake methods include traditional tele-
phone interviews, Web forms, e-mails, faxes, and face-to-face meetings. The
best time to collect this information is as follows:
■ Annual code of conduct conﬁrmation. As already referred to earlier.
■ Promotion of a whistle-blowing hotline. This can take the form
of a telephone hotline, a Web form, or a survey that is proac-
tively sent to employees. Best practices for hotlines are listed at
tnwinc.com/downloads/bestpractices
ethicshotlines.pdf.
■ Exit interviews. A policy of conducting exit interviews of terminated
employees or those who have resigned can help in both prevention and
detection efforts. These interviews may help HR managers determine
whether there are issues regarding management’s integrity or informa-
tion regarding conditions conducive to fraud. HR should also review the
content and information contained in resignation letters, as they may
contain information regarding possible fraud and misconduct existing
within the organization.
■ Proactive surveys. While not widely used, employees can be surveyed
on a routine basis as to their sensibilities toward fraud and unethical
behavior within the company. While most approaches wait until an
employee is so pressured to speak up, the proactive survey approach
anonymously elicits information from employees, usually before it is too
late.
These approaches help tap in to the valuable information on fraud
detection that comes from workers, not accounting databases, thereby

---

## Page 190

Chapter = c14 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
164 Cost Recovery
extending data analysis beyond lifeless ﬁnancial and transactional data and
into the vibrant data stores in employees’ and business partners’ minds. Not
only does this form of analysis broaden the organization’s risk and control
awareness, but gathering information from a large number of people can
also increase the predictability and conﬁdence levels of the assessment.
AUTOMATION OF THE EMPLOYEE FEEDBACK LOOP 8 The following ﬁve tech-
niques can and should be used to automate the employee feedback loop
within organizations:
1. Question database. Rather than start with a blank sheet of paper, prod-
ucts can come equipped with questions speciﬁc to auditing, common
process controls, detecting fraud, and assessing the ethical environment
of a company. These questions are normally worded by psychologists
who have an improved sense on how to ask questions and obtain a
valid response (versus what the employee thinks the company wants to
be answered). The main results of employee and control self-assessment
tools are the survey data and the surveys to be completed in a physical
meeting or through a virtual online means. Survey tools should contain
the following characteristics to be effective:
■ Optional anonymit. As people complete the survey, an option should
be presented to allow for anonymity in the response and the survey
should be sent or secured in a way to ensure responders can remain
anonymous. Please note that some surveys need to be completed and
signed by the person being sent the survey (i.e., code of conduct
surveys).
■ Question types. Survey tools generally provide the following question
types, which can be reported on using search tools once completed
by employees:
■ Yes/no
■ Multiple choice
■ Sliding scale
■ Text and comment box
2. Medium. Given the distributed nature of the workforce, more assess-
ments are being e-mailed or are available through a Web browser. Some
assessments use keypad technology that allow for anonymous voting in
a meeting using a pad with multiple-option buttons.
3. Role-based security. As systems become more sophisticated, databases
that contain the results of the self-assessments need to be secured from
unauthorized access and changes. This mainly takes the form of role-
based security, whereby employees are granted access on the basis of
their needs to interact with the employee feedback database.

---

## Page 191

Chapter = c14 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
Proactive Fraud Audits 165
4. Case management. A single case management system should be used
to log all calls and their follow-up to facilitate management of the
resolution process, testing by internal auditors, and oversight by the
board or the audit committee as the board’s designee. The board should
approve protocols to ensure reported fraud-related issues are dissem-
inated in a timely manner to appropriate parties such as the ethics or
compliance team, human resources, the board and the audit committee,
legal, and security. Distributing reports to these parties of occurrences in
their respective areas of responsibility ensures that no single person or
functional area controls this highly sensitive information and increases
accountability. Charged with the responsibility for having documented
procedures for receiving, retaining, and investigating complaints or tips
alleging the possibility of misconduct or possible fraud, many audit com-
mittees have turned to independent service providers to operate hotlines
and notify the organization of any reported accusations.
5. Collective database. All employee feedback data should be combined
into a collective database. This may require manual input of some feed-
back (i.e., code of conduct results may be manually entered into the
collective database if this function has not yet been automated) yet an
effective feedback loop should analyze the data received and compare
results to norms for similar organizations. Ongoing analysis allows an
organization to reshape its fraud risk management program to address
evolving risks. What’s more is that this process creates a proﬁle of
employee sentiment.
REPORTING ON THE EMPLOYEE FEEDBACK Reporting on employee feedback
databases can be a cost-effective way to create heat maps of employee sat-
isfaction, dissatisfaction, and telltale signs of fraud. It is helpful to examine
survey data from many perspectives, or through many lenses. This pro-
cess could involve an internal dialog or a focus group that explores survey
outcomes in more detail. The following are a few examples of perspectives:
9
■ Through comparisons. Look at differences between demographic
groups. Are there problems that are unique to some business units or
departments? What could be causing these differences? Also, compare
and contrast scores between entities and against the organization overall,
using the organization’s averages as a baseline. If an entity scores below
the organization’s average, what is it telling auditors? Is it a management,
cultural, training, systems, or business-process issue?
■ Through comments. Employee comments are extremely useful in vali-
dating and explaining the survey scores. People are very candid when
they have assurances of conﬁdentiality and anonymity. For example,
suppose one of the areas being assessed is conﬂicts of interest. Low

---

## Page 192

Chapter = c14 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
166 Cost Recovery
scores may tell auditors there is a problem, but they won’t explain the
speciﬁc nature of that problem. Reviewing comments that are associated
with this issue can illuminate those poor scores. Look for a survey appli-
cation that has a lexical search engine that can quickly ﬁnd all words
or phrases related to a topic. Skimming, stealing, and larceny may all
mean the same thing to respondents who are not well-versed in fraud
terminology. Searching for all three at once may provide auditors quick
answers.
■ Through trending. Comparing one entity to another entity is useful, but
the most important comparisons will be those that are internal to that
entity. How well is the entity performing this quarter compared with last
quarter? If the entity still needs to improve in an area, is the trend positive
or negative? Annual fraud or compliance assessments have little value
because they only tell auditors what they should have known months
before. When appropriate, monthly or quarterly surveys can reveal how
the organization is performing, quickly notify auditors when a problem
arises, and show improvement trends.
Analyzing Data Trends Using Internal and External Data Sources
As alluded to in the second fraud detection procedure, the best practice
would be to integrate data from various sources, ﬁnancial and nonﬁnancial,
internal and external, to arrive at an integrated view of the organiza-
tion from a fraud perspective. An example of this vision can be seen in
Exhibit 14.4.
10
Using these data, and many more as limited only by the imagination of
the auditor, the following four-step process emerges:
1. Complete client situational analysis. Identify top fraud risks from an
impact and likelihood perspective for that particular organization. This
would be tailored to take into account any company internal controls
used to minimize fraud risks, as well as a company’s plans to research
results.
2. Assess backgrounds of all parties associated with transactions. Back-
ground searches would be completed of all employees associated with
the transaction process. Aside from employees, vendor backgrounds,
customer backgrounds, and any other business partner would also be
assessed in relation to all available external information.
3. Execute a variety of query reports and calculate baseline statistics. Based
on the identiﬁed frauds at risk, query reports would be combined
to identify business partners, company departments, employees, and
even speciﬁc transactions that appear on a variety of the reports. Base-
line statistics would also be developed for business partners, company

departments, employees, time, and other categories. Then, any new

4. Integrate these approaches into a consolidated analysis. By combining
the results of the preceding approaches, and using a scoring methodol-
ogy, a targeted list would be developed for the organization that could
be at the business partner, department, employee, or transaction level.
This would depend on the level of speciﬁcity the company desires, as
well as their planned response to research any unusual activity.
With the four-step integrated process quickly deﬁned and displayed
in Exhibit 14.5, the following is a further explanation of the process, with
examples, when appropriate.
COMPLETE CLIENT SITUATIONAL ANALYSIS One useful way to assess fraud risk
is to take each fraud type as presented in the Fraud Classiﬁcation System,
previously displayed in this chapter, and assign a likelihood or impact to
each type of fraud. This assessment will be different for each organization,
depending on the industry, control environment, economy, and many other
factors speciﬁc to that organization.

ASSESS BACKGROUNDS OF ALL PARTIES ASSOCIATED WITH TRANSACTIONS Back-
ground searches are generally only completed on employees at companies,
although with current Internet databases, companies should consider com-
pleting background searches on all business partners. Please note that such
an exercise should generally be reserved for areas of expected fraud activ-
ity. For example, the procurement process may be seen as a hotbed for
fraudulent activity at a company. With this in mind, auditors could review
vendors that are either large in material dollar terms, are increasing in size
over the last few years, and are being seen on a variety of potential fraud
activity reports. External data sources expected to be used in reviewing these
vendors (which could also be said for all business partners) include:
■ Excluded Parties List System (epls.gov). This is a list provided as a pub-
lic service by General Services Administration (GSA) for the purpose of
efﬁciently and conveniently disseminating information on parties that
are excluded from receiving federal contracts, certain subcontracts, and
certain federal ﬁnancial and nonﬁnancial assistance and beneﬁts, pur-
suant to the provisions of 31 U.S.C. 6101, note, E.O. 12549, E.O. 12689,
48 CFR 9.404, and each agency’s codiﬁcation of the Common Rule for
Nonprocurement Suspension and Debarment.

---

## Page 195

Chapter = c14 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
Proactive Fraud Audits 169
■ U.S. Treasury OFAC List (ustreas.gov/ofﬁces/enforcement/ofac/). The
Ofﬁce of Foreign Assets Control (OFAC) of the U.S. Department of
the Treasury administers and enforces economic and trade sanctions on
the basis of U.S. foreign policy and national security goals against tar-
geted foreign countries and regimes, terrorists, international narcotics
trafﬁckers, those engaged in activities related to the proliferation of
weapons of mass destruction, and other threats to the national secu-
rity, foreign policy, or economy of the United States. OFAC acts under
presidential national emergency powers as well as authority granted
by speciﬁc legislation to impose controls on transactions and to freeze
assets under U.S. jurisdiction.
■ U.S. Internal Revenue Service Tax Identiﬁcation Number Matching
Program. This free service allows a company to align all of its business
partner tax identiﬁcation numbers to the U.S. IRS listing of numbers so
that the numbers can be tested for their validity and the match between
the business partner’s name and the tax identiﬁcation number can be
assessed for validity.
■ Company information databases. Equifax, Dun and Bradstreet, and
Hoovers are all examples of global company databases that are main-
tained for key business information. These databases can be reviewed
to identify any business issues affecting the company, any pending legal
issues, and the makeup of the organization.
■ U.S. Better Business Bureau (BBB). This can be a helpful tool in review-
ing past complaints on companies, which are logged by actual customers
and other business partners.
■ World Wide Web listings . While not as reliable, doing a search using
Yahoo! or Google can be one method to learning more about a com-
pany. Unfortunately, anyone today can create a Web site for a phony
company or, almost as bad, present only the positive news about their
organization. For these reasons, auditors should read this Web content
with some reservation until it is validated by other independent external
data sources.
While these sources of external information can be interesting, it is best
to set clear objectives when reviewing them. The following are some pro-
posed objectives that will help assess the potential fraudulent nature of the
company:
■ Whether the address and phone number are valid for that company
■ Conﬂicts of interest with current employees
■ Tax identiﬁcation number validation
■ Whether the company can be found to exist in any of the external data
sources

---

## Page 196

Chapter = c14 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
170 Cost Recovery
■ Employees posing as suppliers
■ Three-way relationships between employees, their next of kin, and sup-
pliers
■ Type of company (i.e., a sole proprietor may increase the risk of there
being an issue)
■ Year company started
■ Any past legal issues or other special issues
The use of external databases to assess the nature of business partners
once again works toward the principle that individuals commit fraud, not
computers or companies, so it is best to gain a clear picture of the actual
business partners from a potential for committing fraud perspective.
EXECUTE A VARIETY OF QUERY REPORTS AND CALCULATE BASELINE STATISTICS
Based on the identiﬁed frauds at risk (see Chapter 3 for a further discussion
of this topic), query reports would be executed to identify vendors, com-
pany departments, employees, and even speciﬁc transactions that appear
on a variety of the reports. Baseline statistics would also be developed for
business partners, company departments, employees, time, and other cate-
gories. Then, any new activity would be related to the baseline to identify
exceptional results.
One useful tool to generate report ideas is an Institute of Internal Audi-
tors Research Foundation study entitled Proactively Detecting Fraud Using
Computer Assisted Audit Reports (theiia.org/bookstore/product/proactively-
detecting-occupational-fraud-using-computer-audit-reports-1177.cfm). The
publication uses the three global categories of corporate fraud, as deﬁned
by the ACFE, and provides a list of reports for each of the following 15
major categories of fraud (as can be seen on the Fraud Classiﬁcation System,
previously referred to in this chapter):
1. Bribery or illegal gratuities or economic extortion
2. Conﬂicts of interest
3. Fictitious revenues and timing differences
4. Understated liabilities and expenses
5. Overstated assets or valuation
6. Improper disclosures
7. Nonﬁnancial fraudulent statements
8. Cash larceny
9. Skimming
10. Inventory misuse or larceny
11. Billing schemes
12. Payroll schemes
13. Expenses reimbursement schemes

An example section from this publication related to billing schemes, one
of the top occurring frauds per the RTTN, can be seen here.
Billing Schemes Billing schemes11 occurs when a fraudster causes the victim
organization to issue a payment by submitting invoices for ﬁctitious goods
or services, inﬂated invoices, or invoices for personal purchases. There are
three subcategories of billing schemes, deﬁned as follows:
■ Shell company. Creates a phony organization on the company’s books
for use in paying ﬁctitious invoices.
■ Non-accomplice vendor. Intentional mishandling of vendor payment to
make a ﬁctitious payment to the employee.
■ Personal purchases. Purchases using company accounts such as a com-
pany procurement card.
