INTEGRATE THESE APPROACHES INTO A CONSOLIDATED ANALYSIS By combining
the results of this analysis, a targeted list would be developed for the orga-
nization, which could be at the business partner, department, employee,
or transaction level. This would depend on the level of speciﬁcity the
company desires, as well as its planned response to research any unusual
activity.
One useful approach is to score activity such that a point is scored if
given to each report where a business partner or transaction is listed and
then a further weighting of this score can be done based on the amount
of processing associated with that business partner or transaction. This can
best be explained by an example vendor data analysis and score the activity
accordingly.
Vendor Scoring Model
Step 1. A numeric score, listed after each report and totaling 100 was
given to each vendor that appeared on each of the following reports:
■ Vendors with low transaction, high dollar activity: 10
■ Vendors with missing information (tax ID, telephone, address, and
so on): 10
■ Vendors with a high proportion of round dollar payments: 15
■ Invoices that are exactly 10 times, 100 times, or 1,000 times larger
than another invoice: 20
■ Payments to any vendor that exceeds the 12-month average pay-
ments to that vendor by a speciﬁed percentage (that is, 200
percent) or 3 times the standard deviation for that vendor: 15
■ Vendors paid with a high proportion of manual checks: 15
■ Vendors with little or no sequence between invoice numbers: 15
Step 2. The score was added together to create a numeric score. There-
fore, if a vendor appeared on reports 1, 3, and 6, the vendor was
given a numeric score of 40.
Step 3. The numeric score was then multiplied by the amount of the
vendor payments over the past year to create a weighted score.
Step 4. The vendors were sorted on the numeric score, per step two,
and the top 20 numeric scored vendors were extracted to a spread-
sheet. Then the vendors were sorted on the weighted score, per
step three, and the top 20 weighted scored vendors were extracted
to a spreadsheet.
While each vendor report could be reviewed in isolation, it is more
effective to review them in combination so that the auditor can focus on the
most probable unusual vendor activity. Thus, if a given vendor appeared on
many suspicious reports, that vendor would be more likely to be unusual
or associated with fraudulent activity.

---

## Page 200

Chapter = c14 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
174 Cost Recovery
By explaining and conﬁrming employee responsibilities, organizing and
reviewing employee feedback, and analyzing data trends using internal and
external data sources, the company has a better chance of preventing or
otherwise proactively detecting fraud at the organization.
Recoveries to Find
■ Reducing bottom-line impacts using antifraud controls. 12 The RTTN
study found that:
■ Participants in the survey estimated that U.S. organizations lose 7
percent of their annual revenues to fraud. Applied to the projected
2008 U.S. Gross Domestic Product, this 7 percent ﬁgure translates to
approximately $994 billion in fraud losses.
■ Occupational fraud schemes tend to be extremely costly. The median
loss caused by the occupational frauds in this study was $175,000.
More than one-quarter of the frauds involved losses of at least $1
million.
■ Occupational fraud schemes frequently continue for years before they
are detected. The typical fraud in our study lasted two years from the
time it began until the time it was caught by the victim organization.
There is no elimination of fraud, just a reduction. The implemen-
tation of antifraud controls appears to have a measurable impact on
an organization’s exposure to fraud. Of the antifraud controls exam-
ined in the RTTN survey, there were signiﬁcantly lower losses when
the controls had been implemented. For example, organizations that
conducted surprise audits suffered a median loss of $70,000, while
those that did not had a median loss of $207,000. Similar reductions
in fraud losses were identiﬁed for organizations that had anonymous
fraud hotlines, offered employee support programs, provided fraud
training for managers, and had internal audit or fraud examination
departments. It must also be noted that if it is known to employees
and vendors that computer audit reports are being run and the com-
pany is serious about detecting fraud, it creates a strong deterrent to
anyone afraid of ever being caught in the act.
■ Internal controls are good business. Any company with an effective set
of internal controls should ﬁnd efﬁciency gains in its business processes.
Furthermore, regardless of whether any fraud is ever detected, compa-
nies that have strong antifraud internal controls will detect errors as
much as they detect fraud, which puts cash back into the pocket of the
company.
■ Additional beneﬁts. While not as direct as the foregoing, which have the
potential of placing immediate cash back into the company’s pockets,
reducing fraud also:

---

## Page 201

Chapter = c14 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
Proactive Fraud Audits 175
■ Avoids a catastrophic ﬁnancial statement event from being reported
in the business journals.
■ Reduces federal penalties—Under the federal sentencing guidelines,
there is a 40 percent reduction in penalties for companies using due
diligence in implementing programs to detect and prevent violations
of law.
■ Reduces audit fees. The additional audit work, now required under
Statement of Auditing Standard (SAS) 99, generally translates into
higher fees. Organizations looking to reduce, or at least hold the
line on audit fees, should focus on establishing and managing strong
antifraud programs and controls to mitigate fraud risks and provide
external auditors a foundation of existing controls for audit planning
reliance.
■ Prevents civil lawsuits. Many times employees who experience issues
in the workplace ﬁrst try to resolve these issues internally. If their
complaints are ignored, employees feel compelled to go to an outside
advocate. That could be a private attorney, government regulator, or
news agency. Giving employees an internal outlet can solve problems
without the event becoming public knowledge or an issue for the
courts.
Questions to Ask When Assessing the Opportunity
Taking into consideration the case study experiences and the types of recov-
ery, the following is a list of questions that can help frame the opportunity
size of an antifraud program implementation:
■ Does the company track fraud losses on an annual basis?
■ How many frauds were investigated by general counsel or internal audit
during the last three years?
■ Has the organization established a process for oversight of fraud risks?
■ Who owns this oversight process within the company? For example, is
it a member of senior management, internal audit, or some other group?
■ Does the company maintain strong internal controls as evidenced by
external audit reports?
■ Has the company mapped out its internal controls to the identiﬁed fraud
risks within the organization?
Conclusion
Proactive fraud detection works to reduce not only the cash outlay to fraud
but also the potential brand image risks to the organization of a high-proﬁle

---

## Page 202

Chapter = c14 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:31 am
176 Cost Recovery
fraud. While it can never be measured, employee morale is just never the
same after a fraud, given it is almost always never expected from employees
who are put in such a position of trust. Antifraud controls work to reduce
these potential negative impacts and, when automated, are supercharged to
root out organizational malfeasance.

## Page 203

Chapter = c15 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 4:51 pm
CHAPTER 15
Project Fraud Audits
Overview
This chapter focuses on one speciﬁc fraud type, project fraud, as opposed
to a more general discussion of how fraud can affect an organization as
more fully described in Chapters 5 to 10. There are various ﬁnancial and
nonﬁnancial impacts of project fraud to the organization. The key ﬁnancial
impact is the immediate write-off of ﬁxed assets associated with the project
gone wrong, given that project deliverables generally translate into ﬁxed
assets at the end of the company’s reporting period. Other ﬁnancial impacts
include reduced cash ﬂows to undeserving projects and misappropriated
assets. Nonﬁnancial impacts include employee motivation and brand image,
which ultimately reﬂect themselves in the ﬁnancial statements, given the
relative company performance.
Therefore, projects mired in fraud can have a material effect on the
ﬁnancial statements. Projects could affect the ﬁxed asset balances of a com-
pany whereby an asset is capitalized for far more than it is worth. It also
could be seen in overstated expenses (payroll or otherwise) that were
fraudulently spent by rogue project managers. And, given that the focus
of companies is on ﬁnancial statement accounts (rather than on project
investments), coupled with the supposition that most professionals are not
trained in project and program management, it can be predictably stated that
many companies will go without reporting project fraud until after it is too
late.
This chapter was written in collaboration with J. Ross Publications (jrosspub.com)
and its publication Essential Project Investment Governance and Reporting: Pre-
venting Project Fraud and Ensuring Sarbanes-Oxley Compliance as co-written by
the author of this book along with Steven C. Rollins.
177

---

## Page 204

Chapter = c15 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 4:51 pm
178 Cost Recovery
Case Study 15.1: California State Welfare Automation
Project1
A useful case study to illustrate what can go wrong on a project and how
this can potentially transition into project fraud is the California State Wel-
fare Automation Project. What needs to be recognized up front is that fraud
may not have occurred on this project because it is difﬁcult to discern the
difference between fraud and mismanagement. However, given the com-
bination of misreporting, limited reporting of key facts, and other actions
taken by the project team, project fraud is probable. Furthermore, given
this is a state program, it becomes public information for sharing with all
interested parties (unlike most companies that are unwilling to report their
frauds).
The project began as an attempt to automate the California State Depart-
ment of Social Services welfare system, which provides aid to millions of
state residents. What it became was the largest and most costly system ever
undertaken by the state. The project in 1995 was estimated to spend over $1
billion to automate 14 counties’ welfare processing, using a new statewide
system. Unfortunately, the system was originally estimated to cost $545
million or close to half of what the current estimates (in 1995) projected.
Furthermore, the project started with no strategic plan and, therefore, had
no true goals to achieve at the end of its delivery.
Probably the most obvious ﬂaw was the lack of a mission and measur-
able objectives. Although it may seem unfathomable to have a $1 billion
project with no goals, it is entirely possible if the right political pressures are
exerted from within the organization. Projects can move ahead at the whim
of a corporate CEO who has a vision that needs to be implemented for his
goals to be met in the current ﬁscal period. Projects can also crash and burn
when the lack of such success measures leads project teams to wallow in
their own muck.
Next came the cost estimates, when key activities were simply left out
of the calculation. This can invariably occur, as projects are unique and
unprecedented, making it difﬁcult to clearly see all costs in the initial budget
phases. Yet to overlook key cost estimations such as conversion of welfare
ﬁles from a legacy system into the new automated platform is highly suspect.
This oversight cost $46 million and was followed by an additional $36 million
because of training that was never planned. Did they expect the system to
automatically train the users? The budgetary estimates made no mention
of the costs incurred to date to automate the welfare systems and used
outdated caseload data to estimate usage and maintenance costs. Again,
these oversights may have been incompetence of the project team in dealing
with a system project of this size, or it may point to larger issues within the
project.

---

## Page 205

Chapter = c15 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 4:51 pm
Project Fraud Audits 179
No goals, improperly reported costs, and now ... an overstatement of
the beneﬁts expected from the system. The beneﬁts were based mainly
on reducing staff ($66 million) due to automation, but did not factor in
the massive reductions in staff already taken by counties. Another large
cost saving category was the reduced hardware and software costs at the
county level ($39 million) when the state system was operational, yet many
counties needed their computer infrastructure for other systems running
within their county. Therefore, these savings never materialized. Last, there
were expected savings in error rates ($51 million), which may appear one
day but, since there were never any valid measures of error rates, it has
been practically impossible to assess the levels of positive change. Was this
by accident? Possibly, but it should have been better planned because it is
difﬁcult to believe that a dollar estimate was prepared with no valid previous
benchmark upon which to base the cost savings estimation.
Furthermore, through a more technical review of the architecture, it
was determined that the system itself may not work for the following
reasons:
■ The system was based on a proprietary versus open architecture, which
was becoming more prevalent in the computing world. Even stranger
was that the California State Department issued a new strategic direction
a few years earlier that open architecture was the preferred choice for
new development. Please note that this proprietary system had few peo-
ple to program and support it, which would hurt its long-term chances
for success.
■ It also required signiﬁcant overhead and was not as efﬁcient in storing
case data, given that it was optimized to run reports and not process
transactions.
■ The system’s ability to meet the transaction processing demands of Cal-
ifornia were unproven to date either at past customers or through any
sort of testing.
It is no surprise that the evaluation team for the new computer system
only looked at business requirements “superﬁcially” as, according to a later
report on the project, “an in-depth system-to-system comparison of business
functions was not an objective of the system selection process.” The tech-
nology team involved did not look at the long-term goals of the system but
rather the short-term limited needs of the project in initially automating a
select number of counties. Then, when the evaluation team noted that it was
divided as to its preference of selecting a system, the executive summary of
the report made no mention of this division. It rather suggested that it was
the platform that led to the demise of the project.

---

## Page 206

Chapter = c15 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 4:51 pm
180 Cost Recovery
Case Study Wrap-Up
Was There Fraud in the California State Welfare Automation Project?
Occupational fraud is deﬁned by the Association of Certiﬁed Fraud Exam-
iners as “The use of one’s occupation for personal enrichment through
the deliberate misuse or misapplication of the employing organization’s
resources or assets.”
This deﬁnition encompasses a wide range of misconduct by employees,
managers, and executives. Regardless, all occupational fraud schemes have
four key elements in common. The activity:
1. Is clandestine
2. Violates the perpetrator’s ﬁduciary duties to the victim organization
3. Is committed for the purpose of direct or indirect ﬁnancial beneﬁt to the
perpetrator
4. Costs the employing organization assets, revenue, or reserves
It may never be known whether fraud was committed on this project
but a few things are clear:
■ Many clandestine activities took place. In selecting the ultimate plat-
form for the welfare automation system, there were changes to platform
evaluation.
■ The activities, taken as a whole, appear negligent. Calling into question
the ﬁduciary duties of those responsible.
■ There were many parties that beneﬁted ﬁnancially. To the state of Cali-
fornia, it may have been a cost overrun, but to an army of consultants,
it was a windfall of revenue.
■ Costs to the organization were high.Not only in the overages in cost but
also in the limited beneﬁts that ensued.
Therefore, from this analysis, it is highly possible that some fraud was
committed. Only through additional investigation and analysis would such
a determination become clear, yet, on the surface, something deﬁnitely was
“rotten in the state of Denmark.”
Project Fraud Deﬁned
Using the deﬁnition of fraud from the ACFE, at the highest level, fraud can
be categorized into the following three areas:
2
1. Fraudulent statements. Involving the falsiﬁcation of an organization’s
ﬁnancial statements. This includes frauds that are outright lies and

---

## Page 207

Chapter = c15 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 4:51 pm
Project Fraud Audits 181
earnings management that is a shading of the true picture of the com-
pany’s health.
2. Corruption. When fraudsters wrongfully use their inﬂuence in a business
transaction to procure some beneﬁt for themselves or another person,
contrary to their duty to their employer or the rights of another.
3. Asset misappropriations. Involving the theft or misuse of an organiza-
tion’s assets.
These three major areas are further segregated into subcategories in the
diagram in Exhibit 15.1. This diagram was developed by the Association
of Certiﬁed Fraud Examiners and is part of an accompanying publication,
Corporate Fraud Handbook: Prevention and Detection, written by Joseph T.
Wells, that explores the prevention and detection of each fraud type.
What is intriguing when reviewing theFraud Classiﬁcation Systemis that
projects are not presented as a category. There is no item identiﬁed related
to business cases that are falsiﬁed or project plans that are misreported.
Rather, what may be inferred from the Fraud Classiﬁcation System is that
the following related frauds may have a high correlation to projects.
■ Fraudulent Statements → Nonﬁnancial → Internal Documents
■ Fraudulent Statements → Financial → Asset or Revenue Overstatements
→ Improper Asset Valuations
■ Fraudulent Statements → Financial → Asset or Revenue Overstatements
→ Concealed Liabilities and Expenses
■ Corruption → Bribery
■ Corruption → Conﬂicts of Interest
■ Asset Misappropriation → Cash → Fraudulent Disbursements →
Expense Reimbursement Schemes
■ Asset Misappropriation→ Cash → Fraudulent Disbursements→ Payroll
Schemes
■ Asset Misappropriation → Cash → Fraudulent Disbursements → Billing
Schemes
■ Asset Misappropriation → Inventory and other Assets → Misuse
By reviewing these types of fraud, please do not infer that other types
cannot occur on a project.
Rather, these categories are considered the most likely frauds that could
occur on a project.
Therefore, based on these categories, a deﬁnition for project fraud can
be provided for the purposes of this book: Project fraud is the misrepresenta-
tion of a project’s mission or progress to secure project ﬁnancing, misuse of
project resources, and improper dealings with project vendors for personal
enrichment.

## Page 209

Project Fraud Audits 183
For example, taking the ﬁrst category (Internal Documents), a fairly
comprehensive list of fraudulent internal documents examples as related to
projects, is listed here:
■ Overreported and unsubstantiated business case
■ Unsubstantiated project decisions
■ Underreported initial estimates of project life cycle costs (to get project
approved)
■ Underreported initial estimates of project maintenance costs (to get
project approved)
■ Overreported schedule progress
■ Overreported quality progress
Recoveries to Find
Areas where errors and fraud can occur include the following, which are
brieﬂy explained, and many of which are supported by the case studies cited
earlier.
■ Project deliverables meet the need of the organization. Aside from the
ﬁnancial statement impacts, poorly run or fraudulent projects simply
lose money for the organization. Projects are now taking up over $2.3
trillion in the U.S. economy (25 percent of gross domestic product),
according to a 2005 PMI study using data from the U.S. Department
of Commerce. The Standish Group reports that more than $275 billion
relate to application software projects, and for these technology projects,
the following trends were noted:
■ Only 16.2 percent of projects are completed on time and on budget.
■ 31.1 percent are canceled.
■ The average cost overrun was 189 percent of the original time esti-
mate.
■ Project investments can be fully capitalized rather than written off.Cap-
italized assets are the likely results of a project and are accounted for
mainly through Financial Accounting Standard 144. The main focus of
this accounting standard is on impairment of an asset’s value that shall be
tested for recoverability whenever events or changes in circumstances
indicate that its carrying amount may not be recoverable. The recover-
ability is based on the probabilistic cash ﬂows expected over the life of
the asset. When this recoverability is questioned, two courses of action
will be taken:
■ Assets previously capitalized as assets per the balance sheet will be
written off as expenses on the income statement.

---

## Page 210

Chapter = c15 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 4:51 pm
184 Cost Recovery
■ The useful life of the asset may be reduced (say from 25 years to 5
years) if the asset is not expected to produce measurable beneﬁts over
the originally estimated useful life, thereby increasing depreciation
expense per the income statement.
■ Asset misappropriation fraud. Assets can be misappropriated under the
guise of the project. Examples include:
■ Excessive or unwarranted travel and entertainment charges
■ Assets that are assigned to the project but then taken for personal use
■ Employees that improperly post payroll charges to a project
■ Vendors that are selected based more on conﬂicts of interest than on
their ability to perform on the project
Questions to Ask to Assess the Opportunity
Taking into consideration the case study experiences and the types of recov-
ery, the following is a list of questions that can help frame the opportunity
size of a project fraud review:
Opportunity Size
■ How much spend is assigned to projects at the organization in the
current year and for the next two years?
Overreported and Unsubstantiated Business Case
■ Have project goals, deliverables, and their associated success metrics
been documented and approved by all sponsors?
■ Was a prototype completed to test the viability of the project’s deliver-
ables and in assessing the true market demand?
■ Has the organization completed a similar project in the past? Further-
more, has the company failed in this type of project in the past?
Underreported Initial Estimates of Project Life Cycle Costs
■ Are the initial project cost estimates based on historical precedence or
benchmarks in the marketplace?
■ Has a contingency estimate (e.g., 15 percent) been entered from a cost
perspective?
■ Have maintenance costs for at least one year been estimated as part of
the project?
Overreported Cost and Schedule Progress
■ Have the project time values (hours worked and days in duration) been
calculated by the project team in a bottom-up fashion?

---

## Page 211

Chapter = c15 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 4:51 pm
Project Fraud Audits 185
■ Do status reports calculate earned value on the project from a schedule
and cost perspective?
Conﬂicts of Interest
■ Are the established bidding policies being followed or are rules being
broken for certain vendors?
■ Are certain vendor bids bypassing necessary review procedures?
■ Is the buyer limiting time for submission of bids so only those ven-
dors with advance information have adequate time to prepare bids or
proposals?
■ Are there any known relationships between vendors under review and
employees within the organization?
Expense Reimbursement Schemes
■ Are amounts charged for expense reimbursement reviewed and com-
pared to budgeted amounts, and are variances followed up on regularly?
■ Are expense reimbursements reviewed by the appropriate level of man-
agement prior to payment?
■ Are expenses over a speciﬁed dollar limit or of a certain type approved
by a level of management independent of the project team?
Payroll Schemes
■ Are actual payroll amounts reviewed and compared to budgeted
amounts, and are variances analyzed regularly (these amounts include
regular, overtime, and bonus payments)?
■ Are payroll registers reviewed for material unusual payments prior to
actual payment?
■ Are time clocks used with employee badges to register time in and out?
Billing Schemes
■ Are vendors’ invoices, receiving reports, and purchase orders matched
before the related liability is recorded? Are invoices checked as to prices,
extensions, footings, freight charges, allowances, and credit terms?
■ Does a responsible ofﬁcial approve invoices for payment? Are proce-
dures adequate to ensure that merchandise purchased for direct delivery
to customers is promptly billed to the customers and recorded as both
a receivable and a payable?
■ Is the vendor master ﬁle checked against the employee master ﬁle to
identify any potential fraudulent vendors?
■ Are vendor purchases analyzed for abnormal levels on both a monthly
and yearly basis?

---

## Page 212

Chapter = c15 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 4:51 pm
186 Cost Recovery
Inventory and Other Assets Misuse
■ Is inventory disappearing from storage?
■ Is inventory physically counted on a periodic basis and assessed to the
reported inventory on hand?
■ Is an analytical review of inventory and usage of inventory (with vari-
ances followed up) completed on a regular basis?
Conclusion
Project fraud can represent one of the largest unintentional cash outﬂows for
a company that will never be seen in company headlines as project spon-
sors are simply too high up in the organization to air out its dirty laundry.
To get started, it is probably easiest to talk to the ﬁxed-asset manager at the
organization to understand the current work in process projects, their status,
and expenditures to date. Also, remember that not all project mismanage-
ment is fraud because it could simply be poorly run projects that lead to
problematic company cost outﬂows.
Please note that the publicationEssential Project Investment Governance
and Reporting: Preventing Project Fraud and Ensuring Sarbanes-Oxley Com-
pliance (Fort Lauderdale, FL: J. Ross Publishing), as co-written by the author
of this book along with Steven C. Rollins, has been devoted to this topic
and other project investment governance tactics.

CHAPTER 16
Real Estate Cost Segregation
Overview
Building owners are challenged to invest in their business while maintain-
ing proﬁtability, cash ﬂow, and return on investment. Cost segregation is
an important tool for many property owners because it helps signiﬁcantly
reduce income tax liability in the early years of property ownership and
increase cash ﬂow. Owners can even use cost segregation to look back and
catch up on any missed depreciation deductions.
Cost segregation allows a commercial property owner or lessee to accel-
erate the depreciation on certain assets within a property. Many components
within the property can be reclassiﬁed as short-life assets. This allows these
components to be depreciated over 5, 7, or 15 years rather than over 39
years. To conduct a study, a cost segregation engineer would analyze blue
prints, construction documents, and purchase receipts in addition to per-
forming a site inspection of the property. The engineers would then break
out each component in the property, assign it a depreciable life, and cal-
culate the exact cost, based on either the cost to construct or the purchase
price of the property.
The major beneﬁt of cost segregation is that a property owner can take
advantage of the time value of money through the use of a front-loaded
depreciation schedule. Property owners can look back at a property as far
back as 1987 and catch up on any missed depreciation. This can signiﬁcantly
reduce the tax liability and increase cash ﬂow for a business. In addition, an
engineering-based cost segregation study can produce numerous beneﬁts,
including the ability to write off long-life assets as they need to be replaced,
a reduction in transfer tax, and a reduction in insurance costs. These beneﬁts
will be highlighted in the following case studies.
This chapter was written in collaboration with Bob Bergin at Commercial Property
Consultants (bergincostseg.com).
187

---

## Page 214

Chapter = c16 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 11:45 am
188 Cost Recovery
Case Study 16.1: Franchise Restaurant
Commercial Property Consultants, LLC (CPC) was engaged by the owners
of a franchise restaurant in the mid-south to conduct an engineering-based
cost segregation study for a recently purchased single building located in a
popular suburban retail location.
The objective was to identify property components that could be reclas-
siﬁed to shorter recovery periods to accelerate building depreciation and
defer income taxes.
Property Proﬁle
The property is a single-story building containing approximately 7,300
square feet. The building exterior walls are concrete block and ﬁnished
with brick veneer. The windows are insulated ﬁxed glass in aluminum
frames. Interior ﬁnishes include drywall over metal stud partitions ﬁnished
with paint, vinyl wall covering, Marlite, and stainless-steel wall protection,
and hollow metal and wood doors with frames and hardware. There is a
year-round heating, ventilating, and air conditioning system provided by
gas and electric rooftop units. The kitchen equipment has exhaust hoods,
fans, and a make-up air system. Electrical systems include power distri-
bution equipment, ﬂuorescent and incandescent light ﬁxtures, receptacles,
switches, conduit, and wire. There are multiple kitchen equipment electrical
hookups. Land improvements include asphalt paving, concrete sidewalks,
concrete curbing, concrete Dumpster pad, Dumpster enclosures, and land-
scaping with irrigation. The property has a cost basis of $2,000,000 and was
acquired and placed in service in 2004.
Engineering Process
CPC’s construction engineers conducted a detailed inspection and itemized
the improvements located on this purchased property. They reviewed all
available construction documentation and, along with the inspection infor-
mation, isolated the various components qualifying for shorter cost recovery
period depreciation under the provisions of the Internal Revenue Code and
current tax law. Using construction estimating techniques, each component
is assigned a value that is adjusted for depreciation and reconciled back
to the purchase price. Finally, CPC’s team of senior construction engineers
and tax specialists reviewed and certiﬁed its completeness and accuracy. A
sample diagram of what is produced from such a study is shown in Exhibit
16.1, which highlights the detail involved.

---

## Page 215

Chapter = c16 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 11:45 am
Real Estate Cost Segregation 189
EXHIBIT 16.1 EngineeringStudyBlueprint(Sample)
Project Results
As a result of this engineering-based cost segregation study, the client was
able to reallocate $1,053,010, or 52.7 percent, of the assets to a shorter
recovery time. The client’s projected tax beneﬁt on a Net Present Value
(NPV) basis was projected to be a total of $167,174, with a ﬁrst-year tax
beneﬁt of $115,960.
TaxBeneﬁtsSummary
Cost Basis $2,000,000
Cost Reallocated $1,053,010
Reallocation Percent 52.7%
1st Year Deferred Tax $115,960
Total Deferred Tax $167,174
Case Study 16.2: Apartment Complex
Commercial Property Consultants, LLC (CPC) was engaged by the owners of
a real estate development company to conduct an engineering-based cost
segregation study for a new apartment complex located on the southern
East Coast. The objective was to identify property components that could be

---

## Page 216

Chapter = c16 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 11:45 am
190 Cost Recovery
reclassiﬁed to shorter recovery periods to accelerate building depreciation
and to defer income taxes.
Property Proﬁle
The property is a two- and three-story apartment complex and clubhouse
measuring approximately 247,430 square feet. The wood-framed structures
are enclosed with Hardiplank siding and brick, with insulated glass in vinyl-
clad wood frames. Roof construction consists of a ﬁberglass shingle roof
system. The core design of the apartment complex includes individual apart-
ments consisting of bat rooms, utility rooms, bedrooms, kitchens, living
rooms, and dining rooms. The clubhouse has a clubroom, open and individ-
ual ofﬁce areas, computer room, laundry, mechanical room, exercise room,
and pool equipment and storage rooms. The service systems include stan-
dard plumbing ﬁxtures, copper, steel, PVC, and cast-iron sanitary and waste
piping systems. There is a year-round heating, ventilating, and air condi-
tioning system. Electrical systems include power distribution equipment,
ﬂuorescent and incandescent light ﬁxtures, receptacles, switches, conduit,
and wire. Land improvements for the site include asphalt and concrete
paving, concrete curbing, site signage, concrete sidewalks, underground util-
ities, concrete Dumpster pad and screen wall, and general landscaping with
irrigation. The property has a cost basis of $9,987,284, and was acquired and
placed in service in 2005.
Engineering Process
CPC’s construction engineers performed a complete analysis of all available
construction drawings and speciﬁcations, contractor payment applications,
invoices, and other supporting documentation. They prepared a detailed
analysis of all accumulated data on a property unit basis for cost allo-
cation purposes under the provisions of the Internal Revenue Code. An
onsite inspection was then performed to verify, photograph, and document
the property. Finally, CPC’s team of senior construction engineers and tax
specialists reviewed and certiﬁed its completeness and accuracy.
Project Results
As a result of this engineering-based cost segregation study, the client was
able to reallocate $2,653,008, or 26.6 percent, of the assets to a shorter
recovery time. The client’s projected tax beneﬁt on a Net Present Value
(NPV) basis was projected to be a total of $456,942, with a ﬁrst-year tax
beneﬁt of $116,636.

Case Study Wrap-Up
While the results achieved in these case studies are impressive, it is important
to understand the differences in the several methods that can be used when
conducting a cost segregation study. These results were achieved using a
detailed engineering approach. This method is performed according to IRS
Cost Segregation Audit Techniques Guide and maximizes all legitimate and
validated tax beneﬁts possible.

---

## Page 220

Chapter = c16 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 11:45 am
194 Cost Recovery
There are several methods that can be used when conducting a cost
segregation study. The most widely used method results in what is known
as a residual study. A residual study allows a provider to assign project costs
to short-life assets, and then place the remaining property basis into the 39-
year depreciable life classiﬁcation. These studies will often leave out many
items that should be reclassiﬁed, resulting in lost beneﬁts to the property
owner. The study may even overestimate the amount of personal property,
leaving the owner open to risk if the IRS were to ever audit the study. These
consequences result from the lack of detail included in a residual study.
Not only can a residual study cost an owner thousands in missed bene-
ﬁts, it can also prevent the owner from realizing a great beneﬁt in the years
following the study. Most residual studies do not provide project cost detail
for any of the 39-year assets in a property. This eliminates the ability to write
off long-life assets as they need to be replaced. For example, if the property
owner needs to replace the property roof before its being fully depreciated,
the remaining depreciable balance of the roof will remain on the books for
the life of the property. There will be no write-off beneﬁt afforded to the
owner.
Business owners need to make sure that their cost segregation provider
is conducting a true engineering-based study using the guidelines estab-
lished by the IRS. This means that every asset in a property is assigned both
a depreciable life and a project cost. All short-life items, as well as all 39-
year assets, are accounted for. A true engineering-based study allows the
property owner to realize the greatest beneﬁt from a front-loaded depre-
ciation schedule while keeping within all guidelines required by the IRS.
This engineering-based methodology is deﬁned as the “most methodical
and accurate approach” by the IRS.
In addition to receiving the greatest tax beneﬁt, the property owner
can also take advantage of the ability to write off long-life assets in the
years following the study. If a roof needs to be replaced 10 years after
the property is placed in service, the owner can write off the remaining
depreciable balance of that roof all in one year. This can amount to hundreds
of thousands of dollars in tax beneﬁts.
Property owners owe it to themselves, and their wallets, to make sure
that they are receiving a study that will offer them the greatest beneﬁt both
at the time of the study and in the following years.
Recoveries to Find
The following is a list of expected recoveries, in cash and other forms, from
a cost segregation review:
■ Cash ﬂow. The overall purpose of reclassifying components into their
proper depreciation lives is the increased cash ﬂow that it results in.

---

## Page 221

Chapter = c16 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 11:45 am
Real Estate Cost Segregation 195
The major beneﬁt of cost segregation is that a property owner can take
advantage of the time value of money through the use of a front-loaded
depreciation schedule.
■ Missed depreciation. Property owners can look back at a property as
far back as 1987 and catch up on any missed depreciation. This can
signiﬁcantly reduce the tax liability and increase cash ﬂow for a business.
See examples in Exhibits 16.2 through 16.4.
■ Components not properly identiﬁed and reclassiﬁed in an earlier residual
study. As mentioned earlier, when a proper engineering study has been
performed, it is not uncommon to ﬁnd missed beneﬁts from a previous
residual study. For example, CPC engineers go beyond walls and the vis-
ible to evaluate every single component associated with a property. This
ensures that tax beneﬁts are maximized to the fullest extent and the ﬁnd-
ings would be plausible and easy to document in the event of an audit.

The following list of six questions will help provide assistance in assess-
ing the prudence of a cost segregation study as it relates to certain clients,
buildings, time frames, and tax situations.
1. Who can beneﬁt from a cost segregation study? There are basic criteria
for a building to meet so that it may qualify for a cost segregation study.

Real Estate Cost Segregation 197
First and foremost, the owner of the property must operate as a for-proﬁt
entity and pay federal income tax. Generally speaking, any commercial
property with a basis of $1,000,000 or higher after the land value is
removed will qualify. Multi-unit residential buildings will qualify if they
have ﬁve or more units. It is important to note that the straight-line
depreciation schedule for residential buildings is 27.5 years as compared
to all other commercial property, which have 39-year schedules. The
building should have been acquired after 1987. Finally, a building will
qualify if leasehold improvements have been made totaling $750,000
or higher. As one can see from the wide array of commercial property
types from the case studies, an estimate of the beneﬁts of a study will
be worth pursuing if it meets these general qualiﬁcations.
2. When should a cost segregation study be conducted?The optimal time to
perform a study is in the year the property is placed in service. Due to the
IRS look-back procedures, a taxpayer can recover missed depreciation
on properties placed in service as far back as 1987. Studies can also be
performed before a building is constructed or acquired.
3. Will the price of the cost segregation study be worth the cash ﬂow beneﬁt
received? In most cases, the return on investment of an engineering-
based study typically exceeds 15 to 20 times per dollar invested in the
cost of the study. A reputable engineering-based company will provide
a no-cost, no-obligation estimate of what the cost of the study will be
and what your cash beneﬁt will be.
4. Does the risk of an audit increase with the implementation of a study?
Filing Form 3115 does not send up a red ﬂag to the IRS. It simply notiﬁes
the IRS of a change in depreciation methods. A well-documented study
is unlikely to have any issues. A CPC study exceeds all 13 Elements of
Quality and the Nine Essentials outlined in the IRS Audit Techniques
Guide. If you were to be audited, CPC will defend the study at no
additional cost.
5. Can a REIT (Real Estate Investment Trust) beneﬁt from having a cost seg-
regation study performed? REITs can gain from a cost segregation study
in two ways: First, it can signiﬁcantly reduce taxable income and its dis-
tribution requirement, thereby retaining additional cash ﬂow. Second,
the study permits a REIT to pay dividends in the form of return of capital
(untaxed until the shares are sold) instead of ordinary income. Investors
typically prefer dividends with the greatest percentage of return of cap-
ital. A cost segregation study increases the return-of-capital component,
thereby increasing the taxable equivalent yield. An increased depreci-
ation reduces the overall current taxable income. Because REITs are
required to pay out at least 90 percent of their taxable income in the
form of dividends, lowering the amount required to be paid in the cur-
rent period allows a REIT to better manage future dividends and retain

---

## Page 224

Chapter = c16 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 11:45 am
198 Cost Recovery
cash to fund future acquisitions. REITs are also eligible to derive up to
15 percent of their total rental income from personal property that is
leased under, or in connection with, the lease of real property. Proper
identiﬁcation of 1245 property helps maximize the depreciation deduc-
tion resulting in increased cash ﬂow.
6. Can the implementation of a cost segregation study be combined with a
1031 exchange, and would this be a prudent ﬁnancial or tax strategy?
Let’s start by deﬁning a 1031 exchange and use an example as a refer-
ence. A 1031 exchange is a method for deferring any gain realized when
investment property is sold for a proﬁt and the proceeds are rolled over
into a new investment property of like kind.
For example, Property A is being sold. It was purchased for $1
million and is being been depreciated on a 39-year schedule. It has
been held for 5 years, is valued at $2 million, and has an adjusted basis
of $871,795.
Property B is the replacement property being purchased. The pur-
chase price is $3 million, there is a $1 million cost basis and $871,795
in carryover cost basis. As the table below indicates, the carryover cost
basis will continue to be depreciated over the next 34 years, based on
Property A. The new cost basis of $1 million will begin a new 39-year
depreciation schedule.
$871,795 $1Million
CostBasis CostBasis TotalDep. TaxBeneﬁt(40%)
Years 1–34 $25,641 $25,641 $51,282 $20,513
Years 35–39 $0 $25,641 $25,641 $10,256
Now let’s take a look at the example with a cost segregation
study performed on the new property. Thirty percent of the property
is reclassiﬁed to 5-, 7-, and 15-year property. This accelerated depre-
ciation will result in increased depreciation of $104,904 compared to
the $51,282, based on a 39-year schedule. This results in a yearly tax
beneﬁt of $36,716—a 205 percent increase over the original schedules.
By combining the 1031 exchange and cost segregation, the owner has
deferred $1,000,000 in capital gains and increased his annual depreci-
ation deduction from $25,641 on Property A to $104,904 on the new
property.
With proper planning, these two methods can provide a tremen-
dous tax saving opportunity. Executing these two methods together
should be carefully considered in any sale and subsequent acquisition
of commercial property.

Conclusion
As demonstrated in these case studies, cost segregation is a viable way of
increasing cash ﬂow and is applicable to virtually all types of commercial
property. When analyzing the different areas of potential cost recovery for
a company that owns its own operational facilities, as well as any other
commercial real estate, this should be the ﬁrst path taken. It should also
be considered before a company purchases additional property, as it will
increase the cash ﬂow projections and allow a more accurate assessment of
the purchase. If an IRS audit is of concern, it should be noted that based
on thousands of studies, CPC has not once had a study overturned and will
stand by its ﬁndings in the event of an IRS audit.

Chapter = c17 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 3:16 pm
CHAPTER 17
Research and Development
Tax Credits
Background
The Research and Development (R&D) tax credit was created by the U.S.
Congress as part of the Economic Recovery Tax Act of 1981 to encourage
American industry to invest in research and development activities. The pur-
pose of the credit, documented in Section 41 of the Internal Revenue Code,
was to stimulate R&D activities among businesses through tax incentives.
Because of the stringent requirements that existed under the provisions of
the credit, however, the vast majority of small and midsize companies were
unable to reap any substantial beneﬁt from them.
In simple terms, the program permits taxpayers to reclassify certain line
items in their proﬁt-and-loss statements so that they qualify for 100 percent
reimbursement through credits rather than as deductible expenses. Credits
may be used to offset regular tax obligations or, in the case of prior years’
claims, may result in an actual refund.
Because the beneﬁts are substantially wage-based, Congress initially
required claimants to provide precise time sheets documenting every hour
an employee spent conducting qualiﬁed R&D activities in order to qualify. It
became clear, however, that these requirements created a signiﬁcant burden
on small and midsized companies. Realizing that a majority of innovation
in the United States was in fact transpiring within these small to midsize
ﬁrms, Congress in 2001 liberalized the statutory requirements. Speciﬁcally,
the new regulations eliminated the requirement for detailed contempo-
rary records as the basis for making claims. Furthermore, the research no
longer had to result in a product that was new to the industry; instead, the
The content of this chapter was developed in collaboration with Paradigm Partners
(paradigmlp.com).
200

---

## Page 227

Chapter = c17 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 3:16 pm
Research and Development Tax Credits 201
resulting product or process simply had to be new to the company that
developed it.
The R&D tax credit drives billions of dollars in economic activity and
is credited with keeping thousands of high-skilled jobs here in the United
States. Nevertheless, the program expired at the end of 2007 and was not
renewed until Congress passed the Emergency Economic Stabilization Act
of 2008 (EESA) on October 3, 2008. As part of the EESA, the R&D tax credit
(research credit) was extended through 2009. It should be noted, however,
that nearly every other developed nation provides a similar stimulus to its
domestic industries. It is, therefore, unlikely that this credit, in some form,
will ever disappear completely from the Tax Code. Taxpayers should pre-
pare to pursue these credits at all times, even if the contemporary political
environment results in a temporary lapse of enabling legislation.
An especially appealing feature of the program is that, in its current
form, it allows taxpayers to go back up to three years to claim the credit. For
companies that have paid taxes during that time, this means that a substantial
cash refund may be obtained. The following case study demonstrates how
signiﬁcant these beneﬁts may be to a company.
Case Study 17.1: Engine Service Firm R&D Credits
An R&D tax credit study was performed by a specialized engineering and
tax consulting ﬁrm for a privately held Midwestern ﬁrm that develops, man-
ufactures, and services engines for a variety of industries. The company’s
annual sales are about $75 million and it has more than 300 employees with
an average annual payroll of $7.5 million.
The company’s research and development personnel constantly work
toward the following:
■ Conceptualizing and designing new or improved innovative products
and processes
■ Developing, evaluating, and testing new products and signiﬁcant im-
provements to existing products
■ Investigating new and existing technologies and determining and assess-
ing how they can be applied in the company’s current products and
processes
The company also goes through various stages to design and develop
new products.
■ The ﬁrst stage is the research stage, during which the company performs
extensive research on the feasibility of the product design.

---

## Page 228

Chapter = c17 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 3:16 pm
202 Cost Recovery
■ The next stage is the predesign phase, during which the company
develops and analyzes the prototype layout and schematic. During this
development phase, the company also assigns resources, conducts con-
ceptual, preliminary, and detailed feasibility work, updates business
analyses, and initiates and updates design documentation.
■ The following stage is the schematic design stage. The company under-
goes an extensive quality assurance process to ensure that the design
meets all of the speciﬁcations. Furthermore, the company conducts an
iterative testing process during which it ensures that the design meets
qualiﬁcations and innovative standards.
The R&D tax credit allows companies to go back three open tax years
as well as the current year. In this case, a study was performed for the three
previous tax years of 2003 through 2005 and the current tax year at the time
of 2006. The R&D net tax credits were as follows:
2003: $210,000
2004: $270,000
2005: $280,000
2006: $300,000
The company received a refund check from the IRS of $760,000 for the
three previous tax years and it was able to reduce its tax liability for 2006
by $300,000.
This company is very typical of most manufacturing companies in
designing and developing new products. To the surprise of many, similar
results have been achieved in many industries that lack white-coated R&D
engineers.

Case Study Wrap-Up
It should be noted that while smaller companies may not have such for-
malized processes, they can also obtain substantial beneﬁts proportionate
to their size. Depending on the industry, ﬁrms with annual payrolls begin-
ning at $750,000 may ﬁnd beneﬁts waiting that justify the effort required to
complete a claim.
Reasons Why the Credits Have Not Been Taken in the Past
The principal reason why these credits go unclaimed by qualiﬁed ﬁrms is
simply the lack of awareness. Most small businesses are heavily depen-
dent on their tax preparer, typically a CPA ﬁrm, for ﬁnancial advice of all
kinds. Unfortunately, many rely on small or regional CPA ﬁrms serving a
wide variety of clients who cannot keep up with all the regulations docu-
mented in the 16,845 pages of text that compose the Internal Revenue Code.
In sum, many CPAs are simply unaware that the program’s requirements
were relaxed. It should be noted that even ﬁrms served by very large and
sophisticated CPA ﬁrms may not be receiving all the beneﬁts due to them;
numerous instances where signiﬁcant beneﬁts went unclaimed have been
documented.
There is also widespread misunderstanding of the term research and
development. While the words tend to invoke thoughts of scientists in
white lab coats and dedicated R&D departments, the deﬁnition as adopted
by the IRS is signiﬁcantly broader and applies to everything from small
machine shops to engineering ﬁrms, manufacturers, software developers,
biotechnology ﬁrms, and even to wineries or bakeries that have made any
improvements to their products or processes. According to IRC Section 41,
qualiﬁed activities include the design or development of new or improved
business components, which are speciﬁed to include:
■ Products
■ Processes
■ Formulas
■ Software (internal or external)
■ Techniques
■ Inventions
The deﬁnitions may be broadly interpreted where, for instance, the
installation costs of new equipment being integrated into the production
line may qualify.
It should also be recognized that, going forward, few conventional
tax preparers have the background and credentials necessary to prepare a
qualifying study in support of their client’s claim. As a result of the inﬂux of

---

## Page 230

Chapter = c17 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 3:16 pm
204 Cost Recovery
new claims since the program was liberalized in 2001, the IRS has recently
begun requiring that the authors of these supporting studies be credentialed
engineers or attorneys specializing in intellectual property law. Additional
discussion of this new requirement follows (see “Recent Developments”).
Other reasons for not ﬁling are seen more sporadically:
■ Some businesses fear that ﬁling a revised return for prior years may
trigger an audit. This fear is typically blown out of proportion. A sep-
arate R&D group at the IRS handles R&D studies exclusively. An R&D
audit, in and of itself, will not trigger a general audit unless egregious
errors are discovered in the calculation or substantiation of the credit.
Furthermore, an R&D audit is not a zero-sum game. It essentially com-
prises a review and negotiation of the credit calculations and supporting
documentation. Anything challenged and not overcome is shrunk back,
meaning that the portion unapproved is withheld by the IRS and the
remainder is refunded to the company.
■ Others fear that the research effort to document qualifying activities
may disrupt their ofﬁce operations or cause them to incur signiﬁcant
expenses to amend prior-year returns. While these are possible risks,
prudent oversight and decision-making can easily prevent their occur-
rence. The use of a specialized engineering ﬁrm for the calculation and
substantiation is highly recommended and minimally disruptive.
Additional State Programs
In addition to the beneﬁts of the federal R&D credit program, many states
have implemented programs to reward and encourage R&D within the state.
Currently, there are 33 states that provide R&D tax credit beneﬁts applicable
to state income tax obligations to companies conducting qualiﬁed activities.
Exhibit 17.1 lists the top 10 states in credits awarded in 2003.
Four Requirements for Qualifying Claims
An expense must be for research and experimentation within the mean-
ing of Section 174
1 of the IRC to satisfy the Section 41 qualiﬁed research
requirements, and “it must also be for the purpose of discovering infor-
mation that is ‘technological in nature.’ In addition, the application of the
information must be intended for use in the development of a ‘new or
improved business component’ of the taxpayer. The research must use the
‘scientiﬁc method of experimentation,’ and the purpose of the contemplated
developed technology must be for a new or improved function.”
2
Using the credit therefore requires an understanding of four terms:
1. Technological. Research is undertaken for the purposes of discov-
ering information that is technological in nature if the process of

---

## Page 231

experimentation depended signiﬁcantly on a hard science, such as
physics, biology, engineering, or computer science, as opposed to rely-
ing on a social science, such as economics.
3
2. Scientiﬁc. The term scientiﬁc method of experimentationdescribes those
activities used to design a business component when the component’s
design is unclear or unknown at the commencement of the process.
It must be derived by the scientiﬁc method of testing and discarding
various hypotheses. The process is described as a sequential design
process that develops the overall component through the formulation
of hypotheses for speciﬁc design decisions. It’s followed by testing,
analysis, and then either reﬁnement or rejection.
4
3. Functional purpose. R&D is treated as conducted for a functional pur-
pose only if it relates to a new or improved function, performance,
reliability, or quality. Activities undertaken to ensure achievement of
the intended function, performance, reliability, or quality of the business
component after the beginning of commercial production of the compo-
nent do not constitute qualiﬁed experimentation. If the R&D is related
to style, taste, cosmetic, or seasonal design factors, it is not treated as
conducted for a functional purpose. It would, therefore, not be eligible
for the credit.
5
4. Business component. The three-pronged test—technological in nature,
scientiﬁc method, and functional purpose—is applied on a shrinking-
back basis pursuant to which the three elements of the test are ﬁrst
applied at the level of the entire product, next at the most signiﬁcant
subset of the product, and thereafter the next most signiﬁcant subset.
Thus, the term business component is redeﬁned at each level to mean
the most signiﬁcant subset of elements of the product at which the three
parts of the test are satisﬁed. The shrinkback to the next lower level of

---

## Page 232

Chapter = c17 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 3:16 pm
206 Cost Recovery
the product is continued until either all three parts of the tests are met
or the most basic component of the product is tested and fails.6
The R&D tax credit extension in 2008 did not change the deﬁnitions of
what constitutes a qualiﬁed or unqualiﬁed research expense. Under the cur-
rent version, Section 41 allows a tax credit of 20 percent of qualiﬁed research
expenses that exceed the base amount, and basic research payments. Qual-
iﬁed research expenses are those for qualiﬁed research performed in-house
or under contract. Such expenses include the following:
■ Wages paid to employees engaged in the actual conduct of the research
effort or in the direct supervision or support of such effort
■ Costs of supplies used in the research
■ Payments made to third parties for computer time used in the research
■ 65 percent of the contract fee paid to a third party for research conducted
on the taxpayer’s behalf
The regulations provide that the qualiﬁed research generally includes all
costs incident to the development of an experiment or pilot model, a plan
process, a product, formula, an invention, or a similar property. The regula-
tions provide, however, that the termresearch and experimental expenditure
does not include the following:
■ Ordinary testing or inspection of materials or products for quality control
■ Efﬁciency surveys
■ Management studies
■ Consumer surveys
■ Market research (including advertising or promotions) or promotions
Recent Developments
Since the IRS broadened the rules in late 2001 to enable smaller to midsize
companies to rightfully take their credits, there has been a ﬂood of claims
into the IRS. Naturally, and given the large size of the credit, the IRS has
discovered that numerous claims ﬁled by companies themselves or through
the help of some specialty ﬁrms have been wholly deﬁcient in their docu-
mentation and substantiation of the claims they are making.
A few of these specialty ﬁrms have been found to use inadequate
approaches, such as not being properly staffed with engineers and intel-
lectual property attorneys with engineering backgrounds, both of which
are needed to perform a proper study that can withstand any potential IRS
audit. As a result, the credits were shrunk back signiﬁcantly or disallowed
completely.

---

## Page 233

Chapter = c17 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 3:16 pm
Research and Development Tax Credits 207
There are currently three different methods available to taxpayers to cal-
culate the credit: the regular credit, the alternative incremental credit (AIC),
and the alternative simpliﬁed credit (ASC). The extension has signiﬁcant
changes to both the AIC and ASC:
1. Extension of the credit through tax year 2009
2. Termination of the alternative incremental credit at the end of tax year
2008
3. Increase of the alternative simpliﬁed credit to 14 percent from 12 percent
for tax year 2009
IMPLICATIONS OF MAJOR CHANGES The AIC is being phased out and tax year
2008 will be the last year to use this method of calculation. This is good
news. The AIC has been a very confusing calculation method and could
only be used for the current year. One cannot go back and amend returns
to claim the AIC.
Even better, the alternative simpliﬁed credit is being increased from
12 percent to 14 percent, starting in tax year 2009. The ASC is much easier
to calculate and allows companies to use this method if they cannot calcu-
late their base percentage or if their base percentage is too high. So, many
companies will be transitioning to using the ASC in 2009.
By using the simpliﬁed credit calculation method, companies now will
be able to claim a credit equal to 12 percent of qualifying research expendi-
tures in excess of 50 percent of the average qualifying research expenditures
for the three tax years preceding the tax year for which the credit is being
determined.
If a company has no qualifying research expenditures in any one of the
three previous tax years, a credit of 6 percent of the current year’s qual-
iﬁed research expenditures may be claimed. It is important to note that,
in this case, the credit is based on 100 percent of the current year’s quali-
fying research expenditures and not on an increase in qualifying research
expenditures. This is not changed in the extension.
EXAMPLE OF INCREASE XYZ Company has $400,000, $600,000, $500,000,
and $800,000 of qualiﬁed research expenditures in its 2005–2008 tax years,
respectively. A research credit of $66,000 may be claimed on its 2008 tax
return calculated as follows:
12% × [$800, 000 − (50% × ($400, 000 + $600, 000 + $500, 000)/3)]
= $66, 000
Using 14 percent for 2009, the R&D tax credit would be $77,000, a 16.6
percent increase over $66,000.

---

## Page 234

Chapter = c17 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 3:16 pm
208 Cost Recovery
Although the ASC is easier to calculate, the substantiating document
requirements have been heightened recently and are as rigorous as the reg-
ular credit. More important, the resulting ASC is about 50 to 60 percent of
the regular credit. The increase to 14 percent, however, will narrow that gap.
Questions to Ask When Assessing the Opportunity
The following questions need to be asked to assess the opportunity for the
R&D tax credit at companies:
■ Has the business engaged in activities that may qualify for credits based
on the discussion presented here?
■ Is the business in an industry previously shown to achieve beneﬁts from
R&D credits, notably:
■ Manufacturing
■ Fabrication
■ Engineering
■ Software development
■ Architecture
■ Tool and die machine shops
■ Electronics
■ Biotechnology
■ Pharmaceutical
■ Food sciences and agribusiness
■ Has the business paid taxes for the previous three tax years and the
current year? Note: Sub-S corporations, partnerships, and sole propri-
etorships can pass through credits to the ultimate owners.
■ Has the business previously taken some R & D credits? Note: Incremental
beneﬁts may still be available.
Conclusion
Here are four suggestions when choosing a consulting ﬁrm to perform an
R&D tax credit study:
1. Choose a qualiﬁed engineering ﬁrm. Ask for the biographies of the
individuals who will be assigned to your account and conﬁrm that each
individual involved with your study is a credentialed engineer or an
intellectual property attorney with an engineering background.
2. Choose a ﬁrm that uses a comprehensive project-by-project appro-
ach. Determine whether the company will use a comprehensive

---

## Page 235

Chapter = c17 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 3:16 pm
Research and Development Tax Credits 209
project-by-project approach as well as establish a detailed nexus of
qualiﬁed research expenditures to qualiﬁed research activities. One of
the IRS’s biggest concerns is that some engineering reports were, in fact,
written by individuals with no engineering or scientiﬁc background
and that no nexus is established between a company’s qualifying
expenditures and qualifying activities. Their documentation should
directly connect the project to the employee and the estimated time
spent on that project to each of the years under engagement. It is the
most thorough methodology accepted by the IRS today.
3. Choose a ﬁrm that assumes the risk. To begin a project, most ﬁrms will
ask to bill by the hour (sometimes with a cap) or will offer a contract
based on a lump-sum ﬁxed fee. Some require an up-front payment.
This approach carries too much client-side risk because there are too
many variables that simply cannot be foreseen at the beginning of the
project. Choose a ﬁrm that is willing to assume most of that risk and,
for a nominal fee, will dimension those variables before offering a
contract by working with you to determine the following:
■ What your credits actually are likely to be, per year
■ What your exact use of those credits will be based on prior years’ taxes
■ What your base percent is and whether that affects your use
4. Choose a ﬁrm that includes audit review in their fee.Since the IRS has ele-
vated R&D studies to a Tier I issue, there is a greater chance for an audit
and the onus is on the taxpayer to substantiate the credits. It is imperative
that the consulting ﬁrm you choose be willing to accept the risk of an
audit should it occur and include the audit review as part of its fee. The
company would do this only if it felt conﬁdent enough that its numbers
were correct and its engineering report could withstand the scrutiny of
the IRS auditors. Otherwise, you have an open-ended situation in which
you will have to pay that ﬁrm or legal counsel on an hourly basis.

5. I.R.C. §41(d)(1)(C), (d)(3).
6. I.R.C. §41(d)(2).

CHAPTER 18
Strategic Sourcing
Overview
Strategic sourcing is a process that harnesses the skills of the supply indus-
try to optimize sustainable competitive advantage for the business and its
customers. The goal of the strategic sourcing process is to develop a secure
and responsive supply base capable of meeting current and future business
needs for quality, delivery, cost, quantity, technology, ﬂexibility, and ser-
vice. The sourcing process can be part of a business process outsourcing
(BPO) initiative, whereby sourcing functions are outsourced to an external
ﬁrm. This can be done in a complete fashion, where there is no internal
function, or in an incremental fashion, in which speciﬁc categories of spend
are selected for procurement outsourcing. Of particular interest is knowing
that service providers can be engaged on a fee basis or on a contingency
model, whereby the client company will pay only on the actual realized
savings achieved by reducing the price paid for goods and services, after
those savings have been realized.
Many procurement departments rely on ineffective, fragmented, and
largely manual procedures. They may also not have access to benchmark
data, including pricing and service-level agreements, for similar products or
services that are available in the marketplace. These deﬁciencies limit a pro-
curement department’s ability to leverage spending volumes, assess supply
market opportunities and costs, and drive continuous improvements in sup-
ply costs and performance. The simple fact is that procurement departments,
no matter how well managed, do not have the resources, bandwidth, or in-
house subject matter expertise in all spend categories. Because of this, any
organization can beneﬁt by the outsourcing of selected spend categories by
This chapter was written in collaboration with Source One Management Services,
LLC, (sourceoneinc.com) and Tom Rogers, CPA, with Alliance Cost Containment
(alliancecost.com).
210

---

## Page 237

Chapter = c18 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 11:28 am
Strategic Sourcing 211
which additional resources would assist in driving savings and increasing
operational performance.
A recent Aberdeen study 1 (November 2007) found that 27 percent of
companies surveyed had outsourced some procurement, 13 percent were
looking into it, but a disturbing 60 percent had no plans to outsource their
procurement function. Companies that have moved forward with strategic
sourcing are highlighted here in a number of case studies.
Case Study 18.1: $1.6 Million in Annual Savings for
Food Distribution Company
Facing strong pressure to increase proﬁts, a large food distribution com-
pany engaged Alliance Cost Containment (ACC) to help reduce its operating
expenses. With $400 million in revenue, 4,000 employees, and more than 50
locations across North America, this company faced signiﬁcant challenges
with its corporate cost containment efforts.
ACC was asked to review over $13 million in annual expenses and $3
million in projected capital spend. The results from ACC’s strategic sourc-
ing, negotiation, and implementation methodologies netted $1.6 million in
annual cost savings. In regard to maintenance and capital savings, the VP
of engineering said, “ACC managed to collect and compile a large amount
of disparate information about our ﬂeet in a short amount of time. It was
obvious ACC understood the ins and outs of the materials handling industry
because it wasn’t long before it came up with a strategy and implementation
plan to reduce our expenses with several initiatives.”
In addition to materials handling, maintenance, and capital, ACC deliv-
ered savings with outsourced payroll processing, express mail, packaging,
uniforms, mats, telecommunications, and ofﬁce supplies as follows:
■ MRO/capital, $960,000 in savings
■ Packaging, $270,000 in savings
■ Payroll processing, $170,000 in savings
■ Uniforms, $70,000 in savings
■ Ofﬁce supplies, $60,000 in savings
■ Express mail, $40,000 in savings
■ Telecom, $30,000 in savings
In addition to the signiﬁcant direct cost savings, the client consolidated
suppliers, eliminating 98 suppliers, as a result of this successful engagement
with ACC.
Source: Alliance Cost Containment, “Expense Reduction Analysis Firm Saves $1.6
Million for National Food Distribution Company,” Alliance Cost Containment press
release, October 29, 2008.

Case Study 18.2: Private College Saves More than
$200,000
Alliance Cost Containment’s (ACC) Atlanta, Georgia–based principals
recently completed a successful engagement with a prestigious private col-
lege located in the southeast. This client has multiple campuses and an
enrollment of more than 5,000 students. Following a thorough examination
of the college’s annual expense detail, and by working closely with the staff
of this college, ACC helped generate more than $200,000 in annual cost
savings from both incumbent and alternative suppliers.
The most signiﬁcant savings were achieved in the following areas:
■ $75,000 savings on food service supplies
■ $47,000 savings on ofﬁce supplies
■ $38,000 savings on printing
■ $15,000 savings on cleaning and janitorial products
The college’s vice president for business affairs and personnel was very
satisﬁed with the results of the engagement. “Our primary contact at ACC was
incredibly responsive. He helped us achieve signiﬁcant savings in areas at
our different campus locations across the state. He also assisted in identifying
areas where consolidating expense may be beneﬁcial to us. Plus, the entire
institution capitalized on ACC’s vendor buying power.”
With the help of ACC, the college made a top-down commitment to
reduce costs even if it entailed changing long-standing vendor relationships.
Source: Alliance Cost Containment, “Alliance Cost Containment’s Proprietary Cost
Saving Techniques Save over $200,000 for Private College,” Alliance Cost Contain-
ment press release, November 14, 2008.
Case Study 18.3: Multi-Project Strategic Sourcing
A $130 million global specialty coating company with locations in North
America, Europe, and Asia engaged Source One Management Services, LLC
to assist in the strategic sourcing of its indirect spend items. The com-
pany’s procurement department was already operating with above-average
sourcing practices but asked Source One to assist in areas where its internal
resources were constrained. Initially, the company identiﬁed target spend
categories, including less-than-load (LTL) freight, small parcel, telecommuni-
cations, and packaging. To help drive a best practices sourcing engagement,
the two organizations developed a collaborative and innovative approach
for each project category to obtain the overall best value for its procurement
dollar.

---

## Page 239

Chapter = c18 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 11:28 am
Strategic Sourcing 213
LTL Freight
To produce savings in less-than-load freight shipments, Source One and its
client compiled a shipping proﬁle and lane analysis using 18 months of ship-
ment data. By consolidating shipping to one national carrier to service all of
the company’s long-haul requirements and one primary regional carrier for
each location’s short-haul requirements, the company was able to produce
a 14 percent annual savings.
Small Parcel
The customer wished to retain its incumbent small parcel carrier, but also
wished to produce larger savings than in earlier years. To produce tangible
savings, Source One collaborated with the incumbent supplier. Together,
they did a thorough review of the client’s shipping characteristics. After
auditing, review, and analysis, the company changed its preferred shipping
methods for a particular product from an express shipment service to a
ground shipment service and was able to produce $170,000 in annual sav-
ings. Most important, the organization was able to maintain the same level
of service and delivery dates as its existing shipping processes.
Telecommunications
Before the start of the telecommunications project, the specialty coatings
company had already implemented advanced telecommunications technolo-
gies such as voice over Internet protocol (VoIP) at all of its locations. The
company had also already identiﬁed further network enhancements, which
made large savings opportunities even more difﬁcult to obtain. However,
Source One was engaged to assist in the implementation of the enhance-
ments because of a lack of available in-house resources. To produce further
results, Source One identiﬁed further technologies that were complementary
to the existing network infrastructure at place in the company. Source One
then assisted in the negotiation and implementation of the new MPLS tech-
nology. By implementing the existing VoIP over MPLS (Multiprotocol Label
Switching) and replacing the frame relay network, the company saved over
47 percent in its total telecommunications spend.
Packaging
The packaging project required a combination of several sourcing strategies
and heavy collaboration between Source One, the client, and the suppliers.
After data collection and interviews, the team focused on the functional
requirements for its corrugated packaging and the pallets used to transport
them. Through collaboration with suppliers, the Source One packaging team
and the client company’s engineering department, it was determined that the
client could shift to industry-standard-sized boxes, which would not only

---

## Page 240

Chapter = c18 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 11:28 am
214 Cost Recovery
lower its unit cost of boxes, but would allow them to purchase a standard-
sized pallet, which also had a lower unit cost.
To take the project one step further, Source One used WhyAbe.com
(whyabe.com). Launched in 2005, WhyAbe.com was developed to pro-
vide free procurement tools and promote collaboration between thousands
of buyers and suppliers in a global marketplace to conduct a request
for proposal (RFP). The RFP also incorporated other packaging supplies
and containers that complemented the company’s largest corrugated spend
items. WhyAbe.com allowed Source One to simultaneously invite multiple
suppliers to participate in the RFP, and the company was able to monitor the
incoming questions and bid responses in real time as Source One conducted
the event. Ultimately, pricing of the new corrugated packaging and pallets
reached the market ﬂoor, and the client was able to obtain annual savings
in excess of $200,000.
The customer reﬂected on the engagement, “Our corporation could
never have resourced this effort with available personnel. By outsourcing
portions of our procurement cycle, we achieved savings that would not
otherwise have been possible. In addition, we learned things about the mar-
ketplace and our own operations that make us better purchasers for future
efforts in other areas.”
Source: Source One Management Services, LLC, “Multi-Project Strategic Sourcing,”
Source One Management Services, LLC, Willow Grove, Penn., February 2009.
Case Study Wrap-Up
What Can Be Procured Using Strategic Sourcing?2
Virtually everything that is purchased by a company could beneﬁt from a
strategic sourcing process. In Exhibit 18.1, Source One Management Ser-
vices, LLC makes its best effort to provide a one-image summary of the
areas for strategic sourcing.
What Is the Sourcing Process?3
Purchasing managers and strategic sourcing professionals often follow a
consistent methodology when planning and conducting a sourcing initia-
tive. Having a process provides a framework that, when correctly applied,
can produce sustainable savings in a consistent manner. Creativity in the
application of the strategic sourcing process will have a dramatic effect on
the results achieved, regardless of whether a seven-step sourcing process
(explained here) or a customized internal sourcing process is used.
