1. Establish the project team and the internal benchmark. The benchmark
will set the foundation upon which the sourcing initiative will be built
and measured. Before the benchmark can be established, the project
team must be assembled. The project executive or sponsor should iden-
tify the best resources both internally and externally to work on the
initiative. The project sponsor must ask the following questions to begin
to develop the team:
■ Who within the core sourcing group is best equipped to work on this
initiative?
■ Who can be enlisted from other departments within the company to
participate in the cross-functional team?
■ Should additional resources be recruited from outside the company
(either consultants or business partners)?
■ What is the cost of applying these resources?
The internal benchmark for recurring purchases should objectively
deﬁne what has been done in the past (qualitatively and quantitatively)
and identify the implications to the spend category of future strategic
plans. At a minimum, the benchmark should identify what is being
sourced, from whom, pricing structure, service level requirements, and
future considerations. For new spends, the benchmark should identify

---

## Page 242

Chapter = c18 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 11:28 am
216 Cost Recovery
the purpose of the expenditure and have some preliminary proposals
or cost estimates as a basis for the spend category.
2. Market assessment. Traditionally, the next step in the strategic sourcing
initiative is to conduct a market assessment. Here, the sourcing team
must look at a number of factors in determining the status of the mar-
ketplace. The team must look at market conditions to determine the
feasibility of a sourcing initiative.
■ Recent market events such as natural disasters that cause supply dis-
ruptions may hinder the efforts of both domestic and global sourcing.
Commodity areas can be highly affected by these events. Suppliers
may be implementing force majeure clauses contained in contracts.
■ Currency ﬂuctuations can affect whether offshore sourcing is an attrac-
tive option at this time.
■ What are the competitors doing? Have competitors recently hit the
market in search of price relief or to try to lock up supply?
■ Have new technologies, regulations, or policies recently changed the
marketplace?
■ If now is not the optimum time to source, when will the initiative be
more successful?
3. Collect supplier information. After the initial market assessment, the
team should look to qualify as many suppliers as possible. Limiting the
potential supplier base can only limit the opportunity for a successful
initiative. The larger the potential supplier base, the greater the chance
to identify innovative opportunity and increased value.
After developing the ﬁrst-pass supplier list, the team should estab-
lish contact with each one to gauge its interest and viability in this
initiative. At this stage, most traditional sourcing processes use an RFI
process to develop a short list of suppliers. It is too early in the sourcing
cycle, however, to disqualify suppliers that want to participate. Dis-
qualifying suppliers at this time may cause one to miss a creative or
innovative idea that may lead to the modiﬁcation of the initial require-
ment or speciﬁcation. Potential suppliers must be thought of as part
of the sourcing team, and engaging as many as possible will help to
develop the most innovative and cost-effective strategy for the sourcing
initiative.
4. Develop sourcing strategy. The sourcing strategy should be developed
and approached as a ﬂuid, evolving process. Fine-tuning the sourcing
strategy will be required as the team interacts with the potential supplier
base through implementation and measuring results. Take note, how-
ever, that as market conditions ﬂuctuate, or the goals of the organization
change, the sourcing strategy may need to be revisited. A common mis-
take of strategic sourcing initiatives is to get stuck on the initial process
and strategy regardless of a complex or moving market. The team must

---

## Page 243

Chapter = c18 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 11:28 am
Strategic Sourcing 217
be prepared to revisit the strategy and reincorporate ideas that may have
been overlooked in a prior strategy.
One of the ﬁrst goals of developing the sourcing strategy is to
separate the requirements into minimum needs and maximum desires.
Minimum needs are those aspects of a potential supplier agreement
that must be met (that is, expedited shipping, company logo brand-
ing, speciﬁc payment options). If a supplier is unable to meet these
basic requirements but can provide some unique value, then consider
partnering them with a supplier that can meet the minimum require-
ments. Maximum desires are bonus features of an agreement that will
prove valuable to the company, but are not deal breakers. Establishing
these criteria and communicating them to the supplier will assist in the
qualiﬁcation process.
5. Solicit and evaluate bids.After identifying the sourcing strategy, it is time
to conduct the RFX (RFI, RFP, RFQ, and so on) process. The RFX docu-
ment should be supplier-friendly. One of the most common complaints
of suppliers is that complex requests for proposals do not allow them to
talk about the beneﬁts, ideas, opportunities, and competitive advantages
that they offer, and allow them to only submit a ﬁnancial response. A
straightforward and uncomplicated document will encourage suppliers
to participate and allow them to present their most competitive offer.
Furthermore, keeping the RFX open-ended gives suppliers the freedom
to present more creative solutions and introduce more fresh ideas into
the process.
During the RFX process, open communication is once again a key
element. Suppliers will often have questions about the RFX document
and its content. These questions need to be addressed so that the sup-
pliers can effectively respond to the RFX. The team should schedule
individual time with each supplier to review the document and ensure
that it completely understands the requirements and expectations. Once
the RFX responses are returned, each proposal must be evaluated. Cre-
ating an RFX rating matrix can be helpful in determining the value of
a proposal. A rating system will allow the organization to objectively
recognize both the quantitative and qualitative values of a proposal.
The soft-dollar value presented by a supplier can be just as important
as a bottom line cost reduction. The rating matrices will determine who
meets the minimum requirements and will prioritize and weight the
maximum desires outlined in the proposal.
6. Negotiate with and select suppliers. Negotiations with suppliers will
begin following the initial bid process. Many sourcing professionals see
the negotiation as an emotion-ﬁlled battle with the supplier. In most
negotiations, the best approach is to reduce or altogether remove emo-
tion from the negotiation, and instead rely on a fact-based objective

---

## Page 244

Chapter = c18 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 11:28 am
218 Cost Recovery
market picture for leverage with suppliers. With the proper market
research, a solid business case can be made to the supplier and will
drive them to a competitive offer. The team should look for creative
ways to motivate the supplier by making them aware of some of the
indirect ﬁnancial aspects of winning the business, such as capturing a
new market or taking business from a primary competitor.
7. Implement recommendations. The ﬁnal milestone of the sourcing initia-
tive is to implement the recommended plan of action with the suppliers.
The key to successfully rolling out the results of the initiative is to
exercise change management control. Effective change management
will ensure a smooth transition into the recommended actions, elimi-
nate encumbrances to new processes, and optimize the results of the
project.
The strategic sourcing process should not end at implementation.
Performance metrics should be put in place to ensure the positive results
of the initiative. Suppliers as well as internal processes should be audited
to ensure proper results well after the close of the implementation.
What if There Is Pushback?4
If there were no procurement department, there probably would be no push-
back from internal staff to completing a strategic sourcing initiative. Yet, it
is understandable that any outsourcing, even if for just a few categories of
spend, will lead to internal strife. Employees may question how outsourcing
makes them look as if they are not able to do their job themselves. The real-
ity, however, is that no procurement team can be effective at procuring all
categories of spend. Simply put, there are too many categories of spend in
an organization for a procurement team to be experts in every single cate-
gory. In many cases, a category may be sourced only once every two to three
years (such as in telecommunications) and it is unlikely that any individual in
procurement has the time or energy to closely follow the industry through-
out the contract life cycle. Only forward-thinking people and market leaders
quickly realize this and get help from experts in the other areas of spend.
Many procurement departments will shy away from strategic sourcing,
given the perceived time commitments. The reality is that little time is needed
to get this type of outsourcing off the ground, especially considering an
outsourcing procurement service provider’s inherent mission is to do the
bulk of the work for its clients. This needs to be communicated to nullify
this concern.
Another concern is that a strategic sourcing ﬁrm will hurt supplier rela-
tionships. In reality, the sourcing ﬁrms work with the supply base to improve
relationships between their clients and their client’s suppliers. They also
ensure a transparent relationship between the marketplace and supplier so

---

## Page 245

Chapter = c18 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 11:28 am
Strategic Sourcing 219
the client knows that it is receiving best-in-class pricing. Of special interest is
that many companies continue with the incumbent supplier after a sourcing
process. It is usually done, however, with lower prices or improved quality
of service.
The best way to get started with a strategic sourcing services ﬁrm is to
identify a few pilot categories. The categories may be one that the client
has already sourced (see if the ﬁrm can bring new intellectual capital or
creative ideas) or categories that the client has not had time to get to. The
pilot projects will provide a low impact way for the client and services ﬁrm
to develop a collaborative process and to see whether the relationship will
be productive for both parties.
How Do These Firms Follow through to Reap Savings?
What is not highlighted in the case studies but should be mentioned is that
some strategic sourcing ﬁrms will not stop upon striking the deal. Rather,
sourcing ﬁrms that work on a contingency model, earning their fees based
on the actual savings a company gets by paying less during the relationship
with the supplier, ensures that the sourcing ﬁrm has the incentive to make
sure the savings are realized by their clients.
What may not be apparent here is that while many companies sign
fantastic price deals with suppliers, many never obtain those savings because
they do not have the internal resources to implement and drive compliance.
For example, a corporate ofﬁce may sign a new ofﬁce supply contract with
average savings of 20 percent but if the decentralized ofﬁces do not use
this new ofﬁce supply agreement, given that it is not convenient or they
simply want to get product from the old vendor, all savings are lost. In these
instances, the strategic sourcing ﬁrm will assist in the implementation of the
new agreement, monitor compliance, identify outside supplier activity, and
work with the decentralized department or ofﬁce to explain the beneﬁts of
working with the new supplier or agreement.
Recoveries to Find
While it is possible to look backward and recover savings from past con-
tracts, many strategic sourcing initiatives mainly focus on future spend
activity to generate savings. Savings from improved sourcing and group
purchasing agreements that some ﬁrms can bring to the negotiating table
take the form of:
■ Improved unit pricing
■ Higher discounts off standard pricing

---

## Page 246

Chapter = c18 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 11:28 am
220 Cost Recovery
■ Volume incentives for single sourcing
■ Transition incentives for switching suppliers
Exhibit 18.2 summarizes the representative savings across a variety of
spend categories.
A not so uncommon occurrence is for a large price variance to be found
with some suppliers. While this could be due to a lack of knowledge of that
category of spend, it also can be due to malfeasance. Company employees
may purposely establish bad deals with suppliers so they can obtain side
beneﬁts such as kickbacks, gifts, or other networking opportunities. By hav-
ing a consistent and independent sourcing process in place, there is less of
a chance of such conﬂicts, especially given the fact that the strategic sourc-
ing ﬁrm will monitor pricing on a monthly basis and quickly identify such
anomalies.
Additional beneﬁts of a strategic sourcing process aside from improved
pricing and minimizing conﬂicts of interest include:

■ Aggregated spends by commodity and category
■ Current contract costs, terms, and conditions
■ Fixed and variable cost drivers for total cost of ownership
■ Product speciﬁcation, including quality, availability, and service
requirements

and delivery time performance
■ Development and management of reverse auction events
■ Bid analysis and score-carding of suppliers
■ Ongoing audit and continuous improvement monitoring
Questions to Ask When Assessing the Opportunity
Taking into consideration the case study experiences and the types of sav-
ings, the following is a list of questions that can help frame the opportunity
size of a strategic sourcing initiative:
■ Is purchasing centralized or decentralized?
■ Is contract management centralized or decentralized?
■ How well does the company track data on what it buys?
■ Does the company have long-term supplier relationships that haven’t
been analyzed in a while?
■ Who is spending within the organization?
■ What is the spend beyond the top few categories (that usually represent
direct spend categories)?
■ How does the company know if it has multiple suppliers providing the
same items and services at different price points?
■ What internal and external data were used, and how were the data
obtained when the last contract was negotiated?
■ What different regions, ofﬁces, and divisions are procuring the same
goods?
■ How does the company ensure suppliers are billing them accurately and
consistently for agreed-upon price levels?
■ How do the prices the company is paying compare to other companies
of similar size?
Conclusion
For the most part, no procurement department can be good at sourcing
everything and hence can beneﬁt from engaging a third-party strategic
sourcing ﬁrm. Furthermore, many small-to-midsize companies have no

---

## Page 248

Chapter = c18 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 11:28 am
222 Cost Recovery
procurement department and rely on individual line managers to negotiate
when they may have no formal training in this regard. Lastly, strategic sourc-
ing ﬁrms bring a host of other beneﬁts, including supplier identiﬁcation,
market research, RFP and reverse auction processes, supplier management,
and compliance monitoring to the table, which can usually be given under
a contingency model and, hence, are paid from the savings reaped by these
ﬁrms.

Chapter = c19 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 3:24 pm
CHAPTER 19
Telecommunications Auditing
Overview
Business phone bills range in size and complexity. A company may have
only one type of service billed, or a bundle of different services all on
one bill. The services include local service, long distance, wireless, Internet
access, wide area data networks, other miscellaneous circuits, and equip-
ment rental and maintenance. Whether a company reviews its services itself
or a professional auditing ﬁrm is hired, all businesses should review and
understand the charges on their phone bills. Businesses that have multiple
locations and multiple service providers will ﬁnd it more difﬁcult to review
and understand these bills.
Cooper and Associates, a professional telecommunications auditing ﬁrm
since 1987, says 78 percent of all telecommunication bills can be reduced.
Keep in mind that the 78 percent reduction does not necessarily mean it
is the phone company’s fault. An unused line, for example, is when the
company is simply at fault in not properly managing its own telephone
assets. This is not to say that the telephone company is not at fault. Cooper
and Associates also ﬁnds that 49 percent of these same bills have erroneous
charges from the telephone company that should be corrected and refunded.
These take the form of an overbilled mileage on a circuit, fraudulent slam-
ming charges, or incorrect long-distance rates versus contracted rates. There
are many examples of reductions, and the most common are described in
the case studies that follow.
This chapter was written in collaboration with Jeff Cooper at Cooper and Asso-
ciates (cooperandassoc.com).
223

---

## Page 250

Chapter = c19 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 3:24 pm
224 Cost Recovery
Case Study 19.1: Refund of Local Service Charges at
a Transportation Company
A transportation company renewed its local service that was terminating on
PRI T1s to a promotional rate that was $2,463 a month less than its current
rates. A PRI T1 is a common type of local service access that allows for
enhanced features and is usually ordered under a contract. This renewal
was done 36 months before the audit, although during the audit, it was
discovered that the rates were never reduced. A refund of $106,242 was
obtained, which included interest. The important lesson here is to audit
all contracts and the bills generated from these contracts before renewing
them.
In fairness to the current administration at the time of the audit, the
original contract that was never implemented was approved by a team that
was no longer there. The new management team found no copy of the
contract and had no idea of the overbilling. Without the audit, it’s doubtful
the overbilling would have been discovered because a new contract was
being considered when the audit began and there was no mention of the
overbilled charges during the negotiation process.
Case Study 19.2: Future Savings of Local Service
Charges at a Multi-Location Retail Chain
The retail chain business has 272 stores nationwide. Each store has a stan-
dard number of known lines: three to ﬁve for voice, one for fax, one for
credit card modem, and one for the alarm system. In many cases, there were
at least three other lines being billed that rang no answer. Before Cooper and
Associates were hired, the customer began disconnecting these no-answer
lines only to ﬁnd that some stores had a second alarm line or a second
modem line. The disconnection of these lines caused problems and they
had to be reinstalled.
The lesson here is that it is very important to trace each and every
unknown line to ensure it isn’t being used before disconnecting anything.
There are contingency plans to enact if the lines just cannot be traced.
They include checking bills for usage, running trafﬁc studies, and tem-
porarily disconnecting them for a week. In the eventual audit, Cooper
and Associates tested lines properly and did ﬁnd many unused lines for
closure.

---

## Page 251

Chapter = c19 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 3:24 pm
Telecommunications Auditing 225
Case Study 19.3: California School District
One of Overcharge Recovery Group’s (ORGs) clients is a K–12 school district
in Southern California. This client has a full staff of seasoned people who
diligently review its telecom invoices each month. After much deliberation
and negotiation, this client chose to free up its precious human resources
from this time-consuming and often frustrating project.
The school district recently received over $30,000 in a refund after Over-
charge Recovery Group uncovered several very unusual billing errors. The
auditor noticed that several services detailed in the Southwestern Bell Com-
pany (now AT&T) Customer Service Record were not receiving the entitled
federal e-Rate or California Teleconnect Fund discounts. K–12 school dis-
tricts qualify for signiﬁcant rebates on telecommunications services from the
federal and state governments. The application process can be very complex,
and discounts are often not correctly applied. When the auditor submitted a
claim for investigation, the telephone company’s staffers were stumped. No
one could provide an answer as to why these services weren’t getting the
discounts.
The auditor continued to dig, asking what these services were, and
surprisingly, the telephone company’s people couldn’t answer. After many
months, much research, and many phone calls, ORG found out that the
services in question had been obsolete since 1998. The telephone company
had not offered them for over 10 years. The charges had remained on the
school district’s bill even though those services had not been needed or
used since then. Unfortunately for this client, due to statutes of limitations,
many thousands of dollars could no longer be recovered. The lesson here is
to not only review the telephone company invoices but to do so in a timely
manner or such discounts may not be refunded by the telephone company.
Case Study 19.4: Future Savings of Wireless Charges
at a Media Company
The media company has six distinct regions, each with multiple ofﬁces.
Combined, there were just over 800 wireless phones. Each region had its
own plans that were negotiated separately, either by the ofﬁce manager or
the employee who personally used the phone.
In addition to the inefﬁciencies overall by not pooling the usage from
these regions together, each of the individual plans could have been much
improved. Cooper and Associates found charges for services that would
have been free or at a much reduced cost if new plans were implemented.

---

## Page 252

Chapter = c19 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 3:24 pm
226 Cost Recovery
The combined annual savings from consolidating and improving the plans
came to just under $500,000.
Case Study Wrap-Up
Too many services. The types of bills and services most commonly audited
include the following seven:
1. Local telephone service
2. Long-distance service
3. Wireless service
4. Internet access service
5. Data/WAN network services like MPLS and frame relay
6. Other circuit services such as point-to-point tie lines, off-premises exten-
sions, ring downs, and special access
7. Voice and data equipment services, usually in the form of maintenance
contracts
All of these services could potentially have different vendors, each send-
ing its own invoice. Each vendor could also be sending multiple invoices
for its services. Any business with multiple locations could then get another
set of invoices for each location. Maybe the most difﬁcult part of any audit
is making sure you get all bills received in one month copied. Businesses
with multiple locations normally allow their offsite personnel to order their
own services, which may create new invoices. The billing addresses may
be different, which ends up causing many of the bills to be sent to different
places. The bottom line is that it is very difﬁcult to keep up with what is
being billed every month even when the invoices are clean. But if they’ve
never been reviewed or audited, it is then even more difﬁcult. Like the
lottery, “You have to be in it to win it.”
Changing rates, people, and places. One thing that would help busi-
nesses control their telecom expenses would be to have exit interviews
with the IT and telecom department for all terminated employees. During
this interview, one would ask what that person’s cell phone number was,
voice mail extension, any private lines or modem lines, and so forth. Then
all appropriate disconnect orders could be issued accordingly. But let’s face
it, these things don’t normally happen. That’s why companies continually
pay for services that are no longer used.
In addition to the waste that occurs on the user end, there are many
things that occur on the carrier end that also causes waste. New promotions
and lower rates are always coming out. If the internal company team isn’t

---

## Page 253

Chapter = c19 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 3:24 pm
Telecommunications Auditing 227
proactively looking for these programs, it is not doing its job. This is espe-
cially true with wireless service, where new plans are constantly coming
out. Sure, one may end up starting the term plan over again, but if there is
no intention of changing carriers, then renewing for better rates before the
contract is up won’t harm the organization.
Phone bills are confusing. Phone bills are getting more complex, not
less. Competitive carriers usually don’t spend as much on billing software
as they should. The end result is a bill that does not properly break down the
monthly charges. Even the major carriers don’t do a good job of this, which is
why all audits should be done using the customer service records (CSRs) as
well as the monthly bills. In addition to the confusing nature of most telecom
charges, the taxes and surcharges are getting more complex as well. Many
of these are not mandatory passthroughs, despite what the carriers tell their
customers. Removing or reducing these optional surcharges is important,
because not only does the company save the cost of the surcharges, but
the tax associated with the surcharges, too. That’s right, as impossible as it
sounds, taxes do apply to the surcharges.
Knowing the market. Hiring an outside auditor to review these costs
could provide an advantage over doing it yourself. Much of the simple stuff
is very easy to ﬁnd. But the more complex items will be better served by
having the audit done by a company that specializes in the industry and sees
company plans across the nation. This is especially true if multiple locations
exist in many different cities.
Making the time. Besides the experience factor, the auditor isn’t dis-
tracted by the day-to-day organizational baggage experienced by the average
IT and telecom professional. It is very difﬁcult to audit telecom bills when
one has to stop and start all the time. Obviously, if the internal telecom audit
function is eliminated, then no review for savings will ever be done by the
company.
Recoveries to Find
With the case studies in mind, the following is a list of example recoveries
to be found on bills for the various services.
Local Telephone Service
■ Unused lines that were once for modems, faxes, private lines, and so
on
■ Too many telephone system trunks for the current business climate
■ No term plans in place
■ Overbilled certain surcharges

---

## Page 254

Chapter = c19 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 3:24 pm
228 Cost Recovery
■ Actual rate of services is higher than the contracted rate
■ Paying for services that were never installed
■ Local service plans with ﬂat-rate usage that should be changed to mea-
sured usage to save money
■ Overbilled installation charges on work orders
■ Slamming and cramming charges being billed
As to the last bullet point, slamming is the unauthorized change of the
long-distance provider of a local phone service customer. For example,
a telephone company representative tells her ofﬁce to change the long-
distance service for XYZ Company. Perhaps there is a forged signature or
fake voice print. Then the telephone company sends an electronic request
to the local service carrier telling them to change XYZ’s long-distance ser-
vice to its network. No one at XYZ approved it, but the change is made
nonetheless.
Cramming, the second part of the last bullet point, is more prevalent
than slamming. This is a terrible problem with an easy ﬁx, if anyone really
cared to ﬁx it. There are dozens of companies that have agreements with
large telephone companies. They can add charges to any phone bill, if they
have authority from the end user. The deﬁnition of authority is quite loose
and leads to an opportunity for overcharges.
Then, these dozen or so companies have contracts with hundreds of
smaller companies that provide the selling of these services. Services like
Internet listings, voice mail, calling cards, Web hosting, and dial-up Internet
accounts. These hundreds of smaller companies have thousands of agents
cold-calling unsuspecting customers to get the orders for these services. In 21
years of auditing bills, Cooper and Associates have seen thousands of these
slamming and cramming charges and not one of them has been legitimate.
Long-Distance Service
■ Actual rates of services is higher than the contracted rate
■ No term plans in place
■ Usage is high enough to justify dedicated long-distance access trunks.
Long-distance is less per minute when you bypass the local service lines.
So, if a company has enough usage, it can install long-distance–only
lines and the cost per minute for these calls is much less than if those
same calls were made over local lines.
■ Overbilled certain surcharges and taxes
■ Local toll could be changed to free wide area calling plans
■ Paying for usage from lines that aren’t related to the company
■ Paying monthly fees for services long since disconnected

---

## Page 255

Chapter = c19 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 3:24 pm
Telecommunications Auditing 229
■ Incorrectly programmed telephone system routing free calls over long-
distance access trunks
As to the last bullet point, some customers’ phone systems are
programmed to automatically route long-distance trafﬁc over dedicated long-
distance lines. Then, local calls are programmed to route over the local
service lines. But what happens if a local call routes over these long-distance
lines? The company pays per minute using long-distance rates, instead of
paying local usage rates.
Wireless Service
■ Actual rate of some services is higher than the contracted rate
■ No term plans in place
■ High cost of optional features better served by feature plans
■ Paying for unused phones
■ No proper pool plan in place
As to the last bullet point, a customer with 1,000 phones has various
usage plans on each phone individually. Some users go over, but many are
under. Or way under. If you pool all 1,000 users into a shared pool plan, then
the excess of one phone can be taken up by the underuse of another phone.
Internet Access, Data/WAN, and Other Circuit Services
■ Overbilled mileage
■ Overbilled surcharges
■ Actual rate of services is higher than the contracted rate
■ No term plan in place
■ Service could be replaced by less expensive and similar working service
■ Paying for optional features that aren’t being used
Voice and data equipment is a type of phone system for larger businesses
that don’t have one line per button like most small systems. The incoming
lines are pooled so that each call comes in to a route and is processed
through live bodies or voice mails to get to their destination. This is called
switching a call and is a feature of a PBX. The opposite is a phone system
that usually has an operator answering and then telling the paging system
to have the user pick up Line 1.
■ Paying for maintenance on more ports than are being used
■ Equipment lease billing for insurance in error
■ Equipment lease billing for equipment never installed

---

## Page 256

Chapter = c19 Date: June 22, 2009 Time: 3:24 pm
230 Cost Recovery
Other Recovery-Related Beneﬁts
■ Audits can provide an inventory of existing equipment, circuits, or ven-
dor accounts
■ Audits can provide a baseline for future billing management processes
■ Audits can show how to improve controls on the purchasing and bill-
paying process
Questions to Ask
Taking into consideration the case study experiences and the types of recov-
ery, the following is a list of questions that can help determine the value of
a telecom audit:
■ What is the total monthly spend for telecommunication expense includ-
ing all services? Keep in mind that an audit can reduce future expenses
by 15 percent.
■ Does the company get multiple bills or have multiple locations?
■ What department is in charge of negotiating all contracts and ensuring
that contracts are followed?
■ What billing issues have been identiﬁed by internal company personnel?
■ Is it too time consuming for the company to review every one of the
telecommunication bills?
■ Does the company’s long-distance provider send bills without summary
pages?
■ Is it difﬁcult from the bill to determine what the costs are per minute?
■ When was the last review of long-distance rates?
■ Has someone reviewed the bills to verify all surtaxes and surcharges are
correct?
■ Did someone review the application of sales tax on certain long-distance
calls?
Conclusion
As the author has difﬁculty understanding his own monthly cell phone bill,
it should not be hard to see that a company’s telecom expense becomes a
massive extrapolation of this simple reconciling event. With telecommunica-
tion plans changing more than daily, advancements in technology leading
to improved service at reduced cost, employees who tend to simply pur-
chase services and phones at will, and the complexity of resulting contracts,
companies owe it to themselves to review their telecom plans.

## Page 257

Chapter = c20 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 2:44 pm
CHAPTER 20
Travel and Entertainment Reviews
Overview
Travel and entertainment (T&E) expenses are the second-largest control-
lable expense for most organizations. 1 Regardless of the size, it is more
controllable than other costs, given that T&E processing takes place mostly
within accounts payable departments. A recent survey byAccounts Payable
Now & Tomorrow found that 75.7 percent of companies process T&E
through accounts payable.
2 Through improved controls, automation, and
enhanced negotiation, companies can recover funds from some past errors
and fraud processed through T&E expense handling and reap large future
savings.
The one impediment to holding back an effective T&E improvement
effort is changing corporate culture. Many companies view T&E as a com-
pany beneﬁt, a perk, and do not want to touch the sacred cow. This is
especially the case, given that top executives are usually the worst offend-
ers at mismanaging their T&E to their own beneﬁt; so if the tone at the top
is not at the company in changing these procedures, this is an area that may
not change as much as originally hoped.
Regardless of corporate culture, there are still many beneﬁts that anyone
can’t deny because there would be no impact on them. Such beneﬁts include
improved pricing and service for using preferred vendors, automation to
make it easier on submitters of expense reports, and corporate Web sites to
provide additional services to company employees.
This chapter was written with many references to Travel and Entertainment Best
Practices as authored by Mary S. Schaeffer in collaboration with Expensewatch
(expensewatch.com).
231

---

## Page 258

Chapter = c20 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 2:44 pm
232 Cost Recovery
Case Studies 20.1: How to Make $20,000 in One Year,
Tax-Free: Speciﬁc Schemes
The author was unable to ﬁnd any companies willing to go on record with
their T&E issues that lead to cost recoveries and future savings given the
personal nature of the occurrences. No matter though; as a useful guide,
How to Pad Your Expense Report ... and Get Away with It by Employee X
provides many useful cases of how employees could initiate a variety of
fraudulent schemes to receive inappropriate payments from companies.
The book explains how an employee could pad an expense report,
ever so slightly at times, with the belief that company management will
not be clever enough to detect the wrongdoing. For an avid traveler, the
following additional padding could easily be added to an annual expense
report without anyone noticing. Here, a total of $14,600 after-tax padding
could be created ($20,000 before tax) on 20 business trips:
■ Meals ($30 a day x 4 days x 20 trips) = $2,400
■ Gas and mileage ($20 per trip x 20 trips) = $400
■ Airline ($100 per trip x 20 trips) = $2,000
■ Hotel ($10 per day x 4 days x 20 trips) = $800
■ Entertainment ($50 per trip x 20 trips) = $1,000
■ Other ($50 per trip x 20 trips) = $1,000
■ Airline and other miles for free tickets = $7,000
To create the additional expenses, the employee could follow one of
the many approaches detailed in the book:
■ Book a personal trip as a business one.
■ Book an expensive refundable ticket as well as book a cheaper ticket.
Then, cancel the refundable ticket while submitting it for payment as it
would be for more money than actually paid by the employee for the
nonrefundable ticket.
■ Scan hotel or car rental invoice and increase the amounts using photo-
editing tools.
■ Get extra invoice paper from hotel and type up a phony bill.
■ Complain to reverse charges at a hotel or to apply discount coupons,
get a new reduced invoice, yet submit the original inﬂated invoice.
■ Edit e-mail invoices received from airlines to create inﬂated values.
■ Buy tear-off receipts at ofﬁce supply store and submit phony receipts
for meals.
Source: Employee X, How to Pad Your Expense Report ... And Get Away with It
(Lubbock, Texas: Easy Money Press, 2003).

---

## Page 259

Chapter = c20 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 2:44 pm
Travel and Entertainment Reviews 233
Case Study Wrap-Up
Fraud Seems to Be a Main Focus
When reviewing T&E expenses, companies tend to see fraud as the main
culprit in leaking proﬁts. There are many other areas for savings outlined in
the following, yet this is a valid concern. A recent study by the Association of
Certiﬁed Fraud Examiners (ACFE) identiﬁed Expense Reimbursement fraud
to be the sixth most likely fraud type out of all occupational fraud. (See
Exhibit 20.1.)
Expense reimbursement fraud can be grouped into the following cate-
gories, as deﬁned by the ACFE, and almost all of which were highlighted in
the case study:
■ Mischaracterized—personal expenses that are submitted as business
■ Overstated—inappropriate overstatement of submitted expenses
■ Fictitious receipt—phony receipt used as support
■ Overpurchase—purchase more than needed and keep the rest
■ Collusion—manager and employee scheme the company
Many employees will commit fraud but not completely see their actions
as fraud because they will rationalize that their behavior is to be expected,
given their position at the company. This goes back to a corporate culture
that may accept an executive posting $24.99 a day in miscellaneous expenses
because there is no need for a receipt and he views it as a way to pay for
his daily incidental costs (which may be personal in nature) that he expects
to be repaid for, given his position at the company.

Improved Policies and Compliance Management
Looking at these different schemes, one may wonder where the company’s
T&E policies and compliance management went wrong. One may also
ﬁnd that T&E policies are nonexistent, loosely followed, or not reviewed
against actual expense submissions for compliance. In a recent Accounts
Payable Now & Tomorrow study, 57 percent of those responding indi-
cated they had changed their T&E policies because of the passage of the
Sarbanes-Oxley Act. Sarbanes-Oxley has served as a wakeup call to organi-
zations everywhere that they need to clean up their act when it comes to
T&E.
3
A useful source for best practice T&E policies includes Mary S. Schaef-
fer’s guide on Travel and Entertainment Best Practices (John Wiley & Sons,
as well as on the Internet). A simple search on Google can lead to a host
of sample travel policies from companies and governmental agencies that
can be tailored for the organization. Focus should be placed on expenses
for valid business purposes, disallowed purchases (i.e., liquor, hotel movies,
and so on), submitting documentation, and preferred vendors, if any.
Policies are useful, but it is policy compliance that really saves orga-
nizations. The best written travel policy is worthless if it can be broken
every minute by employees without any repercussions. Most companies
will use a spot-checking approach, which tends to focus on employees or
departments with past issues with T&E policy compliance. This is probably
the best approach, given the magnitude of T&E processing. It is suggested,
though, to avoid uneven policy compliance reviews, that data analysis (see
the following section) be used to highlight top reports for review. Further-
more, to round out the review population, a sprinkling of random selections
and phone calls to employees about T&E expenses ensures appropriate T&E
expense review coverage as well as keeps employees on their toes knowing
that the company is watching.
Probably the best way to ensure policy compliance is through the use of
employee training. Like any good fraud prevention program, if employees
are not trained in what is wrong, they will simply not know when policies
are broken. Training focus should be placed in accounts payable but, more
important, in line management. Those employees who review and approve
T&E submissions need to know what is against policy and how to spot
trends that may be fraudulent. One needs to remember that 99 percent of
people are honest and submitting accurate expenses, so work needs to be
done to identify the needles in the haystack who are not complying with
company policies. A neighborhood watch–type program bolstered by an
effective training program is an effective approach to ensure malevolent
trends are spotted quickly.

---

## Page 261

Chapter = c20 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 2:44 pm
Travel and Entertainment Reviews 235
Corporate Card Use Can Prevent Many Frauds and Help Track Activity
From the case study, it may not be apparent, yet expenses were booked by
the employee using his own credit card or other payment method. Many
employees like to use their own credit cards because they rack up frequent-
ﬂier miles or other goodies based on the dollar amounts put on the cards. Of
course, if this is in the employee’s motivation, he or she might be tempted
to charge something that is not actually business-related.
4 It also provides
employees with an ability to hide their tracks if they decide to commit T&E
fraud.
Corporate cards are the perceived answer, although given the
employee’s motivation noted earlier, companies are concerned about hand-
ing over credit cards to all of their employees. Companies need to release
that fear, given one of the top ways to defraud a company is for an employee
to put through fraudulent invoices and mask the activity by never showing
a payment for the service. Another approach is for the employee to book
an expense (e.g., airfare) and then cancel it to get a credit from the vendor,
but if this refund is never seen by the company, it will pay the employee
based on what it did see (the expense for the bogus airfare). These schemes
would be unmasked if a reconciliation was performed between submitted
expenses and credit card activity, which can be done if the company has
access to the credit card.
While there is no best approach to implementing corporate cards and
many options, probably the safest bet is for the employee to pay the bill, yet
have the company have access to the card for review of the activity. That
way, the employee is not relieved from her responsibility regarding card
usage, given that the employee still pays the bill yet the company has full
access to download, analyze, and otherwise review the activity to ensure
that the activity matches what was ultimately reimbursed to the employee.
It should be noted to the employee that the card is only to be used for
corporate business expenses, which cuts down on weeding out personal
expenses in the reconciliation process. Also note that all points associated
with card usage can be redeemed by the company for hotel, airfare, and
other company beneﬁts that relate to the entity ultimately paying the bill
(the company) rather than the employee.
Improved Purchasing Tools Bring Risks and Rewards
While not discussed in the case studies, the Internet has not only made it
simpler to have employees book their own ﬂights, it has also given them the
tool needed to shop for less-expensive options.
5 While the use of the Inter-
net improves the efﬁciency for the employee, it also opens up the potential
for fraud. Where a travel agent would have once been used to control

---

## Page 262

Chapter = c20 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 2:44 pm
236 Cost Recovery
purchases by providing independence to the process, the employee is now
in control.
A good middle ground may be in Internet-based reporting management
services that still allow the employee to book his travel, yet provide controls
such as travel program management reporting and group-purchasing bene-
ﬁts. For two examples, see Expedia’s service at egencia.com or Travelocity’s
at travelocitybusiness.com. Programs are customized to clients based on only
the services they desire, rather than a full-service agent. Services include:
■ Use compliance policies and preferred suppliers (thereby incorporating
any discounts seamlessly)
■ Allow program management reporting to forecast travel spend
■ Manage employee travel schedules with Web-based tools
■ Provide 24/7 traveler support at no extra charge
■ Manage unused tickets and airline credits online
■ Direct deposit reimbursements to employee bank accounts through
payroll or accounts payable processes
Automation Beneﬁts
Similar to using the Internet, basic automation of the T&E process can bring
a host of additional beneﬁts, which include:
■ Automate enforcement of company ﬁnancial policy
■ Integrate with corporate card programs to seamlessly report and manage
T&E expense activity
■ Aggregate data for negotiating and budgeting
■ Establish a system of approvals
■ Reduce errors in expense processing due to data ﬁelds being checked
for accuracy and completeness prior to submission
■ Increase visibility to the operation of internal controls
Companies like ExpenseWatch (expensewatch.com) and Concur Tech-
nologies (concur.com) offer such automated tools. As an example,
ExpenseWatch.com is a complete Web-based subscription service that
gives small-to-midsized companies comprehensive tools to control and
reduce operating expenses associated with T&E processing. The software-
as-a-service subscription model allows small-to-midsized companies to
affordably control all operating expenses for the ﬁrst time. The subscription-
based pricing includes implementation, training, support, and upgrades.
There are no additional hardware or software costs and no hidden charges.
Because of the low cost of entry point afforded by the on-demand platform,

---

## Page 263

Chapter = c20 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 2:44 pm
Travel and Entertainment Reviews 237
the risks to organizations are minimized if the automated system does not
live up to their expectations or can’t be implemented effectively.6
ExpenseWatch.com controls and reduces travel and entertainment
expenses and streamlines expense reporting across the entire organization.
The online expense reporting system provides easy expense report creation,
robust credit card management, automatic approval routing and escalation,
visibility into spending, and travel expense analysis and reporting.
Data Analysis Technology to Review Spend and Padding
One of the top beneﬁts of automating the T&E process (if a tool like
ExpenseWatch or even Microsoft Excel is used) is that it can lead to a
database of detailed T&E expense information. Most organizations process
T&E expenses through accounts payable, which then posts to the general
ledger at a summary level. Therefore, most companies post the summary air-
fare, lodging, car rental, meals, and so on by the employee’s T&E report (e.g.,
$922 to the airfare general ledger expense account, $270 to the meals gen-
eral ledger accounts, and so on). By posting at this summary level, all details
associated with the travel (daily cost, hotel used, airfare used, employee
name, and so on) are lost.
If an automated approach is taken, a detailed database results that can
be trended in the various detailed categories of spend. Trend summary anal-
ysis by service provider, employee, date of travel, and destinations are all
available options. Such analysis can be used to highlight employees with
unusual increasing trends and extract speciﬁc fraudulent events, but prob-
ably most important, to negotiate better deals with travel service providers.
By knowing the spend activity, a company can improve negotiations for
increased savings.
Recoveries to Find
Areas in which issues can occur include the following, which are brieﬂy
explained, and many of which are supported by the case studies cited in
this chapter:
■ Improved vendor negotiations. Aggregated data for airline, hotel, car
rental, and credit card issuer can be used to negotiate preferred vendor
discounts and other rebates.
■ Corporate credit card points and discounts. Rather than have employees
beneﬁt on their personal cards for such points, the company can increase
its own account of points for later redemption.

---

## Page 264

Chapter = c20 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 2:44 pm
238 Cost Recovery
■ Reduced errors and fraud in T&E payments. Through automation and
improved reviews for policy compliance, companies can reduce issues,
whether or not intentional.
■ Administrative efﬁciencies. PayStream Advisors report that companies
can expect to reduce the cost of processing an expense report by as
much as $35 per report through automation.
7 Companies are now, more
than ever, turning this paper-based process into a seamlessly integrated
automated process of review and approvals.
■ Reduced audit fees. By demonstrating a well-managed process around
T&E expenses, companies show diligence in managing regulatory com-
pliance. External audit fees, in turn, can be reduced because controls
associated with the process can be relied upon rather than having the
auditor perform additional substantive testing.
One other side beneﬁt is that employee satisfaction should be raised by
clearly communicating company expectations, reducing the time to com-
plete an expense report, getting reimbursed more quickly for expenses, and
providing them additional travel services through the Internet.
Questions to Ask When Assessing the Opportunity
Taking into consideration the case study experiences and the types of recov-
ery, the following is a list of questions that can help frame the opportunity
size of a travel and entertainment review:
■ Does the company consider T&E a company beneﬁt and hence is not
willing to take the tough steps to improve compliance with a industry-
accepted travel policy?
■ Will the company be able to ensure even compliance with the travel
policy by ensuring that all employees, including top executives, comply
with the program?
■ Does the company have a formal policy statement for T&E?
■ How were the policies developed? Were best practices or a benchmark-
ing exercise used in the policy development?
■ Have the T&E policies become outdated? When was the last time they
were updated?
■ What process is in place to ensure T&E policy compliance?
■ How is T&E documentation managed? Is it imaged for easier retrieval
and review?
■ Is the company using an automated system to manage the T&E process?
■ What ensures that employees use preferred vendors and associated
corporate cards, if any?

---

## Page 265

Chapter = c20 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 2:44 pm
Travel and Entertainment Reviews 239
■ How are data associated with T&E expenses analyzed? How often are
these analyses completed by the company?
■ When was the last vendor negotiation for a group purchase of airline,
lodging, car rental, or credit card issuance?
Conclusion
Once a company agrees to get beyond its corporate culture and run its
T&E process in an arm’s-length fashion, it can begin to identify savings
through improved policy compliance, automation, and vendor negotiations.
What was once considered to be a laborious process for the employee can
now become a seamlessly integrated process of automated approvals and
repayment to the employee for past expense activity.

CHAPTER 21
Utility Reviews
Overview
Businesses are ﬁnding that their utility spending continues to increase dra-
matically throughout the United States. Many markets struggle with a limited
number of providers, as deregulated options have shrunk in a challeng-
ing economy with regulations that do not always encourage competition.
Smaller companies may also have only a few options in pricing plans from
their vendors. It seems that the utility area can sometimes be limited in
its opportunities although it is generally one of the highest expenses for
companies. But there is good news and there are still some opportunities
for those that are diligent and will research what is available. As vendors
do not always have an objective view of what is best for the company they
serve, it is in the best interest of most companies to review options indepen-
dent of their vendors’ advice and to seek objective third-party representation
when possible to assist them in navigating the myriad choices in the utility
world.
This chapter focuses on some of the savings opportunities that relate
to the rules, rates, and regulations that govern the utilities. Utility Reduction
Analysts (URA), a ﬁrm working in this ﬁeld, has found many interesting
opportunities for companies over the years and will be sharing some of those
stories and ideas in this chapter. Possibilities to reduce a company’s utility
expenses come in the areas of electric, natural gas, water, and waste removal.
These opportunities vary by state and vendor so they need to be reviewed
more thoroughly on the basis of the speciﬁc company’s information. There
are many opportunities that are available in the conservation area (reducing
consumption and usage). But that is not the focus of this chapter. Excellent
engineering ﬁrms and third-party consultants are available throughout the
This chapter was written in collaboration with Utility Reduction Analysts, Inc.
(utilityreduction.com).
240

---

## Page 267

Chapter = c21 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:12 pm
Utility Reviews 241
United States who work beyond cost reduction. When these two areas are
combined, exponential savings can be achieved.
Anyone owning a company receives one bill or numerous bills from
vendors for the following utility services: electric, gas, water, and waste
removal. For some companies, water and electric may be provided by a
municipality and combined in one bill. Larger companies in metropolitan
areas may receive their bill online and have an account executive monitor
it with them quarterly or annually.
In years past, many manufacturers had a utility account representative
that cared for them and helped in multiple ways. Many manufacturers have
seen that service and care decrease, as downsizing has increased across the
United States. The utility vendor typically expects the client to monitor its
bills closely and immediately bring any errors to the vendor’s attention for
correction. It is the position of many utility vendors that the client needs to
be its own advocate. In light of that, a company needs to thoroughly review
its bills, stay aware of market offerings, and bring in additional insight to
itself to ensure that it receives the best rates available to them. Lower rates
will allow them to use captured savings in other necessary areas.
Case Study 21.1: Fortune 1,000 Manufacturer Cuts
Its Utility Costs in Several Ways
A $500 million manufacturer retained Utility Reduction Analysts (URA) to
bring savings recommendations to them. It was referred to URA by the direc-
tor of purchasing because it had met at his previous employer’s company,
where URA helped realize signiﬁcant savings. The current company had
ﬁve locations across the United States and was hoping to save a minimum
of 2 percent, or $140,000 a year, based on its annual utility spending of
$7,000,000. Bills were compiled for the review (12 months of the water,
waste removal, electric, and gas). The staff was very helpful and wanted to
be part of the process, offering help at the corporate and plant levels.
Once the initial analysis had been performed and all data were thor-
oughly reviewed, URA found that the client had a few savings opportunities
in the area of electricity consumption. There was a possible rate change
that would yield modest savings ($7,000 per year) and a few possibilities for
deregulated markets that did not currently have an offering. The rate change
appeared to be a possibility, so the project manager ordered a demand meter
be placed at the company site to conﬁrm their ﬁndings. This would take 90
days to conﬁrm the data and the rate’s appropriateness. In regard to dereg-
ulation, URA would watch the market to see when an opportunity would
arise. It could save $10,000 to $20,000 but that would depend upon the
offerings at any given time. The project manager would watch for a market

---

## Page 268

Chapter = c21 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:12 pm
242 Cost Recovery
decrease in costs, allowing for a realistic electric bill savings to result (3 to
5 percent).
There was a tax refund possibility at one site as well. The analysis depart-
ment spoke with the controller for that location and it worked together to
conﬁrm what percentage of manufacturing went on in that location, so an
industrial processing tax exemption could be discussed with its vendor and
applied for through its state. After the analysis was performed, URA worked
with the client to complete the proper forms for the exemption. This took
numerous calls and a site review was done by the controller. The equipment
needed to be assessed for times used and production information. After this
lengthy process, the electrical audit was sent to the state for review. In four
weeks, the state called the company and began to discuss the request for the
$50,000 refund. After many conversations and defending the calculations and
equipment usage information, a check was conﬁrmed by the state. URA’s
client also received savings of more $15,000 a year going forward.
The URA project manager reviewed a spreadsheet of the client’s waste
usage across its ﬁve locations. Two locations were experiencing a higher
cost than seemed reasonable. She investigated and began to compile market
information from other vendors. She initiated a request for proposal (RFP)
and spoke with the vendors, explaining that they could gain the business of
this notable company in their town. Several quotes were received from the
vendors and she reviewed what was best for the client. The current waste
removal vendor (the largest in that area) was also asked to participate and
to reprice its services. The project manager explained that she wanted them
to be able to stay with the incumbent’s ﬁrm if its pricing was in line with the
market. The pricing came back more favorable from an alternative vendor
who wanted to work hard for its business, so a transition took place. This
change allowed the client to save $40,000 a year on its services (hauling and
recycling).
In total, the manufacturer was able to save $110,000 a year and to receive
a $50,000 refund in the ﬁrst year. Additional opportunities were still being
reviewed in the beginning of year two, as upcoming savings appeared to
be available (based on market opportunities). The manufacturer was not
only pleased with URA’s work but with the improvements it received in
understanding what it was spending. URA provided it with a weekly project
report and quarterly meetings to assess progress.
Case Study 21.2: Hospital Water Cost Leak Plugged
URA was retained by a 600-bed hospital in the Midwest. The hospital’s goal
was to reduce utility expenditures ($200,000 in total) because it felt it was
paying too much in several areas. URA met with the hospital’s executive

---

## Page 269

Chapter = c21 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:12 pm
Utility Reviews 243
director to sign paperwork and initiate the project. URA met with the
accounting and operations staff to gather utility bills and the data necessary
for the review. Twelve months of gas, electric, and water bills were copied
to ensure a thorough review could be performed. This information was
sent to the analysis department, where it compiled spreadsheets comparing
costs with relevant tariffs. Several hundred bills were reviewed because there
were multiple gas, water, and electric meters. There were many meters at
this location and a meter audit was conducted to ensure that the hospital was
paying only for its meters. Errors in billing can occur and companies, in rare
cases, can be paying for a neighboring company’s meter. The meter audit
showed no such errors in billing. Although there were numerous meters at
the hospital, only a few savings opportunities were detected. There was a
deregulation opportunity in electric but the hospital said it could not take
part in it because it had purchased its electricity as a group with other like
facilities. Although it was losing money in this area, it was obligated through
agreements and the relationship with the other locations to be part of the
pool. There were no signiﬁcant savings found elsewhere in gas or electric,
which is interesting, as these are generally areas in which savings are often
detected. The most signiﬁcant opportunity, surprisingly, was found in the
area of water.
A site evaluation was performed to conﬁrm what water meters were in
place. It was uncovered that the hospital was being billed for a 6-inch meter
when only a 2-inch meter was in place. This resulted in an overbilling of
$3,000 a year for the past 7 to 10 years. URA discussed this with the hospital
management team. The team approved that it wanted URA to pursue cor-
rection and a refund for the hospital from the City Water Department. URA
proceeded by compiling the data necessary to prove the overbilling and
coordinate a meeting with the city water department. URA met the water
department representative and discussed the ﬁndings. The water department
was shocked that the hospital thought it was owed a refund and appealed to
URA not to pursue the refund because the water department was a commu-
nity servant as well. The city contended that it was the responsibility of the
hospital to correct overbilling. They seemed willing to continue discussions,
but were unwilling to take responsibility for the error.
URA met with the hospital and further reviewed past data and the city’s
relationship with the hospital. It was uncovered in the meeting with the hos-
pital that the city had installed the meter 10 years earlier and blueprints of
the project were in the hospital’s possession that stated only a 2-inch meter
was needed. The blueprints also had the city water department’s informa-
tion all over them as well and showed that it was actively involved in the
project. This formed a critical connection between the city and hospital and
proved that the city was involved with the error from the beginning. URA
took the blueprint and additional dialog from the hospital back to the water

---

## Page 270

Chapter = c21 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:12 pm
244 Cost Recovery
department and discussions continued. In the end, the hospital was awarded
a $17,000 refund and decreased billing of $3,000 a year.
Case Study 21.3: Small Town Manufacturer—Electric
Refund
URA was retained to serve a respected manufacturer that employed a large
amount of workers in a small town. The CFO had been very skeptical of
hiring a third party to interface with their vendors because it was a family-
owned business and relationships were key to its success. The manufacturer
valued all relationships: clients, staff, and vendors. This was appealing for
URA because it enjoyed working with companies that had high values and
strong relationships. URA found companies like this to be more honest and
ethical to work with and serve. Although initially hesitant to commit to an
engagement, the CFO ﬁnally signed an agreement after URA had met with
the owner, then the CFO, and then the key managers.
URA reviewed the client’s gas, water, and electric bills. In the area
of electricity, URA ran reports to conﬁrm the client’s rates were correct
with the local municipality. It was discovered the client was not on the
correct electric rate with it municipality. It was still on a temporary rate
offered to it when it had built its location many years before. That building
rate was supposed to be temporary (three to six months) but somehow
had been missed and had resulted in $50,000 a year overbilling. Now,
three years later, the excessive billing had reached an estimated $150,000.
Believing the client would be overjoyed at the ﬁnding, the analysis staff
discussed the refund opportunity and projected decreased costs with the
CFO. He was disappointed that the company had overspent, but he was
also concerned about the ﬁnding because his company was the largest
manufacturer and employed a signiﬁcant number of people in the small
town where the plant was situated. Because the electricity was supplied by
the municipality and the company was a key provider of employment in
the community, he wanted to tread lightly and not upset any relationships
and trust in place. There are many beneﬁts to working with the local gov-
ernment and community for the good of all and this client weighed those
seriously.
The CFO took time to review the data and responded to URA with the
following instructions. He wanted URA to coordinate a refund of less than
20 percent of the estimated refund due. He had accessed records and found
that the city had helped the company years earlier in signiﬁcant ways and
he did not want to damage their relationship. So, URA went back to the
city representative and requested a refund for $24,000 and corrected the

---

## Page 271

Chapter = c21 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:12 pm
Utility Reviews 245
billing going forward. Although URA would have loved to collect the whole
amount, it is bound by their agreement to only share in 50 percent of the
savings or refunds received by its clients. It also did not want to press
the issue any further because it could damage the client’s relationship with
the city.
Case Study 21.4: Entertainment Company with
Multiple Locations Chooses Deregulation for
Select Sites
URA was retained by a multiple-site retail location offering entertainment
throughout several cities. Its industry had experienced increased costs in
many areas and it wanted to be sure it was being ﬁscally responsible in
reviewing its utility expenses as its margins got tighter. It wanted to make
sure the bottom line was as healthy as possible, so URA was given the ini-
tiative to review the gas and electric and to ﬁnd opportunities to cut costs.
It had 30 sites and had received quotes from alternative energy providers
that claimed they could reduce their electric costs substantially if they par-
ticipated in an electric deregulated program.
Electric deregulation is a savings opportunity that should be assessed
but only activated after thorough research and much caution. In the past,
regulated utilities offered electric supply to their customers: generation,
transmission, and distribution. Deregulation allows a company to purchase
their electricity from a different company although it receive its electric-
ity over the same power lines. This allows competition that will hopefully
lead to reduced costs and improved customer service. Deregulation has
continued to become available in a growing number of states. Although
available, savings and satisfaction levels vary by company. Some compa-
nies have found substantial savings when choosing a solid provider and
locking in a price that delivers margin compared to their regulated electric
vendor. URA proposes that clients enter into a deregulated option only if
the margin is high enough to insulate the company from losing money if
the program changes down the road. You may have seen news reports on
companies that entered the electric deregulation program and suffered great
losses when the program changed and their nominal savings became hun-
dreds of thousands of dollars in losses once the margin in savings was gone.
The reviews on deregulation differ greatly by state and supplier. Although
the results of deregulated programs vary, it is still a viable option that should
be thoroughly researched for high-usage companies.
Although suppliers were assuring URA’s client that they could certainly
save signiﬁcant dollars, URA moved ahead with caution. URA had heard

---

## Page 272

Chapter = c21 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:12 pm
246 Cost Recovery
similar claims from vendors and always reviewed the numbers in compari-
son to the regulated utility’s costs to compare the alternative opportunity in
direct comparison to the client’s current services and costs. After all locations
were reviewed, the initial savings presented were found to be generous in
comparison to URA’s ﬁndings. In fact, the client would have only broken
even if it had signed on with the alternative provider. The provider’s num-
bers did not include additional fees, line items, or costs that make up the
total cost of electric service that the client would have to pay. Independent
sales agents for suppliers are typically paid on a percentage of the volume of
the sale, so they are beneﬁted by the total volume they secure. In contrast,
URA is paid only on shared savings, and its motivation is to bring only the
best bottom-line services and price to the client.
Reviewing the individual reports, the analysis staff found that only three
of the client’s locations would beneﬁt from the electric deregulation oppor-
tunity. The client would save 15 percent if the three locations were the ones
that elected to go into the program. If it had put all of its locations into the
program, its savings would have been close to zero. With only three loca-
tions, it would save $100,000 for the term of their three-year contract. The
client was surprised that the vendor’s quotes and sales presentation was not
accurate in several ways and he was relieved that URA had guided him to a
practical cost savings that would beneﬁt his company for years to come. This
deregulated option ceased to be beneﬁcial to new clients only 18 months
after this client had signed on. And although others could not locate signif-
icant savings, this discerning client continued to have great savings for the
term of its contract.
Case Study Wrap-Up
These case studies show how additional utility savings and even refunds
may be available to companies. While this may be the case, they are often
difﬁcult to defend and retrieve. Many vendors may expect a great detail of
evidence and effort to activate savings and reclaim any lost dollars. That is
why third-party consultants like Utility Reduction Analysts are often retained
to support a company in these savings and recovery initiatives.
Why Use a Third-Party Utility Auditing Firm?
Using a third-party ﬁrm may increase your savings and refund opportuni-
ties substantially and cost you very little, as many are paid on a shared
savings plan. This allows the company and the consulting ﬁrm to work col-
laboratively and to share in any found money. The consulting ﬁrm actually

---

## Page 273

Chapter = c21 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:12 pm
Utility Reviews 247
creates its own budget when it works on a shared savings plan because it
is receiving only what it locates, funds that were being lost.
The third-party audit ﬁrm has the following advantages:
■ Past successes to draw from when devising plans to recover funds
■ Knowledge of what many companies are paying (vendor and market
intelligence)
■ Experience in negotiating utility expenses and refunds (yielding greater
results)
■ Network of connections with key people at many utility companies
■ Unique third-party advantage (perspective)
■ Ability to support company staff and collaborate on savings oppor-
tunities
Recoveries to Find
There may be opportunities to reduce costs and recover refunds through
the review of these areas:
■ Better rates at the current vendor. Meet and speak with the utility vendor
and see whether there are options to the rate plan. Are there better rates
available to take advantage of if there is less usage? Run the numbers
and see what decreased usage would save the company.
■ Deregulated options (gas and electric). Depending on the market, there
may be deregulated options available to the company for gas and elec-
tric. The local utility Web sites typically explain the program, history of
the program, relevant legislation behind it, and include contact numbers
for approved suppliers. Deregulation can be advantageous for com-
panies if they are able to assess alternative supplier costs in direct
comparison to what they are paying their regulated utility provider.
There are differing components between quotes from suppliers and
companies may misunderstand the value of the alternative option or
contract term they are signing on to. Use caution—make sure you are
comparing apples to apples when you review comparative rates and
programs. Review contracts, terms, conditions, and length of the agree-
ment. Weigh the ups and downs of this commitment.
■ Request for proposal (RFP). An RFP can help a company conﬁrm that it
has the best pricing available in the market. An RFP allows a company
to assess what additional market opportunities are available through
a vendor and other potential providers as it has vendors compete for
their business. Vendors are given the same specs for the RFP so apple-
to-apple pricing is received.

---

## Page 274

Chapter = c21 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:12 pm
248 Cost Recovery
■ Billings to contract differences by your utility vendor (or landlord)?If the
company operates on a contracted rate, check the original agreement
and conﬁrm whether the company is being billed correctly. If not, meet
with the provider and present supporting data. Prepare a narrative to
detail the overbilling and proposed corrections. If possible, ask for a
refund for all overbilling and interest.
■ Estimated bills. Bills should be checked to determine if each billing
period’s read is actual or estimated. Estimated readings should be
checked to see whether usage is in line with previous usage. If bills have
been estimated, request correction from the utility billing department
and refunds or credits, as appropriate.
■ Penalties on the bill (example: power factor/electric). Penalties on the bill
for usage in off times or for exceeding a certain level can be reduced
or eliminated if better negotiated with the utility vendor.
■ Master list of meters. Companies should have a master list of all their
meters and their utility account numbers so they can conﬁrm that they
are being billed on only their meters and so they know where all meters
are located.
Questions to Ask When Assessing the Opportunity
■ What is the volume of utility spending?Would a return of 2 to 5 percent in
utilities (general estimation) be enough to interest the company to spend
the time needed to compile the bills and contracts needed to perform
an audit? If a company spends $5,000,000 on utilities, that represents a
possible savings of $100,000 (2 percent of total volume). Make sure all
parties agree on a measurement of success before initiating a review.
■ Are copies of utility and telecom contracts available for review?Copies of
agreements from utility providers assist utility audit ﬁrms in accurately
auditing the client’s bills and pricing components. Contracts can be coor-
dinated through the vendor but they typically would rather not have to
provide a duplicate copy of the agreement to the client, especially if a
third-party ﬁrm is involved.
■ Is the company decisive? It is crucial to the savings initiative for the key
decision maker to be decisive and motivated to make improvements
and implement cost savings. If a third party audits the company’s util-
ity expenses for weeks or even months (depending on the complexity
of the review) and the decision maker is hesitant to move on savings
plans, the opportunities can evaporate (based on changes in the mar-
ket). Third-party vendors paid on shared savings will not want to assess
savings and refund opportunities for a company that does not intend to
move ahead on recommendations.

---

## Page 275

Chapter = c21 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 1:12 pm
Utility Reviews 249
■ Has a savings analysis been performed in the past three to ﬁve years?If so,
what were the results? Who was involved? Can anything be learned from
the project? These items may be helpful in assessing whether another
review would be prudent.
■ Does the company have multiple utility providers in contiguous areas?If
so, streamlining of vendors may be a good idea. Are there vendors that
could serve other locations currently served by another provider? Com-
bining spending levels may encourage a vendor to be more competitive
with its pricing. If cost reductions and improved customer service can
be achieved from aggregating locations with a single vendor, it is wise
to review.
■ Is your company operating your facility in a different manner from
before? If you are using more utilities or operating with different hours,
these changes may cause a company to pay higher rates if you have not
made the appropriate rate changes with your utility provider. Compa-
nies often change how they use their utilities, yet do not make revisions
to their rate plans with their utility vendors that would beneﬁt their
company.
Conclusion
Utilities are a substantial expense for most companies, especially for those in
the manufacturing industry. If your company spends a considerable amount
on utilities, one should perform a utility audit. Generally, there are savings
opportunities that can be realized by a company once a thorough review and
market assessment is done. Gas, electric, water, and waste removal should
be reviewed for a minimum of the past 12 months. Whether performing
a utility audit internally or with assistance of an experienced third-party
consulting ﬁrm, a company can most likely yield savings and refunds that
will improve its bottom line.
