CHAPTER 22
Selecting a Provider
Overview
In any negotiation, the buyer wants to solve a current problem being faced
through a fair and equitable settlement. The focus should not be to gouge
the vendor, squeezing the last drop of juice from the lemon, because it
limits the ability to work with a long-term focus. Optimally, a win-win rela-
tionship can be established to ensure that both parties get a fair settlement
and are committed to effectuating their part of the deal. Concessions can
still be obtained from the vendor (with its long-term focus of gaining you
as a client) but the focus should not be for the buyer to take the store
for free.
Any short-term focus can come back to haunt your organization. Take,
for example, the research that found 40 to 80 percent of a typical soft-
ware project’s budget goes to ﬁxing defects created earlier in the project.
Was this just programmer sloppiness or poorly negotiated quality metrics in
the contract? More likely, it was due to a vendor overpromising and then
underdelivering because of the pressures to get the sale.
Applying Discipline ... It’s a Good Thing1
Yes, discipline hurts, but it helps to ensure success. When you carefully and
deliberately consider all variables, you draw a line in the sand, which you
can use to measure performance and track lessons learned.
The best practices in software vendor selection will:
■ Streamline the writing and approval of vendors by standardizing key
process deliverables
■ Clearly articulate the associated roles and responsibilities throughout the
process
250

---

## Page 277

Chapter = c22 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 9:56 am
Selecting a Provider 251
■ Minimize the risk of selecting an inappropriate vendor to complete the
project, which will set that company up for failure
You can apply the vendor-vetting process to any purchasing situation,
but it’s generally tailored more toward larger-dollar transactions. We focus
here mostly on consultants and application service providers (ASPs).
The process I give here is based on best practices from other companies,
best-practice reports from consulting ﬁrms, and various books and articles.
Here’s the ﬁve-step process:
1. Form the team
2. Set requirements and success metrics
3. Draft a request for proposals using best practices and perform due
diligence
4. Negotiate with prospective vendors
5. Complete decision analysis
Step 1: Form the Team2
It’s no longer practicable for one person to be adept in all the facets of a
purchasing decision. Consider the following areas when forming the team
to complete the purchasing analysis and vendor negotiation: ﬁnance, legal,
quality assurance, marketing, purchasing, technical product or service spec-
iﬁcations, and an independent party (a devil’s advocate).
Many times, based on the requirements, a ﬁrm will deem a project so
technical that it needs to have more experienced technical engineers on the
team rather than ﬁnancial personnel. In other cases, it might be a cut-and-
dried product purchase so you can give the negotiations to a purchasing
manager who daily deals with these decisions. Regardless, it’s always ben-
eﬁcial to have a devil’s advocate independently ask questions like “What if
we went with a different vendor?” or “Are we sure we are doing the right
thing?”
The overall goal is to increase the credibility of the team and form more
expert power so that the vendor knows it’s receiving a legitimate offer. To
have a maximum impact in negotiations, staff the team with the best and
brightest people you can get for the project’s requirements.
Once you’ve selected the team, brief its members, preferably through a
kickoff meeting, to:
■ Ensure everyone knows the team leader (and agrees with the selection)
■ Deﬁne the role of each team member (e.g.,the ﬁnance guy, the engi-
neer gal, and so on)

---

## Page 278

Chapter = c22 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 9:56 am
252 Cost Recovery
■ Explain the cost and pricing data that support the issues at hand but
never to discuss it with a vendor
■ Coordinate with legal counsel on acceptable negotiating terms and con-
ditions
■ Deﬁne authorization limits for negotiating on the company’s behalf
■ Assign a scribe to take meeting minutes
■ Remind everyone to act as professional as possible
Step 2: Set Requirements and Success Metrics3
No one step in the process is numero uno, but given a choice to pick
one, this would be in the top two. Speciﬁc requirements for purchases, be
it new software training or the expertise to keep an application running
24/7, are the keys to success in selecting a vendor. Without this founda-
tion, the project has a chance of failing because it didn’t meet its original
intentions.
Business case. This is the raison d’ ˆetre for the purchasing decision.
Include at a minimum:
■ The problem and associated business opportunity
■ The solution to the problem
■ Expected time frame to complete the project
■ Speciﬁc beneﬁts for the project and proposed solution
■ Stakeholders in the problem and solution
■ The expected market’s response to the solution
Deliverables. These are the actual items to be received as part of the
relationship with the vendor. They could be as ﬁnite as copies of software
or a network server or could be as vague as a strategy document. Deliv-
erables by themselves usually aren’t very useful but, in combination with
success metrics, can be very powerful in establishing strong contracts with
vendors.
Success metrics. These deﬁne if a deliverable has been completed suc-
cessfully to warrant payment to the vendor. Here are success metrics for
a computer system requirements document and an ASP. Whether you’re
building a system, hosting one, or looking for a more amorphous deliver-
able (such as a strategy document that explains the high-level business value
of the system along with some simple uses of the system), deﬁne the success
metrics for the deliverable in as much detail as possible. This detailing will
help ensure you have some level of measurement when selecting a vendor
and in reviewing deliverable completion.

---

## Page 279

Chapter = c22 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 9:56 am
Selecting a Provider 253
Stakeholders
■ Who assisted in or prepared this business case development.
■ In the development of this project deﬁnition, if the project team and
major stakeholders were not involved (e.g., through a joint application
development session), please explain why.
■ Who the business problem (as set in the general section) and associated
solution affects.
■ Of these users, who are the raving fans (i.e., critical supporter) of the
solution presented by this project and generally state the reasons for
their intents.
■ Of these users, who are the dissenters of the project and generally
state the reasons for their intents.
■ AICPA project team stakeholders, including the project sponsor(s) from
executive management, the project manager, and any project team mem-
bers that can be identiﬁed at this time.
■ Any dependencies on other AICPA teams’ resources.
Step 3: Draft a Request for Proposals4
After the business case, deliverables, and success metrics are set, they can
be rolled in as the ﬁrst part of the request for proposals (RFP).
It might be good to leave out some of the sensitive information from the
RFP. For example, if a new system will save the company $500,000, giving
the vendor such information provides them a bargaining chip in negotiations
(see Step 4). Furthermore, if you outline all the success metrics, the vendor
might say, “Sure, we can do that,” when it might have never completed the
task before. In these cases, keeping some of the success metrics unspeciﬁed
allows room in the due diligence process to understand if a vendor truly
understands what needs to be completed for the task.
If you’re concerned with ﬁnding appropriate vendors, they can be iden-
tiﬁed through various sources. Some of the most common methods for
identifying a vendor are reputation and references, publication advertise-
ments, the Internet, published articles, and marketing literature.
Perform Due Diligence
Due diligence activities center around the evaluation criteria established in
the RFP. Vendor proposals and interviews will help the evaluation team
ensure it makes a proper selection. In addition to asking questions from the
request for proposals, complete the following when reviewing the selected
external party or vendor.

---

## Page 280

Chapter = c22 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 9:56 am
254 Cost Recovery
■ Obtain and review a recent Dun & Bradstreet report online at dnb.com
■ Review morningstar.com, company.sleuth.com, vault.com, newslink.org,
and localbusiness.com reports to identify any recent business and ﬁnan-
cial articles about the company
After you’ve set the deliverables and success metrics for the project
and identiﬁed the vendors for solicitation, the remaining task is establishing
evaluation criteria. These criteria will drive the questions you’ll ask external
parties and vendors in the RFP and the ﬁnal decision analysis (see Step 5).
The following are suggested evaluation criteria and sample questions to ask
vendors in RFPs.
Company Background
■ Date and state of incorporation?
■ Public or privately held?
■ Detail company ownership.
■ Describe expected partner or company principal involvement in our
account.
■ Describe any bankruptcies and lawsuits, past and present, involving
your ﬁrm.
■ Describe all merger and acquisition and divestiture activity over the last
ﬁve years.
■ Describe any innovations your ﬁrm has brought to the recovery audit
industry.
■ For FY 2006 compared to FY 2005, what percent did audit recovery
revenue grow or decline with your operations?
■ Provide current company ﬁnancial statements.
■ List any other services you offer that will help recover lost revenue.
■ Ask for at least three references. And check them! The references pro-
vided should be companies in your industry and of your approximate
size. Include the audit start and completion dates for each.

■ Describe your rate structure.
■ Describe any out-of-pocket expense that we are responsible for during
this audit.
■ Describe your payment terms.
■ When is payment considered earned?
■ If, after the audit is concluded, a claim paid by a vendor was deter-
mined to have been reimbursed in error, does your company return its
compensation for that claim to us?

ﬂow, and thereby raise proﬁtability and shareholder value?
Step 4: Negotiate
Negotiation is both an art and a science. The art portion can’t be learned;
you either born knowing how to do it or you’re not. However, regardless of
your skill, you can learn and apply the science parts of negotiation. (Much
of the information in this section is adapted from The Negotiating Game by
Chester L. Karrass, Collins Business, 1994.)
Negotiation can be separated into:
■ Assessing sources of power
■ Assessing all items on which you’ll negotiate (other than price)
■ Selecting a negotiation approach
Assess Sources of Power
Reward power. Rewards can take on many forms, including money, prop-
erty, improved productivity, and so forth. Generally, the reward power is
in your, the buyer’s, favor because the cash to be exchanged is the key

---

## Page 284

Chapter = c22 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 9:56 am
258 Cost Recovery
power chip. Other rewards to the vendor include the prestige of having
you as a client, the excitement of working on an inﬂuential project, and
the status in the marketplace this project will provide the vendor, among
others.
Punishment power. On the exact opposite end of the spectrum, the
threat of punishment is also generally in your favor, not only from the cash-
at-hand perspective, but also in the potential loss of a long-term customer
in which a vendor will usually make the bulk of its proﬁts.
Legitimacy power. This power is also called the moral power. For exam-
ple, a buyer getting a fair price in return for a vendor’s right to a proﬁt
is an expectation of business. Any attempts by the vendor to instill unfair
practices should be thwarted by this power for a fair deal. This is the only
legitimate and moral thing to do.
Power of other options and competition. Ever since the formation of the
U.S. Federal Trade Commission (and surely before then), competition has
ruled capital markets. This provides an essential power to the buyer in that
you can always walk away from the table and work with another vendor. The
vendor, knowing this dynamic, makes all efforts to thwart competitive offers
by minimizing its skills, while at the same time highlighting its strengths.
This balance should be maintained in your favor (with the vendor working
to earn its business) by ensuring that every buying situation has competitors.
There’s a reason for the golden rule of purchasing in getting three bids for
a product: It creates competition.
Power of knowledge. The clich ´e “knowledge is power” is most true
in a negotiation because the more information you have on the vendor,
the more powerful you are in forcing hands in your favor. For example,
let’s say you review current ﬁnancial articles and discover that one of
the sellers is in ﬁnancial trouble. This information might halt all future
dealings with the vendor, but if you decide to move forward, you
are able to use this information to obtain a better deal, given the risk
you’re taking.
Power of time. If you can avoid saying one sentence in a negotiation, it
would be: “We need your product or service as fast as possible because we
have a deadline we have to hit.” The discount column on the vendor’s retail
price list now magically disappears! You’ve handed all power to the vendor,
who now will take time returning calls because the vendor now knows he
is needed ... desperately.
Use time to your advantage by planning ahead with projects and taking
the perspective of “We want to make the right decision regardless of how
long it takes.”
Expert power. A vendor smells blood when it knows it’s dealing with a
buyer who doesn’t understand the vendor’s business. Therefore, as noted
earlier, you must build a credible negotiation team.

---

## Page 285

Chapter = c22 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 9:56 am
Selecting a Provider 259
Risk power. Risk needs to be spread among parties. For example, a
$1,000 bet in Las Vegas on one round of blackjack is too much for one person
to reasonably bear, but a $20 bet spread among 50 people is palatable.
The bet is still the same but the risk is much lower to the betting group.
Therefore, it’s wise to spread risk between the buyer and seller. One popular
way to do this is to sign a ﬁxed-price contract for key deliverables with
the vendor, which sets the organization’s investment in the project while
transferring the risk to the vendor to complete the project on time and under
budget.
Assess All Items to Negotiate (Other than Price)
Many times, a vendor is sensitive to lowering its price but is willing to change
its position on other factors of the sale. Here are various other options,
or concessions, for negotiation other than price. Remember ... anything is
negotiable.
Terms and conditions Penalty clauses
Warranties Service
Service contracts Delivery schedules and timeliness
Discounts Product liability
Shipping costs Length of contracts
Performance metrics and quality Payment terms
Credit arrangements Larger deposit
Being a vendor reference Signing a longer-term deal
Generally speaking, in a negotiation, 10 percent of the time is spent
on the buyer getting 90 percent of what she wants from the negotiation
while the remaining 90 percent in time is spent on the remaining 10 percent
of concessions. Consider this when mulling about the cost and beneﬁt of
further discussions when 90 percent of your needs are already met.
Select a Negotiation Approach
There are generally two approaches to negotiation: problem solving and
share bargaining. The problem solving method is a collaborative effort bet-
ween two parties; neither party seeks to take the other to the cleaners.
Parties come to meet all objectives. In any negotiation, try your best to see
the problem from the other side’s point of view to best meet all needs. For
example, use options other than price to enhance your position while giving
the vendor the price it wants. In negotiation talk, this is, of course, known
as a win-win situation.

---

## Page 286

Chapter = c22 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 9:56 am
260 Cost Recovery
Shared bargaining leads to the division of cost and beneﬁts between
you and the seller until a settlement point is reached. Otherwise known
as haggling, many people think this is the only way to negotiate. The best
example is a salary negotiation in which the employer tries to go as low
as possible while the employee, of course, uses the opposite approach.
In the end, settle at a midpoint in which they both will lose some posi-
tion. In negotiation talk, this is otherwise known as a lose-lose or win-lose
situation.
Don’t use shared bargaining as your ﬁrst method in negotiating. You
can revert to it at the end of a problem solving discussion to gain some
concessions. Arbitration is another form of win-lose or lose-lose bargaining
in which a third party decides the fate of a deadlock between the parties at
a detriment to one or more of the parties.
Step 5: Complete Decision Analysis5
Decision analysis is a method in which you identify, document, and assess
the variables in a quantitative fashion for the various decision alternatives.
This tool’s beneﬁt is the quantiﬁcation of a very qualitative task—that is,
ﬁnding the best solution. Here are the steps:
1. List all of the alternatives for a given problem or situation. For example,
in a purchasing decision, list the various vendors as alternatives. Some
alternatives might be partnerships among vendors to get the best of both
worlds.
2. Those decision variables that are critical become binary yes-no deci-
sions. In other words, if an alternative doesn’t meet the criteria, then
drop the alternative. For example, if you’re selecting a system that must
integrate with your e-mail package to be effective, drop any system from
the analysis that doesn’t do that. These criteria are generally based on
the evaluation criteria established in the RFP drafting.
3. Weigh the remaining decision variables as to their importance and give
each alternative a score. For example, the decision variable of a vendor
that has completed a similar project might have a weight o fa9i na
best-of-10 weighting. If an alternative one has many past experiences
of similar projects, the vendor might get a score of a 10 in a best-of-
10 weighting. The 9 would be multiplied by the 10 to arrive at a ﬁnal
score for this decision variable of 90. Please note that these criteria are
also generally based on the evaluation criteria established in the RFP
drafting.

---

## Page 287

Chapter = c22 Date: June 23, 2009 Time: 9:56 am
Selecting a Provider 261
4. The ﬁnal weightings scores would be added for each alternative to arrive
at a ﬁnal score for the alternative. You would select the one with the
highest score, given that you assume it has already met all the critical
decision criteria as discussed in the preceding paragraph.
Sample Decision Analysis
It is beneﬁcial to complete decision analysis in a structured manner.
Exhibit 22.1 is a sample decision analysis using an example of moving
records to an outside university. It is based on the Kepner-Tregoe framework
for decision analysis.
Kepner-Tregoe decision analysis is a method whereby the decision vari-
ables in a decision are identiﬁed, documented, and assessed in a quantitative
fashion for the various alternatives in the decision. The method ﬁrst details
all of the alternatives for a given problem or situation. Then, the must-have
decision criteria are listed for the project. Please note that those alternatives
that do not meet must haves are generally out of the analysis. Then, the
method rates the alternatives on desirable decision criteria that are weighted
as to their importance. Finally, any risks associated with each alternative are
assessed and documented. In the following example, University 2 would be
selected, given that it meets all of the must-have requirements, has a higher
desirable score, and there are no associated risks with the decision.
EXHIBIT 22.1

2 Cost is less than $100,000 Y Y Y

## Page 288

262 Cost Recovery
Option 1 was selected, given that it met all critical decision variables
and had the highest decision variable score.
Conclusion
A systematic process won’t ensure success in the vendor selection process
but it will improve your chances while allowing project teams to begin to
measure lessons learned. If you leave this process to ad hoc measures, you
can rarely recall the speciﬁc reasons for your selection. Even when you’ve
selected a solid option, if you can’t measure it because you didn’t plan
deliverance metrics, success will be hit or miss. When big bucks are on the
line, the only acceptable option is a direct hit.

Association of Certiﬁed Fraud Examiners, Austin, Tex., July 2008.

## Page 289

Chapter = c23 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:42 am
CHAPTER 23
Technology Used
in Recovery Efforts
Introduction
The Rush to Technology1
Many companies will simply rush to add technology, feeling that it will solve
a problem, only to ﬁnd that the problem has not been eased. This can be
explained by acknowledging that most people still work on instinct rather
than rational and planned thought. The instinct to panic and implement
software because they are facing a challenging documentation project or a
performance objective by year end rarely works.
In the panic, people normally forget the interdependency relationship
and thus do not realize that they must ﬁrst understand the process, under-
stand the people, and improve the process as best as possible before layering
technology.
Therefore, any group planning on implementing cost recovery pro-
cesses should ﬁrst map out the competencies people need to enact this
change, develop a process that will be accepted by the organization, and
then implement technology (see Exhibit 23.1).
This may mean to a company starting out that it not use prepackaged
software products (offered by some recovery ﬁrms). Rather, it may use paper
and pencil or a simple spreadsheet solution until it feels it has the process
relatively optimized. This relatively is added to the sentence because one
does not want to wait until the cloudless sky arrives to add the technology
layer. This can lead to a waiting game that is never-ending. By starting simple
with technology and making sure that the people and process are engaged,
the third layer is the easiest one to add. And unlike people, the software’s
feelings will not be hurt if it is called in last.
263

---

## Page 290

Chapter = c23 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:42 am
264 Cost Recovery
1. PEOPLE
DEPENDENCY
RELATIONSHIP
2. PROCESS 3. TECHNOLOGY
EXHIBIT 23.1 DependencyRelationshipDiagram
Why Is Recovery Technology Better?
Recovery ﬁrms for the most part followed this transition process, whereby
they hired people who understood the area for review, worked on a process
using at most spreadsheets to automate their work, and then decided that
such efforts are wasteful, given that database and process ﬂow technology
(discussed later) are simply superior. Cost recovery ﬁrms that followed this
linear approach to developing their own technology do have some advan-
tages that are inherent to the way the technology was initially developed.
■ Comprehensive. For example, accounts payable recovery ﬁrms can run
up to 80 reports to identify recoveries.
■ Targeted sampling. By having niche expertise and knowing what to look
for, they begin to build computer reports that look more precisely for
recoveries, which is otherwise known as targeted sampling.
■ Preformatted workpapers for recovery purposes. By building workpapers
that organize the data succinctly and in a consistent format, recovery
efforts are expedited, as it takes less time to identify cost recoveries in
the ﬁeld.
■ Workﬂow technology for recovery purposes. Be it a spreadsheet or com-
plex database, over time the process is automated in a way whereby
linear process steps are taken to a ﬁnal recovery or savings for the client.
The process may begin by analyzing client data, followed by identifying
recoveries, and then authorizing those recoveries by the client before
any recoveries are made from vendors. Each step in this process would
have a different interface that is tailored to that speciﬁc process step.
■ Constantly updated for new opportunities. As recovery and savings
projects are undertaken, the technology system is constantly improved,
usually by an internal information technology department that focuses
solely on the task of improving the recovery system.

---

## Page 291

Chapter = c23 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:42 am
Technology Used in Recovery Efforts 265
To provide an example of how recovery technology developed by an
external ﬁrm or internal department is superior, take, for example, the sub-
ject of duplicate payment reporting. Later in this chapter, a simple duplicate
payment test is explained so that someone could produce the report in
Excel. The simple test will be on vendor, invoice number, and amount, yet
a recovery group will learn over time that such a test is too simplistic to
identify material recoveries. Rather, as an example, the recovery ﬁrm may
run 10 or 12 variant duplicate reports on invoice number, invoice date, ven-
dor number, digits within the invoice number, invoice dates that are close
in time, and then combine all of these variant reports into a super-report.
Further reﬁning of this report may include removing any voided items, items
reversed by debit memos, and even score the remaining transactions as to
their validity for recovery. Now, in thinking back to the original simplistic
report (which may have been what the recovery ﬁrm used for its earlier
clients), the newer batch of reports have a much higher likelihood of success.
Technology Categories
To organize the concepts associated with recovery technology, the follow-
ing are the key categories used by recovery analysts to maximize their
performance and, hence, savings for the client:
■ Data analysis/mining/external data
■ Continuous monitoring
■ Preformatted workpapers/process workﬂow/client management dash-
boards
Data Analysis/Mining/External Data
Why We Need a Database2
Until a formal process is understood, written down, and automated, com-
panies tend to spend close to the same amount of effort in year two and
beyond to complete that same process. Moving work done with pencil and
paper or spreadsheets to a database provides the following beneﬁts:
■ Maintain consistency. Given most recovery work is relatively mundane
and routine in approach, there is no reason why a company should not
draft a methodology and then automate it for posterity. In this manner, a
company-wide approach can quickly be rolled out for recovery efforts.
Furthermore, now that the data are stored consistently, they aid in any
searches across previous efforts within the database.
■ Reduce duplicative testing. By the virtue of automating a system, any
duplicative work within the process should be quickly identiﬁed for

---

## Page 292

Chapter = c23 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:42 am
266 Cost Recovery
removal. For a simple example, multiple duplicate payment reports that
work toward the same goal may be combined into one report for ﬁeld
testing.
■ See new trends. Most recovery efforts require consolidating various com-
pany and vendor data into one database for analysis. For example, in a
health care audit, company eligibility data, administrator eligibility data,
claim information, and provider information all need to be combined
into one table for analysis. This may be the ﬁrst time such an exercise
was ever completed, and in doing so, new trends may be seen in the
data and gaps in data management between the company, administrator,
and service providers.
■ Organize testing and get the dashboard working. Once the database is
built, reporting across the system is more easily effectuated. For exam-
ple, in an accounts payable review, vendors showing up on multiple
reports could have their testing consolidated so that the vendor is con-
tacted once with a litany of questions rather than disjointed testing across
various spreadsheets and by different analysts. In addition to more orga-
nized testing, a dashboard can be developed for senior management to
quickly assess the efforts to date.
A database of recovery testing will not create itself overnight but it has
beneﬁts that make up for the development effort. It is for this reason that
such databases can be a cost recovery ﬁrm’s most guarded asset.
Getting over Data Access Hurdles3
The ﬁrst step in building a database is to ﬁgure out what data are needed
in the database and either enter them manually (not ideal and generally
not necessary) or to request them from owners of such data. This owner
is usually the client (i.e., vendor ledgers, employee eligibility data, building
lease terms, and so on) but could also be external data providers. Given its
signiﬁcance, no publication speaking about data analysis of company data
for cost recovery would be complete without ﬁrst explaining how to request
data from clients.
This can be a stumbling block for many recovery efforts, because com-
panies can impose hurdles as follows:
4
■ Other priorities. The information technology department (IT) probably
has plenty of work and may not be able to quickly download the data
ﬁles. In overcoming this, one approach may be to explain the beneﬁts
to the organization of the cost recovery effort and also show the IT
department that if it produces these data, it’ll help reduce the time they
spend developing other system reports in the future.
■ Poor understanding of data. A company may have a system that works
but when asked to download the data ﬁles residing in the systems, it

---

## Page 293

Chapter = c23 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:42 am
Technology Used in Recovery Efforts 267
isn’t sure precisely how to complete the task. Many times, the analysts
must work directly with the outside software vendor or the external com-
pany that manages it. Obviously, the key is to work with the person who
knows the most about the speciﬁc system. Another approach is to talk to
other companies that use the same computer software so they can pro-
vide better ways to download the data. Or one may obtain a print report
ﬁle from the IT department. These ﬁles are data ﬁle versions of printed
reports (that would otherwise print on a printer unit). They may not be
the best source of data because they are based on programmed logic
that may be faulty, but if it’s the only option, it may be worth the risk.
■ New system is coming soon. There are continual upgrades and patch
updates to computer systems and sometimes major overhaul changes.
The business unit or IT department may ask to wait for updates or
changes before obtaining the related data ﬁles. While this approach
may sound practical, the reality is that 80 percent of the time (based on
chaos report studies from the Standish Group, standishgroup.com), the
systems are late or not delivered at all. So it’s better to have one data
ﬁle in the hand than two after a computer system rollout.
Once the obstacles to gaining data access have been overcome, a simple
step process can be followed to obtain company data ﬁles, as detailed here.
Step 1: Making Arrangements with the Client to Obtain Data
One should meet with the appropriate client personnel (generally the pri-
mary contact for the audit and a key contact in information systems) to make
arrangements to obtain the data. Matters to be discussed include:
■ Reports expected to be run using the extracted data.
■ Speciﬁc data needed.
■ Types of ﬁles needed. Common ﬁle types include comma delimited
format, tab delimited format, Microsoft Access format, and, of course,
the Microsoft Excel format.
■ Record layout of the ﬁle. (The user should arrange to get copies of the
record layout, which is a simple deﬁnition of each data ﬁeld and where
the ﬁelds are positioned in the data ﬁle.)
■ Timing of the transfer.
■ Method of transfer.
■ Arrangements for veriﬁcation information.
The results of this discussion should be formalized into a request letter.
A sample letter that might be used by an analyst is shown here. This letter
can be revised depending on the individual circumstances.

Technology Used in Recovery Efforts 269
Step 2: Transferring the Client’s Data
There are many ways to transfer data to a computer for analysis, depend-
ing on the client’s system architecture. Examples of possible data transfer
methods include:
■ DVD or CD
■ E-mail ﬁle attachments
■ USB ﬂash drive
■ Data tape
■ FTP or network transfers
■ Web storage (e.g., ibackup.com)
The ﬁrst three methods are more likely to be used for small PC systems.
The last three methods are more likely to be used on larger systems (LANs,
minicomputers, or mainframes).
Step 3: Verifying the Data Received from the Client
It is generally good practice to verify client data before processing it. There
are two reasons for this. First, the user can conﬁrm that the data ﬁle received
from the client is complete and accurate. Second, the user can ensure that
the data have been read correctly by the receiving database. Veriﬁcation of
client data is generally accomplished through one or more of the following
procedures:
■ Obtain a printout of the ﬁrst 100 rows and match them onscreen to the
data ﬁle
■ Compute totals for key data ﬁelds (that is, invoice amount) and agree
them to control totals supplied by the client
■ Calculate totals or statistics of the ﬁle (such as ﬁle size) to determine
whether the relative size of the activity appears reasonable
■ Check the sequence (such as check numbers, inventory part numbers,
or invoice numbers) for gaps and duplicates
■ Select a sample of data items and trace the information to client records
Any exceptions, unreconciled amounts, or other indications of prob-
lems should be resolved before applying any automated procedures to the
provided data.
Core Data Analysis Functions5
It is useful to understand the data analysis tools at one’s disposal to
review data housed within a database. While most reporting software can

---

## Page 296

Chapter = c23 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:42 am
270 Cost Recovery
accomplish these tasks, here are a few vendors that can complete these
functions.
■ Microsoft SQL server/Access/Excel—microsoft.com
■ ActiveData for Excel—informationactive.com/ad/?aid=200
■ ACL software—acl.com
■ IDEA software—caseware-idea.com
The following is a list of common features provided by the above soft-
ware. Please note that each software may call these features a different name
but for the most part can complete the task noted in the description.
Feature Description
Aging Produces aged summaries of data based on established
cut-off dates.
Append/Merge Combines two ﬁles with identical ﬁelds into a single ﬁle.
An example would be to merge two years’ worth of
accounts payable history into one ﬁle.
Calculated
Field/Functions
Creates a calculated ﬁeld (which can use a Field/Function
such as ABS for the absolute value of Functions the
ﬁeld) using data within the ﬁle. For example, the net
payroll pay to an employee could be recalculated using
the gross pay ﬁeld and deducting any withholding
taxes.
Cross Tabulate Allows one to analyze character ﬁelds by setting them in
rows and columns. By cross-tabulating character ﬁelds,
one can produce various summaries, explore areas of
interest, and accumulate numeric ﬁelds.
Digital Analysis/
Benford’s Law
Audit technology designed to ﬁnd abnormal duplications
of speciﬁc digits, digit combinations, speciﬁc numbers,
and round numbers in corporate data. Since the
objective is to ﬁnd abnormal duplications, auditors
need a benchmark that indicates a normal level of
duplication. Benford’s Law gives auditors the expected
frequencies of the digits in tabulated data. The premise
is that we would expect authentic and unmanipulated
data to exhibit these patterns. If a data set doesn’t
follow these patterns, this may be a cause for auditor
concern and review. More information can be found
on this topic at nigrini.com.
Duplicates Identiﬁes duplicate items within a speciﬁed ﬁeld in a ﬁle.
For example, this report could be used to identify
duplicate billings of invoices within the sales ﬁle.

---

## Page 297

Chapter = c23 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:42 am
Technology Used in Recovery Efforts 271
Export Creates a ﬁle in another software format (for example,
Excel or Word) for testing. An example would be to
export customer address information to Word for
mail-merging to customer conﬁrmation letters.
Extract/Filter Extracts speciﬁed items from one ﬁle and copies them to
another ﬁle, normally using an “if” or “where”
statement. Examples include extracting all balances
over a predeﬁned limit.
Gaps Identiﬁes gaps within a speciﬁed ﬁeld in a ﬁle. For
example, identify any gaps in check sequence.
Index/Sort Sorts a ﬁle in ascending or descending order. An example
would be sorting a ﬁle by Social Security number to
see if any blank or “999999999” numbers exist.
Join/Relate Combines speciﬁed ﬁelds from two different ﬁles into a
single ﬁle using key ﬁelds. This function is used to
create relational databases on key ﬁelds. It can also be
done in an unmatched fashion to identify differences
between data ﬁles.
Regression Regression analysis using statistical means to calculate a
dependent variable balance (such as net sales) based
on various independent variables (for example,
product purchases, inventory levels, number of
customers, and so on).
Sample Creates random or monetary unit samples from a
speciﬁed population
Statistics Calculates various statistics on a selected numeric ﬁeld.
These may be total positive items, negative items,
average balance, and so on
Stratify Counts the number and dollar value of records of a
population falling within speciﬁed intervals.
Stratiﬁcations also provide a useful view into the
largest, smallest, and average dollar transactions.
Summarize Accumulates numerical values based on a speciﬁed key
ﬁeld. An example would be summarizing travel and
entertainment expense amounts by employee to
identify unusually high payment amounts.
Additional data analysis functions that are more inherent but need to be
recognized include:
■ Data processing limits. For example, Microsoft Excel 2007 has roughly a
one-million-row limit of data that it can analyze. Other tools are limited
only by the size of the hard drive.

■ Data import functions. Some tools can read only PC-based ﬁles while
others can read ﬁles from various sources, including mainframe, PC, and
printed reports, where it will actually extract the data from a computer
version of the report into a tabular database.
■ User interface. Some database tools require a programming degree while
others have user interfaces that simplify the reporting process. For exam-
ple, the interface from ActiveData for Excel (Exhibit 23.2) is much
simpler than extracting duplicate payments in Microsoft Excel with mul-
tiple IF statements (that is, =IF(AND(A3=A2,B3=B2,C3=C2),“Y”,“N”)).
To summarize, once data are loaded into a database, the listed functions
can be executed to learn more about the data. The functions are further

■ Query

■ Graphing

Field/Functions, (3) Duplicates (4) Extract/Filter, (5) Export, (6) Gaps, (7)

queries that could be run include:
■ What duplicate invoice payments exist in the vendor payment table?
■ Who entered a majority of the invoice adjustments?
■ Where did the invoice activity post to the general ledger?
■ When were the most advertising payments made during the year?
■ Why do payments to a vendor not match the vendor’s system records
of company billings?
Data Mining Statistical Functions6
Data mining goes by many deﬁnitions, but for the purposes of this book,
data mining is any data analysis where the user is not querying data (asking
questions) but rather looking for trends in the data without knowing what
they are looking for in the ﬁrst place. This may sound confusing, but a few
examples should clarify the deﬁnition.
Many times, the simplest tests are the most powerful. In one case, a
company had never aged its accounts payable data with the positive and
negative amounts presented in separate agings. Before the separate aging
approach, it did a net dollar aging, which showed that it had an immaterial
amount of invoices older than one year. However, when it aged the invoices
separately, they identiﬁed millions of positive and negative amounts.
Unfortunately, while the positive amounts (payables) related to one set
of vendors, the negative amounts (receivables) related to a different group of
vendors. While the old payable balances weren’t of concern, the receivables,
some over six years old, represented uncollectible balances from vendors
that had been sitting on the company’s books. After further research, the
receivables related to wire payments that were found to have never been
reconciled and were effectively hidden year after year by the accounts
payable management. While running the aging report separately for pos-
itive and negative balances may be considered a routine exercise to some, it
was identiﬁed as a procedure only after statistics were run on the accounts
payable open invoice data ﬁle in which high incidences of negative balances
were identiﬁed. Hence, running statistical analysis of data ﬁles looking for
uncommon occurrences such as negative inventory prices or extraordinary
large invoice amounts for the company can identify recoveries that may not
show up on a speciﬁc query report.
Using one example software tool capable of producing such reports,
ActiveData for Excel, descriptive statistics, stratiﬁcations, agings, and
Benford’s Law analysis can be developed. While statistical analysis is one

---

## Page 300

Chapter = c23 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:42 am
274 Cost Recovery
of the tool’s features, ActiveData can do practically anything with an Excel
spreadsheet, including tests usually reserved for more advanced audit soft-
ware.
Here is an example of descriptive statistics and aging developed in a
few PC mouse clicks. Once ActiveData for Excel is installed (a 30-day free
trial is available at informationactive.com/ad/?aid=200), open the sample
workbook that comes with the product by selecting the ActiveData menu
item and then the Open ActiveData Sample Workbook, as in Exhibit 23.3.
With the spreadsheet open, select the Invoices tab and then the Descrip-
tive Statistics from the ActiveData menu item, as in Exhibit 23.4.
Then select the Amount ﬁeld and the Finished button to generate a
full list of statistics on the data, including positive values, negative values,
averages, and so on, as shown in Exhibit 23.5.
This produces the results shown in Exhibit 23.6.
After obtaining these statistics, one is better equipped to complete an
aging of the information, which can be done by selecting Age from the
ActiveData menu, as in Exhibit 23.7.
Then the Age dialog box can be updated with aging strata. Please note
that this box also provides the range of dates as well as the opportunity to
graph the results, as in Exhibit 23.8.
After this dialog has been updated, select Finished to view the results,
as in Exhibit 23.9.
Many people will quickly go to data mining summarizations (next sec-
tion), as they are comfortable with PivotTable functionality, which gained
popularity in Microsoft Excel. First, though, it is useful to run statistics on
data in an effort to see the entire forest in a few statistical terms.

Data Mining Other Summaries7
Given that data tables can be immense, they need to be slimmed down
to something workable. One approach here is to summarize these data by
who is entering the data or maybe by transaction type. This can help better
organize the data for analysis, thereby allowing the analyst to ﬁlter out what
is less needed for review in the ﬁeld. The following are a few categories for
summarization:
■ Contextual summary. For example, a retail operation runs the risk
of employees processing false customer returns and pocketing the
related cash. While this may be easy to detect in one store with three
employees, it’s almost impossible to detect if there are hundreds of
employees spread across multiple stores. By downloading sales activity
and summarizing the sale returns by employee, one can develop a

Other contextual summarizations include transaction type, vendor, loca-
tion, and zip code, to name a few.
■ Time trend. Time trending is probably the most widely used approach
to analyze ﬁnancial information, such as the classic review of account
balance changes between years. While such reports are regularly run
for ﬁnancial statement balances, the scope of these reports should be
extended to vendor purchases by year, customer sales by month, and
inventory adjustments by quarter, to name a few.
A sample summary report is explained, using Microsoft Excel and its
most popular summary feature, Pivot Tables. This example works to identify
high-dollar payments based on a vendor average payment. To do this, ﬁrst an
average vendor invoice payment needs to be calculated using a contextual

---

## Page 305

Chapter = c23 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:42 am
Technology Used in Recovery Efforts 279
summary on vendor. This can be done through the use of a Pivot Table in
Excel. Once calculated, one needs to associate the vendor average to the
payments to that vendor, which is done using the VLOOKUP() function.
This next step is a step beyond the contextual summary to more of a query.
Hence, this example shows how contextual summaries cannot only be used
on their own but also to aid additional query analysis.
To begin, open a vendor invoice payment table and highlight all the
data by selecting the box between the column A and row 1 (top left portion
of the worksheet). Then, go to the Tools menu and the Pivot Table and Pivot
Chart Report selection. Select OK at the ﬁrst presented screen, noting that a
Pivot Table is desired using Excel information. At Step 2 of the Pivot Table
Wizard, select Next, noting that the proper data range has been identiﬁed.
Then, at Step 3 of the Pivot Table Wizard, select Layout and drag the column
headings at the right of the screen to look like the screen in Exhibit 23.11.
In the above box on the right, the Sum of Invoice Amount ﬁeld may
start out as the Count of Invoice Amount when you ﬁrst attempt this maneu-
ver. To change it from Count to Average (so that the total invoice amount
dollars can be calculated) drag it over and then double click on the Count
of Invoice Amount. From here, simply change it to Average. To close the
Layout window, select OK and select Finish from the previous window.
Once complete, the view shown in Exhibit 23.12 will be created in a new
worksheet in the Excel workbook.

Now that the Pivot Table has been created from average values by ven-
dor, these values need to be copied to an area within the data sheet that
was used to produce this Pivot Table (the original vendor invoice payment
sheet in Excel). Therefore, in the new sheet for the Pivot Table, highlight
Columns A and B and select Edit, Copy from the menu bar. Then, go to
the vendor invoice sheet and select the cell to the right of the data. Go to
Edit, Paste Special from the menu bar and select Values. This will paste the
vendor number and average for each vendor in these columns to the right
of the data.
With the average values calculated and available in the vendor invoice
payment sheet, a VLOOKUP() can be added to carry the average value over
from the pivot table copied data to a new column (column E in Exhibit
23.13).
With the average posted next to each associated vendor payment (see
Exhibit 23.14), all that’s required is to create a calculation ﬁeld that multiplies
the average amount by two and deducts the invoice amount.

The data table could now be sorted on Column F to identify any negative

Text Mining8
When one thinks about data analysis, one will immediately think of analyz-
ing numbers, not text, although the analyzed text can be just as fruitful. Text
mining allows for querying and other data mining of text-based documents
within company computers and servers. These tools have been popularized
by the recent addition of Google Desktop (see google.com for more informa-
tion). Once it was released (and downloaded by millions of users), Microsoft
released its Windows Desktop Search product with similar functionality (it
is now a standard part of Microsoft Vista).
Business users like these tools because they can quickly ﬁnd data stored
deep within their hard drives (such as an e-mail with the words “Aunt
Matilda’s Party”). This could be used by professionals to manage their own
documents or turned on a company server to identify recovery opportuni-
ties within company documents. For example, a search for key terms such
as plug or balance adjustment may identify vendor adjustments to hide sav-
ings that should be passed on to the client. Alternatively, a search on the
client’s e-mail system related to procurement buyers may identify discount
programs that were not entered into the purchase order system but do rep-
resent valid discounts that the client should be maintaining with particular
vendors.
Advanced search tools will allow for more intensive search techniques
and can search through more data types than the standard set (i.e., beyond
Word, Excel, Adobe, HTML, and basic text pages). Other more advanced
tools will create a visual or graphical representation of the text. Please note
that the original documents can be hyperlinked so that they are available
for drill-down analysis. For example, a hyperlinked report could be created
of all key words that a recovery analyst is searching for, such asadjustment,
plug, discount, and so on, and then presented to the user similar to a Google
search for deeper digging and research.

---

## Page 308

Chapter = c23 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:42 am
282 Cost Recovery
External Databases Relationships
As discussed more fully in the Audit Fee Benchmarking chapter, external
databases of useful information can be tapped into (usually for a fee) for
identifying opportunities or potential issues at a client. In the audit fee
benchmarking example, the data can be used to identify price variances
between external audit ﬁrms completing a ﬁnancial statement audit. This
can be even tailored down to a local market where the price for an audit
of a $100 million retailer can be related to all other retailers of between $75
million and $100 million in size in the Houston, Texas, market (where the
retailer is located).
So, similar to external databases that have pricing information, other
external databases have vendor-related information for validation purposes
and to understand more about these vendors. The analysis may identify
opportunities for completing vendor audits of companies more prone to
error or fraud, given their ﬁnancial and market condition. On a more con-
tinual basis, new vendors (which are material to the organization and added
in the last month) could be due diligence reviewed to ensure they are a
valid business (not a shell company) and do not present any unnecessary
risks to the organization (that is, outstanding legal issues, major competi-
tor problems, and going concern announcements). Some products in this
arena also support watch lists so that targeted e-mails can be sent to the
audit team for any major changes affecting the business partner (for exam-
ple, hoovers.com). For those that are just starting out in this area, a simple
Google search can be completed of the vendor and possibly other research
on industry-associated Web sites, if applicable.
Assessing Vendors against Watch Lists9
As an example of this type of analysis, nobody today wants to ﬁnd out
her company is linked with terrorist organizations through a customer,
supplier, or employee relationship. The U.S. Treasury maintains a list of
names and addresses of such organizations for all to download from its
Web site.
With some simple queries in Microsoft Excel, Access, or other report
writer, this list can be compared to the company’s vendor lists.
The U.S. Treasury’s link to “Specially Designated Nationals” is
treas.gov/ofﬁces/enforcement/ofac/sdn/index.shtml. Probably the best for-
mat is the ASCII-delimited format for Windows computers: treas.gov/ofﬁces/
enforcement/ofac/sdn/sdallw32.exe. This will run an unzip ﬁle that will
place a plethora of data tables in a folder of a user’s choice on her PC.

---

## Page 309

Chapter = c23 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:42 am
Technology Used in Recovery Efforts 283
In this list are two ﬁles that are most useful: ADD.CSV and SDN.CSV. These
are comma-delimited ﬁles that automatically open in Microsoft Excel. The
ADD.CSV ﬁle is the addresses and the SDN.CSV are the names of the
Specially Designated Nationals. Since these ﬁles do not have column head-
ings (to tell what each column is in Excel), the names of the columns are
in the description ﬁle: treas.gov/ofﬁces/enforcement/ofac/sdn/dat
spec.txt.
For the most part, these column headings are easily understood on the basis
of their contents. (For example, in the SDN.CSV ﬁle, column B is clearly the
list of names of the Specially Designated Nationals.) These column headings
have been added to the image in Exhibit 23.15.
Unfortunately, the names and addresses are provided in two ﬁles instead
of one combined ﬁle. Therefore, to relate the information from the name
spreadsheet (SDN.CSV) to the address spreadsheet (ADD.CSV), a relation-
ship would need to be completed on column A (Ent
Num), which is the
unique number that ties together the names and addresses.
Though the next steps aren’t difﬁcult, they are complicated. While a
wealth of additional information can be downloaded in the aforementioned
data ﬁles, the focus of this exercise is on the names and addresses of the
Specially Designated Nationals. Therefore, the names are in SDN.CSV ﬁle
(column B) and the street address line is in the ADD.CSV ﬁle (column C).
These two columns can be compared using the VLOOKUP() function in
Excel after the TRIM(), UPPER(), and LEFT() functions are used. Here’s
what these functions do in a little more detail:
■ VLOOKUP()—Takes a cell’s value and tries to identify matches in a
column of data. If there’s a match, it will return a column next to the
matched column.
■ TRIM()—Will remove all spaces from a ﬁeld except between words.
■ LEFT()—Will take a speciﬁed number of characters from the cell, starting
from the leftmost section of the cell.
■ UPPER()—Formats a cell into uppercase.

These functions could be used to align the name ﬁeld from the SDN.CSV
ﬁle to the address in the ADD.CSV ﬁle, which can be done by placing
this function: =VLOOKUP(A1,SDN.CSV!A$1:B$3310,2,FALSE) in cell G1 and
then copying the formula down for the entire column G. (Just make sure
to have both the SDN.CSV and ADD.CSV ﬁles open when creating this
function.) The results can be seen in Exhibit 23.16.
With the names and addresses in the ADD.CSV ﬁle, it can be aligned to
an address ﬁle from a company using the following step process.
■ Step 1. Convert the ﬁeld to be matched (assume B2 is one address line
from the company’s supplier master ﬁle) using the functions given earlier
in the following order:
Cell C2 in Excel =UPPER(TRIM(LEFT(B2,8)))
The nested function above will take the ﬁrst eight characters of the
address line ﬁeld, take any spaces out of it at the beginning and end,
and turn it all to uppercase text. This normalizes the ﬁeld to make it
more likely to match given that people will input information into an
address line differently.
■ Step 2. Follow the same process in Step 1 for converting the address ﬁeld
from the Specially Designated Nationals list. This could be done in col-
umn B of the ADD.CSV ﬁle (deleting the Add
Num column’s contents,
which aren’t required for this analysis).
■ Step 3. Sort both columns you plan to match in Ascending order. In the
example discussed here, this would be the converted address from the
supplier master ﬁle (column C) and the Specially Designated Nationals
list (column B).
■ Step 4. Use the VLookup() function to match the normalized address
line cell (C2 in the current example) to the entire listing of Specially
Designated Nationals’ address lines. Do that with this function:
Cell D2 in Company Supplier Master ﬁle

## Page 311

Chapter = c23 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:42 am
Technology Used in Recovery Efforts 285
Here are the component deﬁnitions of the function:
■ VLOOKUP(C2). Matches the cell that is the normalized address line from
the company’s supplier master ﬁle.
■ ADD.CSV!$B$1:$C$6820, —This tells Excel to look within the ADD.CSV
Excel spreadsheet and the columns B1 through C6820 for the address
line.
■ 2—This tells Excel for the cell references B1 through C6820 to look
within column C and return the value in column C (the full address of
the SDN). Therefore, if this function returns a blank or N/A value, then
there was no match. If there is a match, the information in column C
for the matched row will be written in this cell.
■ FALSE—This tells Excel that the only matches that are useful are perfect
matches.
For more information on VLOOKUP, please see Microsoft’s Web
site: http://ofﬁce.microsoft.com/en-us/excel/HA010563201033.aspx?pid =
CL100570551033.
The previous explanation on the use of the VLOOKUP function might
seem complex to some, so there are alternatives to running this match.
Another approach is to review the ActiveData for Excel add-in to Microsoft
Excel (informationactive.com/ad/?aid=200), which automates the matching
process and will even complete a fuzzy match. Such close matches are
based on the difference in the number of characters for the ﬁeld being
reviewed. (In our examples, the address ﬁeld was the ﬁeld used for match-
ing.) For fuzzy matching examples, “123 Golf Course Dr.” and “123 Golf
Course Drive” would match because there’s only a three-character differ-
ence between the cells. Another example is “12-34 University Blvd.” and
“1234 University Blvd,” which shows a two-character difference with the
lack of punctuation in the second address listing.
ActiveData for Excel’s Merge Sheets function (from the ActiveData menu
item in Excel, select Sheet > Merge Sheets) uses the Levenshtein fuzzy
match to identify any matches between two ﬁelds that are between 0 and
10 characters in difference. (See Exhibit 23.17.)
This is a more ideal method to matching than the ﬁrst eight characters in
the address because the character differences can be anywhere in the ﬁelds
(such as the beginning, middle, or end).
Graphing
“A picture is worth a thousand words” is a well-known saying for good
reason, because graphs provide the ability for recovery analysts to quickly
see trends or outliers in their client’s data. While trends are interesting, it is

more the outliers that lead to additional analysis. The following are some
popular graphs and examples of what may be found when reviewing them:
■ Bar and line graphs . Identify months of increased activity within an
invoice payables stream that may be more prone to error than other,
less active months.
■ Pie chart. Identify top categories of spend, based on an analysis of the
invoice distribution to the general ledger, that are more likely to have a
recovery effort completed than other less material areas.
■ Radar. This graph is effective in showing vendors that meet a rating
scale for various categories such as a pentagon radar graph showing a
vendor that appears on up to ﬁve reports.
■ Scattergraphs. As further explained in the next section, a scattergraph is
made of various points, each with an X- and a Y-axis.
A useful reference text for creating charts and graphs in Microsoft Excel
(probably the most widely used product for business graphs) is Charts &
Graphs for Excel 2007 as written by Bill Jelen and available at mrexcel.com.
Making the Scattergraph10
Few people take advantage of scattergraphs, which can be the most power-
ful in showing outliers (a key reason for doing graphs in a recovery exercise

in the ﬁrst place). Scattergraphs can be developed for any data point that
has an X- and a Y-axis designation. For example, as shown in Exhibit 23.18,
more than 5,700 customers can be represented in one picture with outliers
quickly showing themselves. If one counts the dots in the boxed areas of
the graph, they will ﬁnd that as few as 30 customers out of 5,726 can be
quickly isolated for further analysis.
To begin developing the graph, the ﬁrst step is to receive from the client
a list of customer accounts with its total sales represented in amount and
count and volume form for the past two years. A difference column can then
be calculated for the count and volume difference between this and last year,
as well as an invoice amount difference for the difference in sales amounts
for the customer between this year and last year. With these data in one
Excel worksheet, the graph can be created by selecting Insert → Chart and
selecting the (XY) Scatter type graph. When prompted for the data range,
the invoice amount and count cells would be selected, including the titles
of the data. Once complete, select the Series tab to make sure it looks like
Exhibit 23.19, with the X range being the invoice amount data and the Y
range being the invoice count data.
Select the Next button to be brought to Step 3, where a chart title can be
added. It is also suggested that the Legend tab be selected so that the Show
legend dialog box can be deselected, thereby saving space on the graph.
After these adjustments, the Next button can be selected one last time to
