It’s uncanny that 5,726 unique data points are represented in the sample
scattergraph, and even more so that the vast majority (close to 5,700) cluster
around the center axis of the graph. This leaves 30 exceptional customer
accounts to review more closely because these represent the outlier cus-
tomer accounts from a value or volume change perspective over the past
two years.
Continuous Monitoring
Internal auditors are those auditors that operate within a company to review
and test internal controls that could relate to ﬁnancial reporting, managing
ﬁnancial assets, or identify cash recoveries. Traditionally, internal audit-
ing’s testing of controls has been performed on a retrospective and cyclical
basis, often many months after business activities have occurred. The testing

---

## Page 315

Chapter = c23 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:42 am
Technology Used in Recovery Efforts 289
procedures have often been based on a sampling approach and included
activities such as reviews of policies, procedures, approvals, and reconcili-
ations. Today, however, it is recognized that this approach affords internal
auditors only a narrow scope of evaluation, and is often too late to be of real
value to business performance or regulatory compliance. Continuous audit-
ing is a method used to perform control and risk assessments automatically
on a more frequent basis.
11
Technology is key to enabling such an approach. Continuous auditing
changes the audit paradigm from periodic reviews of a sample of transac-
tions to ongoing audit testing of 100 percent of transactions. It becomes an
integral part of modern auditing at many levels. It also should be closely tied
to management activities such as performance monitoring, balanced score-
card, and enterprise risk management (ERM). A continuous audit approach
allows internal auditors to fully understand critical control points, rules, and
exceptions. With automated, frequent analyses of data, they are able to per-
form control and risk assessments in real time or near real time. They can
analyze key business systems for both anomalies at the transaction level and
for data-driven indicators of control deﬁciencies and emerging risk. Finally,
with continuous auditing, the analysis results are integrated into all aspects of
the audit process, from the development and maintenance of the enterprise
audit plan to the conduct and follow-up of speciﬁc audits.
12
Continuous controls monitoring has taken many forms and deﬁnitions
over the past few years. The need for multiple versions ensues from the
actors completing the analysis (i.e., auditors, management, regulators, and
so on) who have a different need for the resulting analysis. This has led
to various names, including continuous auditing, continuous assurance,
continuous monitoring, and continuous risk assessment. Further confusion
surrounded what constituted continuous because the time ranges varied
from one second to one year.
Internal auditors are not the only ones who can beneﬁt from continuous
auditing, as many external recovery ﬁrms have begun to implement contin-
uous technology tools within their clients. In that way, the client is able to
be an internal auditor even if she does not technically work in that depart-
ment. Generally speaking, such continuous tools would be installed in the
accounts payable or travel and entertainment areas but could be installed
anywhere a company wants to continuously monitor its internal controls
(for cash recoveries).
For a sample application of this technology, almost any company could
beneﬁt from a duplicate payment report or a ghost employee report (identi-
fying employees who were paid from payroll but do not exist in the human
resources database). The automated tool should support an easy user inter-
face that would allow for the data ﬁle and associated ﬁelds to be selected
for analysis. Then, at the push of a button, the reports should be executed

---

## Page 316

Chapter = c23 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:42 am
290 Cost Recovery
with the results saved in the software product or exported to a spreadsheet
for further analysis. Many times, these reports provide 80 percent coverage
of what a user needs in completing a review of an area, with the ﬁnal 20
percent based on a customized analysis, given the client’s precise needs.
13
While these reports could be developed internally, many companies do
not have staff who are competent to build such reports and then maintain
them. Another approach could be to hire a consultant to assist in the devel-
opment process, yet it is probably most efﬁcient to start with the automated
tool sets that are available in the marketplace. Regardless of the approach,
automated routines save the time in building and maintaining quality tests.
Such time savings provide more time to the user to analyze and understand
the data in conjunction with the business rather than spending time running
reports. Given that the goal is to maximize analysis of the data (rather than
running reports), automated routines can help a user along in ensuring that
their time is maximized.
14
As an example (see Exhibit 23.20) service provider, ACL Services pro-
vides continuous control monitoring solutions that are multiplatform capable
and can work in conjunction with all sources of business data—ERP systems
(including SAP, Oracle, and PeopleSoft), and mainframe and custom-built
applications—whether data entry and transaction processing are outsourced
or internally managed. Organizations can quickly realize net gains to bottom-
line results by proactively identifying opportunities to reduce operational
costs, minimize proﬁt erosion, and mitigate the risk of fraud, while driving
down the cost of compliance. ACL Continuous Controls Monitoring for the
EXHIBIT 23.20 ACLCCMWebView

---

## Page 317

Chapter = c23 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:42 am
Technology Used in Recovery Efforts 291
Purchase-to-Payment Cycle provides a turnkey suite of monitoring analyt-
ics designed to uncover critical control exposures within the requisitions,
purchasing, receiving, payables, and payments processes.
When thinking of the beneﬁts of continuous auditing, the following
come to mind:
15
■ Increased focus on control testing. By organizing control tests into
a system, it displays commitment to the cause of completing control
testing, which increases the probability of managing the tests on a con-
sistent basis. In other words, “We bought it, now we better use it” can
become the mantra of a continuous controls monitoring system. Unlike
ad hoc reporting that is simply that, ad hoc, continuous controls moni-
toring becomes an operational process that is managed just like accounts
payable processing.
■ Moving to a proactive detection mind-set. The focus of much of the
Sarbanes-Oxley work completed by companies in the past few years
was on preventive controls. While every business process needs some
preventive controls, the reality is that they can generally be more expen-
sive, inefﬁcient, and complicated to maintain. For instance, if we wanted
to stop ﬁres in our house, we could hide every match, lock down every
heat-producing device, and stop electricity from entering the home.
Then we could monitor to ensure that none of these deviants enter
the home. While the new controls would work to ensure not even a
spark would occur, it would be an unlivable home, where the occu-
pants spend more time fretting that a ﬁre may erupt than actually living.
Relating this analogy to a company, internal controls can be enacted at
every turn of a business process to help prevent errors and fraud, yet it
may make for an inefﬁcient and unwieldy process... one that is sure to
be overridden by the process owners.
Another approach to managing ﬁre is to be reasonably careful not
to leave ﬂammable materials lying around and installing an effective
ﬁre alarm system. This system could even be monitored by a security
company that can call the ﬁre department if you are not home to smell
the smoke. With this established, all that is needed is to make sure
the alarm batteries work every three months, performing a system test
a few times a year, and then waiting for the bell to ring. This more
reasonable ﬁre control model assumes that, on average, ﬁres will not
automatically erupt and, in turn, constantly analyzes the data (smoke
and heat), reacting only when there is an issue. The same could be said
for business processes in which associated data could be constantly
analyzed, triggers could be set for sending reports to management and
the audit function, and the system itself could be tested on a quarterly
basis to ensure it is still working properly.

---

## Page 318

Chapter = c23 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:42 am
292 Cost Recovery
The alarm system approach is considered a proactive detection
approach for rooting out errors and fraud, which has even gained sup-
port from the caretakers of the new Sarbanes-Oxley regulation, the
PCAOB. Its Auditing Standard 2 states, “The auditor should evaluate
whether to test preventive controls, detective controls, or a combination
of both for individual relevant assertions related to individual signiﬁ-
cant accounts. For instance, when performing tests of preventive and
detective controls, the auditor might conclude that a deﬁcient preven-
tive control could be compensated for by an effective detective control
and, therefore, not result in a signiﬁcant deﬁciency or material weak-
ness. For example, a monthly reconciliation control procedure, which
is a detective control, might detect an out-of-balance situation resulting
from an unauthorized transaction being initiated due to an ineffective
authorization procedure, which is a preventive control. When determin-
ing whether the detective control is effective, the auditor should evaluate
whether the detective control is sufﬁcient to achieve the control objec-
tive to which the preventive control relates.” Therefore, as long as the
detective control is effective in catching wrongdoing, and performed in
a timely manner to ensure accurate ﬁnancial statement reporting, it can
be used as a control in support of a company’s Sarbanes-Oxley com-
pliance. What’s more, the cost of such compliance, both in cash and
personnel time, is minimized.
■ Creates a uniﬁed system for analysis. One common condition in orga-
nizations is that their data are stored in disparate systems. For example,
companies could have different divisional general ledgers stored in var-
ious computer systems. A continuous controls monitoring system cares
only for the data from these systems that it normalizes into a common
format for processing. Once common, the same automated tests can be
run across the entire organizational data. Furthermore, disparate system
data can be analyzed in one system (i.e., matching employee to vendor
data that are stored in different systems and formats).
■ Errors and fraud live in 1 percent of transactions. While systematic errors
can be found through sampling a population and large variances should
be identiﬁed in analytical procedures, detecting actual recoveries is gen-
erally more difﬁcult through standard audit procedures because it can
be seen as too small. Take most of the large ﬁnancial statement frauds
in the past few years. Most were due to topside journal entries, some-
times hidden through nefarious means such as posting entries under
an approval limit and doing so across various business entities within
the company. Using this small-dose approach, management was able
to fool the auditors, who relied mainly on manual testing and sam-
pling. It is no surprise then that a study completed by COSO noted that
the most common problem in 80 percent of ﬁnancial statement fraud

---

## Page 319

Chapter = c23 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:42 am
Technology Used in Recovery Efforts 293
cases was the auditor’s failure to gather sufﬁcient audit evidence. Fur-
thermore, aside from ﬁnancial statement fraud, asset misappropriation,
the most common type of fraud, had a median loss of only $93,000,
according to the Association of Certiﬁed Fraud Examiners Report to the
Nation study, a ﬁgure far under the scope of most auditors. Therefore,
such small variances will be effectively detected only through 100 per-
cent analysis of the company data, not through sampling or high-level
analytics.
Pre-Formatted Workpapers/Process Workﬂow/Client Management
Dashboards
While a majority of this chapter has been focused on the ubiquitous need
for data analysis throughout the recovery process, it would be an oversight
to not mention the value of preformatted workpapers and process workﬂow
that is built into recovery ﬁrms over time. Again, as discussed previously,
most recovery ﬁrms start their process with pencil and paper and then pos-
sibly move to a set of Excel spreadsheets. Such spreadsheets not only help
them manage their work effort but also any client status reports (also known
as client dashboards). Only the very few will further organize their process
ﬂow into a database that manages their entire process. It must be again
noted that by building workpapers that organize the data succinctly and in
a consistent format, recovery efforts are expedited, as it takes less time to
identify cost recoveries in the ﬁeld. This saves personnel time on standard
audit tasks and also allows the recovery ﬁrm to focus on ﬁner recovery areas
that it normally would not have had the time to focus on if the process ﬂow
did not provide the opportunity. Another beneﬁt is simply the paper and
postage cost reduction, as normally, nothing needs to be printed once it is
organized into an automated workpaper system.
Process workﬂow technology relative to cost recovery is generally is
seen in the following areas:
16
■ Audit planning. Based on the risk and opportunity assessment, audit
plans can be set for the coming review based on the areas with the
most opportunity and least risk.
■ Audit documentation management. This is where all documents asso-
ciated with an audit are managed, which would include instructions on
how to complete the audit step for that workpaper. Changes can then be
made and, if needed, a rollback to previous iterations of documents can
be executed. Once complete, workpapers can be signed off on by the
staff and manager. All the while, workpapers can be cross-referenced so

---

## Page 320

Chapter = c23 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:42 am
294 Cost Recovery
that an audit trail can be achieved within the workpapers that explain
the achievement of the engagement objectives. One tricky aspect of
document management is to ensure that attachments such as spread-
sheets, word processing documents, ﬂowcharts, and invoice images can
be properly managed by the database. Many databases will work with
a document management system so that the database is not affected by
the large size of these attachments. If it is expected that attachments will
be over one gigabyte in size, however, or there is a desire to track revi-
sions to documents, it may be best to pair the audit workpaper system
with a document management system. Most companies use an intranet
or a document management service such as Microsoft’s SharePoint plat-
form for such a purpose, and when they want to refer to a document,
they do so with a hyperlink to the document. Essentially, the document
is stored with a Web service (be it internal to the company or through
an external partner) rather than in the actual database.
■ Project management/resource planning/time management. Once a plan
has been set, engagements need to be tracked from many per-
spectives.
■ Project deliverables. Deliverables and key milestones are completed
on time. Earned value can also be calculated, which is equivalent to
a percentage of completion.
■ Human resources. Resources are not overloaded or staying idle, but
rather are being optimized in the ﬁscal period plan. Furthermore,
resources are not simply applied on the basis of availability, but
also their competency in completing the assigned tasks at hand. To
track resource allocation to project, a time management component
is included so that personnel can track their time by engagement and
task at that engagement.
■ Budget and actual costs. Costs associated with the engagement are
tracked and reconciled to established budgets. To track expenses
associated with a project, travel and entertainment can be entered
by employees and summarized for analysis in relation to the bud-
get. Other costs (e.g., vendor invoices) can also be tracked through
an entry screen for basic invoice information, such as the invoice
amounts.
■ Findings management. With issues identiﬁed in an engagement stored
in a database, company resources can be assigned to remedy the issues.
Therefore, rather than rely on to-do notes and e-mails, a workﬂow can
be established that:
■ Presents a personalized dashboard of all alerts for the user to follow
up on key database events (e.g., audit ﬁndings not responded to by
the client or vendors).

---

## Page 321

Chapter = c23 Date: July 2, 2009 Time: 11:42 am
Technology Used in Recovery Efforts 295
■ E-mail alerts to speciﬁc parties based on critical actions that need to
be taken. Rather than relying on the pull of a dashboard, such alerts
are pushed to the appropriate user for action.
■ Supports a workﬂow that has alternate pathways for alerts that are not
dealt with in the proper time frame (e.g., alerting a higher authority of
the issue if a cash recovery follow-up with a vendor is not completed
in a timely fashion).
■ Summarizes in reports the status of all open ﬁndings for client report-
ing and internal management reporting.
One example product that comes to mind in the area of ﬁndings man-
agement is WizCount, which is a product from WizSoft, Inc. (wizsoft.com). It
focuses squarely on account reconciliations, which is mostly done between
the client’s account of what was paid to a vendor and the vendor’s records.
When running the account reconciliation, WizCount ﬁnds which transactions
reconcile and which transactions remain open (unreconciled), and thus are
the ﬁndings for review.
When searching for possible reconciliations, there are many methods
for matching transactions. The method selection is based on user-deﬁned
criteria for calculating the reconciliation mark or score. When only one pos-
sibility has a high mark, it is selected as a proper reconciliation (this is an
automatic reconciliation). When several possibilities have high marks, how-
ever (and therefore the program cannot determine which one is the proper
reconciliation), WizCount displays all the reconciling possibilities and the
user selects the proper reconciliation. Examples on how the transactions
can be scored include the maximum difference in days between the trans-
action dates in the books and the transaction dates in the vendor statements
and a number of characters in the remarks or description ﬁeld of the trans-
action on both the client’s and vendor’s records. A view of the WizCount
product can be seen in Exhibit 23.21.
■ Best practices management. Best-practice tests and recommendations
to clients are developed over time as engagements are completed. This
is a way of managing the knowledge gained on audits so that person-
nel who completed the audit can train others completing similar future
audits.
Conclusion
Everywhere one looks, information is being digitized. For example, whereas
vendor invoice imaging was only for the rich and famous, now companies
that do not use such technology are seen as behind the curve. The author

296 Cost Recovery

himself even uses a product called NeatReceipts, which allows all invoices
associated with his small business to be quickly scanned and categorized in
a database. At year end, when tax returns need to be completed, a simple
report from this system is all that is needed and if there is any question on
any one purchase, rather than going to a shoebox of receipts, the scanned
image can be automatically called up for review.
With this digitization comes the opportunity for data analysis. While a
central focus for the majority of this chapter, there is a reason. Essentially,
the goal of much of the technology in the cost recovery arena is simply to
better organize data into a database. Once there, it can be more quickly
analyzed, understood, and acted upon to ﬁnd additional recoveries for the
client.

CHAPTER 24
Conclusion
Where Do We Go from Here
If this publication was effective, the reader should be able to now consider
more than 15 areas to recover past proﬁts and establish processes to ensure
any future proﬁts are not leaked to errors and fraud. While this is a great
number of areas to start with, it is by no means a complete list, as ideas for
recovering past proﬁts and holding on to future ones grows by the day. Just
by working with recovery ﬁrms myself, I have seen how a new accidental
ﬁnding at a client leads them to consider an entirely new business model.
The trick is that they keep looking, day in and day out, to help companies
capture savings.
What Else Can You Do?
To stay on top of cost recovery, check out ﬁndmillions.net. The goal of the
Web site is to make it easier for companies to better understand the nature
of recovery ﬁrms and to get started saving money. It will point you to:
■ Comprehensive list of providers
■ Recovery library
■ E-zine
■ Training events
■ Free recovery locator
■ Other services
As any recovery ﬁrm will tell you, the sooner you start, the sooner you
save. Of course, the longer you wait, the better chance you have of never
recovering anything. So there is no reason to wait, especially given that
almost every cost recovery service works under a contingency model.
298

my free assessment service, whereby in about 15 minutes I can help to risk
assess your best opportunities and identify new areas for asset recovery
previously not considered by your company. Please note that we do the
work for you to track down the information you need to decide on which
proﬁt recovery service is best for your situation. Given that there are so
many providers, it only makes sense to have us help you prioritize and pick
the best provider for your needs. Also note that we will not have any vendor
contact you unless you express interest in talking to a speciﬁc vendor.
I hope that you continue on in your cost recovery efforts and if I can be
of any assistance, please contact me to help you assess your opportunities.

Appendix 3
Records Retention and Right
to Audit
These two contractual clauses should be adjusted for your company and
added to key vendor contracts to ensure records are properly maintained
and audits can be performed by the company.
Retention of records.During the term of this Agreement and for three (3)
years thereafter, Agency shall retain all records relating to Company’s
account including, but not limited to, hard copies of all invoices, signed
client authorizations to perform and purchase work, releases, legal clear-
ances, assignments, rights, samples of all assignments/advertisements,
contracts, papers, correspondence, accounts; electronic accounting, job,
and time records from Agency’s accounting software system(s); and
other information in Agency’s possession relating to this Agreement and
Agency’s business (Agency Records).
Where commercially feasible, all contracts with third-party suppli-
ers shall contain the record retention and audit requirements contained
in this section and the Audit of Records section, permitting Company
to obtain and access such records to the same extent permitted by
Company herein with regard to Agency Records.
Audit of records. Company or its appointed representative shall have
the right to review Agency’s books, records and accounting practices as
they pertain to services rendered to Company, which Agency agrees to
make available to Company, within ﬁve (5) business days of Company
providing Agency with written notice. Agency agrees to provide Com-
pany or the selected representative with access to the relevant records
(electronic and hard copy) and shall provide complete cooperation with
audit requests.
Source: Advertising Audit & Risk Management (aarmusa.com).
312

---

## Page 339

Chapter = Appendix03 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:44 am
Appendix 3 313
During the term of this Agreement and for three (3) years thereafter,
Agency shall maintain accurate and complete hard-copy and electronic
records speciﬁcally relating to Agency’s ﬁnances and obligations here-
under in accordance with generally accepted accounting principles
and practices, consistently applied and in a format that shall easily
permit audit. For purposes of an audit, Company shall, at its option,
request Agency provide reports and data ﬁles generated directly from
its accounting software system (System-Generated Reports) to review
such records, instead of non-System-Generated Reports. Company shall
have the right to inspect, copy and audit either directly or through its
authorized representatives or agents, in person or by electronic access,
including, without limitation, all books, records, ledgers, accounts,
accounting software data ﬁles, correspondence, instructions, speciﬁ-
cations, plans, drawings, receipts, manuals, memoranda, production
charges, media placement charges, in-house charges, employee expense
reports (including third-party support), travel charges (including outside
travel agency), talent costs, fees, time records and accounting practices
pertinent to this Agreement.
Notwithstanding the above, Agency shall not be required to provide
or make available to Company for such audit its individual payroll or
salary data (Salary Costs). However, Company may request lump-sum
Salary Costs by Agency department by job function expended on its
account. At Company’s request and direction, Agency agrees to have
its independent auditors test such salary data and report in writing to
Company, that all actual Salary Costs reported by Agency to Company
on each annual fee reconciliation calculation or other document using
Salary Costs as a component are accurate. The independent auditors
shall not be required to share any individual payroll or salary data of
Agency with Company.
It is expressly understood that Agency maintains a cost accounting
system, which includes time-tracking and reporting by employee, as
well as the proﬁtability of the Company account. Agency shall provide
Company with at least ninety (90) days’ written notice prior to any subs-
tantive changes in Agency’s time-tracking and or cost accounting system.
If any records audit reveals that Agency has overcharged Company,
Agency shall immediately refund such overcharge (Overcharge) to Com-
pany. If the Overcharge is greater than ﬁve percent (5%) of the billed
charges in any consecutive twelve-month period, then Company’s audit
expenses shall be reimbursed to Company by Agency. If Agency has not
retained or cannot produce Agency Records which Company requests
and as a result thereof Company is required to pay a ﬁne, penalty
or other monetary amount (Penalty Payment), Agency shall reimburse
Company the Penalty Payment and any other costs incurred by Com-
pany as a result of Agency’s failure to retain Agency Records for the
required time period.

Appendix 4 315
Purchasing Payables and Payments
Organizations are increasing the automation of their purchasing, payables,
and payments processes to reduce costs, gain efﬁciencies, and support com-
pliance with regulatory requirements and business policies.
Even the most sophisticated enterprise systems may not incorporate all
of the controls necessary to ensure complete operational effectiveness or
guard against error, misuse, and fraud. Users of such systems are adept at
ﬁnding ways around built-in application controls to keep up with demand-
ing workloads. Less scrupulous users are even more skilled at exploiting
areas of weakness, given the opportunity. Compounding the issue, built-in
application controls are time-consuming and costly to maintain, and rarely
keep up with business processes or regulatory changes.
These issues, combined with high volumes of transactions, increased
levels of operational complexity, and an often diverse range of IT platforms
and systems, make managing the purchase-to-payment cycle challenging.
Understanding which transactions are critical to review for potential risk
makes monitoring controls even more difﬁcult.
Increased regulation—in particular Section 404 of the Sarbanes-Oxley
Act—have further increased organizational workloads. Scarce accounts
payable resources have been redeployed to assist in documenting and test-
ing internal controls. While organizations have made signiﬁcant stafﬁng and
ﬁnancial investments to meet these compliance requirements, the level of
effort is not sustainable year over year, and management must look to other
solutions to enable ongoing compliance with regulatory demands.
Accuracy and reliability can suffer in this context. A recent study by the
Institute of Management and Administration (IOMA) found that 4.6 percent
of invoices contain errors and 44 percent of companies pay without the orig-
inal invoices. Also, the error rate appeared to be unaffected by automation
and was spread uniformly across organizations of all sizes.
1
The ﬁnancial consequences of payment errors, duplicate payments,
and vendor and employee fraud can run into millions of dollars in losses
each year. The consequences of noncompliance with increased regulatory
requirements can be even more severe. Organizations face the poten-
tial consequences of harsh ﬁnes, negative publicity, damage to corporate
brand and reputation, and loss of investor and consumer conﬁdence and
trust.
Financial executives responsible for compliance and business perfor-
mance optimization must ensure effective controls are in place within the
purchase-to-payment cycle. With the ACL AuditExchange 2009 platform,
1Institute of Management and Administration, USA (IOMA) AP Department Bench-
marks and Analysis, 2007.

---

## Page 342

Chapter = Appendix02 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:47 am
316 Appendix 4
organizations can use analytic scripts to identify potential control weak-
nesses and inefﬁciencies that may jeopardize regulatory compliance and
directly affect bottom-line performance.
ACL AuditExchange 2009
ACL AuditExchange 2009 is the world’s ﬁrst managed analytics platform
for audit. Designed speciﬁcally for audit teams, it dramatically improves
productivity and performance. By capturing and storing critical data and
analytic scripts in one secure, central location, it enables your whole team
to share and reuse that information more effectively and efﬁciently.
Running analytic scripts on the AuditExchange platform provides orga-
nizations with an independent mechanism to automatically monitor the
effectiveness of their internal controls. Using market-leading ACL analytic
technology, organizations can drive down the cost of regulatory compliance
and minimize the ﬁnancial risks stemming from control gaps, weaknesses,
and unauthorized controls changes.
ACL scripts can be automated to run on a repetitive or continuous basis
and uncover critical control exposures within the requisition, purchasing,
receiving, payables, and payment processes. Scripts can be designed to
support the Committee of Sponsoring Organizations (COSO) framework,
recognized as the internal controls standard for supporting Sarbanes-Oxley
Section 404 compliance.
Automating scripted analytics on the AuditExchange platform allows
organizations to take the point-in-time analysis and interrogation of data
performed during traditional audit reviews and embed them in an organiza-
tion’s day-to-day operations. Run automatically on a continuous basis, these
analytics identify suspicious activity, errors, and exceptions that may be dis-
guised through the structuring and processing of transactions over time or
hidden within high data volumes.
Prompt notiﬁcation of control exceptions and anomalies, based on com-
plex transactional analyses against speciﬁed control parameters and business
rules enables quick action in response to variances in expected activity. Man-
agement can then be notiﬁed of control breaches, quickly review quantiﬁed
exposure of business risk, and investigate and resolve potential problems
before they escalate. As a result, organizations can better assure compliance,
contain costs, and minimize losses.

ACL Analytics Technology
Building on more than 20 years of experience working with the audit and
controls professions, ACL’s analytic scripts leverage market proven technol-
ogy to extend best practices in controls compliance and transaction analysis
expertise to core business processes such as the purchase-to-payment cycle,
billing and receivables, payroll, inventory management, and general ledger
reconciliation.
ACL’s market-leading analytics technology can rapidly access and
analyze unlimited volumes of data with seamless data extraction and aggre-
gation from virtually any enterprise application while maintaining data
integrity. Powerful ACL analytics uncover anomalies and isolate errors within
the transactional data underlying business processes and ﬁnancial reporting.
This results in comprehensive insight into control weaknesses and
potential business risks, enabling sound business decisions and faster
response to organizational, market, and regulatory changes.

Customer Successes
A national retail organization implemented ACL analytics technology to
conduct quarterly reviews of its unexercised discounts. Within the ﬁrst six
months of operation, the company identiﬁed $2 million in discounts not
taken. Through timely identiﬁcation, in-house resources were able to recover
the funds faster, improve cash ﬂow, and save an estimated $600,000 in fees
that would normally have been paid to a third-party cost recovery ﬁrm.
Advertising Audit & Risk Management (AARM) relies on ACL analyt-
ics technology to recover 1 to 2 percent of its clients’ annual advertising
expenditures—a ﬁgure that often totals several million dollars.
A Fortune 500 power utility company uses ACL analytics technology to
enhance payment controls within both its existing business intelligence sys-
tem and its accounts payable procedures. By checking all payments for
duplicates, the organization identiﬁed $12.5 million in recoverable over-
payments. Through an independent check of data integrity, the analytics
detected changes in vendor names with no corresponding revisions to the
master vendor proﬁle, resulting in situations where the same invoice was
paid twice.
A global energy ﬁrm involved in frequent acquisitions of new compa-
nies uses ACL analytics technology to continuously monitor for duplicate
payments during postmerger system integration. Providing the data access
necessary to compare invoices from legacy systems in the acquired com-
panies to the parent company’s own ERP system, ACL analytics initially
identiﬁed up to 20 duplicate invoices a day.
A large multinational manufacturer uses ACL analytics technology to
compare pricing for common consumables purchased across the organiza-
tion. One common item was found to have a unit purchase cost ranging
over a factor of 100, pointing to a signiﬁcant undiscovered weakness in the
organization’s procurement strategy.
Client Beneﬁts
Support for Sarbanes-Oxley Section 404 Compliance
With demands for higher standards of corporate governance and greater
ﬁscal transparency, continuous controls monitoring of business transactions
provides an increased level of assurance and conﬁdence to management,
auditors, board directors, shareholders, and other key stakeholders. Sec-
tion 404 of the Sarbanes-Oxley Act requires management certiﬁcation and
external attestation that the internal controls and procedures underlying
ﬁnancial report preparation are effective. Analytic scripts running on the

---

## Page 346

Chapter = Appendix02 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:47 am
320 Appendix 4
AuditExchange platform can automate internal controls testing in key ﬁnan-
cial and operational processes across the enterprise through transaction
analysis at the source level, enabling organizations to gain independent and
comprehensive assurance of the effectiveness of internal controls.
ROI through Improved Cost Recovery
The longer issues such as duplicate payments and vendor or employee
fraud are left undetected, the greater their ﬁnancial impact and the harder
the recovery process. Analytic scripts running on the AuditExchange plat-
form can enable organizations to rapidly identify (and subsequently correct)
sources of error, support faster and more comprehensive recovery by inter-
nal resources, and realize savings on fees and commissions to external cost
recovery ﬁrms (which typically charge from 20 to 50 percent of the recovered
amounts). The result is increased conﬁdence and assurance in organizational
business processes and immediate improvement of bottom-line results.
Adaptability for a Changing Environment
Every organization’s controls and operational policies are unique—even in
common business processes such as the purchase-to-payment cycle. The
ﬂexible nature of AuditExchange means that analytic script parameters can
be altered as circumstances change—for example, when purchasing author-
ity or approval limits change, staff take on new or expanded responsibilities,
or business risk proﬁles demand more frequent testing of key controls.
Controls Assurance in Outsourcing Arrangements
Outsourcing of ﬁnance and accounting processes—including those in the
purchase-to-payment cycle—is increasingly seen as an opportunity for orga-
nizations to cut costs and gain efﬁciencies. But shifting the how and the
where of data entry and transaction processing does not reduce man-
agement’s responsibilities for the effectiveness of internal controls, good
governance, and compliance readiness. Analytic scripts running on the Audi-
tExchange platform can help organizations gain visibility into the internal
controls of their outsourcing partners to independently verify compliance
with their own business rules and policies.
Company Overview
ACL Services Ltd. is the leading global provider of technology for audit
and compliance professionals. Combining market-leading audit analytics
software and professional services expertise, ACL solutions give auditors

conﬁdence in the effectiveness of internal controls and the integrity of the
transactions underlying business operations.
Since 1987, ACL has enabled auditors to assure sustainable compliance,

formance. ACL delivers its solutions to more than 215,000 licensed users

## Page 348

Chapter = c05 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:49 am
Appendix 5
Tapping the Strategic Potential
of Procurement
F
ew business functions play a more critical role across the entire global
organization than procurement. And for a select group of leading com-
panies, procurement has become a strategic, proactive organization that
contributes to proﬁtability, growth, and competitive advantage. Here’s how
it’s done.
If you can imagine the pleasant surprise of an executive who ﬁnds that a
little-noticed business unit is contributing hundreds of millions to the bottom
line, you’ll understand the reaction of many of today’s CEOs who come to
fully understand the potential of their procurement function to generate
signiﬁcant business returns.
That’s right, you read correctly: procurement. That often anonymous
function—with the traditional mission of obtaining high-quality goods and
services for the lowest possible cost—has become a strategic, proactive orga-
nization with the potential of contributing as much as, or more than, other
business functions to proﬁtability, growth, and competitive advantage.
Procurement began to have a higher proﬁle during the most recent
global economic downturn, when senior management started paying atten-
tion to how much value and cost savings were hidden in all the material
and supplies companies must purchase as part of the cost of doing business.
In fact, for the typical company, procurement costs can represent anywhere
from 50 percent to 75 percent of annual revenues, so even small reductions
in that cost base can have a big impact. One research study by the Aberdeen
Group found that it would take a $5 increase in sales to equal the impact of
a $1 reduction in procurement costs.
1
This appendix was written by Gregory S. Spray, the executive director of solution
strategy for Accenture’s customer contact, ﬁnance and accounting, and procurement
BPO services.
322

---

## Page 349

Chapter = c05 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:49 am
Appendix 5 323
So, although few executives today would view procurement as they
might have 20 or 30 years ago—merely as a cost center with mostly clerical
responsibilities—most companies continue to undervalue and underopti-
mize their procurement functions. However, based on Accenture research
and experience, and on the insights of several leading chief procurement
ofﬁcers, we are able to highlight the strategies and approaches of a select
group of leading companies that have successfully unlocked the secrets
of driving high performance through the procurement and supply chain
functions.
At the heart of procurement’s potential to generate value is its inherent
connectedness to the rest of the business. Ideally designed and enabled,
procurement not only has visibility but serves a critical role across an entire
global organization: to oversee the basic tools and processes that different
areas of the business are putting in place to serve customers, manage suppli-
ers, and develop new products and services, and to point all those resources
toward optimal business ends.
Reconceived not merely as a valve controlling the optimal inﬂux of
goods and services but also as a distributor of organizational value—as an
arbitrator or collaborator connecting parts of the business, creating new
synergies—procurement ends up being placed in a powerful position to
guide strategic direction and support better business decisions.
Steve Welch, former chief procurement ofﬁcer of AT&T, believes that in
light of procurement’s potential, its mission should be redeﬁned. “The new
mission is to create and convert supply chain opportunities into bottom-
line opportunities that meet the strategic needs of their clients,” says Welch.
“That is, the proper domain now for procurement is the sum of supply
chain objectives of the corporation’s strategic business units, not just the
cost-reduction objectives of the procurement function. If you can connect
to that entire supply chain, it unleashes business value.”
Paul Massih, global head of downstream procurement for energy giant
Chevron Corporation, puts it this way, with a nod to the long-running adver-
tising campaign for the BASF corporation: “We don’t make the business
decisions—we help the business make those decisions better.”
Lower cost, higher productivity, and more effective operations are the
hallmarks of procurement mastery, which, in turn, has a demonstrable link
to high performance.
A recent Accenture study
2 about this link shows a clear pattern of
distinctive capabilities—one of the building blocks of high performance
according to Accenture’s ongoing research program—among the high per-
formers. Ten percent of the surveyed organizations warrant “master” status
in regard to their procurement achievements. These organizations exceed
the survey average across the board—in strategy, technology, sourcing and
category management, requisition-to-pay, supplier relationship manage-
ment, and workforce and organization.

---

## Page 350

Chapter = c05 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:49 am
324 Appendix 5
On $1 billion of controlled, normalized investment, procurement masters achieve 30 percent
higher savings with costs that are 50 percent lower.

EXHIBIT A5.1 ProcurementMasters
Source: “High Performance through Procurement: Accenture Research and Insights
into Procurement Performance Mastery,” Accenture, June 2007. Copyright © 2009
Accenture. All rights reserved.
Procurement mastery pays off for these high performers. They exceed
the average in a number of key areas: total cost of ownership (TCO); total
controllable spend; TCO reduction as a percentage of procurement oper-
ating cost; leverage in new product design and introduction; and supplier
management.
The masters have procurement productivity levels that are 30 percent
higher than those of lower-performing companies. Yet their procurement
organizations typically cost only half as much to run. Based on controlled
normalized spend from one year to the next, procurement masters save
almost 10 times as much as it costs them to operate their procurement orga-
nizations. This is more than four times the savings realized by companies
that have not achieved procurement mastery. (See Exhibit A5.1.)
Challenges
Yet even companies that excel in procurement face signiﬁcant challenges
in their ongoing efforts to outpace the competition. Visibility, business

---

## Page 351

Chapter = c05 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:49 am
Appendix 5 325
acumen, and reputation constitute one set of challenges. Because procure-
ment has traditionally been perceived primarily as a support function, it has
not enjoyed much status in the corporate hierarchy. “The only experience
that lifts you out of that position,” notes former chief procurement ofﬁcer
Welch, “is to deliver strategic value to the company. If you’re not doing that,
you’re just performing.”
Procurement professionals thus need more general business skills as
a foundation for delivering greater value. They also need ways to get
out and see how ideal processes are actually being performed in real
life.
One global procurement chief asks a provocative question: “As a pro-
curement professional, are you out with your sales force, for example, and
interacting with the customer to see how general policies translate into real
behaviors? Are you following along with supply and product deliveries to
see how things are really operating?” She notes that historically, procure-
ment people “are not trained and rewarded to stay close to the customer
and to the workers actually performing the processes. Yet that up-close-and-
personal approach is the only way that we can transform procurement into
a value-generating enterprise.”
Getting the basics right is critical. As Chevron’s Massih notes, “Procure-
ment begins with making sure the basic transactional processes are efﬁcient
and reliable, supporting the business twenty-four/seven/three sixty-ﬁve.”
That’s the price of admission, says Massih. “If you can’t get that right, the
rest of the value-generation goal doesn’t matter.”
Getting those transactional processes right is not easy, given the pres-
sures on supply capacity. “The marketplace continues to be relentless in
terms of shop capacities and manufacturing capacities,” Massih continues.
“Prices may have ﬂattened out a little bit, but the capacities are just not
there. So many companies are looking at ﬁfteen to twenty-ﬁve percent
schedule overruns just because they cannot secure the necessary sup-
plies. So if a CPO could develop better anticipatory capabilities—having
the analytics in place to let the business know, ‘Hey, here’s why
your business project is at risk’—that value would be almost immea-
surable.”
Massih also feels that the global talent shortage is likely to be felt acutely
in the procurement ﬁeld, especially at the entry to mid-line level. Speciﬁc
skills are also a concern, from sourcing to supplier management to category
management to commercial contracting.
“We currently cannot recruit enough qualiﬁed people in the United
States,” Massih says. “So we are looking to other places, such as India and
China. We need to hire multiple thousands of people each year to match
our growth needs and the normal loss from attrition, so we have to take a
global approach to ﬁlling that need.”

---

## Page 352

Chapter = c05 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:49 am
326 Appendix 5
Key Success Factors
The experiences of CPOs working for companies that have mastered
procurement point to a list of consistent success factors in creating a pro-
curement organization that helps drive high performance.
Collaborating across the Business
If the new mission of the procurement function is “helping the business
make its decisions better,” procurement must have the processes and skills
in place to understand the company’s critical business functions.
Another sourcing and procurement executive, who works for a major
U.S. entertainment and media company, emphasizes how critical collabora-
tion is. “You have to be collaborative with the business,” he says. “You have
to demonstrate that you understand their needs at least as well as they do.
And you need to be proactive—you need to go to them with an idea and a
business case, and not wait for them to come to you.”
“The CPOs that I admire the most today,” says Chevron’s Massih, “have
the ability to speak the business language, to collaborate, to work across
functional teams at the highest levels in the organization, and then translate
all that into a supply chain strategy. That kind of CPO is able to balance the
business needs and the supply market.” Procurement professionals who will
succeed in that kind of environment will do so, he adds, “not only because
of the hard skills that they bring to the table—category management and
supply management and so forth—but also because of their knowledge of
the business and their skills in communication and leadership.”
Yet a collaborative mindset is not enough. New programs and tools are
needed to translate those mindsets into practical innovations.
One of the capabilities Chevron has introduced is a market intelligence
group that works both with and across the business functions, collaborating
with them to ﬁnd opportunities for greater efﬁciencies. For example, when
the company looked into its steel purchasing, recalls Massih, “We discov-
ered that we had more than one hundred people working on steel pricing
around the world—amounting to an annual spend exceeding seven hundred
and ﬁfty million dollars—but there was no real collaboration among them.
By establishing a market intelligence group, we’ve provided the means for
greater visibility and collaboration across the company—driving millions of
dollars of value to the company’s bottom line.”
Establishing Trusted Supplier Relationships
Because the traditional us-versus-them mentality between a company and
its suppliers can be a serious impediment to procurement transformation,
collaboration also has a critical external dimension. Procurement masters are

---

## Page 353

Chapter = c05 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:49 am
Appendix 5 327
able to establish closer and more trusted relationships with their suppliers,
based on a deep knowledge of their business and on a mind-set that says
that the optimal functioning of every part of a value chain is important to
the performance of the whole.
Establishing that deeper relationship may take some time, however.
Kevin Smith, who is in charge of the global supply management and pur-
chasing function for Delphi Corp., a mobile electronics and transportation
systems company, notes that many organizations are now in a difﬁcult sit-
uation as they approach suppliers, promoting a collaborative mindset and
asking them to work together to create business value. “Now that companies
have wrung out all the price concessions they could from their suppli-
ers, they now say they want to collaborate,” notes Smith. “But, with some
justiﬁcation, suppliers don’t trust their buyers.”
Dave Nelson, the former CPO of Deere & Company and Honda Motor
Co.’s U.S. manufacturing division, among other companies, and the author
of several books on purchasing and supply chain management, tells the
story of a supplier fair panel discussion that featured U.S. and Japanese auto
manufacturers. “Someone in the audience asked the panelists to explain the
conditions under which they would terminate a supplier,” relates Nelson.
“Both the U.S. companies said that they publish monthly reports showing
how suppliers stack up against their competitors. If suppliers show up in
the underperforming section of that report and remain there too long, they
will eventually be released.
“Then the Japanese representative spoke up: ‘We’ve never ﬁred a sup-
plier in our entire history. If a supplier is not meeting our expectations, it
means we’ve made a mistake, too; so we go to them and see what they
need. Do they need different skill sets? Better processes? If so, we’ll help
them develop or improve those skills and processes so we can bring them
up to our expectations.”’
Concludes Nelson: “That commitment to the success of the entire
extended enterprise, including business partners and suppliers, is not the
entire story of why the Japanese, and not the U.S., now have the world’s
leading auto manufacturer. But it’s one big part of the story.”
Using Outsourcing to Extend the Value-Producing Potential of Procurement
Another increasingly popular form of collaboration used to extend the value-
generating ability of procurement organizations is outsourcing. As Nelson
says, “For companies that have, say, $500 million or more in direct spend,
the opportunities today to leverage the capabilities of a procurement out-
sourcing provider are something to be closely considered.”
Outsourcing is an especially important option for companies that
may not have a high-level professional procurement program in place.

---

## Page 354

Chapter = c05 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:49 am
328 Appendix 5
Looking at the six key transaction management functions at the heart of procurement,
procurement masters are more likely to outsource or contract out such activities than are low
performers. 28%

EXHIBIT A5.2 OutsourcingandProcurementMastery
Source: “High Performance through Procurement: Accenture Research and Insights
into Procurement Performance Mastery,” Accenture, June 2007. Copyright © 2009
Accenture. All rights reserved.
Accenture research into procurement mastery found that across the board
of procurement activities—from order management to helpdesk to cata-
logue management and goods receiving—procurement masters were far
more likely to engage outsourcing service providers. (See Exhibit A5.2.)
In Nelson’s view, companies looking to transform their procurement
function have an important decision to make: “Are they going to go after
the management experience and the category-level expertise needed to
become world class? Are they going to create a system themselves that
is going to handle procurement across different regions and countries,
and different kinds of businesses? Those companies need to compare the
business case for internal capability development versus an outsourcing
option.”
Chief procurement ofﬁcers who have leveraged outsourcing as part
of their overall transformation strategy usually recommend a phased
approach—demonstrating to the business that outsourcing can meet its
needs in one area and then expanding from there.
“If I’m running a large global operation,” says Nelson, “it’s unlikely I’m
going to start out applying outsourcing to all businesses, everywhere around
the world. I’m more likely to take one manufacturing plant or one type of
commodity and see if outsourcing works for that.” This approach offers more

---

## Page 355

Chapter = c05 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:49 am
Appendix 5 329
control over the risk and the impact. “If it works, proving the business case
and getting take-up in other areas would not be much of an effort. Other
business leads will be coming to me asking how they can replicate the initial
trial success in their business or region.”
Combining Process Excellence with Technology Innovation
Our research points strongly to process excellence as a key to maximizing
the value of procurement. As Steve Welch notes, “Up to this point, procure-
ment organizations have achieved a great deal of cost savings simply by
manipulating markets, buyers, and suppliers. Now we’ve about drained that
well. We are moving toward a more process-oriented approach—changing
processes and designs, becoming more involved in engineering and prod-
uct design.” Becoming more involved in downstream processes—growing
contractors and suppliers into the procurement space, and helping increase
suppliers’ capabilities—is essential.
At the same time, CPOs need to look at new technology solutions
to help digitize the entire supply chain. The analytic, insight-generating
technology capabilities available today are especially important. Chevron’s
Massih points out that procurement must remain agile and ﬂexible. “This will
allow procurement to change at a rate equal to or faster than the business.
You have to be able to predict through demand management and plan-
ning, and by digitizing the supply chain. Those technological capabilities
can help procurement professionals distill information that can identify true
procurement-related business insights.”
For his part, Steve Welch anticipates new, Web-based desktop tools that
will make information available for every procurement manager, enabling
them to see the current status of the ﬂow of what he calls “supply chain
events, not just ﬁnancial ﬁgures.”
An executive who heads procurement for a major European ﬁnancial
institution concurs, noting that business intelligence technologies offer the
promise of propelling procurement toward its value-generating potential.
“As the supply chain and purchasing functions become more intelligent,”
says this executive, “with an infrastructure in place attuned to supply chain
events, procurement could become a real-time function, with companies
optimizing the value chain rapidly, and maybe even paying for things only
as they need them.”
Making a Commitment to Talent Development
As procurement has evolved, so have the skills needed to run and man-
age procurement toward optimal business ends. The skills have become
less tactical and more business-oriented. Developing the talent needed to
continue propelling procurement toward its true potential can no longer

---

## Page 356

Chapter = c05 Date: June 30, 2009 Time: 11:49 am
330 Appendix 5
be left to chance. Focused programs geared toward nurturing procurement
capabilities must be put into place.
Rotating promising employees through a variety of functional jobs,
including procurement, is one practice that has worked for a number of
companies. Chevron, for example, instituted a program in which the com-
pany takes the top 20 percent of its younger managers, segments them, and
then rotates them through different business functions.
“We attempt to put our best procurement people out into the business on
an 18-month cycle,” explains Massih. “In turn, other business functions rotate
their top performers into procurement on the same cycle. The learning curve
is very steep everywhere these people go, but that’s part of the excitement
and the challenge. The broad sweep of experience these younger managers
get is invaluable. They know more, they understand more, and our initial
analysis suggests they are more likely to stay with Chevron because they are
more engaged in our business.”
Fulﬁlling Potential
If procurement is to fulﬁll its business potential, it must transform itself from
a support function to a value-generating function. And the way it does that
is by getting as close to the business as it can. Procurement should focus
on the business, not on supply relationships. The procurement organization
brings the analytic skills, the view across the enterprise, and the supply
relationships to provide critical strategic direction.
In the long term, executives looking to drive high performance through
their procurement organization should consider the process, technology,
and workforce development initiatives that create procurement mastery.
The best news for procurement executives today is that there is great
untapped potential in any function that has been underused from the stand-
point of innovation. Mastering procurement drives value and leverage that
has not been used strategically by companies. And that means that procure-
ment is particularly ripe for transformation.

Cost ReCoveRy
RiChaRd B. Lanza
Lanza
Pantone Black PMS 173 C GLOSSY 
Praise for 
Cost ReC oveR y
“Is your company leaking cash without even knowing it? Lanza’s easy-to-read 
book gives practical points on how to increase profits with little or no added cost. 
This is the corporate equivalent of finding money on the sidewalk; just reach 
down and pick it up!”
—Joseph T. Wells, CFE, CPA, founder and Chairman,  
Association of Certified Fraud Examiners 
“Rich pours a great deal of his extensive knowledge into this guide. It is an 
encyclopedic approach to the hows and whys of cost recovery services. Not only 
does the reader learn about the basic mechanics of how cost recovery works, but 
also has access to numerous case studies showing how it works in key areas such 
as payables, advertising, health benefits, leasing, and telecommunications. The 
guide may prove to be the masterwork in this field.”
—Steven M. Bragg, CFO, Xedar Corporation
“An easy action reference tool on how reduced disbursements can increase income 
significantly more than an equivalent rise in sales. Your ROI on this book will 
exceed any other investment!”
—David H. Margulies, former Senior Managing Director at  
Bear Stearns, responsible for Controller’s Shared Services
“In an economy where revenue streams are constricted, using Cost Recovery 
techniques to find money or save money seems like an obvious strategy, but it’s 
perceived as a daunting task for most organizations. Rich Lanza’s Cost Recovery

ow every organization can generate cash  
recoveries, stop profit leaks, move away 
from control issues, and work toward process 
improvements with the tools found in Cost 
Recovery: Turning Your Accounts Payable Depart-
ment into a Profit Center. Authoritatively showing 
how to incorporate profit recovery technology  
to achieve stellar bottom-line results, this  
insightful and highly readable book provides  
step-by-step guidance for completing a cost 
recovery effort of any company’s spending.
Audit technology expert Richard Lanza clearly 
explains how to utilize free services offered by cost 
recovery consultants as well as how to assess risk 
based on the company’s control issues to identify  
the top areas of likelihood for recovery and process 
improvements. This important book looks at 
cost recovery from every angle, with in-depth 
coverage of areas including telecommunications, 
utilities, healthcare, advertising, media, freight, 
tax reduction, project fraud, and more.
Filled with illustrative case studies, Cost Recovery: 
Turning Your Accounts Payable Department into a 
Profit Center is a time-saving road map revealing:
• Why every company should consider 
recovery services 
• How to overcome obstacles to 
implementation of cost recovery
• The opportunities—and risks—
involved in recovery 
ISBN: 978-0-470-32238-3
• How the cost recovery market works 
and which trends affect it 
• How to select the best possible 
provider
• What the cost recovery auditor does
• The recovery technology used by firms 
to focus their testing efforts 
The only book available to look holistically at the 
cost recovery process, Cost Recovery: Turning Your 
Accounts Payable Department into a Profit Center 
makes it easier for companies to better understand 
the nature of recovery firms and to start saving 
money . . . before it disappears.
RICHARD B. LANZA , 
CPA, CFE, PMP ,  is the 
President of Cash Recovery 
Partners, LLC, the author  
of twelve publications and,  
over 100 articles for major  
audit and accounting publications. With nearly 
two decades of experience in audit technology 
and recovery auditing, he has become a leading 
authority in these areas. He also provides training 
courses in audit software and recovery, including 
forty-three speaking engagements in the last 
eighteen months. He has consulted on how to 
save money through the use of technology and 
recovery auditing for companies ranging in size 
from $30 million to $30 billion.
